   <TITLE>Chapter 16</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 16</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>There was one point which Anne, on returning to her family,



<P>would have been more thankful to ascertain even than Mr Elliot's



<P>being in love with Elizabeth, which was, her father's not being



<P>in love with Mrs Clay; and she was very far from easy about it,



<P>when she had been at home a few hours.&nbsp; On going down to breakfast



<P>the next morning, she found there had just been a decent pretence



<P>on the lady's side of meaning to leave them.&nbsp; She could imagine

Mrs Clay



<P>to have said, that "now Miss Anne was come, she could not suppose herself



<P>at all wanted;" for Elizabeth was replying in a sort of whisper,



<P>"That must not be any reason, indeed.&nbsp; I assure you I feel it none.



<P>She is nothing to me, compared with you;"&nbsp; and she was in full

time



<P>to hear her father say, "My dear madam, this must not be.&nbsp; As yet,



<P>you have seen nothing of Bath.&nbsp; You have been here only to be useful.



<P>You must not run away from us now.&nbsp; You must stay to be acquainted



<P>with Mrs Wallis, the beautiful Mrs Wallis.&nbsp; To your fine mind,



<P>I well know the sight of beauty is a real gratification."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He spoke and looked so much in earnest, that Anne was not surprised



<P>to see Mrs Clay stealing a glance at Elizabeth and herself.



<P>Her countenance, perhaps, might express some watchfulness;



<P>but the praise of the fine mind did not appear to excite a thought



<P>in her sister.&nbsp; The lady could not but yield to such joint entreaties,



<P>and promise to stay.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In the course of the same morning, Anne and her father chancing to be



<P>alone together, he began to compliment her on her improved looks;



<P>he thought her "less thin in her person, in her cheeks; her skin,



<P>her complexion, greatly improved; clearer, fresher.&nbsp; Had she been



<P>using any thing in particular?"&nbsp; "No, nothing."&nbsp; "Merely Gowland,"



<P>he supposed.&nbsp; "No, nothing at all."&nbsp; "Ha! he was surprised

at that;"



<P>and added, "certainly you cannot do better than to continue as you are;



<P>you cannot be better than well; or I should recommend Gowland,



<P>the constant use of Gowland, during the spring months.&nbsp; Mrs Clay

has been



<P>using it at my recommendation, and you see what it has done for her.



<P>You see how it has carried away her freckles."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>If Elizabeth could but have heard this!&nbsp; Such personal praise



<P>might have struck her, especially as it did not appear to Anne



<P>that the freckles were at all lessened.&nbsp; But everything must



<P>take its chance.&nbsp; The evil of a marriage would be much diminished,



<P>if Elizabeth were also to marry.&nbsp; As for herself, she might always



<P>command a home with Lady Russell.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell's composed mind and polite manners were put to some trial



<P>on this point, in her intercourse in Camden Place.&nbsp; The sight of

Mrs Clay



<P>in such favour, and of Anne so overlooked, was a perpetual provocation



<P>to her there; and vexed her as much when she was away, as a person in

Bath



<P>who drinks the water, gets all the new publications, and has



<P>a very large acquaintance, has time to be vexed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As Mr Elliot became known to her, she grew more charitable,



<P>or more indifferent, towards the others.&nbsp; His manners were



<P>an immediate recommendation; and on conversing with him she found



<P>the solid so fully supporting the superficial, that she was at first,



<P>as she told Anne, almost ready to exclaim, "Can this be Mr Elliot?"



<P>and could not seriously picture to herself a more agreeable



<P>or estimable man.&nbsp; Everything united in him; good understanding,



<P>correct opinions, knowledge of the world, and a warm heart.



<P>He had strong feelings of family attachment and family honour,



<P>without pride or weakness; he lived with the liberality of a man of

fortune,



<P>without display; he judged for himself in everything essential,



<P>without defying public opinion in any point of worldly decorum.



<P>He was steady, observant, moderate, candid; never run away with by spirits



<P>or by selfishness, which fancied itself strong feeling; and yet,



<P>with a sensibility to what was amiable and lovely, and a value



<P>for all the felicities of domestic life, which characters of



<P>fancied enthusiasm and violent agitation seldom really possess.



<P>She was sure that he had not been happy in marriage.&nbsp; Colonel Wallis



<P>said it, and Lady Russell saw it; but it had been no unhappiness



<P>to sour his mind, nor (she began pretty soon to suspect) to prevent

his



<P>thinking of a second choice.&nbsp; Her satisfaction in Mr Elliot



<P>outweighed all the plague of Mrs Clay.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was now some years since Anne had begun to learn that she



<P>and her excellent friend could sometimes think differently;



<P>and it did not surprise her, therefore, that Lady Russell



<P>should see nothing suspicious or inconsistent, nothing to require



<P>more motives than appeared, in Mr Elliot's great desire of a reconciliation.



<P>In Lady Russell's view, it was perfectly natural that Mr Elliot,



<P>at a mature time of life, should feel it a most desirable object,



<P>and what would very generally recommend him among all sensible people,



<P>to be on good terms with the head of his family; the simplest process



<P>in the world of time upon a head naturally clear, and only erring



<P>in the heyday of youth.&nbsp; Anne presumed, however, still to smile

about it,



<P>and at last to mention "Elizabeth."&nbsp; Lady Russell listened, and

looked,



<P>and made only this cautious reply:-- "Elizabeth! very well;



<P>time will explain."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was a reference to the future, which Anne, after a little observation,



<P>felt she must submit to.&nbsp; She could determine nothing at present.



<P>In that house Elizabeth must be first; and she was in the habit



<P>of such general observance as "Miss Elliot," that any particularity



<P>of attention seemed almost impossible.&nbsp; Mr Elliot, too,



<P>it must be remembered, had not been a widower seven months.



<P>A little delay on his side might be very excusable.&nbsp; In fact,



<P>Anne could never see the crape round his hat, without fearing that



<P>she was the inexcusable one, in attributing to him such imaginations;



<P>for though his marriage had not been very happy, still it had existed



<P>so many years that she could not comprehend a very rapid recovery



<P>from the awful impression of its being dissolved.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>However it might end, he was without any question their



<P>pleasantest acquaintance in Bath:&nbsp; she saw nobody equal to him;



<P>and it was a great indulgence now and then to talk to him about Lyme,



<P>which he seemed to have as lively a wish to see again, and to see more

of,



<P>as herself.&nbsp; They went through the particulars of their first meeting



<P>a great many times.&nbsp; He gave her to understand that he had



<P>looked at her with some earnestness.&nbsp; She knew it well;



<P>and she remembered another person's look also.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They did not always think alike.&nbsp; His value for rank and connexion



<P>she perceived was greater than hers.&nbsp; It was not merely complaisance,



<P>it must be a liking to the cause, which made him enter warmly



<P>into her father and sister's solicitudes on a subject which



<P>she thought unworthy to excite them.&nbsp; The Bath paper one morning



<P>announced the arrival of the Dowager Viscountess Dalrymple,



<P>and her daughter, the Honourable Miss Carteret; and all the comfort



<P>of No. --, Camden Place, was swept away for many days; for the Dalrymples



<P>(in Anne's opinion, most unfortunately) were cousins of the Elliots;



<P>and the agony was how to introduce themselves properly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne had never seen her father and sister before in contact with nobility,



<P>and she must acknowledge herself disappointed.&nbsp; She had hoped



<P>better things from their high ideas of their own situation in life,



<P>and was reduced to form a wish which she had never foreseen;



<P>a wish that they had more pride; for "our cousins Lady Dalrymple



<P>and Miss Carteret;" "our cousins, the Dalrymples," sounded in her ears



<P>all day long.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sir Walter had once been in company with the late viscount,



<P>but had never seen any of the rest of the family; and the difficulties



<P>of the case arose from there having been a suspension of all intercourse



<P>by letters of ceremony, ever since the death of that said late viscount,



<P>when, in consequence of a dangerous illness of Sir Walter's



<P>at the same time, there had been an unlucky omission at Kellynch.



<P>No letter of condolence had been sent to Ireland.&nbsp; The neglect



<P>had been visited on the head of the sinner; for when poor Lady Elliot



<P>died herself, no letter of condolence was received at Kellynch,



<P>and, consequently, there was but too much reason to apprehend



<P>that the Dalrymples considered the relationship as closed.



<P>How to have this anxious business set to rights, and be admitted



<P>as cousins again, was the question:&nbsp; and it was a question which,



<P>in a more rational manner, neither Lady Russell nor Mr Elliot



<P>thought unimportant.&nbsp; "Family connexions were always worth preserving,



<P>good company always worth seeking; Lady Dalrymple had taken a house,



<P>for three months, in Laura Place, and would be living in style.



<P>She had been at Bath the year before, and Lady Russell had heard her



<P>spoken of as a charming woman.&nbsp; It was very desirable that



<P>the connexion should be renewed, if it could be done, without any



<P>compromise of propriety on the side of the Elliots."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sir Walter, however, would choose his own means, and at last wrote



<P>a very fine letter of ample explanation, regret, and entreaty,



<P>to his right honourable cousin.&nbsp; Neither Lady Russell nor Mr Elliot



<P>could admire the letter; but it did all that was wanted,



<P>in bringing three lines of scrawl from the Dowager Viscountess.



<P>"She was very much honoured, and should be happy in their acquaintance."



<P>The toils of the business were over, the sweets began.&nbsp; They visited



<P>in Laura Place, they had the cards of Dowager Viscountess Dalrymple,



<P>and the Honourable Miss Carteret, to be arranged wherever they might



<P>be most visible:&nbsp; and "Our cousins in Laura Place,"--"Our cousin,



<P>Lady Dalrymple and Miss Carteret," were talked of to everybody.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne was ashamed.&nbsp; Had Lady Dalrymple and her daughter even been



<P>very agreeable, she would still have been ashamed of the agitation



<P>they created, but they were nothing.&nbsp; There was no superiority

of manner,



<P>accomplishment, or understanding.&nbsp; Lady Dalrymple had acquired



<P>the name of "a charming woman," because she had a smile and a civil

answer



<P>for everybody.&nbsp; Miss Carteret, with still less to say, was so plain



<P>and so awkward, that she would never have been tolerated in Camden Place



<P>but for her birth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell confessed she had expected something better; but yet



<P>"it was an acquaintance worth having;" and when Anne ventured to speak



<P>her opinion of them to Mr Elliot, he agreed to their being nothing



<P>in themselves, but still maintained that, as a family connexion,



<P>as good company, as those who would collect good company around them,



<P>they had their value.&nbsp; Anne smiled and said,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"My idea of good company, Mr Elliot, is the company of clever,



<P>well-informed people, who have a great deal of conversation;



<P>that is what I call good company."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You are mistaken," said he gently, "that is not good company;



<P>that is the best.&nbsp; Good company requires only birth, education,



<P>and manners, and with regard to education is not very nice.



<P>Birth and good manners are essential; but a little learning is



<P>by no means a dangerous thing in good company; on the contrary,



<P>it will do very well.&nbsp; My cousin Anne shakes her head.



<P>She is not satisfied.&nbsp; She is fastidious.&nbsp; My dear cousin"



<P>(sitting down by her), "you have a better right to be fastidious



<P>than almost any other woman I know; but will it answer?



<P>Will it make you happy?&nbsp; Will it not be wiser to accept the society



<P>of those good ladies in Laura Place, and enjoy all the advantages



<P>of the connexion as far as possible?&nbsp; You may depend upon it,



<P>that they will move in the first set in Bath this winter,



<P>and as rank is rank, your being known to be related to them



<P>will have its use in fixing your family (our family let me say)



<P>in that degree of consideration which we must all wish for."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes," sighed Anne, "we shall, indeed, be known to be related to them!"



<P>then recollecting herself, and not wishing to be answered, she added,



<P>"I certainly do think there has been by far too much trouble taken



<P>to procure the acquaintance.&nbsp; I suppose" (smiling) "I have more

pride



<P>than any of you; but I confess it does vex me, that we should be



<P>so solicitous to have the relationship acknowledged, which we may



<P>be very sure is a matter of perfect indifference to them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Pardon me, dear cousin, you are unjust in your own claims.



<P>In London, perhaps, in your present quiet style of living,



<P>it might be as you say:&nbsp; but in Bath; Sir Walter Elliot and his

family



<P>will always be worth knowing:&nbsp; always acceptable as acquaintance."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well," said Anne, "I certainly am proud, too proud to enjoy a welcome



<P>which depends so entirely upon place."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I love your indignation," said he; "it is very natural.



<P>But here you are in Bath, and the object is to be established here



<P>with all the credit and dignity which ought to belong to Sir Walter

Elliot.



<P>You talk of being proud; I am called proud, I know, and I shall not

wish



<P>to believe myself otherwise; for our pride, if investigated,



<P>would have the same object, I have no doubt, though the kind may seem



<P>a little different.&nbsp; In one point, I am sure, my dear cousin,"



<P>(he continued, speaking lower, though there was no one else in the room)



<P>"in one point, I am sure, we must feel alike.&nbsp; We must feel that



<P>every addition to your father's society, among his equals or superiors,



<P>may be of use in diverting his thoughts from those who are beneath him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He looked, as he spoke, to the seat which Mrs Clay had been



<P>lately occupying:&nbsp; a sufficient explanation of what he particularly

meant;



<P>and though Anne could not believe in their having the same sort of pride,



<P>she was pleased with him for not liking Mrs Clay; and her conscience



<P>admitted that his wishing to promote her father's getting



<P>great acquaintance was more than excusable in the view of defeating

her.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <TITLE>Chapter 02</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 2</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Mr Shepherd, a civil, cautious lawyer, who, whatever might be his hold



<P>or his views on Sir Walter, would rather have the disagreeable



<P>prompted by anybody else, excused himself from offering the slightest

hint,



<P>and only begged leave to recommend an implicit reference to



<P>the excellent judgement of Lady Russell, from whose known good sense



<P>he fully expected to have just such resolute measures advised as



<P>he meant to see finally adopted.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell was most anxiously zealous on the subject, and gave it



<P>much serious consideration.&nbsp; She was a woman rather of sound than

of



<P>quick abilities, whose difficulties in coming to any decision



<P>in this instance were great, from the opposition of two leading principles.



<P>She was of strict integrity herself, with a delicate sense of honour;



<P>but she was as desirous of saving Sir Walter's feelings, as solicitous



<P>for the credit of the family, as aristocratic in her ideas of what



<P>was due to them, as anybody of sense and honesty could well be.



<P>She was a benevolent, charitable, good woman, and capable of



<P>strong attachments, most correct in her conduct, strict in her notions



<P>of decorum, and with manners that were held a standard of good-breeding.



<P>She had a cultivated mind, and was, generally speaking,



<P>rational and consistent; but she had prejudices on the side of ancestry;



<P>she had a value for rank and consequence, which blinded her a little



<P>to the faults of those who possessed them.&nbsp; Herself the widow of



<P>only a knight, she gave the dignity of a baronet all its due;



<P>and Sir Walter, independent of his claims as an old acquaintance,



<P>an attentive neighbour, an obliging landlord, the husband of her



<P>very dear friend, the father of Anne and her sisters, was,



<P>as being Sir Walter, in her apprehension, entitled to a great deal



<P>of compassion and consideration under his present difficulties.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They must retrench; that did not admit of a doubt.&nbsp; But she was



<P>very anxious to have it done with the least possible pain to him



<P>and Elizabeth. She drew up plans of economy, she made exact calculations,



<P>and she did what nobody else thought of doing:&nbsp; she consulted Anne,



<P>who never seemed considered by the others as having any interest



<P>in the question. She consulted, and in a degree was influenced by her



<P>in marking out the scheme of retrenchment which was at last submitted



<P>to Sir Walter. Every emendation of Anne's had been on the side of



<P>honesty against importance.&nbsp; She wanted more vigorous measures,



<P>a more complete reformation, a quicker release from debt,



<P>a much higher tone of indifference for everything but justice and equity.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"If we can persuade your father to all this," said Lady Russell,



<P>looking over her paper, "much may be done.&nbsp; If he will adopt



<P>these regulations, in seven years he will be clear; and I hope



<P>we may be able to convince him and Elizabeth, that Kellynch Hall has



<P>a respectability in itself which cannot be affected by these reductions;



<P>and that the true dignity of Sir Walter Elliot will be very far from



<P>lessened in the eyes of sensible people, by acting like a man of principle.



<P>What will he be doing, in fact, but what very many of our first families



<P>have done, or ought to do?&nbsp; There will be nothing singular in his

case;



<P>and it is singularity which often makes the worst part of our suffering,



<P>as it always does of our conduct.&nbsp; I have great hope of prevailing.



<P>We must be serious and decided; for after all, the person who



<P>has contracted debts must pay them; and though a great deal is due to



<P>the feelings of the gentleman, and the head of a house, like your father,



<P>there is still more due to the character of an honest man."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This was the principle on which Anne wanted her father to be proceeding,



<P>his friends to be urging him.&nbsp; She considered it as an act



<P>of indispensable duty to clear away the claims of creditors with



<P>all the expedition which the most comprehensive retrenchments



<P>could secure, and saw no dignity in anything short of it.



<P>She wanted it to be prescribed, and felt as a duty.&nbsp; She rated



<P>Lady Russell's influence highly; and as to the severe degree



<P>of self-denial which her own conscience prompted, she believed



<P>there might be little more difficulty in persuading them to a complete,



<P>than to half a reformation.&nbsp; Her knowledge of her father



<P>and Elizabeth inclined her to think that the sacrifice of one pair



<P>of horses would be hardly less painful than of both, and so on,



<P>through the whole list of Lady Russell's too gentle reductions.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>How Anne's more rigid requisitions might have been taken



<P>is of little consequence.&nbsp; Lady Russell's had no success at all:



<P>could not be put up with, were not to be borne. "What! every comfort



<P>of life knocked off!&nbsp; Journeys, London, servants, horses, table--



<P>contractions and restrictions every where!&nbsp; To live no longer



<P>with the decencies even of a private gentleman!&nbsp; No, he would sooner



<P>quit Kellynch Hall at once, than remain in it on such disgraceful terms."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Quit Kellynch Hall."&nbsp; The hint was immediately taken up by Mr

Shepherd,



<P>whose interest was involved in the reality of Sir Walter's retrenching,



<P>and who was perfectly persuaded that nothing would be done without



<P>a change of abode.&nbsp; "Since the idea had been started in the very

quarter



<P>which ought to dictate, he had no scruple," he said, "in confessing



<P>his judgement to be entirely on that side.&nbsp; It did not appear to

him



<P>that Sir Walter could materially alter his style of living in a house



<P>which had such a character of hospitality and ancient dignity to support.



<P>In any other place Sir Walter might judge for himself; and would



<P>be looked up to, as regulating the modes of life in whatever way



<P>he might choose to model his household."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sir Walter would quit Kellynch Hall; and after a very few days more



<P>of doubt and indecision, the great question of whither he should go



<P>was settled, and the first outline of this important change made out.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There had been three alternatives, London, Bath, or another house



<P>in the country.&nbsp; All Anne's wishes had been for the latter.



<P>A small house in their own neighbourhood, where they might still have



<P>Lady Russell's society, still be near Mary, and still have the pleasure



<P>of sometimes seeing the lawns and groves of Kellynch, was the object



<P>of her ambition.&nbsp; But the usual fate of Anne attended her,



<P>in having something very opposite from her inclination fixed on.



<P>She disliked Bath, and did not think it agreed with her;



<P>and Bath was to be her home.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sir Walter had at first thought more of London; but Mr Shepherd felt



<P>that he could not be trusted in London, and had been skillful enough



<P>to dissuade him from it, and make Bath preferred.&nbsp; It was a much

safer



<P>place for a gentleman in his predicament:&nbsp; he might there be important



<P>at comparatively little expense.&nbsp; Two material advantages of Bath



<P>over London had of course been given all their weight:&nbsp; its more

convenient



<P>distance from Kellynch, only fifty miles, and Lady Russell's spending



<P>some part of every winter there; and to the very great satisfaction



<P>of Lady Russell, whose first views on the projected change had been



<P>for Bath, Sir Walter and Elizabeth were induced to believe that



<P>they should lose neither consequence nor enjoyment by settling there.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell felt obliged to oppose her dear Anne's known wishes.



<P>It would be too much to expect Sir Walter to descend into a small house



<P>in his own neighbourhood.&nbsp; Anne herself would have found



<P>the mortifications of it more than she foresaw, and to Sir Walter's



<P>feelings they must have been dreadful.&nbsp; And with regard to Anne's



<P>dislike of Bath, she considered it as a prejudice and mistake arising,



<P>first, from the circumstance of her having been three years



<P>at school there, after her mother's death; and secondly,



<P>from her happening to be not in perfectly good spirits the only winter



<P>which she had afterwards spent there with herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell was fond of Bath, in short, and disposed to think



<P>it must suit them all; and as to her young friend's health,



<P>by passing all the warm months with her at Kellynch Lodge,



<P>every danger would be avoided; and it was in fact, a change which must



<P>do both health and spirits good.&nbsp; Anne had been too little from

home,



<P>too little seen. Her spirits were not high.&nbsp; A larger society



<P>would improve them.&nbsp; She wanted her to be more known.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The undesirableness of any other house in the same neighbourhood



<P>for Sir Walter was certainly much strengthened by one part,



<P>and a very material part of the scheme, which had been happily



<P>engrafted on the beginning.&nbsp; He was not only to quit his home,



<P>but to see it in the hands of others; a trial of fortitude,



<P>which stronger heads than Sir Walter's have found too much.



<P>Kellynch Hall was to be let.&nbsp; This, however, was a profound secret,



<P>not to be breathed beyond their own circle.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sir Walter could not have borne the degradation of being known



<P>to design letting his house.&nbsp; Mr Shepherd had once mentioned the

word



<P>"advertise," but never dared approach it again.&nbsp; Sir Walter spurned



<P>the idea of its being offered in any manner; forbad the slightest hint



<P>being dropped of his having such an intention; and it was only on



<P>the supposition of his being spontaneously solicited by some most



<P>unexceptionable applicant, on his own terms, and as a great favour,



<P>that he would let it at all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>How quick come the reasons for approving what we like!&nbsp; Lady Russell

had



<P>another excellent one at hand, for being extremely glad that Sir Walter



<P>and his family were to remove from the country.&nbsp; Elizabeth had

been



<P>lately forming an intimacy, which she wished to see interrupted.



<P>It was with the daughter of Mr Shepherd, who had returned,



<P>after an unprosperous marriage, to her father's house, with



<P>the additional burden of two children.&nbsp; She was a clever young

woman,



<P>who understood the art of pleasing--the art of pleasing, at least,



<P>at Kellynch Hall; and who had made herself so acceptable to Miss Elliot,



<P>as to have been already staying there more than once, in spite of all



<P>that Lady Russell, who thought it a friendship quite out of place,



<P>could hint of caution and reserve.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell, indeed, had scarcely any influence with Elizabeth,



<P>and seemed to love her, rather because she would love her,



<P>than because Elizabeth deserved it.&nbsp; She had never received from

her more



<P>than outward attention, nothing beyond the observances of complaisance;



<P>had never succeeded in any point which she wanted to carry,



<P>against previous inclination.&nbsp; She had been repeatedly very earnest



<P>in trying to get Anne included in the visit to London, sensibly open



<P>to all the injustice and all the discredit of the selfish arrangements



<P>which shut her out, and on many lesser occasions had endeavoured



<P>to give Elizabeth the advantage of her own better judgement and experience;



<P>but always in vain:&nbsp; Elizabeth would go her own way; and never

had she



<P>pursued it in more decided opposition to Lady Russell than in



<P>this selection of Mrs Clay; turning from the society of so deserving



<P>a sister, to bestow her affection and confidence on one who ought



<P>to have been nothing to her but the object of distant civility.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>From situation, Mrs Clay was, in Lady Russell's estimate, a very unequal,



<P>and in her character she believed a very dangerous companion;



<P>and a removal that would leave Mrs Clay behind, and bring a choice



<P>of more suitable intimates within Miss Elliot's reach, was therefore



<P>an object of first-rate importance.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 03</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 3</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>"I must take leave to observe, Sir Walter," said Mr Shepherd



<P>one morning at Kellynch Hall, as he laid down the newspaper,



<P>"that the present juncture is much in our favour.&nbsp; This peace will



<P>be turning all our rich naval officers ashore.&nbsp; They will be



<P>all wanting a home.&nbsp; Could not be a better time, Sir Walter,



<P>for having a choice of tenants, very responsible tenants.



<P>Many a noble fortune has been made during the war.&nbsp; If a rich admiral



<P>were to come in our way, Sir Walter--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"He would be a very lucky man, Shepherd," replied Sir Walter;



<P>"that's all I have to remark.&nbsp; A prize indeed would Kellynch Hall



<P>be to him; rather the greatest prize of all, let him have taken



<P>ever so many before; hey, Shepherd?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Shepherd laughed, as he knew he must, at this wit, and then added--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I presume to observe, Sir Walter, that, in the way of business,



<P>gentlemen of the navy are well to deal with.&nbsp; I have had a little

knowledge



<P>of their methods of doing business; and I am free to confess that they



<P>have very liberal notions, and are as likely to make desirable tenants



<P>as any set of people one should meet with.&nbsp; Therefore, Sir Walter,



<P>what I would take leave to suggest is, that if in consequence of



<P>any rumours getting abroad of your intention; which must be contemplated



<P>as a possible thing, because we know how difficult it is to keep



<P>the actions and designs of one part of the world from the notice



<P>and curiosity of the other; consequence has its tax; I, John Shepherd,



<P>might conceal any family-matters that I chose, for nobody would think

it



<P>worth their while to observe me; but Sir Walter Elliot has eyes upon

him



<P>which it may be very difficult to elude; and therefore, thus much



<P>I venture upon, that it will not greatly surprise me if,



<P>with all our caution, some rumour of the truth should get abroad;



<P>in the supposition of which, as I was going to observe, since applications



<P>will unquestionably follow, I should think any from our wealthy



<P>naval commanders particularly worth attending to; and beg leave to add,



<P>that two hours will bring me over at any time, to save you



<P>the trouble of replying."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sir Walter only nodded.&nbsp; But soon afterwards, rising and pacing

the room,



<P>he observed sarcastically--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"There are few among the gentlemen of the navy, I imagine, who would



<P>not be surprised to find themselves in a house of this description."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"They would look around them, no doubt, and bless their good fortune,"



<P>said Mrs Clay, for Mrs Clay was present:&nbsp; her father had driven

her over,



<P>nothing being of so much use to Mrs Clay's health as a drive to Kellynch:



<P>"but I quite agree with my father in thinking a sailor might be



<P>a very desirable tenant.&nbsp; I have known a good deal of the profession;



<P>and besides their liberality, they are so neat and careful



<P>in all their ways!&nbsp; These valuable pictures of yours, Sir Walter,



<P>if you chose to leave them, would be perfectly safe.&nbsp; Everything

in



<P>and about the house would be taken such excellent care of!



<P>The gardens and shrubberies would be kept in almost as high order



<P>as they are now.&nbsp; You need not be afraid, Miss Elliot, of your

own



<P>sweet flower gardens being neglected."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"As to all that," rejoined Sir Walter coolly, "supposing I were induced



<P>to let my house, I have by no means made up my mind as to the privileges



<P>to be annexed to it.&nbsp; I am not particularly disposed to favour

a tenant.



<P>The park would be open to him of course, and few navy officers,



<P>or men of any other description, can have had such a range;



<P>but what restrictions I might impose on the use of the pleasure-grounds,



<P>is another thing.&nbsp; I am not fond of the idea of my shrubberies

being



<P>always approachable; and I should recommend Miss Elliot to be on her

guard



<P>with respect to her flower garden.&nbsp; I am very little disposed



<P>to grant a tenant of Kellynch Hall any extraordinary favour,



<P>I assure you, be he sailor or soldier."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a short pause, Mr Shepherd presumed to say--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"In all these cases, there are established usages which



<P>make everything plain and easy between landlord and tenant.



<P>Your interest, Sir Walter, is in pretty safe hands.&nbsp; Depend upon

me



<P>for taking care that no tenant has more than his just rights.



<P>I venture to hint, that Sir Walter Elliot cannot be half so jealous



<P>for his own, as John Shepherd will be for him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Here Anne spoke--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"The navy, I think, who have done so much for us, have at least



<P>an equal claim with any other set of men, for all the comforts and



<P>all the privileges which any home can give.&nbsp; Sailors work hard

enough



<P>for their comforts, we must all allow."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Very true, very true.&nbsp; What Miss Anne says, is very true,"



<P>was Mr Shepherd's rejoinder, and "Oh! certainly," was his daughter's;



<P>but Sir Walter's remark was, soon afterwards--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"The profession has its utility, but I should be sorry to see



<P>any friend of mine belonging to it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Indeed!" was the reply, and with a look of surprise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes; it is in two points offensive to me; I have two strong grounds



<P>of objection to it.&nbsp; First, as being the means of bringing persons



<P>of obscure birth into undue distinction, and raising men to honours



<P>which their fathers and grandfathers never dreamt of; and secondly,



<P>as it cuts up a man's youth and vigour most horribly; a sailor grows

old



<P>sooner than any other man.&nbsp; I have observed it all my life.



<P>A man is in greater danger in the navy of being insulted by the rise



<P>of one whose father, his father might have disdained to speak to,



<P>and of becoming prematurely an object of disgust himself, than in



<P>any other line.&nbsp; One day last spring, in town, I was in company



<P>with two men, striking instances of what I am talking of;



<P>Lord St Ives, whose father we all know to have been a country curate,



<P>without bread to eat; I was to give place to Lord St Ives,



<P>and a certain Admiral Baldwin, the most deplorable-looking personage



<P>you can imagine; his face the colour of mahogany, rough and rugged



<P>to the last degree; all lines and wrinkles, nine grey hairs of a side,



<P>and nothing but a dab of powder at top.&nbsp; `In the name of heaven,



<P>who is that old fellow?' said I to a friend of mine who was standing

near,



<P>(Sir Basil Morley).&nbsp; `Old fellow!' cried Sir Basil, `it is Admiral

Baldwin.



<P>What do you take his age to be?'&nbsp; `Sixty,' said I, `or perhaps

sixty-two.'



<P>`Forty,' replied Sir Basil, `forty, and no more.'&nbsp; Picture to yourselves



<P>my amazement; I shall not easily forget Admiral Baldwin.



<P>I never saw quite so wretched an example of what a sea-faring life can

do;



<P>but to a degree, I know it is the same with them all:&nbsp; they are

all



<P>knocked about, and exposed to every climate, and every weather,



<P>till they are not fit to be seen.&nbsp; It is a pity they are not knocked



<P>on the head at once, before they reach Admiral Baldwin's age."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Nay, Sir Walter," cried Mrs Clay, "this is being severe indeed.



<P>Have a little mercy on the poor men.&nbsp; We are not all born to be

handsome.



<P>The sea is no beautifier, certainly; sailors do grow old betimes;



<P>I have observed it; they soon lose the look of youth.&nbsp; But then,



<P>is not it the same with many other professions, perhaps most other?



<P>Soldiers, in active service, are not at all better off:&nbsp; and even

in



<P>the quieter professions, there is a toil and a labour of the mind,



<P>if not of the body, which seldom leaves a man's looks to the natural



<P>effect of time.&nbsp; The lawyer plods, quite care-worn; the physician



<P>is up at all hours, and travelling in all weather; and even



<P>the clergyman--" she stopt a moment to consider what might



<P>do for the clergyman;--"and even the clergyman, you know is obliged



<P>to go into infected rooms, and expose his health and looks to



<P>all the injury of a poisonous atmosphere.&nbsp; In fact, as I have



<P>long been convinced, though every profession is necessary and honourable



<P>in its turn, it is only the lot of those who are not obliged to follow

any,



<P>who can live in a regular way, in the country, choosing their own hours,



<P>following their own pursuits, and living on their own property,



<P>without the torment of trying for more; it is only their lot, I say,



<P>to hold the blessings of health and a good appearance to the utmost:



<P>I know no other set of men but what lose something of their personableness



<P>when they cease to be quite young."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It seemed as if Mr Shepherd, in this anxiety to bespeak



<P>Sir Walter's good will towards a naval officer as tenant,



<P>had been gifted with foresight; for the very first application



<P>for the house was from an Admiral Croft, with whom he shortly afterwards



<P>fell into company in attending the quarter sessions at Taunton; and

indeed,



<P>he had received a hint of the Admiral from a London correspondent.



<P>By the report which he hastened over to Kellynch to make,



<P>Admiral Croft was a native of Somersetshire, who having acquired



<P>a very handsome fortune, was wishing to settle in his own country,



<P>and had come down to Taunton in order to look at some advertised places



<P>in that immediate neighbourhood, which, however, had not suited him;



<P>that accidentally hearing--(it was just as he had foretold,



<P>Mr Shepherd observed, Sir Walter's concerns could not be kept a secret,)--



<P>accidentally hearing of the possibility of Kellynch Hall being to let,



<P>and understanding his (Mr Shepherd's) connection with the owner,



<P>he had introduced himself to him in order to make particular inquiries,



<P>and had, in the course of a pretty long conference, expressed as strong



<P>an inclination for the place as a man who knew it only by description



<P>could feel; and given Mr Shepherd, in his explicit account of himself,



<P>every proof of his being a most responsible, eligible tenant.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And who is Admiral Croft?" was Sir Walter's cold suspicious inquiry.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Shepherd answered for his being of a gentleman's family,



<P>and mentioned a place; and Anne, after the little pause which followed,



<P>added--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"He is a rear admiral of the white.&nbsp; He was in the Trafalgar action,



<P>and has been in the East Indies since; he was stationed there,



<P>I believe, several years."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Then I take it for granted," observed Sir Walter, "that his face



<P>is about as orange as the cuffs and capes of my livery."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Shepherd hastened to assure him, that Admiral Croft was a very hale,



<P>hearty, well-looking man, a little weather-beaten, to be sure,



<P>but not much, and quite the gentleman in all his notions and behaviour;



<P>not likely to make the smallest difficulty about terms, only wanted



<P>a comfortable home, and to get into it as soon as possible;



<P>knew he must pay for his convenience; knew what rent a ready-furnished



<P>house of that consequence might fetch; should not have been surprised



<P>if Sir Walter had asked more; had inquired about the manor;



<P>would be glad of the deputation, certainly, but made no great point

of it;



<P>said he sometimes took out a gun, but never killed; quite the gentleman.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Shepherd was eloquent on the subject; pointing out all



<P>the circumstances of the Admiral's family, which made him



<P>peculiarly desirable as a tenant.&nbsp; He was a married man,



<P>and without children; the very state to be wished for.&nbsp; A house

was



<P>never taken good care of, Mr Shepherd observed, without a lady:



<P>he did not know, whether furniture might not be in danger of suffering



<P>as much where there was no lady, as where there were many children.



<P>A lady, without a family, was the very best preserver of furniture



<P>in the world.&nbsp; He had seen Mrs Croft, too; she was at Taunton



<P>with the admiral, and had been present almost all the time they were



<P>talking the matter over.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And a very well-spoken, genteel, shrewd lady, she seemed to be,"



<P>continued he; "asked more questions about the house, and terms,



<P>and taxes, than the Admiral himself, and seemed more conversant



<P>with business; and moreover, Sir Walter, I found she was not quite



<P>unconnected in this country, any more than her husband; that is to say,



<P>she is sister to a gentleman who did live amongst us once;



<P>she told me so herself:&nbsp; sister to the gentleman who lived



<P>a few years back at Monkford.&nbsp; Bless me! what was his name?



<P>At this moment I cannot recollect his name, though I have heard it so

lately.



<P>Penelope, my dear, can you help me to the name of the gentleman



<P>who lived at Monkford:&nbsp; Mrs Croft's brother?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But Mrs Clay was talking so eagerly with Miss Elliot, that she did not



<P>hear the appeal.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I have no conception whom you can mean, Shepherd; I remember



<P>no gentleman resident at Monkford since the time of old Governor Trent."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Bless me! how very odd!&nbsp; I shall forget my own name soon, I suppose.



<P>A name that I am so very well acquainted with; knew the gentleman



<P>so well by sight; seen him a hundred times; came to consult me once,



<P>I remember, about a trespass of one of his neighbours; farmer's man



<P>breaking into his orchard; wall torn down; apples stolen;



<P>caught in the fact; and afterwards, contrary to my judgement,



<P>submitted to an amicable compromise.&nbsp; Very odd indeed!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After waiting another moment--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You mean Mr Wentworth, I suppose?" said Anne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Shepherd was all gratitude.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Wentworth was the very name!&nbsp; Mr Wentworth was the very man.



<P>He had the curacy of Monkford, you know, Sir Walter, some time back,



<P>for two or three years.&nbsp; Came there about the year ---5, I take

it.



<P>You remember him, I am sure."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Wentworth?&nbsp; Oh! ay,--Mr Wentworth, the curate of Monkford.



<P>You misled me by the term gentleman.&nbsp; I thought you were speaking

of



<P>some man of property:&nbsp; Mr Wentworth was nobody, I remember;



<P>quite unconnected; nothing to do with the Strafford family.



<P>One wonders how the names of many of our nobility become so common."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As Mr Shepherd perceived that this connexion of the Crofts did them



<P>no service with Sir Walter, he mentioned it no more; returning,



<P>with all his zeal, to dwell on the circumstances more indisputably



<P>in their favour; their age, and number, and fortune; the high idea



<P>they had formed of Kellynch Hall, and extreme solicitude for



<P>the advantage of renting it; making it appear as if they ranked



<P>nothing beyond the happiness of being the tenants of Sir Walter Elliot:



<P>an extraordinary taste, certainly, could they have been supposed in



<P>the secret of Sir Walter's estimate of the dues of a tenant.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It succeeded, however; and though Sir Walter must ever look with



<P>an evil eye on anyone intending to inhabit that house, and think them



<P>infinitely too well off in being permitted to rent it on the highest

terms,



<P>he was talked into allowing Mr Shepherd to proceed in the treaty,



<P>and authorising him to wait on Admiral Croft, who still remained



<P>at Taunton, and fix a day for the house being seen.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sir Walter was not very wise; but still he had experience enough



<P>of the world to feel, that a more unobjectionable tenant,



<P>in all essentials, than Admiral Croft bid fair to be, could hardly offer.



<P>So far went his understanding; and his vanity supplied a little



<P>additional soothing, in the Admiral's situation in life, which was just



<P>high enough, and not too high.&nbsp; "I have let my house to Admiral

Croft,"



<P>would sound extremely well; very much better than to any mere Mr--;



<P>a Mr (save, perhaps, some half dozen in the nation,) always needs



<P>a note of explanation.&nbsp; An admiral speaks his own consequence,



<P>and, at the same time, can never make a baronet look small.



<P>In all their dealings and intercourse, Sir Walter Elliot must ever



<P>have the precedence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Nothing could be done without a reference to Elizabeth:



<P>but her inclination was growing so strong for a removal,



<P>that she was happy to have it fixed and expedited by a tenant at hand;



<P>and not a word to suspend decision was uttered by her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Shepherd was completely empowered to act; and no sooner had



<P>such an end been reached, than Anne, who had been a most attentive listener



<P>to the whole, left the room, to seek the comfort of cool air for her



<P>flushed cheeks; and as she walked along a favourite grove, said,



<P>with a gentle sigh, "A few months more, and he, perhaps,



<P>may be walking here.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 17</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 17</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>While Sir Walter and Elizabeth were assiduously pushing their



<P>good fortune in Laura Place, Anne was renewing an acquaintance



<P>of a very different description.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She had called on her former governess, and had heard from her



<P>of there being an old school-fellow in Bath, who had the two strong

claims



<P>on her attention of past kindness and present suffering.&nbsp; Miss

Hamilton,



<P>now Mrs Smith, had shewn her kindness in one of those periods of her

life



<P>when it had been most valuable.&nbsp; Anne had gone unhappy to school,



<P>grieving for the loss of a mother whom she had dearly loved,



<P>feeling her separation from home, and suffering as a girl of fourteen,



<P>of strong sensibility and not high spirits, must suffer at such a time;



<P>and Miss Hamilton, three years older than herself, but still from the

want



<P>of near relations and a settled home, remaining another year at school,



<P>had been useful and good to her in a way which had considerably lessened



<P>her misery, and could never be remembered with indifference.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Miss Hamilton had left school, had married not long afterwards,



<P>was said to have married a man of fortune, and this was all



<P>that Anne had known of her, till now that their governess's account



<P>brought her situation forward in a more decided but very different form.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She was a widow and poor.&nbsp; Her husband had been extravagant;



<P>and at his death, about two years before, had left his affairs



<P>dreadfully involved.&nbsp; She had had difficulties of every sort



<P>to contend with, and in addition to these distresses had been afflicted



<P>with a severe rheumatic fever, which, finally settling in her legs,



<P>had made her for the present a cripple.&nbsp; She had come to Bath



<P>on that account, and was now in lodgings near the hot baths,



<P>living in a very humble way, unable even to afford herself



<P>the comfort of a servant, and of course almost excluded from society.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Their mutual friend answered for the satisfaction which a visit



<P>from Miss Elliot would give Mrs Smith, and Anne therefore



<P>lost no time in going.&nbsp; She mentioned nothing of what she had heard,



<P>or what she intended, at home.&nbsp; It would excite no proper interest

there.



<P>She only consulted Lady Russell, who entered thoroughly into her sentiments,



<P>and was most happy to convey her as near to Mrs Smith's lodgings



<P>in Westgate Buildings, as Anne chose to be taken.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The visit was paid, their acquaintance re-established, their interest



<P>in each other more than re-kindled.&nbsp; The first ten minutes



<P>had its awkwardness and its emotion.&nbsp; Twelve years were gone



<P>since they had parted, and each presented a somewhat different person



<P>from what the other had imagined.&nbsp; Twelve years had changed Anne



<P>from the blooming, silent, unformed girl of fifteen, to the elegant



<P>little woman of seven-and-twenty, with every beauty except bloom,



<P>and with manners as consciously right as they were invariably gentle;



<P>and twelve years had transformed the fine-looking, well-grown Miss Hamilton,



<P>in all the glow of health and confidence of superiority, into a poor,



<P>infirm, helpless widow, receiving the visit of her former protegee



<P>as a favour; but all that was uncomfortable in the meeting had soon



<P>passed away, and left only the interesting charm of remembering



<P>former partialities and talking over old times.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne found in Mrs Smith the good sense and agreeable manners which



<P>she had almost ventured to depend on, and a disposition to converse



<P>and be cheerful beyond her expectation.&nbsp; Neither the dissipations



<P>of the past--and she had lived very much in the world--nor the restrictions



<P>of the present, neither sickness nor sorrow seemed to have



<P>closed her heart or ruined her spirits.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In the course of a second visit she talked with great openness,



<P>and Anne's astonishment increased.&nbsp; She could scarcely imagine



<P>a more cheerless situation in itself than Mrs Smith's.&nbsp; She had

been



<P>very fond of her husband:&nbsp; she had buried him.&nbsp; She had been



<P>used to affluence:&nbsp; it was gone.&nbsp; She had no child to connect

her



<P>with life and happiness again, no relations to assist in the arrangement



<P>of perplexed affairs, no health to make all the rest supportable.



<P>Her accommodations were limited to a noisy parlour, and a dark bedroom



<P>behind, with no possibility of moving from one to the other without



<P>assistance, which there was only one servant in the house to afford,



<P>and she never quitted the house but to be conveyed into the warm bath.



<P>Yet, in spite of all this, Anne had reason to believe that she had



<P>moments only of languor and depression, to hours of occupation



<P>and enjoyment.&nbsp; How could it be?&nbsp; She watched, observed, reflected,



<P>and finally determined that this was not a case of fortitude



<P>or of resignation only.&nbsp; A submissive spirit might be patient,



<P>a strong understanding would supply resolution, but here was something

more;



<P>here was that elasticity of mind, that disposition to be comforted,



<P>that power of turning readily from evil to good, and of finding employment



<P>which carried her out of herself, which was from nature alone.



<P>It was the choicest gift of Heaven; and Anne viewed her friend



<P>as one of those instances in which, by a merciful appointment,



<P>it seems designed to counterbalance almost every other want.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There had been a time, Mrs Smith told her, when her spirits



<P>had nearly failed.&nbsp; She could not call herself an invalid now,



<P>compared with her state on first reaching Bath.&nbsp; Then she had,

indeed,



<P>been a pitiable object; for she had caught cold on the journey,



<P>and had hardly taken possession of her lodgings before she was again



<P>confined to her bed and suffering under severe and constant pain;



<P>and all this among strangers, with the absolute necessity of having



<P>a regular nurse, and finances at that moment particularly unfit



<P>to meet any extraordinary expense.&nbsp; She had weathered it, however,



<P>and could truly say that it had done her good.&nbsp; It had increased



<P>her comforts by making her feel herself to be in good hands.



<P>She had seen too much of the world, to expect sudden or disinterested



<P>attachment anywhere, but her illness had proved to her that her landlady



<P>had a character to preserve, and would not use her ill; and she had

been



<P>particularly fortunate in her nurse, as a sister of her landlady,



<P>a nurse by profession, and who had always a home in that house



<P>when unemployed, chanced to be at liberty just in time to attend her.



<P>"And she," said Mrs Smith, "besides nursing me most admirably,



<P>has really proved an invaluable acquaintance.&nbsp; As soon as I could



<P>use my hands she taught me to knit, which has been a great amusement;



<P>and she put me in the way of making these little thread-cases,



<P>pin-cushions and card-racks, which you always find me so busy about,



<P>and which supply me with the means of doing a little good



<P>to one or two very poor families in this neighbourhood.



<P>She had a large acquaintance, of course professionally, among those



<P>who can afford to buy, and she disposes of my merchandize.



<P>She always takes the right time for applying.&nbsp; Everybody's heart

is open,



<P>you know, when they have recently escaped from severe pain,



<P>or are recovering the blessing of health, and Nurse Rooke



<P>thoroughly understands when to speak.&nbsp; She is a shrewd, intelligent,



<P>sensible woman.&nbsp; Hers is a line for seeing human nature; and she

has



<P>a fund of good sense and observation, which, as a companion, make her



<P>infinitely superior to thousands of those who having only received



<P>`the best education in the world,' know nothing worth attending to.



<P>Call it gossip, if you will, but when Nurse Rooke has half an hour's



<P>leisure to bestow on me, she is sure to have something to relate



<P>that is entertaining and profitable:&nbsp; something that makes one



<P>know one's species better.&nbsp; One likes to hear what is going on,



<P>to be au fait as to the newest modes of being trifling and silly.



<P>To me, who live so much alone, her conversation, I assure you, is a

treat."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne, far from wishing to cavil at the pleasure, replied,



<P>"I can easily believe it.&nbsp; Women of that class have great opportunities,



<P>and if they are intelligent may be well worth listening to.



<P>Such varieties of human nature as they are in the habit of witnessing!



<P>And it is not merely in its follies, that they are well read;



<P>for they see it occasionally under every circumstance that can be



<P>most interesting or affecting.&nbsp; What instances must pass before

them



<P>of ardent, disinterested, self-denying attachment, of heroism, fortitude,



<P>patience, resignation:&nbsp; of all the conflicts and all the sacrifices



<P>that ennoble us most.&nbsp; A sick chamber may often furnish



<P>the worth of volumes."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes," said Mrs Smith more doubtingly, "sometimes it may,



<P>though I fear its lessons are not often in the elevated style you describe.



<P>Here and there, human nature may be great in times of trial;



<P>but generally speaking, it is its weakness and not its strength



<P>that appears in a sick chamber:&nbsp; it is selfishness and impatience



<P>rather than generosity and fortitude, that one hears of.



<P>There is so little real friendship in the world! and unfortunately"



<P>(speaking low and tremulously) "there are so many who forget



<P>to think seriously till it is almost too late."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne saw the misery of such feelings.&nbsp; The husband had not been



<P>what he ought, and the wife had been led among that part of mankind



<P>which made her think worse of the world than she hoped it deserved.



<P>It was but a passing emotion however with Mrs Smith; she shook it off,



<P>and soon added in a different tone--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I do not suppose the situation my friend Mrs Rooke is in at present,



<P>will furnish much either to interest or edify me.&nbsp; She is only

nursing



<P>Mrs Wallis of Marlborough Buildings; a mere pretty, silly, expensive,



<P>fashionable woman, I believe; and of course will have nothing to report



<P>but of lace and finery.&nbsp; I mean to make my profit of Mrs Wallis,

however.



<P>She has plenty of money, and I intend she shall buy all



<P>the high-priced things I have in hand now."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne had called several times on her friend, before the existence



<P>of such a person was known in Camden Place.&nbsp; At last, it became

necessary



<P>to speak of her. Sir Walter, Elizabeth and Mrs Clay, returned one morning



<P>from Laura Place, with a sudden invitation from Lady Dalrymple



<P>for the same evening, and Anne was already engaged, to spend that evening



<P>in Westgate Buildings.&nbsp; She was not sorry for the excuse.



<P>They were only asked, she was sure, because Lady Dalrymple being



<P>kept at home by a bad cold, was glad to make use of the relationship



<P>which had been so pressed on her; and she declined on her own account



<P>with great alacrity--"She was engaged to spend the evening



<P>with an old schoolfellow."&nbsp; They were not much interested in anything



<P>relative to Anne; but still there were questions enough asked,



<P>to make it understood what this old schoolfellow was; and Elizabeth



<P>was disdainful, and Sir Walter severe.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Westgate Buildings!" said he, "and who is Miss Anne Elliot



<P>to be visiting in Westgate Buildings?&nbsp; A Mrs Smith.&nbsp; A widow

Mrs Smith;



<P>and who was her husband?&nbsp; One of five thousand Mr Smiths whose

names



<P>are to be met with everywhere.&nbsp; And what is her attraction?



<P>That she is old and sickly.&nbsp; Upon my word, Miss Anne Elliot,



<P>you have the most extraordinary taste!&nbsp; Everything that revolts



<P>other people, low company, paltry rooms, foul air, disgusting associations



<P>are inviting to you.&nbsp; But surely you may put off this old lady



<P>till to-morrow:&nbsp; she is not so near her end, I presume,



<P>but that she may hope to see another day.&nbsp; What is her age?&nbsp;

Forty?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, sir, she is not one-and-thirty; but I do not think I can



<P>put off my engagement, because it is the only evening for some time



<P>which will at once suit her and myself.&nbsp; She goes into the warm

bath



<P>to-morrow, and for the rest of the week, you know, we are engaged."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But what does Lady Russell think of this acquaintance?" asked Elizabeth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"She sees nothing to blame in it," replied Anne; "on the contrary,



<P>she approves it, and has generally taken me when I have



<P>called on Mrs Smith.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Westgate Buildings must have been rather surprised by the appearance



<P>of a carriage drawn up near its pavement," observed Sir Walter.



<P>"Sir Henry Russell's widow, indeed, has no honours to distinguish her

arms,



<P>but still it is a handsome equipage, and no doubt is well known



<P>to convey a Miss Elliot.&nbsp; A widow Mrs Smith lodging in Westgate

Buildings!



<P>A poor widow barely able to live, between thirty and forty;



<P>a mere Mrs Smith, an every-day Mrs Smith, of all people and all names



<P>in the world, to be the chosen friend of Miss Anne Elliot,



<P>and to be preferred by her to her own family connections among the nobility



<P>of England and Ireland!&nbsp; Mrs Smith!&nbsp; Such a name!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Clay, who had been present while all this passed, now thought it



<P>advisable to leave the room, and Anne could have said much,



<P>and did long to say a little in defense of her friend's



<P>not very dissimilar claims to theirs, but her sense of personal respect



<P>to her father prevented her.&nbsp; She made no reply.&nbsp; She left

it



<P>to himself to recollect, that Mrs Smith was not the only widow



<P>in Bath between thirty and forty, with little to live on,



<P>and no sirname of dignity.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne kept her appointment; the others kept theirs, and of course



<P>she heard the next morning that they had had a delightful evening.



<P>She had been the only one of the set absent, for Sir Walter



<P>and Elizabeth had not only been quite at her ladyship's service themselves,



<P>but had actually been happy to be employed by her in collecting others,



<P>and had been at the trouble of inviting both Lady Russell and Mr Elliot;



<P>and Mr Elliot had made a point of leaving Colonel Wallis early,



<P>and Lady Russell had fresh arranged all her evening engagements



<P>in order to wait on her.&nbsp; Anne had the whole history of all that



<P>such an evening could supply from Lady Russell.&nbsp; To her,



<P>its greatest interest must be, in having been very much talked of



<P>between her friend and Mr Elliot; in having been wished for, regretted,



<P>and at the same time honoured for staying away in such a cause.



<P>Her kind, compassionate visits to this old schoolfellow,



<P>sick and reduced, seemed to have quite delighted Mr Elliot.



<P>He thought her a most extraordinary young woman; in her temper, manners,



<P>mind, a model of female excellence.&nbsp; He could meet even Lady Russell



<P>in a discussion of her merits; and Anne could not be given to understand



<P>so much by her friend, could not know herself to be so highly rated



<P>by a sensible man, without many of those agreeable sensations



<P>which her friend meant to create.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell was now perfectly decided in her opinion of Mr Elliot.



<P>She was as much convinced of his meaning to gain Anne in time as of



<P>his deserving her, and was beginning to calculate the number of weeks



<P>which would free him from all the remaining restraints of widowhood,



<P>and leave him at liberty to exert his most open powers of pleasing.



<P>She would not speak to Anne with half the certainty she felt on the

subject,



<P>she would venture on little more than hints of what might be hereafter,



<P>of a possible attachment on his side, of the desirableness of the alliance,



<P>supposing such attachment to be real and returned.&nbsp; Anne heard

her,



<P>and made no violent exclamations; she only smiled, blushed,



<P>and gently shook her head.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I am no match-maker, as you well know," said Lady Russell,



<P>"being much too well aware of the uncertainty of all human events



<P>and calculations.&nbsp; I only mean that if Mr Elliot should some time

hence



<P>pay his addresses to you, and if you should be disposed to accept him,



<P>I think there would be every possibility of your being happy together.



<P>A most suitable connection everybody must consider it, but I think



<P>it might be a very happy one."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Mr Elliot is an exceedingly agreeable man, and in many respects



<P>I think highly of him," said Anne; "but we should not suit."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell let this pass, and only said in rejoinder, "I own that



<P>to be able to regard you as the future mistress of Kellynch,



<P>the future Lady Elliot, to look forward and see you occupying



<P>your dear mother's place, succeeding to all her rights,



<P>and all her popularity, as well as to all her virtues, would be



<P>the highest possible gratification to me.&nbsp; You are your mother's

self



<P>in countenance and disposition; and if I might be allowed to fancy you



<P>such as she was, in situation and name, and home, presiding and blessing



<P>in the same spot, and only superior to her in being more highly valued!



<P>My dearest Anne, it would give me more delight than is often felt



<P>at my time of life!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne was obliged to turn away, to rise, to walk to a distant table,



<P>and, leaning there in pretended employment, try to subdue the feelings



<P>this picture excited.&nbsp; For a few moments her imagination and her

heart



<P>were bewitched.&nbsp; The idea of becoming what her mother had been;



<P>of having the precious name of "Lady Elliot" first revived in herself;



<P>of being restored to Kellynch, calling it her home again,



<P>her home for ever, was a charm which she could not immediately resist.



<P>Lady Russell said not another word, willing to leave the matter



<P>to its own operation; and believing that, could Mr Elliot at that moment



<P>with propriety have spoken for himself!--she believed, in short,



<P>what Anne did not believe.&nbsp; The same image of Mr Elliot speaking



<P>for himself brought Anne to composure again.&nbsp; The charm of Kellynch



<P>and of "Lady Elliot" all faded away.&nbsp; She never could accept him.



<P>And it was not only that her feelings were still adverse to any man



<P>save one; her judgement, on a serious consideration of the possibilities



<P>of such a case was against Mr Elliot.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Though they had now been acquainted a month, she could not be satisfied



<P>that she really knew his character.&nbsp; That he was a sensible man,



<P>an agreeable man, that he talked well, professed good opinions,



<P>seemed to judge properly and as a man of principle, this was all



<P>clear enough.&nbsp; He certainly knew what was right, nor could she

fix



<P>on any one article of moral duty evidently transgressed; but yet she

would



<P>have been afraid to answer for his conduct.&nbsp; She distrusted the

past,



<P>if not the present.&nbsp; The names which occasionally dropt



<P>of former associates, the allusions to former practices and pursuits,



<P>suggested suspicions not favourable of what he had been.



<P>She saw that there had been bad habits; that Sunday travelling



<P>had been a common thing; that there had been a period of his life



<P>(and probably not a short one) when he had been, at least,



<P>careless in all serious matters; and, though he might now think



<P>very differently, who could answer for the true sentiments of a clever,



<P>cautious man, grown old enough to appreciate a fair character?



<P>How could it ever be ascertained that his mind was truly cleansed?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Elliot was rational, discreet, polished, but he was not open.



<P>There was never any burst of feeling, any warmth of indignation or delight,



<P>at the evil or good of others.&nbsp; This, to Anne, was a decided imperfection.



<P>Her early impressions were incurable.&nbsp; She prized the frank,



<P>the open-hearted, the eager character beyond all others.



<P>Warmth and enthusiasm did captivate her still.&nbsp; She felt that she

could



<P>so much more depend upon the sincerity of those who sometimes looked



<P>or said a careless or a hasty thing, than of those whose presence of

mind



<P>never varied, whose tongue never slipped.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Elliot was too generally agreeable.&nbsp; Various as were the tempers



<P>in her father's house, he pleased them all.&nbsp; He endured too well,



<P>stood too well with every body.&nbsp; He had spoken to her with some



<P>degree of openness of Mrs Clay; had appeared completely to see



<P>what Mrs Clay was about, and to hold her in contempt; and yet



<P>Mrs Clay found him as agreeable as any body.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell saw either less or more than her young friend,



<P>for she saw nothing to excite distrust.&nbsp; She could not imagine



<P>a man more exactly what he ought to be than Mr Elliot; nor did she



<P>ever enjoy a sweeter feeling than the hope of seeing him receive



<P>the hand of her beloved Anne in Kellynch church, in the course of



<P>the following autumn.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <TITLE>Chapter 01</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B><FONT SIZE=+2>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

Persuasion</FONT></B>



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+1>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

by Jane Austen</FONT></B>



<P><B>Chapter 1</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Sir Walter Elliot, of Kellynch Hall, in Somersetshire, was a man who,



<P>for his own amusement, never took up any book but the Baronetage;



<P>there he found occupation for an idle hour, and consolation in a



<P>distressed one; there his faculties were roused into admiration and



<P>respect, by contemplating the limited remnant of the earliest patents;



<P>there any unwelcome sensations, arising from domestic affairs



<P>changed naturally into pity and contempt as he turned over



<P>the almost endless creations of the last century; and there,



<P>if every other leaf were powerless, he could read his own history



<P>with an interest which never failed.&nbsp; This was the page at which



<P>the favorite volume always opened:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "ELLIOT OF KELLYNCH HALL.



<P>"Walter Elliot, born March 1, 1760, married, July 15, 1784, Elizabeth,



<P>daughter of James Stevenson, Esq. of South Park, in the county of



<P>Gloucester, by which lady (who died 1800) he has issue Elizabeth,



<P>born June 1, 1785; Anne, born August 9, 1787; a still-born son,



<P>November 5, 1789; Mary, born November 20, 1791."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Precisely such had the paragraph originally stood from the printer's

hands;



<P>but Sir Walter had improved it by adding, for the information of



<P>himself and his family, these words, after the date of Mary's birth--



<P>"Married, December 16, 1810, Charles, son and heir of Charles



<P>Musgrove, Esq. of Uppercross, in the county of Somerset,"



<P>and by inserting most accurately the day of the month on which



<P>he had lost his wife.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then followed the history and rise of the ancient and respectable family,



<P>in the usual terms; how it had been first settled in Cheshire;



<P>how mentioned in Dugdale, serving the office of high sheriff,



<P>representing a borough in three successive parliaments,



<P>exertions of loyalty, and dignity of baronet, in the first year



<P>of Charles II, with all the Marys and Elizabeths they had married;



<P>forming altogether two handsome duodecimo pages, and concluding with



<P>the arms and motto:--"Principal seat, Kellynch Hall, in the county



<P>of Somerset," and Sir Walter's handwriting again in this finale:--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Heir presumptive, William Walter Elliot, Esq., great grandson of



<P>the second Sir Walter."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Vanity was the beginning and the end of Sir Walter Elliot's character;



<P>vanity of person and of situation.&nbsp; He had been remarkably handsome



<P>in his youth; and, at fifty-four, was still a very fine man.



<P>Few women could think more of their personal appearance than he did,



<P>nor could the valet of any new made lord be more delighted with



<P>the place he held in society.&nbsp; He considered the blessing of beauty



<P>as inferior only to the blessing of a baronetcy; and the Sir Walter

Elliot,



<P>who united these gifts, was the constant object of his warmest respect



<P>and devotion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His good looks and his rank had one fair claim on his attachment;



<P>since to them he must have owed a wife of very superior character



<P>to any thing deserved by his own.&nbsp; Lady Elliot had been an excellent

woman,



<P>sensible and amiable; whose judgement and conduct, if they might be



<P>pardoned the youthful infatuation which made her Lady Elliot,



<P>had never required indulgence afterwards.--She had humoured,



<P>or softened, or concealed his failings, and promoted his real



<P>respectability for seventeen years; and though not the very happiest



<P>being in the world herself, had found enough in her duties, her friends,



<P>and her children, to attach her to life, and make it no matter of



<P>indifference to her when she was called on to quit them.



<P>--Three girls, the two eldest sixteen and fourteen, was an awful legacy



<P>for a mother to bequeath, an awful charge rather, to confide to



<P>the authority and guidance of a conceited, silly father.



<P>She had, however, one very intimate friend, a sensible, deserving woman,



<P>who had been brought, by strong attachment to herself, to settle



<P>close by her, in the village of Kellynch; and on her kindness and advice,



<P>Lady Elliot mainly relied for the best help and maintenance of



<P>the good principles and instruction which she had been anxiously



<P>giving her daughters.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This friend, and Sir Walter, did not marry, whatever might have been



<P>anticipated on that head by their acquaintance.&nbsp; Thirteen years



<P>had passed away since Lady Elliot's death, and they were still



<P>near neighbours and intimate friends, and one remained a widower,



<P>the other a widow.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>That Lady Russell, of steady age and character, and extremely



<P>well provided for, should have no thought of a second marriage,



<P>needs no apology to the public, which is rather apt to be unreasonably



<P>discontented when a woman does marry again, than when she does not;



<P>but Sir Walter's continuing in singleness requires explanation.



<P>Be it known then, that Sir Walter, like a good father, (having met with



<P>one or two private disappointments in very unreasonable applications),



<P>prided himself on remaining single for his dear daughters' sake.



<P>For one daughter, his eldest, he would really have given up any thing,



<P>which he had not been very much tempted to do.&nbsp; Elizabeth had succeeded,



<P>at sixteen, to all that was possible, of her mother's rights



<P>and consequence; and being very handsome, and very like himself,



<P>her influence had always been great, and they had gone on together



<P>most happily.&nbsp; His two other children were of very inferior value.



<P>Mary had acquired a little artificial importance, by becoming



<P>Mrs Charles Musgrove; but Anne, with an elegance of mind and sweetness



<P>of character, which must have placed her high with any people



<P>of real understanding, was nobody with either father or sister;



<P>her word had no weight, her convenience was always to give way--



<P>she was only Anne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To Lady Russell, indeed, she was a most dear and highly valued



<P>god-daughter, favourite, and friend.&nbsp; Lady Russell loved them all;



<P>but it was only in Anne that she could fancy the mother to revive again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A few years before, Anne Elliot had been a very pretty girl,



<P>but her bloom had vanished early; and as even in its height,



<P>her father had found little to admire in her, (so totally different



<P>were her delicate features and mild dark eyes from his own),



<P>there could be nothing in them, now that she was faded and thin,



<P>to excite his esteem. He had never indulged much hope, he had now none,



<P>of ever reading her name in any other page of his favourite work.



<P>All equality of alliance must rest with Elizabeth, for Mary had merely



<P>connected herself with an old country family of respectability and



<P>large fortune, and had therefore given all the honour and received none:



<P>Elizabeth would, one day or other, marry suitably.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It sometimes happens that a woman is handsomer at twenty-nine than



<P>she was ten years before; and, generally speaking, if there has been



<P>neither ill health nor anxiety, it is a time of life at which scarcely

any



<P>charm is lost.&nbsp; It was so with Elizabeth, still the same handsome



<P>Miss Elliot that she had begun to be thirteen years ago, and Sir Walter



<P>might be excused, therefore, in forgetting her age, or, at least,



<P>be deemed only half a fool, for thinking himself and Elizabeth



<P>as blooming as ever, amidst the wreck of the good looks of everybody

else;



<P>for he could plainly see how old all the rest of his family and



<P>acquaintance were growing.&nbsp; Anne haggard, Mary coarse, every face



<P>in the neighbourhood worsting, and the rapid increase of the crow's

foot



<P>about Lady Russell's temples had long been a distress to him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Elizabeth did not quite equal her father in personal contentment.



<P>Thirteen years had seen her mistress of Kellynch Hall, presiding and



<P>directing with a self-possession and decision which could never have

given



<P>the idea of her being younger than she was.&nbsp; For thirteen years

had
