<P>she been doing the honours, and laying down the domestic law at home,



<P>and leading the way to the chaise and four, and walking immediately

after



<P>Lady Russell out of all the drawing-rooms and dining-rooms in the country.



<P>Thirteen winters' revolving frosts had seen her opening every ball



<P>of credit which a scanty neighbourhood afforded, and thirteen springs



<P>shewn their blossoms, as she travelled up to London with her father,



<P>for a few weeks' annual enjoyment of the great world.&nbsp; She had



<P>the remembrance of all this, she had the consciousness of being



<P>nine-and-twenty to give her some regrets and some apprehensions;



<P>she was fully satisfied of being still quite as handsome as ever,



<P>but she felt her approach to the years of danger, and would have rejoiced



<P>to be certain of being properly solicited by baronet-blood within



<P>the next twelvemonth or two.&nbsp; Then might she again take up



<P>the book of books with as much enjoyment as in her early youth,



<P>but now she liked it not.&nbsp; Always to be presented with the date

of



<P>her own birth and see no marriage follow but that of a youngest sister,



<P>made the book an evil; and more than once, when her father had left

it



<P>open on the table near her, had she closed it, with averted eyes,



<P>and pushed it away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She had had a disappointment, moreover, which that book,



<P>and especially the history of her own family, must ever present



<P>the remembrance of.&nbsp; The heir presumptive, the very William Walter



<P>Elliot, Esq., whose rights had been so generously supported



<P>by her father, had disappointed her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She had, while a very young girl, as soon as she had known him to be,



<P>in the event of her having no brother, the future baronet,



<P>meant to marry him, and her father had always meant that she should.



<P>He had not been known to them as a boy; but soon after Lady Elliot's

death,



<P>Sir Walter had sought the acquaintance, and though his overtures



<P>had not been met with any warmth, he had persevered in seeking it,



<P>making allowance for the modest drawing-back of youth; and, in one of



<P>their spring excursions to London, when Elizabeth was in her first bloom,



<P>Mr Elliot had been forced into the introduction.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He was at that time a very young man, just engaged in the study of the

law;



<P>and Elizabeth found him extremely agreeable, and every plan in his favour



<P>was confirmed.&nbsp; He was invited to Kellynch Hall; he was talked

of



<P>and expected all the rest of the year; but he never came.

<BR>The following spring he was seen again in town, found equally agreeable,



<P>again encouraged, invited, and expected, and again he did not come;



<P>and the next tidings were that he was married.&nbsp; Instead of pushing



<P>his fortune in the line marked out for the heir of the house of Elliot,



<P>he had purchased independence by uniting himself to a rich woman



<P>of inferior birth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sir Walter has resented it.&nbsp; As the head of the house, he felt

that



<P>he ought to have been consulted, especially after taking the young man



<P>so publicly by the hand; "For they must have been seen together,"



<P>he observed, "once at Tattersal's, and twice in the lobby of



<P>the House of Commons."&nbsp; His disapprobation was expressed,



<P>but apparently very little regarded.&nbsp; Mr Elliot had attempted no

apology,



<P>and shewn himself as unsolicitous of being longer noticed by the family,



<P>as Sir Walter considered him unworthy of it:&nbsp; all acquaintance

between



<P>them had ceased.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This very awkward history of Mr Elliot was still, after an interval



<P>of several years, felt with anger by Elizabeth, who had liked the man



<P>for himself, and still more for being her father's heir, and whose



<P>strong family pride could see only in him a proper match for Sir Walter



<P>Elliot's eldest daughter.&nbsp; There was not a baronet from A to Z

whom



<P>her feelings could have so willingly acknowledged as an equal.



<P>Yet so miserably had he conducted himself, that though she was



<P>at this present time (the summer of 1814) wearing black ribbons



<P>for his wife, she could not admit him to be worth thinking of again.



<P>The disgrace of his first marriage might, perhaps, as there was



<P>no reason to suppose it perpetuated by offspring, have been got over,



<P>had he not done worse; but he had, as by the accustomary intervention



<P>of kind friends, they had been informed, spoken most disrespectfully



<P>of them all, most slightingly and contemptuously of the very blood



<P>he belonged to, and the honours which were hereafter to be his own.



<P>This could not be pardoned.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Such were Elizabeth Elliot's sentiments and sensations; such the cares



<P>to alloy, the agitations to vary, the sameness and the elegance,



<P>the prosperity and the nothingness of her scene of life;



<P>such the feelings to give interest to a long, uneventful residence



<P>in one country circle, to fill the vacancies which there were no habits



<P>of utility abroad, no talents or accomplishments for home, to occupy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But now, another occupation and solicitude of mind was beginning to

be



<P>added to these.&nbsp; Her father was growing distressed for money.



<P>She knew, that when he now took up the Baronetage, it was to drive



<P>the heavy bills of his tradespeople, and the unwelcome hints of



<P>Mr Shepherd, his agent, from his thoughts.&nbsp; The Kellynch property

was good,



<P>but not equal to Sir Walter's apprehension of the state required



<P>in its possessor.&nbsp; While Lady Elliot lived, there had been method,



<P>moderation, and economy, which had just kept him within his income;



<P>but with her had died all such right-mindedness, and from that period



<P>he had been constantly exceeding it.&nbsp; It had not been possible



<P>for him to spend less; he had done nothing but what Sir Walter Elliot



<P>was imperiously called on to do; but blameless as he was, he was



<P>not only growing dreadfully in debt, but was hearing of it so often,



<P>that it became vain to attempt concealing it longer, even partially,



<P>from his daughter.&nbsp; He had given her some hints of it the last

spring



<P>in town; he had gone so far even as to say, "Can we retrench?



<P>Does it occur to you that there is any one article in which



<P>we can retrench?" and Elizabeth, to do her justice, had, in the first



<P>ardour of female alarm, set seriously to think what could be done,



<P>and had finally proposed these two branches of economy, to cut off



<P>some unnecessary charities, and to refrain from new furnishing



<P>the drawing-room; to which expedients she afterwards added



<P>the happy thought of their taking no present down to Anne,



<P>as had been the usual yearly custom.&nbsp; But these measures,



<P>however good in themselves, were insufficient for the real extent



<P>of the evil, the whole of which Sir Walter found himself obliged



<P>to confess to her soon afterwards.&nbsp; Elizabeth had nothing to propose



<P>of deeper efficacy.&nbsp; She felt herself ill-used and unfortunate,



<P>as did her father; and they were neither of them able to devise



<P>any means of lessening their expenses without compromising their dignity,



<P>or relinquishing their comforts in a way not to be borne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was only a small part of his estate that Sir Walter could dispose

of;



<P>but had every acre been alienable, it would have made no difference.

<BR>He had condescended to mortgage as far as he had the power,



<P>but he would never condescend to sell.&nbsp; No; he would never disgrace



<P>his name so far.&nbsp; The Kellynch estate should be transmitted whole



<P>and entire, as he had received it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Their two confidential friends, Mr Shepherd, who lived in



<P>the neighbouring market town, and Lady Russell, were called to advise

them;



<P>and both father and daughter seemed to expect that something should

be



<P>struck out by one or the other to remove their embarrassments



<P>and reduce their expenditure, without involving the loss of



<P>any indulgence of taste or pride.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 15</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 15</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Sir Walter had taken a very good house in Camden Place,



<P>a lofty dignified situation, such as becomes a man of consequence;



<P>and both he and Elizabeth were settled there, much to their satisfaction.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne entered it with a sinking heart, anticipating an imprisonment



<P>of many months, and anxiously saying to herself, "Oh! when shall I



<P>leave you again?"&nbsp; A degree of unexpected cordiality, however,



<P>in the welcome she received, did her good.&nbsp; Her father and sister



<P>were glad to see her, for the sake of shewing her the house and furniture,



<P>and met her with kindness.&nbsp; Her making a fourth, when they



<P>sat down to dinner, was noticed as an advantage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Clay was very pleasant, and very smiling, but her courtesies and

smiles



<P>were more a matter of course.&nbsp; Anne had always felt that she would



<P>pretend what was proper on her arrival, but the complaisance of the

others



<P>was unlooked for.&nbsp; They were evidently in excellent spirits,



<P>and she was soon to listen to the causes.&nbsp; They had no inclination



<P>to listen to her.&nbsp; After laying out for some compliments of being



<P>deeply regretted in their old neighbourhood, which Anne could not pay,



<P>they had only a few faint enquiries to make, before the talk must be



<P>all their own.&nbsp; Uppercross excited no interest, Kellynch very little:



<P>it was all Bath.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They had the pleasure of assuring her that Bath more than answered



<P>their expectations in every respect.&nbsp; Their house was undoubtedly



<P>the best in Camden Place; their drawing-rooms had many decided advantages



<P>over all the others which they had either seen or heard of,



<P>and the superiority was not less in the style of the fitting-up,



<P>or the taste of the furniture.&nbsp; Their acquaintance was



<P>exceedingly sought after.&nbsp; Everybody was wanting to visit them.



<P>They had drawn back from many introductions, and still were



<P>perpetually having cards left by people of whom they knew nothing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Here were funds of enjoyment.&nbsp; Could Anne wonder that her father



<P>and sister were happy?&nbsp; She might not wonder, but she must sigh



<P>that her father should feel no degradation in his change, should see



<P>nothing to regret in the duties and dignity of the resident landholder,



<P>should find so much to be vain of in the littlenesses of a town;



<P>and she must sigh, and smile, and wonder too, as Elizabeth threw open



<P>the folding-doors and walked with exultation from one drawing-room



<P>to the other, boasting of their space; at the possibility of that woman,



<P>who had been mistress of Kellynch Hall, finding extent to be proud of



<P>between two walls, perhaps thirty feet asunder.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But this was not all which they had to make them happy.



<P>They had Mr Elliot too.&nbsp; Anne had a great deal to hear of Mr Elliot.



<P>He was not only pardoned, they were delighted with him.



<P>He had been in Bath about a fortnight; (he had passed through Bath



<P>in November, in his way to London, when the intelligence of



<P>Sir Walter's being settled there had of course reached him,



<P>though only twenty-four hours in the place, but he had not been able



<P>to avail himself of it;) but he had now been a fortnight in Bath,



<P>and his first object on arriving, had been to leave his card



<P>in Camden Place, following it up by such assiduous endeavours to meet,



<P>and when they did meet, by such great openness of conduct,



<P>such readiness to apologize for the past, such solicitude to be received



<P>as a relation again, that their former good understanding



<P>was completely re-established.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They had not a fault to find in him.&nbsp; He had explained away



<P>all the appearance of neglect on his own side.&nbsp; It had originated



<P>in misapprehension entirely.&nbsp; He had never had an idea of



<P>throwing himself off; he had feared that he was thrown off,



<P>but knew not why, and delicacy had kept him silent.&nbsp; Upon the hint



<P>of having spoken disrespectfully or carelessly of the family



<P>and the family honours, he was quite indignant.&nbsp; He, who had ever

boasted



<P>of being an Elliot, and whose feelings, as to connection,



<P>were only too strict to suit the unfeudal tone of the present day.



<P>He was astonished, indeed, but his character and general conduct



<P>must refute it.&nbsp; He could refer Sir Walter to all who knew him;



<P>and certainly, the pains he had been taking on this, the first opportunity



<P>of reconciliation, to be restored to the footing of a relation



<P>and heir-presumptive, was a strong proof of his opinions on the subject.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The circumstances of his marriage, too, were found to admit of



<P>much extenuation.&nbsp; This was an article not to be entered on by

himself;



<P>but a very intimate friend of his, a Colonel Wallis, a highly



<P>respectable man, perfectly the gentleman, (and not an ill-looking man,



<P>Sir Walter added), who was living in very good style in Marlborough



<P>Buildings, and had, at his own particular request, been admitted



<P>to their acquaintance through Mr Elliot, had mentioned one or two things



<P>relative to the marriage, which made a material difference



<P>in the discredit of it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Colonel Wallis had known Mr Elliot long, had been well acquainted



<P>also with his wife, had perfectly understood the whole story.



<P>She was certainly not a woman of family, but well educated,



<P>accomplished, rich, and excessively in love with his friend.



<P>There had been the charm.&nbsp; She had sought him.&nbsp; Without that

attraction,



<P>not all her money would have tempted Elliot, and Sir Walter was,



<P>moreover, assured of her having been a very fine woman.



<P>Here was a great deal to soften the business.&nbsp; A very fine woman



<P>with a large fortune, in love with him!&nbsp; Sir Walter seemed to admit

it



<P>as complete apology; and though Elizabeth could not see the circumstance



<P>in quite so favourable a light, she allowed it be a great extenuation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Elliot had called repeatedly, had dined with them once,



<P>evidently delighted by the distinction of being asked, for they



<P>gave no dinners in general; delighted, in short, by every proof



<P>of cousinly notice, and placing his whole happiness in being



<P>on intimate terms in Camden Place.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne listened, but without quite understanding it.&nbsp; Allowances,



<P>large allowances, she knew, must be made for the ideas of those who

spoke.



<P>She heard it all under embellishment.&nbsp; All that sounded extravagant



<P>or irrational in the progress of the reconciliation might have no origin



<P>but in the language of the relators.&nbsp; Still, however, she had



<P>the sensation of there being something more than immediately appeared,



<P>in Mr Elliot's wishing, after an interval of so many years,



<P>to be well received by them.&nbsp; In a worldly view, he had nothing

to gain



<P>by being on terms with Sir Walter; nothing to risk by a state of variance.



<P>In all probability he was already the richer of the two,



<P>and the Kellynch estate would as surely be his hereafter as the title.



<P>A sensible man, and he had looked like a very sensible man,



<P>why should it be an object to him?&nbsp; She could only offer one solution;



<P>it was, perhaps, for Elizabeth's sake.&nbsp; There might really have

been



<P>a liking formerly, though convenience and accident had drawn him



<P>a different way; and now that he could afford to please himself,



<P>he might mean to pay his addresses to her.&nbsp; Elizabeth was certainly



<P>very handsome, with well-bred, elegant manners, and her character



<P>might never have been penetrated by Mr Elliot, knowing her but in public,



<P>and when very young himself.&nbsp; How her temper and understanding



<P>might bear the investigation of his present keener time of life



<P>was another concern and rather a fearful one.&nbsp; Most earnestly did

she wish



<P>that he might not be too nice, or too observant if Elizabeth



<P>were his object; and that Elizabeth was disposed to believe herself

so,



<P>and that her friend Mrs Clay was encouraging the idea, seemed apparent



<P>by a glance or two between them, while Mr Elliot's frequent visits



<P>were talked of.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne mentioned the glimpses she had had of him at Lyme, but without



<P>being much attended to.&nbsp; "Oh! yes, perhaps, it had been Mr Elliot.



<P>They did not know.&nbsp; It might be him, perhaps."&nbsp; They could

not listen



<P>to her description of him.&nbsp; They were describing him themselves;



<P>Sir Walter especially.&nbsp; He did justice to his very gentlemanlike



<P>appearance, his air of elegance and fashion, his good shaped face,



<P>his sensible eye; but, at the same time, "must lament his being



<P>very much under-hung, a defect which time seemed to have increased;



<P>nor could he pretend to say that ten years had not altered



<P>almost every feature for the worse.&nbsp; Mr Elliot appeared to think



<P>that he (Sir Walter) was looking exactly as he had done when



<P>they last parted;" but Sir Walter had "not been able to return



<P>the compliment entirely, which had embarrassed him.&nbsp; He did not

mean



<P>to complain, however.&nbsp; Mr Elliot was better to look at than most

men,



<P>and he had no objection to being seen with him anywhere."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Elliot, and his friends in Marlborough Buildings, were talked of



<P>the whole evening.&nbsp; "Colonel Wallis had been so impatient to be



<P>introduced to them! and Mr Elliot so anxious that he should!"



<P>and there was a Mrs Wallis, at present known only to them by description,



<P>as she was in daily expectation of her confinement; but Mr Elliot



<P>spoke of her as "a most charming woman, quite worthy of being known



<P>in Camden Place," and as soon as she recovered they were to be acquainted.



<P>Sir Walter thought much of Mrs Wallis; she was said to be



<P>an excessively pretty woman, beautiful.&nbsp; "He longed to see her.



<P>He hoped she might make some amends for the many very plain faces



<P>he was continually passing in the streets.&nbsp; The worst of Bath was



<P>the number of its plain women.&nbsp; He did not mean to say that there

were



<P>no pretty women, but the number of the plain was out of all proportion.



<P>He had frequently observed, as he walked, that one handsome face



<P>would be followed by thirty, or five-and-thirty frights; and once,



<P>as he had stood in a shop on Bond Street, he had counted



<P>eighty-seven women go by, one after another, without there being



<P>a tolerable face among them.&nbsp; It had been a frosty morning,



<P>to be sure, a sharp frost, which hardly one woman in a thousand



<P>could stand the test of.&nbsp; But still, there certainly were



<P>a dreadful multitude of ugly women in Bath; and as for the men!



<P>they were infinitely worse.&nbsp; Such scarecrows as the streets were

full of!



<P>It was evident how little the women were used to the sight of anything



<P>tolerable, by the effect which a man of decent appearance produced.



<P>He had never walked anywhere arm-in-arm with Colonel Wallis



<P>(who was a fine military figure, though sandy-haired) without observing



<P>that every woman's eye was upon him; every woman's eye was sure to be



<P>upon Colonel Wallis."&nbsp; Modest Sir Walter!&nbsp; He was not allowed



<P>to escape, however.&nbsp; His daughter and Mrs Clay united in hinting



<P>that Colonel Wallis's companion might have as good a figure



<P>as Colonel Wallis, and certainly was not sandy-haired.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"How is Mary looking?" said Sir Walter, in the height of his good humour.



<P>"The last time I saw her she had a red nose, but I hope that may not



<P>happen every day."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! no, that must have been quite accidental.&nbsp; In general she

has been



<P>in very good health and very good looks since Michaelmas."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"If I thought it would not tempt her to go out in sharp winds,



<P>and grow coarse, I would send her a new hat and pelisse."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne was considering whether she should venture to suggest that a gown,



<P>or a cap, would not be liable to any such misuse, when a knock at the

door



<P>suspended everything.&nbsp; "A knock at the door! and so late!



<P>It was ten o'clock.&nbsp; Could it be Mr Elliot?&nbsp; They knew he

was to dine



<P>in Lansdown Crescent.&nbsp; It was possible that he might stop in his

way home



<P>to ask them how they did.&nbsp; They could think of no one else.



<P>Mrs Clay decidedly thought it Mr Elliot's knock."&nbsp; Mrs Clay was

right.



<P>With all the state which a butler and foot-boy could give,



<P>Mr Elliot was ushered into the room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was the same, the very same man, with no difference but of dress.



<P>Anne drew a little back, while the others received his compliments,



<P>and her sister his apologies for calling at so unusual an hour,



<P>but "he could not be so near without wishing to know that neither she



<P>nor her friend had taken cold the day before," &amp;c. &amp;c; which

was



<P>all as politely done, and as politely taken, as possible, but her part



<P>must follow then.&nbsp; Sir Walter talked of his youngest daughter;



<P>"Mr Elliot must give him leave to present him to his youngest daughter"



<P>(there was no occasion for remembering Mary); and Anne, smiling and



<P>blushing, very becomingly shewed to Mr Elliot the pretty features



<P>which he had by no means forgotten, and instantly saw, with amusement



<P>at his little start of surprise, that he had not been at all aware



<P>of who she was.&nbsp; He looked completely astonished, but not more

astonished



<P>than pleased; his eyes brightened! and with the most perfect alacrity



<P>he welcomed the relationship, alluded to the past, and entreated



<P>to be received as an acquaintance already.&nbsp; He was quite as good-looking



<P>as he had appeared at Lyme, his countenance improved by speaking,



<P>and his manners were so exactly what they ought to be, so polished,



<P>so easy, so particularly agreeable, that she could compare them



<P>in excellence to only one person's manners.&nbsp; They were not the

same,



<P>but they were, perhaps, equally good.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He sat down with them, and improved their conversation very much.



<P>There could be no doubt of his being a sensible man.&nbsp; Ten minutes



<P>were enough to certify that.&nbsp; His tone, his expressions,



<P>his choice of subject, his knowing where to stop; it was all



<P>the operation of a sensible, discerning mind.&nbsp; As soon as he could,



<P>he began to talk to her of Lyme, wanting to compare opinions



<P>respecting the place, but especially wanting to speak of the circumstance



<P>of their happening to be guests in the same inn at the same time;



<P>to give his own route, understand something of hers, and regret that



<P>he should have lost such an opportunity of paying his respects to her.



<P>She gave him a short account of her party and business at Lyme.



<P>His regret increased as he listened.&nbsp; He had spent his whole



<P>solitary evening in the room adjoining theirs; had heard voices,



<P>mirth continually; thought they must be a most delightful set of people,



<P>longed to be with them, but certainly without the smallest suspicion



<P>of his possessing the shadow of a right to introduce himself.



<P>If he had but asked who the party were!&nbsp; The name of Musgrove would



<P>have told him enough.&nbsp; "Well, it would serve to cure him of



<P>an absurd practice of never asking a question at an inn,



<P>which he had adopted, when quite a young man, on the principal



<P>of its being very ungenteel to be curious.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"The notions of a young man of one or two and twenty," said he,



<P>"as to what is necessary in manners to make him quite the thing,



<P>are more absurd, I believe, than those of any other set of beings



<P>in the world.&nbsp; The folly of the means they often employ



<P>is only to be equalled by the folly of what they have in view."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But he must not be addressing his reflections to Anne alone:



<P>he knew it; he was soon diffused again among the others,



<P>and it was only at intervals that he could return to Lyme.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His enquiries, however, produced at length an account of the scene



<P>she had been engaged in there, soon after his leaving the place.



<P>Having alluded to "an accident,"&nbsp; he must hear the whole.



<P>When he questioned, Sir Walter and Elizabeth began to question also,



<P>but the difference in their manner of doing it could not be unfelt.



<P>She could only compare Mr Elliot to Lady Russell, in the wish



<P>of really comprehending what had passed, and in the degree of concern



<P>for what she must have suffered in witnessing it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He staid an hour with them.&nbsp; The elegant little clock on the mantel-



<P>piece had struck "eleven with its silver sounds," and the watchman



<P>was beginning to be heard at a distance telling the same tale,



<P>before Mr Elliot or any of them seemed to feel that he had been there

long.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne could not have supposed it possible that her first evening in



<P>Camden Place could have passed so well!



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 14</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 14</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Though Charles and Mary had remained at Lyme much longer after



<P>Mr and Mrs Musgrove's going than Anne conceived they could have been



<P>at all wanted, they were yet the first of the family to be at home again;



<P>and as soon as possible after their return to Uppercross



<P>they drove over to the Lodge.&nbsp; They had left Louisa beginning to

sit up;



<P>but her head, though clear, was exceedingly weak, and her nerves



<P>susceptible to the highest extreme of tenderness; and though



<P>she might be pronounced to be altogether doing very well,



<P>it was still impossible to say when she might be able to bear



<P>the removal home; and her father and mother, who must return



<P>in time to receive their younger children for the Christmas holidays,



<P>had hardly a hope of being allowed to bring her with them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They had been all in lodgings together.&nbsp; Mrs Musgrove had



<P>got Mrs Harville's children away as much as she could, every possible



<P>supply from Uppercross had been furnished, to lighten the inconvenience



<P>to the Harvilles, while the Harvilles had been wanting them



<P>to come to dinner every day; and in short, it seemed to have been



<P>only a struggle on each side as to which should be most disinterested



<P>and hospitable.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mary had had her evils; but upon the whole, as was evident



<P>by her staying so long, she had found more to enjoy than to suffer.



<P>Charles Hayter had been at Lyme oftener than suited her; and when



<P>they dined with the Harvilles there had been only a maid-servant to

wait,



<P>and at first Mrs Harville had always given Mrs Musgrove precedence;



<P>but then, she had received so very handsome an apology from her



<P>on finding out whose daughter she was, and there had been so much



<P>going on every day, there had been so many walks between their lodgings



<P>and the Harvilles, and she had got books from the library,



<P>and changed them so often, that the balance had certainly been



<P>much in favour of Lyme.&nbsp; She had been taken to Charmouth too,



<P>and she had bathed, and she had gone to church, and there were a great

many



<P>more people to look at in the church at Lyme than at Uppercross;



<P>and all this, joined to the sense of being so very useful,



<P>had made really an agreeable fortnight.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne enquired after Captain Benwick, Mary's face was clouded directly.



<P>Charles laughed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! Captain Benwick is very well, I believe, but he is



<P>a very odd young man.&nbsp; I do not know what he would be at.



<P>We asked him to come home with us for a day or two:&nbsp; Charles undertook



<P>to give him some shooting, and he seemed quite delighted, and, for my

part,



<P>I thought it was all settled; when behold! on Tuesday night,



<P>he made a very awkward sort of excuse; `he never shot' and he had



<P>`been quite misunderstood,' and he had promised this and he had



<P>promised that, and the end of it was, I found, that he did not mean

to come.



<P>I suppose he was afraid of finding it dull; but upon my word



<P>I should have thought we were lively enough at the Cottage



<P>for such a heart-broken man as Captain Benwick."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Charles laughed again and said, "Now Mary, you know very well



<P>how it really was.&nbsp; It was all your doing," (turning to Anne.)



<P>"He fancied that if he went with us, he should find you close by:



<P>he fancied everybody to be living in Uppercross; and when he discovered



<P>that Lady Russell lived three miles off, his heart failed him,



<P>and he had not courage to come.&nbsp; That is the fact, upon my honour,



<P>Mary knows it is."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But Mary did not give into it very graciously, whether from



<P>not considering Captain Benwick entitled by birth and situation



<P>to be in love with an Elliot, or from not wanting to believe



<P>Anne a greater attraction to Uppercross than herself, must be



<P>left to be guessed.&nbsp; Anne's good-will, however, was not to be lessened



<P>by what she heard.&nbsp; She boldly acknowledged herself flattered,



<P>and continued her enquiries.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! he talks of you," cried Charles, "in such terms--"



<P>Mary interrupted him. "I declare, Charles, I never heard him



<P>mention Anne twice all the time I was there.&nbsp; I declare, Anne,



<P>he never talks of you at all."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No," admitted Charles, "I do not know that he ever does, in a general



<P>way; but however, it is a very clear thing that he admires you exceedingly.



<P>His head is full of some books that he is reading upon your recommendation,



<P>and he wants to talk to you about them; he has found out something or

other



<P>in one of them which he thinks-- oh! I cannot pretend to remember it,



<P>but it was something very fine--I overheard him telling Henrietta



<P>all about it; and then `Miss Elliot' was spoken of in the highest terms!



<P>Now Mary, I declare it was so, I heard it myself, and you were



<P>in the other room.&nbsp; `Elegance, sweetness, beauty.' Oh! there was

no end



<P>of Miss Elliot's charms."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And I am sure," cried Mary, warmly, "it was a very little to his credit,



<P>if he did.&nbsp; Miss Harville only died last June.&nbsp; Such a heart



<P>is very little worth having; is it, Lady Russell?&nbsp; I am sure



<P>you will agree with me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I must see Captain Benwick before I decide," said Lady Russell, smiling.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And that you are very likely to do very soon, I can tell you, ma'am,"



<P>said Charles.&nbsp; "Though he had not nerves for coming away with us,



<P>and setting off again afterwards to pay a formal visit here,



<P>he will make his way over to Kellynch one day by himself,



<P>you may depend on it.&nbsp; I told him the distance and the road,



<P>and I told him of the church's being so very well worth seeing;



<P>for as he has a taste for those sort of things, I thought that would



<P>be a good excuse, and he listened with all his understanding and soul;



<P>and I am sure from his manner that you will have him calling here soon.



<P>So, I give you notice, Lady Russell."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Any acquaintance of Anne's will always be welcome to me,"



<P>was Lady Russell's kind answer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! as to being Anne's acquaintance," said Mary, "I think he is rather



<P>my acquaintance, for I have been seeing him every day this last fortnight."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, as your joint acquaintance, then, I shall be very happy



<P>to see Captain Benwick."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You will not find anything very agreeable in him, I assure you, ma'am.



<P>He is one of the dullest young men that ever lived.&nbsp; He has walked

with me,



<P>sometimes, from one end of the sands to the other, without saying a

word.



<P>He is not at all a well-bred young man.&nbsp; I am sure you will not

like him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"There we differ, Mary," said Anne.&nbsp; "I think Lady Russell would

like him.



<P>I think she would be so much pleased with his mind, that she would



<P>very soon see no deficiency in his manner."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"So do I, Anne," said Charles.&nbsp; "I am sure Lady Russell would like

him.



<P>He is just Lady Russell's sort.&nbsp; Give him a book, and he will



<P>read all day long."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, that he will!" exclaimed Mary, tauntingly.&nbsp; "He will sit

poring



<P>over his book, and not know when a person speaks to him, or when one



<P>drop's one's scissors, or anything that happens.&nbsp; Do you think



<P>Lady Russell would like that?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell could not help laughing.&nbsp; "Upon my word," said she,



<P>"I should not have supposed that my opinion of any one could have



<P>admitted of such difference of conjecture, steady and matter of fact



<P>as I may call myself.&nbsp; I have really a curiosity to see the person



<P>who can give occasion to such directly opposite notions.



<P>I wish he may be induced to call here.&nbsp; And when he does, Mary,



<P>you may depend upon hearing my opinion; but I am determined



<P>not to judge him beforehand."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You will not like him, I will answer for it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell began talking of something else.&nbsp; Mary spoke with

animation



<P>of their meeting with, or rather missing, Mr Elliot so extraordinarily.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"He is a man," said Lady Russell, "whom I have no wish to see.



<P>His declining to be on cordial terms with the head of his family,



<P>has left a very strong impression in his disfavour with me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This decision checked Mary's eagerness, and stopped her short



<P>in the midst of the Elliot countenance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>With regard to Captain Wentworth, though Anne hazarded no enquiries,



<P>there was voluntary communication sufficient.&nbsp; His spirits had

been



<P>greatly recovering lately as might be expected.&nbsp; As Louisa improved,



<P>he had improved, and he was now quite a different creature



<P>from what he had been the first week.&nbsp; He had not seen Louisa;



<P>and was so extremely fearful of any ill consequence to her



<P>from an interview, that he did not press for it at all; and,



<P>on the contrary, seemed to have a plan of going away for a week



<P>or ten days, till her head was stronger.&nbsp; He had talked of going



<P>down to Plymouth for a week, and wanted to persuade Captain Benwick



<P>to go with him; but, as Charles maintained to the last, Captain Benwick



<P>seemed much more disposed to ride over to Kellynch.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There can be no doubt that Lady Russell and Anne were both



<P>occasionally thinking of Captain Benwick, from this time.



<P>Lady Russell could not hear the door-bell without feeling that it might



<P>be his herald; nor could Anne return from any stroll of solitary indulgence



<P>in her father's grounds, or any visit of charity in the village,



<P>without wondering whether she might see him or hear of him.



<P>Captain Benwick came not, however.&nbsp; He was either less disposed

for it



<P>than Charles had imagined, or he was too shy; and after giving him



<P>a week's indulgence, Lady Russell determined him to be unworthy



<P>of the interest which he had been beginning to excite.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The Musgroves came back to receive their happy boys and girls from school,



<P>bringing with them Mrs Harville's little children, to improve the noise



<P>of Uppercross, and lessen that of Lyme.&nbsp; Henrietta remained with

Louisa;



<P>but all the rest of the family were again in their usual quarters.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell and Anne paid their compliments to them once,



<P>when Anne could not but feel that Uppercross was already quite alive

again.



<P>Though neither Henrietta, nor Louisa, nor Charles Hayter,



<P>nor Captain Wentworth were there, the room presented as strong a contrast



<P>as could be wished to the last state she had seen it in.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Immediately surrounding Mrs Musgrove were the little Harvilles,



<P>whom she was sedulously guarding from the tyranny of the two children



<P>from the Cottage, expressly arrived to amuse them.&nbsp; On one side



<P>was a table occupied by some chattering girls, cutting up silk



<P>and gold paper; and on the other were tressels and trays,



<P>bending under the weight of brawn and cold pies, where riotous boys



<P>were holding high revel; the whole completed by a roaring Christmas

fire,



<P>which seemed determined to be heard, in spite of all the noise



<P>of the others.&nbsp; Charles and Mary also came in, of course,



<P>during their visit, and Mr Musgrove made a point of paying his respects



<P>to Lady Russell, and sat down close to her for ten minutes,



<P>talking with a very raised voice, but from the clamour of the children



<P>on his knees, generally in vain.&nbsp; It was a fine family-piece.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne, judging from her own temperament, would have deemed



<P>such a domestic hurricane a bad restorative of the nerves,



<P>which Louisa's illness must have so greatly shaken.&nbsp; But Mrs Musgrove,



<P>who got Anne near her on purpose to thank her most cordially,



<P>again and again, for all her attentions to them, concluded



<P>a short recapitulation of what she had suffered herself by observing,



<P>with a happy glance round the room, that after all she had gone through,



<P>nothing was so likely to do her good as a little quiet cheerfulness



<P>at home.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Louisa was now recovering apace.&nbsp; Her mother could even think of

her



<P>being able to join their party at home, before her brothers and sisters



<P>went to school again.&nbsp; The Harvilles had promised to come with

her



<P>and stay at Uppercross, whenever she returned.&nbsp; Captain Wentworth

was gone,



<P>for the present, to see his brother in Shropshire.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I hope I shall remember, in future," said Lady Russell, as soon as



<P>they were reseated in the carriage, "not to call at Uppercross



<P>in the Christmas holidays."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Everybody has their taste in noises as well as in other matters;



<P>and sounds are quite innoxious, or most distressing, by their sort



<P>rather than their quantity.&nbsp; When Lady Russell not long afterwards,



<P>was entering Bath on a wet afternoon, and driving through



<P>the long course of streets from the Old Bridge to Camden Place,



<P>amidst the dash of other carriages, the heavy rumble of carts and drays,



<P>the bawling of newspapermen, muffin-men and milkmen, and the ceaseless



<P>clink of pattens, she made no complaint.&nbsp; No, these were noises



<P>which belonged to the winter pleasures; her spirits rose



<P>under their influence; and like Mrs Musgrove, she was feeling,



<P>though not saying, that after being long in the country, nothing could

be



<P>so good for her as a little quiet cheerfulness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne did not share these feelings.&nbsp; She persisted in a very determined,



<P>though very silent disinclination for Bath; caught the first dim view



<P>of the extensive buildings, smoking in rain, without any wish



<P>of seeing them better; felt their progress through the streets to be,



<P>however disagreeable, yet too rapid; for who would be glad to see her



<P>when she arrived?&nbsp; And looked back, with fond regret, to the bustles



<P>of Uppercross and the seclusion of Kellynch.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Elizabeth's last letter had communicated a piece of news of some interest.



<P>Mr Elliot was in Bath.&nbsp; He had called in Camden Place; had called



<P>a second time, a third; had been pointedly attentive.&nbsp; If Elizabeth



<P>and her father did not deceive themselves, had been taking much pains



<P>to seek the acquaintance, and proclaim the value of the connection,



<P>as he had formerly taken pains to shew neglect.&nbsp; This was very

wonderful



<P>if it were true; and Lady Russell was in a state of very agreeable



<P>curiosity and perplexity about Mr Elliot, already recanting the sentiment



<P>she had so lately expressed to Mary, of his being "a man whom she had



<P>no wish to see."&nbsp; She had a great wish to see him.&nbsp; If he

really sought



<P>to reconcile himself like a dutiful branch, he must be forgiven



<P>for having dismembered himself from the paternal tree.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne was not animated to an equal pitch by the circumstance,



<P>but she felt that she would rather see Mr Elliot again than not,



<P>which was more than she could say for many other persons in Bath.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She was put down in Camden Place; and Lady Russell then drove



<P>to her own lodgings, in Rivers Street.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 04</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 4</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>He was not Mr Wentworth, the former curate of Monkford,



<P>however suspicious appearances may be, but a Captain Frederick Wentworth,



<P>his brother, who being made commander in consequence of the action



<P>off St Domingo, and not immediately employed, had come into Somersetshire,



<P>in the summer of 1806; and having no parent living, found a home



<P>for half a year at Monkford.&nbsp; He was, at that time, a remarkably

fine



<P>young man, with a great deal of intelligence, spirit, and brilliancy;



<P>and Anne an extremely pretty girl, with gentleness, modesty, taste,



<P>and feeling.&nbsp; Half the sum of attraction, on either side, might

have



<P>been enough, for he had nothing to do, and she had hardly anybody to

love;



<P>but the encounter of such lavish recommendations could not fail.



<P>They were gradually acquainted, and when acquainted, rapidly and



<P>deeply in love.&nbsp; It would be difficult to say which had seen



<P>highest perfection in the other, or which had been the happiest:



<P>she, in receiving his declarations and proposals, or he in



<P>having them accepted.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A short period of exquisite felicity followed, and but a short one.



<P>Troubles soon arose.&nbsp; Sir Walter, on being applied to, without

actually



<P>withholding his consent, or saying it should never be, gave it all



<P>the negative of great astonishment, great coldness, great silence,



<P>and a professed resolution of doing nothing for his daughter.



<P>He thought it a very degrading alliance; and Lady Russell, though with



<P>more tempered and pardonable pride, received it as a most unfortunate

one.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne Elliot, with all her claims of birth, beauty, and mind,



<P>to throw herself away at nineteen; involve herself at nineteen



<P>in an engagement with a young man, who had nothing but himself



<P>to recommend him, and no hopes of attaining affluence, but in the chances



<P>of a most uncertain profession, and no connexions to secure



<P>even his farther rise in the profession, would be, indeed, a throwing

away,



<P>which she grieved to think of!&nbsp; Anne Elliot, so young; known to

so few,



<P>to be snatched off by a stranger without alliance or fortune;



<P>or rather sunk by him into a state of most wearing, anxious,



<P>youth-killing dependence!&nbsp; It must not be, if by any fair interference



<P>of friendship, any representations from one who had almost a mother's

love,



<P>and mother's rights, it would be prevented.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Wentworth had no fortune.&nbsp; He had been lucky in his profession;



<P>but spending freely, what had come freely, had realized nothing.



<P>But he was confident that he should soon be rich:&nbsp; full of life

and ardour,



<P>he knew that he should soon have a ship, and soon be on a station



<P>that would lead to everything he wanted.&nbsp; He had always been lucky;



<P>he knew he knew he should be so still.&nbsp; Such confidence, powerful



<P>in its own warmth, and bewitching in the wit which often expressed it,



<P>must have been enough for Anne; but Lady Russell saw it very differently.



<P>His sanguine temper, and fearlessness of mind, operated very differently



<P>on her.&nbsp; She saw in it but an aggravation of the evil.&nbsp; It

only added



<P>a dangerous character to himself.&nbsp; He was brilliant, he was headstrong.



<P>Lady Russell had little taste for wit, and of anything approaching to



<P>imprudence a horror.&nbsp; She deprecated the connexion in every light.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Such opposition, as these feelings produced, was more than



<P>Anne could combat.&nbsp; Young and gentle as she was, it might yet



<P>have been possible to withstand her father's ill-will,



<P>though unsoftened by one kind word or look on the part of her sister;



<P>but Lady Russell, whom she had always loved and relied on, could not,



<P>with such steadiness of opinion, and such tenderness of manner,



<P>be continually advising her in vain.&nbsp; She was persuaded to believe



<P>the engagement a wrong thing:&nbsp; indiscreet, improper, hardly capable



<P>of success, and not deserving it.&nbsp; But it was not a merely selfish

caution,



<P>under which she acted, in putting an end to it.&nbsp; Had she not



<P>imagined herself consulting his good, even more than her own,



<P>she could hardly have given him up.&nbsp; The belief of being prudent,



<P>and self-denying, principally for his advantage, was her chief consolation,



<P>under the misery of a parting, a final parting; and every consolation



<P>was required, for she had to encounter all the additional pain of opinions,



<P>on his side, totally unconvinced and unbending, and of his feeling himself



<P>ill used by so forced a relinquishment.&nbsp; He had left the country



<P>in consequence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A few months had seen the beginning and the end of their acquaintance;



<P>but not with a few months ended Anne's share of suffering from it.



<P>Her attachment and regrets had, for a long time, clouded every



<P>enjoyment of youth, and an early loss of bloom and spirits



<P>had been their lasting effect.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>More than seven years were gone since this little history



<P>of sorrowful interest had reached its close; and time had



<P>softened down much, perhaps nearly all of peculiar attachment to him,



<P>but she had been too dependent on time alone; no aid had been given



<P>in change of place (except in one visit to Bath soon after the rupture),



<P>or in any novelty or enlargement of society.&nbsp; No one had ever



<P>come within the Kellynch circle, who could bear a comparison with



<P>Frederick Wentworth, as he stood in her memory.&nbsp; No second attachment,



<P>the only thoroughly natural, happy, and sufficient cure,



<P>at her time of life, had been possible to the nice tone of her mind,



<P>the fastidiousness of her taste, in the small limits of the society



<P>around them.&nbsp; She had been solicited, when about two-and-twenty,



<P>to change her name, by the young man, who not long afterwards found



<P>a more willing mind in her younger sister; and Lady Russell had



<P>lamented her refusal; for Charles Musgrove was the eldest son of a man,



<P>whose landed property and general importance were second in that country,



<P>only to Sir Walter's, and of good character and appearance;



<P>and however Lady Russell might have asked yet for something more,



<P>while Anne was nineteen, she would have rejoiced to see her at twenty-two



<P>so respectably removed from the partialities and injustice of



<P>her father's house, and settled so permanently near herself.



<P>But in this case, Anne had left nothing for advice to do;



<P>and though Lady Russell, as satisfied as ever with her own discretion,



<P>never wished the past undone, she began now to have the anxiety



<P>which borders on hopelessness for Anne's being tempted, by some man



<P>of talents and independence, to enter a state for which she held her



<P>to be peculiarly fitted by her warm affections and domestic habits.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They knew not each other's opinion, either its constancy or its change,



<P>on the one leading point of Anne's conduct, for the subject was never



<P>alluded to; but Anne, at seven-and-twenty, thought very differently



<P>from what she had been made to think at nineteen.&nbsp; She did not

blame



<P>Lady Russell, she did not blame herself for having been guided by her;



<P>but she felt that were any young person, in similar circumstances,



<P>to apply to her for counsel, they would never receive any of such



<P>certain immediate wretchedness, such uncertain future good.



<P>She was persuaded that under every disadvantage of disapprobation at

home,



<P>and every anxiety attending his profession, all their probable fears,



<P>delays, and disappointments, she should yet have been a happier woman



<P>in maintaining the engagement, than she had been in the sacrifice of

it;



<P>and this, she fully believed, had the usual share, had even more than



<P>the usual share of all such solicitudes and suspense been theirs,



<P>without reference to the actual results of their case, which,



<P>as it happened, would have bestowed earlier prosperity than



<P>could be reasonably calculated on.&nbsp; All his sanguine expectations,



<P>all his confidence had been justified.&nbsp; His genius and ardour



<P>had seemed to foresee and to command his prosperous path.



<P>He had, very soon after their engagement ceased, got employ:



<P>and all that he had told her would follow, had taken place.



<P>He had distinguished himself, and early gained the other step in rank,



<P>and must now, by successive captures, have made a handsome fortune.



<P>She had only navy lists and newspapers for her authority,



<P>but she could not doubt his being rich; and, in favour of his constancy,



<P>she had no reason to believe him married.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>How eloquent could Anne Elliot have been! how eloquent, at least,



<P>were her wishes on the side of early warm attachment, and a cheerful



<P>confidence in futurity, against that over-anxious caution which



<P>seems to insult exertion and distrust Providence!&nbsp; She had been

forced



<P>into prudence in her youth, she learned romance as she grew older:



<P>the natural sequel of an unnatural beginning.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>With all these circumstances, recollections and feelings,



<P>she could not hear that Captain Wentworth's sister was likely



<P>to live at Kellynch without a revival of former pain; and many a stroll,



<P>and many a sigh, were necessary to dispel the agitation of the idea.



<P>She often told herself it was folly, before she could harden her nerves



<P>sufficiently to feel the continual discussion of the Crofts



<P>and their business no evil.&nbsp; She was assisted, however, by that



<P>perfect indifference and apparent unconsciousness, among the only three



<P>of her own friends in the secret of the past, which seemed almost to

deny



<P>any recollection of it.&nbsp; She could do justice to the superiority



<P>of Lady Russell's motives in this, over those of her father and Elizabeth;



<P>she could honour all the better feelings of her calmness;



<P>but the general air of oblivion among them was highly important



<P>from whatever it sprung; and in the event of Admiral Croft's really



<P>taking Kellynch Hall, she rejoiced anew over the conviction which



<P>had always been most grateful to her, of the past being known to



<P>those three only among her connexions, by whom no syllable,



<P>she believed, would ever be whispered, and in the trust that among his,



<P>the brother only with whom he had been residing, had received



<P>any information of their short-lived engagement.&nbsp; That brother

had been



<P>long removed from the country and being a sensible man, and, moreover,



<P>a single man at the time, she had a fond dependence on no human creature's



<P>having heard of it from him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The sister, Mrs Croft, had then been out of England, accompanying



<P>her husband on a foreign station, and her own sister, Mary,



<P>had been at school while it all occurred; and never admitted by



<P>the pride of some, and the delicacy of others, to the smallest knowledge



<P>of it afterwards.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>With these supports, she hoped that the acquaintance between herself



<P>and the Crofts, which, with Lady Russell, still resident in Kellynch,



<P>and Mary fixed only three miles off, must be anticipated,



<P>need not involve any particular awkwardness.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 10</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 10</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Other opportunities of making her observations could not fail to occur.



<P>Anne had soon been in company with all the four together often enough



<P>to have an opinion, though too wise to acknowledge as much at home,



<P>where she knew it would have satisfied neither husband nor wife;



<P>for while she considered Louisa to be rather the favourite,



<P>she could not but think, as far as she might dare to judge from memory



<P>and experience, that Captain Wentworth was not in love with either.



<P>They were more in love with him; yet there it was not love.



<P>It was a little fever of admiration; but it might, probably must,



<P>end in love with some.&nbsp; Charles Hayter seemed aware of being slighted,



<P>and yet Henrietta had sometimes the air of being divided between them.



<P>Anne longed for the power of representing to them all what they were

about,



<P>and of pointing out some of the evils they were exposing themselves

to.



<P>She did not attribute guile to any.&nbsp; It was the highest satisfaction



<P>to her to believe Captain Wentworth not in the least aware



<P>of the pain he was occasioning.&nbsp; There was no triumph, no pitiful

triumph



<P>in his manner.&nbsp; He had, probably, never heard, and never thought

of



<P>any claims of Charles Hayter.&nbsp; He was only wrong in accepting



<P>the attentions (for accepting must be the word) of two young women at

once.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a short struggle, however, Charles Hayter seemed to quit the field.



<P>Three days had passed without his coming once to Uppercross;



<P>a most decided change.&nbsp; He had even refused one regular invitation

to dinner;



<P>and having been found on the occasion by Mr Musgrove with some large

books



<P>before him, Mr and Mrs Musgrove were sure all could not be right,



<P>and talked, with grave faces, of his studying himself to death.



<P>It was Mary's hope and belief that he had received a positive dismissal



<P>from Henrietta, and her husband lived under the constant dependence



<P>of seeing him to-morrow.&nbsp; Anne could only feel that Charles Hayter



<P>was wise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One morning, about this time Charles Musgrove and Captain Wentworth



<P>being gone a-shooting together, as the sisters in the Cottage



<P>were sitting quietly at work, they were visited at the window



<P>by the sisters from the Mansion-house.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was a very fine November day, and the Miss Musgroves came



<P>through the little grounds, and stopped for no other purpose than to

say,



<P>that they were going to take a long walk, and therefore concluded



<P>Mary could not like to go with them; and when Mary immediately replied,



<P>with some jealousy at not being supposed a good walker, "Oh, yes,



<P>I should like to join you very much, I am very fond of a long walk;"



<P>Anne felt persuaded, by the looks of the two girls, that it was precisely



<P>what they did not wish, and admired again the sort of necessity



<P>which the family habits seemed to produce, of everything being



<P>to be communicated, and everything being to be done together,



<P>however undesired and inconvenient.&nbsp; She tried to dissuade Mary

from going,



<P>but in vain; and that being the case, thought it best to accept



<P>the Miss Musgroves' much more cordial invitation to herself to go likewise,



<P>as she might be useful in turning back with her sister, and lessening



<P>the interference in any plan of their own.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I cannot imagine why they should suppose I should not like a long walk,"



<P>said Mary, as she went up stairs.&nbsp; "Everybody is always supposing



<P>that I am not a good walker; and yet they would not have been pleased,



<P>if we had refused to join them.&nbsp; When people come in this manner



<P>on purpose to ask us, how can one say no?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Just as they were setting off, the gentlemen returned.&nbsp; They had

taken out



<P>a young dog, who had spoilt their sport, and sent them back early.



<P>Their time and strength, and spirits, were, therefore, exactly ready



<P>for this walk, and they entered into it with pleasure.&nbsp; Could Anne



<P>have foreseen such a junction, she would have staid at home; but,



<P>from some feelings of interest and curiosity, she fancied now that it

was



<P>too late to retract, and the whole six set forward together



<P>in the direction chosen by the Miss Musgroves, who evidently



<P>considered the walk as under their guidance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne's object was, not to be in the way of anybody; and where



<P>the narrow paths across the fields made many separations necessary,



<P>to keep with her brother and sister.&nbsp; Her pleasure in the walk



<P>must arise from the exercise and the day, from the view of



<P>the last smiles of the year upon the tawny leaves, and withered hedges,



<P>and from repeating to herself some few of the thousand poetical



<P>descriptions extant of autumn, that season of peculiar and



<P>inexhaustible influence on the mind of taste and tenderness,



<P>that season which had drawn from every poet, worthy of being read,



<P>some attempt at description, or some lines of feeling.



<P>She occupied her mind as much as possible in such like musings



<P>and quotations; but it was not possible, that when within reach



<P>of Captain Wentworth's conversation with either of the Miss Musgroves,



<P>she should not try to hear it; yet she caught little very remarkable.



<P>It was mere lively chat, such as any young persons, on an intimate footing,



<P>might fall into.&nbsp; He was more engaged with Louisa than with Henrietta.



<P>Louisa certainly put more forward for his notice than her sister.



<P>This distinction appeared to increase, and there was one speech



<P>of Louisa's which struck her.&nbsp; After one of the many praises of

the day,



<P>which were continually bursting forth, Captain Wentworth added: --

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What glorious weather for the Admiral and my sister!&nbsp; They meant

to take



<P>a long drive this morning; perhaps we may hail them from



<P>some of these hills.&nbsp; They talked of coming into this side of the

country.



<P>I wonder whereabouts they will upset to-day.&nbsp; Oh! it does happen



<P>very often, I assure you; but my sister makes nothing of it;



<P>she would as lieve be tossed out as not."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ah! You make the most of it, I know," cried Louisa, "but if it were



<P>really so, I should do just the same in her place.&nbsp; If I loved

a man,



<P>as she loves the Admiral, I would always be with him, nothing should

ever



<P>separate us, and I would rather be overturned by him, than driven safely



<P>by anybody else."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was spoken with enthusiasm.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Had you?" cried he, catching the same tone; "I honour you!"



<P>And there was silence between them for a little while.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne could not immediately fall into a quotation again.&nbsp; The sweet

scenes



<P>of autumn were for a while put by, unless some tender sonnet,



<P>fraught with the apt analogy of the declining year, with declining



<P>happiness, and the images of youth and hope, and spring, all gone together,



<P>blessed her memory.&nbsp; She roused herself to say, as they struck

by order



<P>into another path, "Is not this one of the ways to Winthrop?"



<P>But nobody heard, or, at least, nobody answered her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Winthrop, however, or its environs--for young men are, sometimes



<P>to be met with, strolling about near home--was their destination;



<P>and after another half mile of gradual ascent through large enclosures,



<P>where the ploughs at work, and the fresh made path spoke the farmer



<P>counteracting the sweets of poetical despondence, and meaning



<P>to have spring again, they gained the summit of the most considerable

hill,



<P>which parted Uppercross and Winthrop, and soon commanded a full view



<P>of the latter, at the foot of the hill on the other side.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Winthrop, without beauty and without dignity, was stretched before them



<P>an indifferent house, standing low, and hemmed in by the barns and



<P>buildings of a farm-yard.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mary exclaimed, "Bless me! here is Winthrop.&nbsp; I declare I had no

idea!



<P>Well now, I think we had better turn back; I am excessively tired."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Henrietta, conscious and ashamed, and seeing no cousin Charles



<P>walking along any path, or leaning against any gate, was ready



<P>to do as Mary wished; but "No!" said Charles Musgrove, and "No, no!"



<P>cried Louisa more eagerly, and taking her sister aside, seemed to be



<P>arguing the matter warmly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Charles, in the meanwhile, was very decidedly declaring his resolution



<P>of calling on his aunt, now that he was so near; and very evidently,



<P>though more fearfully, trying to induce his wife to go too.



<P>But this was one of the points on which the lady shewed her strength;



<P>and when he recommended the advantage of resting herself a quarter



<P>of an hour at Winthrop, as she felt so tired, she resolutely answered,



<P>"Oh! no, indeed! walking up that hill again would do her more harm



<P>than any sitting down could do her good;" and, in short,



<P>her look and manner declared, that go she would not.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a little succession of these sort of debates and consultations,



<P>it was settled between Charles and his two sisters, that he



<P>and Henrietta should just run down for a few minutes, to see their aunt



<P>and cousins, while the rest of the party waited for them at the top



<P>of the hill.&nbsp; Louisa seemed the principal arranger of the plan;



<P>and, as she went a little way with them, down the hill, still talking



<P>to Henrietta, Mary took the opportunity of looking scornfully around

her,



<P>and saying to Captain Wentworth--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It is very unpleasant, having such connexions!&nbsp; But, I assure

you,



<P>I have never been in the house above twice in my life."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She received no other answer, than an artificial, assenting smile,



<P>followed by a contemptuous glance, as he turned away, which Anne



<P>perfectly knew the meaning of.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The brow of the hill, where they remained, was a cheerful spot:



<P>Louisa returned; and Mary, finding a comfortable seat for herself



<P>on the step of a stile, was very well satisfied so long as the others



<P>all stood about her; but when Louisa drew Captain Wentworth away,



<P>to try for a gleaning of nuts in an adjoining hedge-row,



<P>and they were gone by degrees quite out of sight and sound,



<P>Mary was happy no longer; she quarrelled with her own seat,



<P>was sure Louisa had got a much better somewhere, and nothing could



<P>prevent her from going to look for a better also.&nbsp; She turned through



<P>the same gate, but could not see them.&nbsp; Anne found a nice seat



<P>for her, on a dry sunny bank, under the hedge-row, in which



<P>she had no doubt of their still being, in some spot or other.



<P>Mary sat down for a moment, but it would not do; she was sure Louisa



<P>had found a better seat somewhere else, and she would go on



<P>till she overtook her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne, really tired herself, was glad to sit down; and she very soon

heard



<P>Captain Wentworth and Louisa in the hedge-row, behind her, as if



<P>making their way back along the rough, wild sort of channel, down the



<P>centre.&nbsp; They were speaking as they drew near.&nbsp; Louisa's voice

was



<P>the first distinguished.&nbsp; She seemed to be in the middle of some



<P>eager speech.&nbsp; What Anne first heard was--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And so, I made her go.&nbsp; I could not bear that she should be frightened



<P>from the visit by such nonsense.&nbsp; What! would I be turned back

from



<P>doing a thing that I had determined to do, and that I knew to be right,



<P>by the airs and interference of such a person, or of any person I may

say?



<P>No, I have no idea of being so easily persuaded.&nbsp; When I have



<P>made up my mind, I have made it; and Henrietta seemed entirely



<P>to have made up hers to call at Winthrop to-day; and yet, she was as

near



<P>giving it up, out of nonsensical complaisance!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"She would have turned back then, but for you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"She would indeed.&nbsp; I am almost ashamed to say it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Happy for her, to have such a mind as yours at hand!&nbsp; After the

hints



<P>you gave just now, which did but confirm my own observations,



<P>the last time I was in company with him,&nbsp; I need not affect



<P>to have no comprehension of what is going on.&nbsp; I see that more

than



<P>a mere dutiful morning visit to your aunt was in question;



<P>and woe betide him, and her too, when it comes to things of consequence,



<P>when they are placed in circumstances requiring fortitude and



<P>strength of mind, if she have not resolution enough to resist



<P>idle interference in such a trifle as this.&nbsp; Your sister is



<P>an amiable creature; but yours is the character of decision and firmness,



<P>I see.&nbsp; If you value her conduct or happiness, infuse as much



<P>of your own spirit into her as you can.&nbsp; But this, no doubt,



<P>you have been always doing.&nbsp; It is the worst evil of too yielding



<P>and indecisive a character, that no influence over it can be depended

on.



<P>You are never sure of a good impression being durable; everybody



<P>may sway it.&nbsp; Let those who would be happy be firm.&nbsp; Here

is a nut,"



<P>said he, catching one down from an upper bough.&nbsp; "to exemplify:



<P>a beautiful glossy nut, which, blessed with original strength,



<P>has outlived all the storms of autumn.&nbsp; Not a puncture, not



<P>a weak spot anywhere.&nbsp; This nut," he continued, with playful solemnity,



<P>"while so many of his brethren have fallen and been trodden under foot,



<P>is still in possession of all the happiness that a hazel nut can be



<P>supposed capable of."&nbsp; Then returning to his former earnest tone--



<P>"My first wish for all whom I am interested in, is that they should

be firm.



<P>If Louisa Musgrove would be beautiful and happy in her November of life,



<P>she will cherish all her present powers of mind."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had done, and was unanswered.&nbsp; It would have surprised Anne

if Louisa



<P>could have readily answered such a speech:&nbsp; words of such interest,



<P>spoken with such serious warmth!&nbsp; She could imagine what Louisa

was feeling.



<P>For herself, she feared to move, lest she should be seen.



<P>While she remained, a bush of low rambling holly protected her,



<P>and they were moving on.&nbsp; Before they were beyond her hearing,



<P>however, Louisa spoke again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Mary is good-natured enough in many respects," said she;



<P>"but she does sometimes provoke me excessively, by her nonsense



<P>and pride-- the Elliot pride.&nbsp; She has a great deal too much



<P>of the Elliot pride.&nbsp; We do so wish that Charles had married Anne

instead.



<P>I suppose you know he wanted to marry Anne?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a moment's pause, Captain Wentworth said--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Do you mean that she refused him?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! yes; certainly."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"When did that happen?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I do not exactly know, for Henrietta and I were at school at the time;



<P>but I believe about a year before he married Mary.&nbsp; I wish she

had



<P>accepted him.&nbsp; We should all have liked her a great deal better;



<P>and papa and mamma always think it was her great friend



<P>Lady Russell's doing, that she did not.&nbsp; They think Charles



<P>might not be learned and bookish enough to please Lady Russell,



<P>and that therefore, she persuaded Anne to refuse him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The sounds were retreating, and Anne distinguished no more.



<P>Her own emotions still kept her fixed.&nbsp; She had much to recover

from,



<P>before she could move.&nbsp; The listener's proverbial fate was



<P>not absolutely hers; she had heard no evil of herself, but she had heard



<P>a great deal of very painful import.&nbsp; She saw how her own character



<P>was considered by Captain Wentworth, and there had been just that degree



<P>of feeling and curiosity about her in his manner which must give her



<P>extreme agitation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As soon as she could, she went after Mary, and having found,



<P>and walked back with her to their former station, by the stile,



<P>felt some comfort in their whole party being immediately afterwards



<P>collected, and once more in motion together.&nbsp; Her spirits wanted



<P>the solitude and silence which only numbers could give.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Charles and Henrietta returned, bringing, as may be conjectured,



<P>Charles Hayter with them.&nbsp; The minutiae of the business Anne



<P>could not attempt to understand; even Captain Wentworth did not seem



<P>admitted to perfect confidence here; but that there had been a withdrawing



<P>on the gentleman's side, and a relenting on the lady's, and that they



<P>were now very glad to be together again, did not admit a doubt.



<P>Henrietta looked a little ashamed, but very well pleased;--



<P>Charles Hayter exceedingly happy:&nbsp; and they were devoted to each

other



<P>almost from the first instant of their all setting forward for Uppercross.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Everything now marked out Louisa for Captain Wentworth;



<P>nothing could be plainer; and where many divisions were necessary,



<P>or even where they were not, they walked side by side nearly as much



<P>as the other two.&nbsp; In a long strip of meadow land, where there

was



<P>ample space for all, they were thus divided, forming three distinct

parties;



<P>and to that party of the three which boasted least animation,



<P>and least complaisance, Anne necessarily belonged.&nbsp; She joined

Charles



<P>and Mary, and was tired enough to be very glad of Charles's other arm;



<P>but Charles, though in very good humour with her, was out of temper



<P>with his wife.&nbsp; Mary had shewn herself disobliging to him,



<P>and was now to reap the consequence, which consequence was



<P>his dropping her arm almost every moment to cut off the heads



<P>of some nettles in the hedge with his switch; and when Mary began



<P>to complain of it, and lament her being ill-used, according to custom,



<P>in being on the hedge side, while Anne was never incommoded on the other,



<P>he dropped the arms of both to hunt after a weasel which he had



<P>a momentary glance of, and they could hardly get him along at all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This long meadow bordered a lane, which their footpath, at the end of

it



<P>was to cross, and when the party had all reached the gate of exit,



<P>the carriage advancing in the same direction, which had been



<P>some time heard, was just coming up, and proved to be Admiral Croft's

gig.



<P>He and his wife had taken their intended drive, and were returning home.



<P>Upon hearing how long a walk the young people had engaged in,



<P>they kindly offered a seat to any lady who might be particularly tired;



<P>it would save her a full mile, and they were going through Uppercross.



<P>The invitation was general, and generally declined.&nbsp; The Miss Musgroves



<P>were not at all tired, and Mary was either offended, by not being asked



<P>before any of the others, or what Louisa called the Elliot pride



<P>could not endure to make a third in a one horse chaise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The walking party had crossed the lane, and were surmounting an



<P>opposite stile, and the Admiral was putting his horse in motion again,



<P>when Captain Wentworth cleared the hedge in a moment to say something



<P>to his sister.&nbsp; The something might be guessed by its effects.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Miss Elliot, I am sure you are tired," cried Mrs Croft.



<P>"Do let us have the pleasure of taking you home.&nbsp; Here is excellent

room



<P>for three, I assure you.&nbsp; If we were all like you, I believe we

might



<P>sit four.&nbsp; You must, indeed, you must."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne was still in the lane; and though instinctively beginning to decline,



<P>she was not allowed to proceed.&nbsp; The Admiral's kind urgency



<P>came in support of his wife's; they would not be refused;



<P>they compressed themselves into the smallest possible space



<P>to leave her a corner, and Captain Wentworth, without saying a word,



<P>turned to her, and quietly obliged her to be assisted into the carriage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Yes; he had done it.&nbsp; She was in the carriage, and felt that he

had



<P>placed her there, that his will and his hands had done it,



<P>that she owed it to his perception of her fatigue, and his resolution



<P>to give her rest.&nbsp; She was very much affected by the view of



<P>his disposition towards her, which all these things made apparent.



<P>This little circumstance seemed the completion of all that had gone

before.



<P>She understood him.&nbsp; He could not forgive her, but he could not



<P>be unfeeling.&nbsp; Though condemning her for the past, and considering

it



<P>with high and unjust resentment, though perfectly careless of her,



<P>and though becoming attached to another, still he could not see her

suffer,



<P>without the desire of giving her relief.&nbsp; It was a remainder



<P>of former sentiment; it was an impulse of pure, though unacknowledged



<P>friendship; it was a proof of his own warm and amiable heart,



<P>which she could not contemplate without emotions so compounded



<P>of pleasure and pain, that she knew not which prevailed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Her answers to the kindness and the remarks of her companions



<P>were at first unconsciously given.&nbsp; They had travelled half their

way



<P>along the rough lane, before she was quite awake to what they said.



<P>She then found them talking of "Frederick."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"He certainly means to have one or other of those two girls, Sophy,"



<P>said the Admiral; "but there is no saying which.&nbsp; He has been



<P>running after them, too, long enough, one would think, to make up his

mind.



<P>Ay, this comes of the peace.&nbsp; If it were war now, he would have



<P>settled it long ago.&nbsp; We sailors, Miss Elliot, cannot afford to

make



<P>long courtships in time of war.&nbsp; How many days was it, my dear,
