<P>between the first time of my seeing you and our sitting down together



<P>in our lodgings at North Yarmouth?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"We had better not talk about it, my dear," replied Mrs Croft, pleasantly;



<P>"for if Miss Elliot were to hear how soon we came to an understanding,



<P>she would never be persuaded that we could be happy together.



<P>I had known you by character, however, long before."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, and I had heard of you as a very pretty girl, and what were we



<P>to wait for besides?&nbsp; I do not like having such things so long

in hand.



<P>I wish Frederick would spread a little more canvass, and bring us home



<P>one of these young ladies to Kellynch.&nbsp; Then there would always



<P>be company for them.&nbsp; And very nice young ladies they both are;



<P>I hardly know one from the other."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Very good humoured, unaffected girls, indeed," said Mrs Croft,



<P>in a tone of calmer praise, such as made Anne suspect that



<P>her keener powers might not consider either of them as quite worthy



<P>of her brother; "and a very respectable family.&nbsp; One could not

be



<P>connected with better people.&nbsp; My dear Admiral, that post!



<P>we shall certainly take that post."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But by coolly giving the reins a better direction herself they happily



<P>passed the danger; and by once afterwards judiciously putting out



<P>her hand they neither fell into a rut, nor ran foul of a dung-cart;



<P>and Anne, with some amusement at their style of driving,



<P>which she imagined no bad representation of the general guidance



<P>of their affairs, found herself safely deposited by them at the Cottage.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 11</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 11</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>The time now approached for Lady Russell's return:&nbsp; the day was

even fixed;



<P>and Anne, being engaged to join her as soon as she was resettled,



<P>was looking forward to an early removal to Kellynch, and beginning



<P>to think how her own comfort was likely to be affected by it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It would place her in the same village with Captain Wentworth,



<P>within half a mile of him; they would have to frequent the same church,



<P>and there must be intercourse between the two families.



<P>This was against her; but on the other hand, he spent so much of his

time



<P>at Uppercross, that in removing thence she might be considered rather



<P>as leaving him behind, than as going towards him; and, upon the whole,



<P>she believed she must, on this interesting question, be the gainer,



<P>almost as certainly as in her change of domestic society,



<P>in leaving poor Mary for Lady Russell.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She wished it might be possible for her to avoid ever seeing



<P>Captain Wentworth at the Hall:&nbsp; those rooms had witnessed



<P>former meetings which would be brought too painfully before her;



<P>but she was yet more anxious for the possibility of Lady Russell and



<P>Captain Wentworth never meeting anywhere.&nbsp; They did not like each

other,



<P>and no renewal of acquaintance now could do any good; and were Lady

Russell



<P>to see them together, she might think that he had too much self-possession,



<P>and she too little.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>These points formed her chief solicitude in anticipating



<P>her removal from Uppercross, where she felt she had been stationed



<P>quite long enough.&nbsp; Her usefulness to little Charles would always



<P>give some sweetness to the memory of her two months' visit there,



<P>but he was gaining strength apace, and she had nothing else to stay

for.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The conclusion of her visit, however, was diversified in a way



<P>which she had not at all imagined.&nbsp; Captain Wentworth, after being

unseen



<P>and unheard of at Uppercross for two whole days, appeared again among

them



<P>to justify himself by a relation of what had kept him away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A letter from his friend, Captain Harville, having found him out at

last,



<P>had brought intelligence of Captain Harville's being settled



<P>with his family at Lyme for the winter; of their being therefore,



<P>quite unknowingly, within twenty miles of each other.&nbsp; Captain

Harville



<P>had never been in good health since a severe wound which he received



<P>two years before, and Captain Wentworth's anxiety to see him



<P>had determined him to go immediately to Lyme.&nbsp; He had been there



<P>for four-and-twenty hours.&nbsp; His acquittal was complete,



<P>his friendship warmly honoured, a lively interest excited for his friend,



<P>and his description of the fine country about Lyme so feelingly attended

to



<P>by the party, that an earnest desire to see Lyme themselves,



<P>and a project for going thither was the consequence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The young people were all wild to see Lyme.&nbsp; Captain Wentworth

talked



<P>of going there again himself, it was only seventeen miles from Uppercross;



<P>though November, the weather was by no means bad; and, in short,



<P>Louisa, who was the most eager of the eager, having formed



<P>the resolution to go, and besides the pleasure of doing as she liked,



<P>being now armed with the idea of merit in maintaining her own way,



<P>bore down all the wishes of her father and mother for putting it off



<P>till summer; and to Lyme they were to go--Charles, Mary, Anne, Henrietta,



<P>Louisa, and Captain Wentworth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The first heedless scheme had been to go in the morning and return at

night;



<P>but to this Mr Musgrove, for the sake of his horses, would not consent;



<P>and when it came to be rationally considered, a day in



<P>the middle of November would not leave much time for seeing a new place,



<P>after deducting seven hours, as the nature of the country required,



<P>for going and returning.&nbsp; They were, consequently, to stay the

night there,



<P>and not to be expected back till the next day's dinner.&nbsp; This was

felt



<P>to be a considerable amendment; and though they all met at the Great

House



<P>at rather an early breakfast hour, and set off very punctually,



<P>it was so much past noon before the two carriages, Mr Musgrove's coach



<P>containing the four ladies, and Charles's curricle, in which



<P>he drove Captain Wentworth, were descending the long hill into Lyme,



<P>and entering upon the still steeper street of the town itself,



<P>that it was very evident they would not have more than time



<P>for looking about them, before the light and warmth of the day were

gone.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After securing accommodations, and ordering a dinner at one of the inns,



<P>the next thing to be done was unquestionably to walk directly



<P>down to the sea.&nbsp; They were come too late in the year for any amusement



<P>or variety which Lyme, as a public place, might offer.&nbsp; The rooms



<P>were shut up, the lodgers almost all gone, scarcely any family



<P>but of the residents left; and, as there is nothing to admire



<P>in the buildings themselves, the remarkable situation of the town,



<P>the principal street almost hurrying into the water, the walk to the

Cobb,



<P>skirting round the pleasant little bay, which, in the season,



<P>is animated with bathing machines and company; the Cobb itself,



<P>its old wonders and new improvements, with the very beautiful



<P>line of cliffs stretching out to the east of the town, are what



<P>the stranger's eye will seek; and a very strange stranger it must be,



<P>who does not see charms in the immediate environs of Lyme,



<P>to make him wish to know it better.&nbsp; The scenes in its neighbourhood,



<P>Charmouth, with its high grounds and extensive sweeps of country,



<P>and still more, its sweet, retired bay, backed by dark cliffs,



<P>where fragments of low rock among the sands, make it the happiest spot



<P>for watching the flow of the tide, for sitting in unwearied contemplation;



<P>the woody varieties of the cheerful village of Up Lyme; and, above all,



<P>Pinny, with its green chasms between romantic rocks, where



<P>the scattered forest trees and orchards of luxuriant growth,



<P>declare that many a generation must have passed away since the first



<P>partial falling of the cliff prepared the ground for such a state,



<P>where a scene so wonderful and so lovely is exhibited, as may



<P>more than equal any of the resembling scenes of the far-famed



<P>Isle of Wight:&nbsp; these places must be visited, and visited again,



<P>to make the worth of Lyme understood.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The party from Uppercross passing down by the now deserted



<P>and melancholy looking rooms, and still descending, soon found themselves



<P>on the sea-shore; and lingering only, as all must linger and gaze



<P>on a first return to the sea, who ever deserved to look on it at all,



<P>proceeded towards the Cobb, equally their object in itself



<P>and on Captain Wentworth's account:&nbsp; for in a small house,



<P>near the foot of an old pier of unknown date, were the Harvilles settled.



<P>Captain Wentworth turned in to call on his friend; the others walked

on,



<P>and he was to join them on the Cobb.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They were by no means tired of wondering and admiring; and not even

Louisa



<P>seemed to feel that they had parted with Captain Wentworth long,



<P>when they saw him coming after them, with three companions,



<P>all well known already, by description, to be Captain and Mrs Harville,



<P>and a Captain Benwick, who was staying with them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Benwick had some time ago been first lieutenant of the Laconia;



<P>and the account which Captain Wentworth had given of him,



<P>on his return from Lyme before, his warm praise of him as



<P>an excellent young man and an officer, whom he had always valued highly,



<P>which must have stamped him well in the esteem of every listener,



<P>had been followed by a little history of his private life,



<P>which rendered him perfectly interesting in the eyes of all the ladies.



<P>He had been engaged to Captain Harville's sister, and was now



<P>mourning her loss.&nbsp; They had been a year or two waiting for fortune



<P>and promotion.&nbsp; Fortune came, his prize-money as lieutenant being

great;



<P>promotion, too, came at last; but Fanny Harville did not live to know

it.



<P>She had died the preceding summer while he was at sea.&nbsp; Captain

Wentworth



<P>believed it impossible for man to be more attached to woman



<P>than poor Benwick had been to Fanny Harville, or to be more deeply



<P>afflicted under the dreadful change.&nbsp; He considered his disposition



<P>as of the sort which must suffer heavily, uniting very strong feelings



<P>with quiet, serious, and retiring manners, and a decided taste for reading,



<P>and sedentary pursuits.&nbsp; To finish the interest of the story,



<P>the friendship between him and the Harvilles seemed, if possible,



<P>augmented by the event which closed all their views of alliance,



<P>and Captain Benwick was now living with them entirely.&nbsp; Captain

Harville



<P>had taken his present house for half a year; his taste, and his health,



<P>and his fortune, all directing him to a residence inexpensive,



<P>and by the sea; and the grandeur of the country, and the retirement



<P>of Lyme in the winter, appeared exactly adapted to Captain Benwick's



<P>state of mind.&nbsp; The sympathy and good-will excited towards Captain

Benwick



<P>was very great.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And yet," said Anne to herself, as they now moved forward



<P>to meet the party, "he has not, perhaps, a more sorrowing heart



<P>than I have.&nbsp; I cannot believe his prospects so blighted for ever.



<P>He is younger than I am; younger in feeling, if not in fact;



<P>younger as a man.&nbsp; He will rally again, and be happy with another."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They all met, and were introduced.&nbsp; Captain Harville was a tall,



<P>dark man, with a sensible, benevolent countenance; a little lame;



<P>and from strong features and want of health, looking much older



<P>than Captain Wentworth.&nbsp; Captain Benwick looked, and was,



<P>the youngest of the three, and, compared with either of them,



<P>a little man.&nbsp; He had a pleasing face and a melancholy air,



<P>just as he ought to have, and drew back from conversation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Harville, though not equalling Captain Wentworth in manners,



<P>was a perfect gentleman, unaffected, warm, and obliging.



<P>Mrs Harville, a degree less polished than her husband, seemed, however,



<P>to have the same good feelings; and nothing could be more pleasant



<P>than their desire of considering the whole party as friends of their

own,



<P>because the friends of Captain Wentworth, or more kindly hospitable



<P>than their entreaties for their all promising to dine with them.



<P>The dinner, already ordered at the inn, was at last, though unwillingly,



<P>accepted as a excuse; but they seemed almost hurt that Captain Wentworth



<P>should have brought any such party to Lyme, without considering it



<P>as a thing of course that they should dine with them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was so much attachment to Captain Wentworth in all this,



<P>and such a bewitching charm in a degree of hospitality so uncommon,



<P>so unlike the usual style of give-and-take invitations, and dinners



<P>of formality and display, that Anne felt her spirits not likely to be



<P>benefited by an increasing acquaintance among his brother-officers.



<P>"These would have been all my friends," was her thought;



<P>and she had to struggle against a great tendency to lowness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>On quitting the Cobb, they all went in-doors with their new friends,



<P>and found rooms so small as none but those who invite from the heart



<P>could think capable of accommodating so many.&nbsp; Anne had



<P>a moment's astonishment on the subject herself; but it was soon lost



<P>in the pleasanter feelings which sprang from the sight of all



<P>the ingenious contrivances and nice arrangements of Captain Harville,



<P>to turn the actual space to the best account, to supply the deficiencies



<P>of lodging-house furniture, and defend the windows and doors



<P>against the winter storms to be expected.&nbsp; The varieties in



<P>the fitting-up of the rooms, where the common necessaries



<P>provided by the owner, in the common indifferent plight,



<P>were contrasted with some few articles of a rare species of wood,



<P>excellently worked up, and with something curious and valuable



<P>from all the distant countries Captain Harville had visited,



<P>were more than amusing to Anne; connected as it all was with his profession,



<P>the fruit of its labours, the effect of its influence on his habits,



<P>the picture of repose and domestic happiness it presented,



<P>made it to her a something more, or less, than gratification.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Harville was no reader; but he had contrived



<P>excellent accommodations, and fashioned very pretty shelves,



<P>for a tolerable collection of well-bound volumes, the property of



<P>Captain Benwick.&nbsp; His lameness prevented him from taking much exercise;



<P>but a mind of usefulness and ingenuity seemed to furnish him with



<P>constant employment within.&nbsp; He drew, he varnished, he carpentered,



<P>he glued; he made toys for the children; he fashioned new netting-needles



<P>and pins with improvements; and if everything else was done,



<P>sat down to his large fishing-net at one corner of the room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne thought she left great happiness behind her when they



<P>quitted the house; and Louisa, by whom she found herself walking,



<P>burst forth into raptures of admiration and delight on the character



<P>of the navy; their friendliness, their brotherliness, their openness,



<P>their uprightness; protesting that she was convinced of sailors having



<P>more worth and warmth than any other set of men in England;



<P>that they only knew how to live, and they only deserved to be



<P>respected and loved.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They went back to dress and dine; and so well had the scheme



<P>answered already, that nothing was found amiss; though its being



<P>"so entirely out of season," and the "no thoroughfare of Lyme,"



<P>and the "no expectation of company," had brought many apologies



<P>from the heads of the inn.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne found herself by this time growing so much more hardened



<P>to being in Captain Wentworth's company than she had at first imagined



<P>could ever be, that the sitting down to the same table with him now,



<P>and the interchange of the common civilities attending on it



<P>(they never got beyond), was become a mere nothing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The nights were too dark for the ladies to meet again till the morrow,



<P>but Captain Harville had promised them a visit in the evening;



<P>and he came, bringing his friend also, which was more than



<P>had been expected, it having been agreed that Captain Benwick



<P>had all the appearance of being oppressed by the presence of



<P>so many strangers.&nbsp; He ventured among them again, however,



<P>though his spirits certainly did not seem fit for the mirth



<P>of the party in general.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>While Captains Wentworth and Harville led the talk on one side of the

room,



<P>and by recurring to former days, supplied anecdotes in abundance



<P>to occupy and entertain the others, it fell to Anne's lot to be placed



<P>rather apart with Captain Benwick; and a very good impulse



<P>of her nature obliged her to begin an acquaintance with him.



<P>He was shy, and disposed to abstraction; but the engaging mildness of



<P>her countenance, and gentleness of her manners, soon had their effect;



<P>and Anne was well repaid the first trouble of exertion.



<P>He was evidently a young man of considerable taste in reading,



<P>though principally in poetry; and besides the persuasion of having



<P>given him at least an evening's indulgence in the discussion of subjects,



<P>which his usual companions had probably no concern in, she had the hope



<P>of being of real use to him in some suggestions as to the duty and



<P>benefit of struggling against affliction, which had naturally grown

out



<P>of their conversation.&nbsp; For, though shy, he did not seem reserved;



<P>it had rather the appearance of feelings glad to burst their



<P>usual restraints; and having talked of poetry, the richness of



<P>the present age, and gone through a brief comparison of opinion



<P>as to the first-rate poets, trying to ascertain whether Marmion



<P>or The Lady of the Lake were to be preferred, and how ranked the Giaour



<P>and The Bride of Abydos; and moreover, how the Giaour was to be pronounced,



<P>he showed himself so intimately acquainted with all the tenderest songs



<P>of the one poet, and all the impassioned descriptions of hopeless agony



<P>of the other; he repeated, with such tremulous feeling, the various

lines



<P>which imaged a broken heart, or a mind destroyed by wretchedness,



<P>and looked so entirely as if he meant to be understood,



<P>that she ventured to hope he did not always read only poetry,



<P>and to say, that she thought it was the misfortune of poetry to be



<P>seldom safely enjoyed by those who enjoyed it completely;



<P>and that the strong feelings which alone could estimate it truly



<P>were the very feelings which ought to taste it but sparingly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His looks shewing him not pained, but pleased with this allusion



<P>to his situation, she was emboldened to go on; and feeling in herself



<P>the right of seniority of mind, she ventured to recommend



<P>a larger allowance of prose in his daily study; and on being requested



<P>to particularize, mentioned such works of our best moralists,



<P>such collections of the finest letters, such memoirs of characters



<P>of worth and suffering, as occurred to her at the moment



<P>as calculated to rouse and fortify the mind by the highest precepts,



<P>and the strongest examples of moral and religious endurances.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Benwick listened attentively, and seemed grateful for



<P>the interest implied; and though with a shake of the head,



<P>and sighs which declared his little faith in the efficacy of any books



<P>on grief like his, noted down the names of those she recommended,



<P>and promised to procure and read them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When the evening was over, Anne could not but be amused at the idea



<P>of her coming to Lyme to preach patience and resignation to a young

man



<P>whom she had never seen before; nor could she help fearing,



<P>on more serious reflection, that, like many other great moralists



<P>and preachers, she had been eloquent on a point in which her own conduct



<P>would ill bear examination.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 05</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 5</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>On the morning appointed for Admiral and Mrs Croft's seeing Kellynch

Hall,



<P>Anne found it most natural to take her almost daily walk to Lady Russell's,



<P>and keep out of the way till all was over; when she found it most natural



<P>to be sorry that she had missed the opportunity of seeing them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This meeting of the two parties proved highly satisfactory,



<P>and decided the whole business at once.&nbsp; Each lady was previously



<P>well disposed for an agreement, and saw nothing, therefore,



<P>but good manners in the other; and with regard to the gentlemen,



<P>there was such an hearty good humour, such an open, trusting liberality



<P>on the Admiral's side, as could not but influence Sir Walter,



<P>who had besides been flattered into his very best and most polished



<P>behaviour by Mr Shepherd's assurances of his being known, by report,



<P>to the Admiral, as a model of good breeding.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The house and grounds, and furniture, were approved, the Crofts



<P>were approved, terms, time, every thing, and every body, was right;



<P>and Mr Shepherd's clerks were set to work, without there having been



<P>a single preliminary difference to modify of all that



<P>"This indenture sheweth."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sir Walter, without hesitation, declared the Admiral to be



<P>the best-looking sailor he had ever met with, and went so far as to

say,



<P>that if his own man might have had the arranging of his hair,



<P>he should not be ashamed of being seen with him any where;



<P>and the Admiral, with sympathetic cordiality, observed to his wife



<P>as they drove back through the park, "I thought we should soon



<P>come to a deal, my dear, in spite of what they told us at Taunton.



<P>The Baronet will never set the Thames on fire, but there seems to be



<P>no harm in him."&nbsp; reciprocal compliments, which would have been



<P>esteemed about equal.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The Crofts were to have possession at Michaelmas; and as Sir Walter



<P>proposed removing to Bath in the course of the preceding month,



<P>there was no time to be lost in making every dependent arrangement.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell, convinced that Anne would not be allowed to be of any

use,



<P>or any importance, in the choice of the house which they were



<P>going to secure, was very unwilling to have her hurried away so soon,



<P>and wanted to make it possible for her to stay behind till she might



<P>convey her to Bath herself after Christmas; but having engagements



<P>of her own which must take her from Kellynch for several weeks,



<P>she was unable to give the full invitation she wished, and Anne



<P>though dreading the possible heats of September in all the white glare



<P>of Bath, and grieving to forego all the influence so sweet and so sad



<P>of the autumnal months in the country, did not think that,



<P>everything considered, she wished to remain.&nbsp; It would be most

right,



<P>and most wise, and, therefore must involve least suffering



<P>to go with the others.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Something occurred, however, to give her a different duty.



<P>Mary, often a little unwell, and always thinking a great deal



<P>of her own complaints, and always in the habit of claiming Anne



<P>when anything was the matter, was indisposed; and foreseeing



<P>that she should not have a day's health all the autumn, entreated,



<P>or rather required her, for it was hardly entreaty, to come to



<P>Uppercross Cottage, and bear her company as long as she should want

her,



<P>instead of going to Bath.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I cannot possibly do without Anne," was Mary's reasoning;



<P>and Elizabeth's reply was, "Then I am sure Anne had better stay,



<P>for nobody will want her in Bath."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To be claimed as a good, though in an improper style, is at least



<P>better than being rejected as no good at all; and Anne, glad to



<P>be thought of some use, glad to have anything marked out as a duty,



<P>and certainly not sorry to have the scene of it in the country,



<P>and her own dear country, readily agreed to stay.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This invitation of Mary's removed all Lady Russell's difficulties,



<P>and it was consequently soon settled that Anne should not go to Bath



<P>till Lady Russell took her, and that all the intervening time



<P>should be divided between Uppercross Cottage and Kellynch Lodge.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So far all was perfectly right; but Lady Russell was almost startled



<P>by the wrong of one part of the Kellynch Hall plan, when it burst on

her,



<P>which was, Mrs Clay's being engaged to go to Bath with Sir Walter



<P>and Elizabeth, as a most important and valuable assistant to the latter



<P>in all the business before her.&nbsp; Lady Russell was extremely sorry



<P>that such a measure should have been resorted to at all, wondered,



<P>grieved, and feared; and the affront it contained to Anne,



<P>in Mrs Clay's being of so much use, while Anne could be of none,



<P>was a very sore aggravation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne herself was become hardened to such affronts; but she felt



<P>the imprudence of the arrangement quite as keenly as Lady Russell.



<P>With a great deal of quiet observation, and a knowledge,



<P>which she often wished less, of her father's character, she was



<P>sensible that results the most serious to his family from the intimacy



<P>were more than possible.&nbsp; She did not imagine that her father



<P>had at present an idea of the kind.&nbsp; Mrs Clay had freckles,



<P>and a projecting tooth, and a clumsy wrist, which he was continually



<P>making severe remarks upon, in her absence; but she was young,



<P>and certainly altogether well-looking, and possessed, in an acute mind



<P>and assiduous pleasing manners, infinitely more dangerous attractions



<P>than any merely personal might have been.&nbsp; Anne was so impressed



<P>by the degree of their danger, that she could not excuse herself



<P>from trying to make it perceptible to her sister.&nbsp; She had little

hope



<P>of success; but Elizabeth, who in the event of such a reverse would

be



<P>so much more to be pitied than herself, should never, she thought,



<P>have reason to reproach her for giving no warning.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She spoke, and seemed only to offend.&nbsp; Elizabeth could not conceive



<P>how such an absurd suspicion should occur to her, and indignantly



<P>answered for each party's perfectly knowing their situation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Mrs Clay," said she, warmly, "never forgets who she is;



<P>and as I am rather better acquainted with her sentiments than you can

be,



<P>I can assure you, that upon the subject of marriage they are



<P>particularly nice, and that she reprobates all inequality of condition



<P>and rank more strongly than most people.&nbsp; And as to my father,



<P>I really should not have thought that he, who has kept himself single



<P>so long for our sakes, need be suspected now.&nbsp; If Mrs Clay were



<P>a very beautiful woman, I grant you, it might be wrong to have her



<P>so much with me; not that anything in the world, I am sure,



<P>would induce my father to make a degrading match, but he might



<P>be rendered unhappy.&nbsp; But poor Mrs Clay who, with all her merits,



<P>can never have been reckoned tolerably pretty, I really think poor



<P>Mrs Clay may be staying here in perfect safety.&nbsp; One would imagine



<P>you had never heard my father speak of her personal misfortunes,



<P>though I know you must fifty times.&nbsp; That tooth of her's



<P>and those freckles.&nbsp; Freckles do not disgust me so very much



<P>as they do him.&nbsp; I have known a face not materially disfigured

by a few,



<P>but he abominates them.&nbsp; You must have heard him notice



<P>Mrs Clay's freckles."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"There is hardly any personal defect," replied Anne,



<P>"which an agreeable manner might not gradually reconcile one to."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I think very differently," answered Elizabeth, shortly;



<P>"an agreeable manner may set off handsome features, but can never



<P>alter plain ones.&nbsp; However, at any rate, as I have a great deal

more



<P>at stake on this point than anybody else can have, I think it



<P>rather unnecessary in you to be advising me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne had done; glad that it was over, and not absolutely hopeless



<P>of doing good.&nbsp; Elizabeth, though resenting the suspicion,



<P>might yet be made observant by it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The last office of the four carriage-horses was to draw Sir Walter,



<P>Miss Elliot, and Mrs Clay to Bath.&nbsp; The party drove off



<P>in very good spirits;&nbsp; Sir Walter prepared with condescending bows



<P>for all the afflicted tenantry and cottagers who might have had a hint



<P>to show themselves, and Anne walked up at the same time,



<P>in a sort of desolate tranquility, to the Lodge, where she was to spend



<P>the first week.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Her friend was not in better spirits than herself. Lady Russell felt

this



<P>break-up of the family exceedingly.&nbsp; Their respectability was



<P>as dear to her as her own, and a daily intercourse had become



<P>precious by habit.&nbsp; It was painful to look upon their deserted

grounds,



<P>and still worse to anticipate the new hands they were to fall into;



<P>and to escape the solitariness and the melancholy of so altered a village,



<P>and be out of the way when Admiral and Mrs Croft first arrived,



<P>she had determined to make her own absence from home begin



<P>when she must give up Anne.&nbsp; Accordingly their removal was made

together,



<P>and Anne was set down at Uppercross Cottage, in the first stage



<P>of Lady Russell's journey.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Uppercross was a moderate-sized village, which a few years back



<P>had been completely in the old English style, containing only



<P>two houses superior in appearance to those of the yeomen and labourers;



<P>the mansion of the squire, with its high walls, great gates, and old

trees,



<P>substantial and unmodernized, and the compact, tight parsonage,



<P>enclosed in its own neat garden, with a vine and a pear-tree



<P>trained round its casements; but upon the marriage of the young 'squire,



<P>it had received the improvement of a farm-house elevated into a cottage,



<P>for his residence, and Uppercross Cottage, with its veranda,



<P>French windows, and other prettiness, was quite as likely to catch



<P>the traveller's eye as the more consistent and considerable aspect



<P>and premises of the Great House, about a quarter of a mile farther on.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Here Anne had often been staying.&nbsp; She knew the ways of Uppercross



<P>as well as those of Kellynch.&nbsp; The two families were so continually

meeting,



<P>so much in the habit of running in and out of each other's house



<P>at all hours, that it was rather a surprise to her to find Mary alone;



<P>but being alone, her being unwell and out of spirits was almost



<P>a matter of course.&nbsp; Though better endowed than the elder sister,



<P>Mary had not Anne's understanding nor temper.&nbsp; While well, and

happy,



<P>and properly attended to, she had great good humour and excellent spirits;



<P>but any indisposition sunk her completely.&nbsp; She had no resources



<P>for solitude; and inheriting a considerable share of the Elliot



<P>self-importance, was very prone to add to every other distress



<P>that of fancying herself neglected and ill-used.&nbsp; In person, she

was



<P>inferior to both sisters, and had, even in her bloom, only reached



<P>the dignity of being "a fine girl."&nbsp; She was now lying on the faded

sofa



<P>of the pretty little drawing-room, the once elegant furniture of which



<P>had been gradually growing shabby, under the influence of four summers



<P>and two children; and, on Anne's appearing, greeted her with--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"So, you are come at last!&nbsp; I began to think I should never see

you.



<P>I am so ill I can hardly speak.&nbsp; I have not seen a creature



<P>the whole morning!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I am sorry to find you unwell," replied Anne.&nbsp; "You sent me



<P>such a good account of yourself on Thursday!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, I made the best of it; I always do:&nbsp; but I was very far from

well



<P>at the time; and I do not think I ever was so ill in my life



<P>as I have been all this morning:&nbsp; very unfit to be left alone,

I am sure.



<P>Suppose I were to be seized of a sudden in some dreadful way,



<P>and not able to ring the bell!&nbsp; So, Lady Russell would not get

out.



<P>I do not think she has been in this house three times this summer."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne said what was proper, and enquired after her husband.



<P>"Oh! Charles is out shooting.&nbsp; I have not seen him since seven

o'clock.



<P>He would go, though I told him how ill I was.&nbsp; He said he should

not



<P>stay out long; but he has never come back, and now it is almost one.



<P>I assure you, I have not seen a soul this whole long morning."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You have had your little boys with you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, as long as I could bear their noise; but they are so unmanageable



<P>that they do me more harm than good.&nbsp; Little Charles does not mind



<P>a word I say, and Walter is growing quite as bad."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, you will soon be better now," replied Anne, cheerfully.



<P>"You know I always cure you when I come.&nbsp; How are your neighbours



<P>at the Great House?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I can give you no account of them.&nbsp; I have not seen one of them

to-day,



<P>except Mr Musgrove, who just stopped and spoke through the window,



<P>but without getting off his horse; and though I told him how ill I was,



<P>not one of them have been near me.&nbsp; It did not happen to suit



<P>the Miss Musgroves, I suppose, and they never put themselves



<P>out of their way."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You will see them yet, perhaps, before the morning is gone.



<P>It is early."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I never want them, I assure you.&nbsp; They talk and laugh a great

deal



<P>too much for me.&nbsp; Oh! Anne, I am so very unwell!&nbsp; It was quite

unkind



<P>of you not to come on Thursday."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"My dear Mary, recollect what a comfortable account you sent me of yourself!



<P>You wrote in the cheerfullest manner, and said you were perfectly well,



<P>and in no hurry for me; and that being the case, you must be aware



<P>that my wish would be to remain with Lady Russell to the last:



<P>and besides what I felt on her account, I have really been so busy,



<P>have had so much to do, that I could not very conveniently have



<P>left Kellynch sooner."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Dear me! what can you possibly have to do?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"A great many things, I assure you.&nbsp; More than I can recollect



<P>in a moment; but I can tell you some.&nbsp; I have been making



<P>a duplicate of the catalogue of my father's books and pictures.



<P>I have been several times in the garden with Mackenzie,



<P>trying to understand, and make him understand, which of Elizabeth's

plants



<P>are for Lady Russell.&nbsp; I have had all my own little concerns



<P>to arrange, books and music to divide, and all my trunks to repack,



<P>from not having understood in time what was intended as to the waggons:



<P>and one thing I have had to do, Mary, of a more trying nature:



<P>going to almost every house in the parish, as a sort of take-leave.



<P>I was told that they wished it.&nbsp; But all these things took up



<P>a great deal of time."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! well!" and after a moment's pause, "but you have never asked me



<P>one word about our dinner at the Pooles yesterday."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Did you go then?&nbsp; I have made no enquiries, because I concluded



<P>you must have been obliged to give up the party."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh yes! I went.&nbsp; I was very well yesterday; nothing at all



<P>the matter with me till this morning.&nbsp; It would have been strange



<P>if I had not gone."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I am very glad you were well enough, and I hope you had a pleasant

party."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Nothing remarkable.&nbsp; One always knows beforehand what the dinner

will be,



<P>and who will be there; and it is so very uncomfortable not having



<P>a carriage of one's own.&nbsp; Mr and Mrs Musgrove took me, and we were



<P>so crowded!&nbsp; They are both so very large, and take up so much room;



<P>and Mr Musgrove always sits forward.&nbsp; So, there was I, crowded

into



<P>the back seat with Henrietta and Louise; and I think it very likely



<P>that my illness to-day may be owing to it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A little further perseverance in patience and forced cheerfulness



<P>on Anne's side produced nearly a cure on Mary's.&nbsp; She could soon



<P>sit upright on the sofa, and began to hope she might be able



<P>to leave it by dinner-time.&nbsp; Then, forgetting to think of it,



<P>she was at the other end of the room, beautifying a nosegay;



<P>then, she ate her cold meat; and then she was well enough



<P>to propose a little walk.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Where shall we go?" said she, when they were ready.&nbsp; "I suppose



<P>you will not like to call at the Great House before they have



<P>been to see you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I have not the smallest objection on that account," replied Anne.



<P>"I should never think of standing on such ceremony with people I know



<P>so well as Mrs and the Miss Musgroves."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! but they ought to call upon you as soon as possible.



<P>They ought to feel what is due to you as my sister.&nbsp; However,



<P>we may as well go and sit with them a little&nbsp; while, and when we



<P>have that over, we can enjoy our walk."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne had always thought such a style of intercourse highly imprudent;



<P>but she had ceased to endeavour to check it, from believing that,



<P>though there were on each side continual subjects of offence,



<P>neither family could now do without it.&nbsp; To the Great House accordingly



<P>they went, to sit the full half hour in the old-fashioned square parlour,



<P>with a small carpet and shining floor, to which the present



<P>daughters of the house were gradually giving the proper air of confusion



<P>by a grand piano-forte and a harp, flower-stands and little tables



<P>placed in every direction.&nbsp; Oh! could the originals of the portraits



<P>against the wainscot, could the gentlemen in brown velvet and



<P>the ladies in blue satin have seen what was going on, have been conscious



<P>of such an overthrow of all order and neatness!&nbsp; The portraits

themselves



<P>seemed to be staring in astonishment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The Musgroves, like their houses, were in a state of alteration,



<P>perhaps of improvement.&nbsp; The father and mother were in the old



<P>English style, and the young people in the new.&nbsp; Mr and Mrs Musgrove



<P>were a very good sort of people; friendly and hospitable,



<P>not much educated, and not at all elegant.&nbsp; Their children had



<P>more modern minds and manners.&nbsp; There was a numerous family;



<P>but the only two grown up, excepting Charles, were Henrietta and Louisa,



<P>young ladies of nineteen and twenty, who had brought from school at

Exeter



<P>all the usual stock of accomplishments, and were now like thousands



<P>of other young ladies, living to be fashionable, happy, and merry.



<P>Their dress had every advantage, their faces were rather pretty,



<P>their spirits extremely good, their manner unembarrassed and pleasant;



<P>they were of consequence at home, and favourites abroad.



<P>Anne always contemplated them as some of the happiest creatures



<P>of her acquaintance; but still, saved as we all are, by some



<P>comfortable feeling of superiority from wishing for the possibility



<P>of exchange, she would not have given up her own more elegant



<P>and cultivated mind for all their enjoyments; and envied them nothing



<P>but that seemingly perfect good understanding and agreement together,



<P>that good-humoured mutual affection, of which she had known



<P>so little herself with either of her sisters.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They were received with great cordiality.&nbsp; Nothing seemed amiss



<P>on the side of the Great House family, which was generally,



<P>as Anne very well knew, the least to blame.&nbsp; The half hour was



<P>chatted away pleasantly enough; and she was not at all surprised



<P>at the end of it, to have their walking party joined by both



<P>the Miss Musgroves, at Mary's particular invitation.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 13</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 13</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>The remainder of Anne's time at Uppercross, comprehending only two days,



<P>was spent entirely at the Mansion House; and she had the satisfaction



<P>of knowing herself extremely useful there, both as an immediate companion,



<P>and as assisting in all those arrangements for the future, which,



<P>in Mr and Mrs Musgrove's distressed state of spirits, would have



<P>been difficulties.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They had an early account from Lyme the next morning.&nbsp; Louisa was



<P>much the same.&nbsp; No symptoms worse than before had appeared.



<P>Charles came a few hours afterwards, to bring a later and



<P>more particular account.&nbsp; He was tolerably cheerful.&nbsp; A speedy

cure



<P>must not be hoped, but everything was going on as well



<P>as the nature of the case admitted.&nbsp; In speaking of the Harvilles,



<P>he seemed unable to satisfy his own sense of their kindness,



<P>especially of Mrs Harville's exertions as a nurse.&nbsp; "She really

left



<P>nothing for Mary to do.&nbsp; He and Mary had been persuaded to go early



<P>to their inn last night.&nbsp; Mary had been hysterical again this morning.



<P>When he came away, she was going to walk out with Captain Benwick,



<P>which, he hoped, would do her good.&nbsp; He almost wished she had been



<P>prevailed on to come home the day before; but the truth was,



<P>that Mrs Harville left nothing for anybody to do."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Charles was to return to Lyme the same afternoon, and his father



<P>had at first half a mind to go with him, but the ladies could not consent.



<P>It would be going only to multiply trouble to the others,



<P>and increase his own distress; and a much better scheme followed



<P>and was acted upon.&nbsp; A chaise was sent for from Crewkherne,



<P>and Charles conveyed back a far more useful person in the old nursery-maid



<P>of the family, one who having brought up all the children,



<P>and seen the very last, the lingering and long-petted Master Harry,



<P>sent to school after his brothers, was now living in her deserted nursery



<P>to mend stockings and dress all the blains and bruises she could



<P>get near her, and who, consequently, was only too happy in being



<P>allowed to go and help nurse dear Miss Louisa.&nbsp; Vague wishes of



<P>getting Sarah thither, had occurred before to Mrs Musgrove and Henrietta;



<P>but without Anne, it would hardly have been resolved on,



<P>and found practicable so soon.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They were indebted, the next day, to Charles Hayter, for all



<P>the minute knowledge of Louisa, which it was so essential to obtain



<P>every twenty-four hours.&nbsp; He made it his business to go to Lyme,



<P>and his account was still encouraging.&nbsp; The intervals of sense



<P>and consciousness were believed to be stronger.&nbsp; Every report agreed



<P>in Captain Wentworth's appearing fixed in Lyme.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne was to leave them on the morrow, an event which they all dreaded.



<P>"What should they do without her?&nbsp; They were wretched comforters



<P>for one another."&nbsp; And so much was said in this way, that Anne

thought



<P>she could not do better than impart among them the general inclination



<P>to which she was privy, and persuaded them all to go to Lyme at once.



<P>She had little difficulty; it was soon determined that they would go;



<P>go to-morrow, fix themselves at the inn, or get into lodgings,



<P>as it suited, and there remain till dear Louisa could be moved.



<P>They must be taking off some trouble from the good people she was with;



<P>they might at least relieve Mrs Harville from the care of her own children;



<P>and in short, they were so happy in the decision, that Anne was delighted



<P>with what she had done, and felt that she could not spend her



<P>last morning at Uppercross better than in assisting their preparations,



<P>and sending them off at an early hour, though her being left



<P>to the solitary range of the house was the consequence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She was the last, excepting the little boys at the cottage,



<P>she was the very last, the only remaining one of all that had filled



<P>and animated both houses, of all that had given Uppercross



<P>its cheerful character.&nbsp; A few days had made a change indeed!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>If Louisa recovered, it would all be well again.&nbsp; More than



<P>former happiness would be restored.&nbsp; There could not be a doubt,



<P>to her mind there was none, of what would follow her recovery.



<P>A few months hence, and the room now so deserted, occupied but by



<P>her silent, pensive self, might be filled again with all that was happy



<P>and gay, all that was glowing and bright in prosperous love,



<P>all that was most unlike Anne Elliot!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>An hour's complete leisure for such reflections as these,



<P>on a dark November day, a small thick rain almost blotting out



<P>the very few objects ever to be discerned from the windows, was enough



<P>to make the sound of Lady Russell's carriage exceedingly welcome;



<P>and yet, though desirous to be gone, she could not quit the Mansion

House,



<P>or look an adieu to the Cottage, with its black, dripping and



<P>comfortless veranda, or even notice through the misty glasses



<P>the last humble tenements of the village, without a saddened heart.



<P>Scenes had passed in Uppercross which made it precious.



<P>It stood the record of many sensations of pain, once severe,



<P>but now softened; and of some instances of relenting feeling,



<P>some breathings of friendship and reconciliation, which could



<P>never be looked for again, and which could never cease to be dear.



<P>She left it all behind her, all but the recollection that



<P>such things had been.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne had never entered Kellynch since her quitting Lady Russell's house



<P>in September.&nbsp; It had not been necessary, and the few occasions

of



<P>its being possible for her to go to the Hall she had contrived to evade



<P>and escape from.&nbsp; Her first return was to resume her place in the

modern



<P>and elegant apartments of the Lodge, and to gladden the eyes



<P>of its mistress.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was some anxiety mixed with Lady Russell's joy in meeting her.



<P>She knew who had been frequenting Uppercross.&nbsp; But happily,



<P>either Anne was improved in plumpness and looks, or Lady Russell



<P>fancied her so; and Anne, in receiving her compliments on the occasion,



<P>had the amusement of connecting them with the silent admiration



<P>of her cousin, and of hoping that she was to be blessed with



<P>a second spring of youth and beauty.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When they came to converse, she was soon sensible of some mental change.



<P>The subjects of which her heart had been full on leaving Kellynch,



<P>and which she had felt slighted, and been compelled to smother



<P>among the Musgroves, were now become but of secondary interest.



<P>She had lately lost sight even of her father and sister and Bath.



<P>Their concerns had been sunk under those of Uppercross;



<P>and when Lady Russell reverted to their former hopes and fears,



<P>and spoke her satisfaction in the house in Camden Place,



<P>which had been taken, and her regret that Mrs Clay should still



<P>be with them, Anne would have been ashamed to have it known



<P>how much more she was thinking of Lyme and Louisa Musgrove,



<P>and all her acquaintance there; how much more interesting to her



<P>was the home and the friendship of the Harvilles and Captain Benwick,



<P>than her own father's house in Camden Place, or her own sister's intimacy



<P>with Mrs Clay.&nbsp; She was actually forced to exert herself



<P>to meet Lady Russell with anything like the appearance of equal solicitude,



<P>on topics which had by nature the first claim on her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was a little awkwardness at first in their discourse



<P>on another subject.&nbsp; They must speak of the accident at Lyme.



<P>Lady Russell had not been arrived five minutes the day before,



<P>when a full account of the whole had burst on her; but still it must



<P>be talked of, she must make enquiries, she must regret the imprudence,



<P>lament the result, and Captain Wentworth's name must be mentioned by

both.



<P>Anne was conscious of not doing it so well as Lady Russell.



<P>She could not speak the name, and look straight forward to



<P>Lady Russell's eye, till she had adopted the expedient of telling her



<P>briefly what she thought of the attachment between him and Louisa.



<P>When this was told, his name distressed her no longer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell had only to listen composedly, and wish them happy,



<P>but internally her heart revelled in angry pleasure, in pleased contempt,



<P>that the man who at twenty-three had seemed to understand somewhat



<P>of the value of an Anne Elliot, should, eight years afterwards,



<P>be charmed by a Louisa Musgrove.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The first three or four days passed most quietly, with no circumstance



<P>to mark them excepting the receipt of a note or two from Lyme,



<P>which found their way to Anne, she could not tell how, and brought



<P>a rather improving account of Louisa.&nbsp; At the end of that period,



<P>Lady Russell's politeness could repose no longer, and the fainter



<P>self-threatenings of the past became in a decided tone,



<P>"I must call on Mrs Croft; I really must call upon her soon.



<P>Anne, have you courage to go with me, and pay a visit in that house?



<P>It will be some trial to us both."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne did not shrink from it; on the contrary, she truly felt as she

said,



<P>in observing--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I think you are very likely to suffer the most of the two;



<P>your feelings are less reconciled to the change than mine.



<P>By remaining in the neighbourhood, I am become inured to it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She could have said more on the subject; for she had in fact



<P>so high an opinion of the Crofts, and considered her father



<P>so very fortunate in his tenants, felt the parish to be so sure



<P>of a good example, and the poor of the best attention and relief,



<P>that however sorry and ashamed for the necessity of the removal,



<P>she could not but in conscience feel that they were gone



<P>who deserved not to stay, and that Kellynch Hall had passed



<P>into better hands than its owners'.&nbsp; These convictions must unquestionably



<P>have their own pain, and severe was its kind; but they precluded



<P>that pain which Lady Russell would suffer in entering the house again,



<P>and returning through the well-known apartments.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In such moments Anne had no power of saying to herself,



<P>"These rooms ought to belong only to us.&nbsp; Oh, how fallen



<P>in their destination!&nbsp; How unworthily occupied!&nbsp; An ancient

family



<P>to be so driven away!&nbsp; Strangers filling their place!"



<P>No, except when she thought of her mother, and remembered where



<P>she had been used to sit and preside, she had no sigh of that description



<P>to heave.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Croft always met her with a kindness which gave her the pleasure



<P>of fancying herself a favourite, and on the present occasion,



<P>receiving her in that house, there was particular attention.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The sad accident at Lyme was soon the prevailing topic,



<P>and on comparing their latest accounts of the invalid, it appeared



<P>that each lady dated her intelligence from the same hour of yestermorn;



<P>that Captain Wentworth had been in Kellynch yesterday (the first time



<P>since the accident), had brought Anne the last note, which she had



<P>not been able to trace the exact steps of; had staid a few hours



<P>and then returned again to Lyme, and without any present intention



<P>of quitting it any more.&nbsp; He had enquired after her, she found,



<P>particularly; had expressed his hope of Miss Elliot's not being



<P>the worse for her exertions, and had spoken of those exertions as great.



<P>This was handsome, and gave her more pleasure than almost anything else



<P>could have done.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As to the sad catastrophe itself, it could be canvassed only in one

style



<P>by a couple of steady, sensible women, whose judgements had to work



<P>on ascertained events; and it was perfectly decided that it had been



<P>the consequence of much thoughtlessness and much imprudence;



<P>that its effects were most alarming, and that it was frightful to think,



<P>how long Miss Musgrove's recovery might yet be doubtful, and how liable



<P>she would still remain to suffer from the concussion hereafter!



<P>The Admiral wound it up summarily by exclaiming--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ay, a very bad business indeed.&nbsp; A new sort of way this,



<P>for a young fellow to be making love, by breaking his mistress's head,



<P>is not it, Miss Elliot?&nbsp; This is breaking a head and giving a plaster,



<P>truly!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Admiral Croft's manners were not quite of the tone to suit Lady Russell,



<P>but they delighted Anne.&nbsp; His goodness of heart and simplicity



<P>of character were irresistible.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Now, this must be very bad for you," said he, suddenly rousing from



<P>a little reverie, "to be coming and finding us here.&nbsp; I had not



<P>recollected it before, I declare, but it must be very bad.



<P>But now, do not stand upon ceremony.&nbsp; Get up and go over all the

rooms



<P>in the house if you like it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Another time, Sir, I thank you, not now."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, whenever it suits you.&nbsp; You can slip in from the shrubbery



<P>at any time; and there you will find we keep our umbrellas hanging up



<P>by that door.&nbsp; A good place is not it?&nbsp; But," (checking himself),



<P>"you will not think it a good place, for yours were always kept



<P>in the butler's room.&nbsp; Ay, so it always is, I believe.



<P>One man's ways may be as good as another's, but we all like our own

best.



<P>And so you must judge for yourself, whether it would be better for you



<P>to go about the house or not."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne, finding she might decline it, did so, very gratefully.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"We have made very few changes either," continued the Admiral,



<P>after thinking a moment.&nbsp; "Very few.&nbsp; We told you about the

laundry-door,



<P>at Uppercross.&nbsp; That has been a very great improvement.



<P>The wonder was, how any family upon earth could bear with the inconvenience



<P>of its opening as it did, so long!&nbsp; You will tell Sir Walter



<P>what we have done, and that Mr Shepherd thinks it the greatest improvement



<P>the house ever had.&nbsp; Indeed, I must do ourselves the justice to

say,



<P>that the few alterations we have made have been all very much



<P>for the better.&nbsp; My wife should have the credit of them, however.



<P>I have done very little besides sending away some of the large



<P>looking-glasses from my dressing-room, which was your father's.



<P>A very good man, and very much the gentleman I am sure:



<P>but I should think, Miss Elliot," (looking with serious reflection),



<P>"I should think he must be rather a dressy man for his time of life.



<P>Such a number of looking-glasses! oh Lord! there was no getting away



<P>from one's self.&nbsp; So I got Sophy to lend me a hand, and we soon



<P>shifted their quarters; and now I am quite snug, with my



<P>little shaving glass in one corner, and another great thing



<P>that I never go near."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne, amused in spite of herself, was rather distressed for an answer,



<P>and the Admiral, fearing he might not have been civil enough,



<P>took up the subject again, to say--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"The next time you write to your good father, Miss Elliot,



<P>pray give him my compliments and Mrs Croft's, and say that we are



<P>settled here quite to our liking, and have no fault at all to find



<P>with the place.&nbsp; The breakfast-room chimney smokes a little,



<P>I grant you, but it is only when the wind is due north and blows hard,



<P>which may not happen three times a winter.&nbsp; And take it altogether,



<P>now that we have been into most of the houses hereabouts and can judge,



<P>there is not one that we like better than this.&nbsp; Pray say so,



<P>with my compliments.&nbsp; He will be glad to hear it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Russell and Mrs Croft were very well pleased with each other:



<P>but the acquaintance which this visit began was fated not to proceed



<P>far at present; for when it was returned, the Crofts announced



<P>themselves to be going away for a few weeks, to visit their connexions



<P>in the north of the county, and probably might not be at home again



<P>before Lady Russell would be removing to Bath.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So ended all danger to Anne of meeting Captain Wentworth at Kellynch

Hall,



<P>or of seeing him in company with her friend.&nbsp; Everything was safe

enough,



<P>and she smiled over the many anxious feelings she had wasted



<P>on the subject.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">
