   <TITLE>Chapter 06</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 6</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Anne had not wanted this visit to Uppercross, to learn that a removal



<P>from one set of people to another, though at a distance of only three

miles,



<P>will often include a total change of conversation, opinion, and idea.



<P>She had never been staying there before, without being struck by it,



<P>or without wishing that other Elliots could have her advantage



<P>in seeing how unknown, or unconsidered there, were the affairs



<P>which at Kellynch Hall were treated as of such general publicity



<P>and pervading interest; yet, with all this experience, she believed



<P>she must now submit to feel that another lesson, in the art of knowing



<P>our own nothingness beyond our own circle, was become necessary for

her;



<P>for certainly, coming as she did, with a heart full of the subject



<P>which had been completely occupying both houses in Kellynch for many

weeks,



<P>she had expected rather more curiosity and sympathy than she found



<P>in the separate but very similar remark of Mr and Mrs Musgrove:



<P>"So, Miss Anne, Sir Walter and your sister are gone; and what part of

Bath



<P>do you think they will settle in?" and this, without much



<P>waiting for an answer; or in the young ladies' addition of,



<P>"I hope we shall be in Bath in the winter; but remember, papa,



<P>if we do go, we must be in a good situation:&nbsp; none of your



<P>Queen Squares for us!" or in the anxious supplement from Mary, of--



<P>"Upon my word, I shall be pretty well off, when you are all gone away



<P>to be happy at Bath!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She could only resolve to avoid such self-delusion in future,



<P>and think with heightened gratitude of the extraordinary blessing



<P>of having one such truly sympathising friend as Lady Russell.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The Mr Musgroves had their own game to guard, and to destroy,



<P>their own horses, dogs, and newspapers to engage them, and the females



<P>were fully occupied in all the other common subjects of housekeeping,



<P>neighbours, dress, dancing, and music.&nbsp; She acknowledged it to

be



<P>very fitting, that every little social commonwealth should dictate



<P>its own matters of discourse; and hoped, ere long, to become



<P>a not unworthy member of the one she was now transplanted into.



<P>With the prospect of spending at least two months at Uppercross,



<P>it was highly incumbent on her to clothe her imagination, her memory,



<P>and all her ideas in as much of Uppercross as possible.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She had no dread of these two months.&nbsp; Mary was not so repulsive



<P>and unsisterly as Elizabeth, nor so inaccessible to all influence of

hers;



<P>neither was there anything among the other component parts



<P>of the cottage inimical to comfort.&nbsp; She was always on friendly

terms



<P>with her brother-in-law; and in the children, who loved her nearly as

well,



<P>and respected her a great deal more than their mother, she had



<P>an object of interest, amusement, and wholesome exertion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Charles Musgrove was civil and agreeable; in sense and temper he was



<P>undoubtedly superior to his wife, but not of powers, or conversation,



<P>or grace, to make the past, as they were connected together,



<P>at all a dangerous contemplation; though, at the same time,



<P>Anne could believe, with Lady Russell, that a more equal match



<P>might have greatly improved him; and that a woman of real understanding



<P>might have given more consequence to his character, and more usefulness,



<P>rationality, and elegance to his habits and pursuits.&nbsp; As it was,



<P>he did nothing with much zeal, but sport; and his time was otherwise



<P>trifled away, without benefit from books or anything else.



<P>He had very good spirits, which never seemed much affected by



<P>his wife's occasional lowness, bore with her unreasonableness



<P>sometimes to Anne's admiration, and upon the whole, though there was



<P>very often a little disagreement (in which she had sometimes more share



<P>than she wished, being appealed to by both parties), they might pass



<P>for a happy couple.&nbsp; They were always perfectly agreed in the want



<P>of more money, and a strong inclination for a handsome present



<P>from his father; but here, as on most topics, he had the superiority,



<P>for while Mary thought it a great shame that such a present was not

made,



<P>he always contended for his father's having many other uses for his

money,



<P>and a right to spend it as he liked.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As to the management of their children, his theory was much better



<P>than his wife's, and his practice not so bad.&nbsp; "I could manage

them



<P>very well, if it were not for Mary's interference," was what



<P>Anne often heard him say, and had a good deal of faith in;



<P>but when listening in turn to Mary's reproach of "Charles spoils



<P>the children so that I cannot get them into any order," she never had



<P>the smallest temptation to say, "Very true."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One of the least agreeable circumstances of her residence there



<P>was her being treated with too much confidence by all parties,



<P>and being too much in the secret of the complaints of each house.



<P>Known to have some influence with her sister, she was continually requested,



<P>or at least receiving hints to exert it, beyond what was practicable.



<P>"I wish you could persuade Mary not to be always fancying herself ill,"



<P>was Charles's language; and, in an unhappy mood, thus spoke Mary:



<P>"I do believe if Charles were to see me dying, he would not think



<P>there was anything the matter with me.&nbsp; I am sure, Anne, if you

would,



<P>you might persuade him that I really am very ill--a great deal worse



<P>than I ever own."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mary's declaration was, "I hate sending the children to the Great House,



<P>though their grandmamma is always wanting to see them, for she humours



<P>and indulges them to such a degree, and gives them so much trash



<P>and sweet things, that they are sure to come back sick and cross



<P>for the rest of the day."&nbsp; And Mrs Musgrove took the first opportunity



<P>of being alone with Anne, to say, "Oh! Miss Anne, I cannot help wishing



<P>Mrs Charles had a little of your method with those children.



<P>They are quite different creatures with you!&nbsp; But to be sure,



<P>in general they are so spoilt!&nbsp; It is a pity you cannot put your

sister



<P>in the way of managing them.&nbsp; They are as fine healthy children



<P>as ever were seen, poor little dears! without partiality;



<P>but Mrs Charles knows no more how they should be treated--!



<P>Bless me! how troublesome they are sometimes.&nbsp; I assure you, Miss

Anne,



<P>it prevents my wishing to see them at our house so often as



<P>I otherwise should.&nbsp; I believe Mrs Charles is not quite pleased



<P>with my not inviting them oftener; but you know it is very bad



<P>to have children with one that one is obligated to be checking



<P>every moment; "don't do this," and "don't do that;"&nbsp; or that one

can



<P>only keep in tolerable order by more cake than is good for them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She had this communication, moreover, from Mary.&nbsp; "Mrs Musgrove

thinks



<P>all her servants so steady, that it would be high treason



<P>to call it in question; but I am sure, without exaggeration,



<P>that her upper house-maid and laundry-maid, instead of being



<P>in their business, are gadding about the village, all day long.



<P>I meet them wherever I go; and I declare, I never go twice into my nursery



<P>without seeing something of them.&nbsp; If Jemima were not the trustiest,



<P>steadiest creature in the world, it would be enough to spoil her;



<P>for she tells me, they are always tempting her to take a walk with them."



<P>And on Mrs Musgrove's side, it was, "I make a rule of never interfering



<P>in any of my daughter-in-law's concerns, for I know it would not do;



<P>but I shall tell you, Miss Anne, because you may be able to set things



<P>to rights, that I have no very good opinion of Mrs Charles's nursery-maid:



<P>I hear strange stories of her; she is always upon the gad; and from



<P>my own knowledge, I can declare, she is such a fine-dressing lady,



<P>that she is enough to ruin any servants she comes near.



<P>Mrs Charles quite swears by her, I know; but I just give you this hint,



<P>that you may be upon the watch; because, if you see anything amiss,



<P>you need not be afraid of mentioning it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Again, it was Mary's complaint, that Mrs Musgrove was very apt



<P>not to give her the precedence that was her due, when they dined



<P>at the Great House with other families; and she did not see any reason



<P>why she was to be considered so much at home as to lose her place.



<P>And one day when Anne was walking with only the Musgroves, one of them



<P>after talking of rank, people of rank, and jealousy of rank, said,



<P>"I have no scruple of observing to you, how nonsensical some persons

are



<P>about their place, because all the world knows how easy and indifferent



<P>you are about it; but I wish anybody could give Mary a hint that



<P>it would be a great deal better if she were not so very tenacious,



<P>especially if she would not be always putting herself forward to take



<P>place of mamma.&nbsp; Nobody doubts her right to have precedence of

mamma,



<P>but it would be more becoming in her not to be always insisting on it.



<P>It is not that mamma cares about it the least in the world,



<P>but I know it is taken notice of by many persons."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>How was Anne to set all these matters to rights?&nbsp; She could do

little more



<P>than listen patiently, soften every grievance, and excuse each



<P>to the other; give them all hints of the forbearance necessary



<P>between such near neighbours, and make those hints broadest



<P>which were meant for her sister's benefit.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In all other respects, her visit began and proceeded very well.



<P>Her own spirits improved by change of place and subject,



<P>by being removed three miles from Kellynch; Mary's ailments lessened



<P>by having a constant companion, and their daily intercourse



<P>with the other family, since there was neither superior affection,



<P>confidence, nor employment in the cottage, to be interrupted by it,



<P>was rather an advantage.&nbsp; It was certainly carried nearly as far

as possible,



<P>for they met every morning, and hardly ever spent an evening asunder;



<P>but she believed they should not have done so well without the sight



<P>of Mr and Mrs Musgrove's respectable forms in the usual places,



<P>or without the talking, laughing, and singing of their daughters.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She played a great deal better than either of the Miss Musgroves,



<P>but having no voice, no knowledge of the harp, and no fond parents,



<P>to sit by and fancy themselves delighted, her performance was



<P>little thought of, only out of civility, or to refresh the others,



<P>as she was well aware.&nbsp; She knew that when she played she was



<P>giving pleasure only to herself; but this was no new sensation.



<P>Excepting one short period of her life, she had never, since the age



<P>of fourteen, never since the loss of her dear mother, know the happiness



<P>of being listened to, or encouraged by any just appreciation or real

taste.



<P>In music she had been always used to feel alone in the world;



<P>and Mr and Mrs Musgrove's fond partiality for their own daughters'



<P>performance, and total indifference to any other person's,



<P>gave her much more pleasure for their sakes, than mortification



<P>for her own.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The party at the Great House was sometimes increased by other company.



<P>The neighbourhood was not large, but the Musgroves were visited



<P>by everybody, and had more dinner-parties, and more callers,



<P>more visitors by invitation and by chance, than any other family.



<P>There were more completely popular.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The girls were wild for dancing; and the evenings ended, occasionally,



<P>in an unpremeditated little ball.&nbsp; There was a family of cousins



<P>within a walk of Uppercross, in less affluent circumstances,



<P>who depended on the Musgroves for all their pleasures:&nbsp; they would

come



<P>at any time, and help play at anything, or dance anywhere; and Anne,



<P>very much preferring the office of musician to a more active post,



<P>played country dances to them by the hour together; a kindness which



<P>always recommended her musical powers to the notice of Mr and Mrs Musgrove



<P>more than anything else, and often drew this compliment;--



<P>"Well done, Miss Anne! very well done indeed!&nbsp; Lord bless me!



<P>how those little fingers of yours fly about!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So passed the first three weeks.&nbsp; Michaelmas came; and now Anne's

heart



<P>must be in Kellynch again.&nbsp; A beloved home made over to others;



<P>all the precious rooms and furniture, groves, and prospects,



<P>beginning to own other eyes and other limbs!&nbsp; She could not



<P>think of much else on the 29th of September; and she had this



<P>sympathetic touch in the evening from Mary, who, on having occasion



<P>to note down the day of the month, exclaimed, "Dear me, is not this



<P>the day the Crofts were to come to Kellynch?&nbsp; I am glad I did not



<P>think of it before.&nbsp; How low it makes me!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The Crofts took possession with true naval alertness, and were



<P>to be visited.&nbsp; Mary deplored the necessity for herself.



<P>"Nobody knew how much she should suffer.&nbsp; She should put it off



<P>as long as she could;" but was not easy till she had talked Charles



<P>into driving her over on an early day, and was in a very animated,



<P>comfortable state of imaginary agitation, when she came back.



<P>Anne had very sincerely rejoiced in there being no means of her going.



<P>She wished, however to see the Crofts, and was glad to be within



<P>when the visit was returned.&nbsp; They came:&nbsp; the master of the

house



<P>was not at home, but the two sisters were together; and as it chanced



<P>that Mrs Croft fell to the share of Anne, while the Admiral sat by Mary,



<P>and made himself very agreeable by his good-humoured notice



<P>of her little boys, she was well able to watch for a likeness,



<P>and if it failed her in the features, to catch it in the voice,



<P>or in the turn of sentiment and expression.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Croft, though neither tall nor fat, had a squareness,



<P>uprightness, and vigour of form, which gave importance to her person.



<P>She had bright dark eyes, good teeth, and altogether an agreeable face;



<P>though her reddened and weather-beaten complexion, the consequence



<P>of her having been almost as much at sea as her husband, made her seem

to



<P>have lived some years longer in the world than her real eight-and-thirty.



<P>Her manners were open, easy, and decided, like one who had



<P>no distrust of herself, and no doubts of what to do; without any



<P>approach to coarseness, however, or any want of good humour.



<P>Anne gave her credit, indeed, for feelings of great consideration



<P>towards herself, in all that related to Kellynch, and it pleased her:



<P>especially, as she had satisfied herself in the very first half minute,



<P>in the instant even of introduction, that there was not the smallest



<P>symptom of any knowledge or suspicion on Mrs Croft's side, to give a

bias



<P>of any sort.&nbsp; She was quite easy on that head, and consequently



<P>full of strength and courage, till for a moment electrified by



<P>Mrs Croft's suddenly saying,--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It was you, and not your sister, I find, that my brother had



<P>the pleasure of being acquainted with, when he was in this country."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne hoped she had outlived the age of blushing; but the age of emotion



<P>she certainly had not.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Perhaps you may not have heard that he is married?" added Mrs Croft.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She could now answer as she ought; and was happy to feel,



<P>when Mrs Croft's next words explained it to be Mr Wentworth



<P>of whom she spoke, that she had said nothing which might not do



<P>for either brother. She immediately felt how reasonable it was,



<P>that Mrs Croft should be thinking and speaking of Edward,



<P>and not of Frederick; and with shame at her own forgetfulness



<P>applied herself to the knowledge of their former neighbour's



<P>present state with proper interest.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The rest was all tranquillity; till, just as they were moving,



<P>she heard the Admiral say to Mary--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"We are expecting a brother of Mrs Croft's here soon; I dare say



<P>you know him by name."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He was cut short by the eager attacks of the little boys,



<P>clinging to him like an old friend, and declaring he should not go;



<P>and being too much engrossed by proposals of carrying them away



<P>in his coat pockets, &amp;c., to have another moment for finishing



<P>or recollecting what he had begun, Anne was left to persuade herself,



<P>as well as she could, that the same brother must still be in question.



<P>She could not, however, reach such a degree of certainty,



<P>as not to be anxious to hear whether anything had been said on the subject



<P>at the other house, where the Crofts had previously been calling.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The folks of the Great House were to spend the evening of this day



<P>at the Cottage; and it being now too late in the year for such visits



<P>to be made on foot, the coach was beginning to be listened for,



<P>when the youngest Miss Musgrove walked in.&nbsp; That she was coming



<P>to apologize, and that they should have to spend the evening by themselves,



<P>was the first black idea; and Mary was quite ready to be affronted,



<P>when Louisa made all right by saying, that she only came on foot,



<P>to leave more room for the harp, which was bringing in the carriage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And I will tell you our reason," she added, "and all about it.



<P>I am come on to give you notice, that papa and mamma are



<P>out of spirits this evening, especially mamma; she is thinking so much



<P>of poor Richard!&nbsp; And we agreed it would be best to have the harp,



<P>for it seems to amuse her more than the piano-forte.&nbsp; I will tell

you



<P>why she is out of spirits.&nbsp; When the Crofts called this morning,



<P>(they called here afterwards, did not they?), they happened to say,



<P>that her brother, Captain Wentworth, is just returned to England,



<P>or paid off, or something, and is coming to see them almost directly;



<P>and most unluckily it came into mamma's head, when they were gone,



<P>that Wentworth, or something very like it, was the name of



<P>poor Richard's captain at one time; I do not know when or where,



<P>but a great while before he died, poor fellow!&nbsp; And upon looking

over



<P>his letters and things, she found it was so, and is perfectly sure



<P>that this must be the very man, and her head is quite full of it,



<P>and of poor Richard!&nbsp; So we must be as merry as we can, that she

may not



<P>be dwelling upon such gloomy things."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The real circumstances of this pathetic piece of family history were,



<P>that the Musgroves had had the ill fortune of a very troublesome,



<P>hopeless son; and the good fortune to lose him before he reached



<P>his twentieth year; that he had been sent to sea because he was stupid



<P>and unmanageable on shore; that he had been very little cared for



<P>at any time by his family, though quite as much as he deserved;



<P>seldom heard of, and scarcely at all regretted, when the intelligence



<P>of his death abroad had worked its way to Uppercross, two years before.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had, in fact, though his sisters were now doing all they could for

him,



<P>by calling him "poor Richard," been nothing better than a thick-headed,



<P>unfeeling, unprofitable Dick Musgrove, who had never done anything



<P>to entitle himself to more than the abbreviation of his name,



<P>living or dead.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had been several years at sea, and had, in the course of those removals



<P>to which all midshipmen are liable, and especially such midshipmen



<P>as every captain wishes to get rid of, been six months on board



<P>Captain Frederick Wentworth's frigate, the Laconia; and from the Laconia



<P>he had, under the influence of his captain, written the only two letters



<P>which his father and mother had ever received from him during the whole



<P>of his absence; that is to say, the only two disinterested letters;



<P>all the rest had been mere applications for money.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In each letter he had spoken well of his captain; but yet,



<P>so little were they in the habit of attending to such matters,



<P>so unobservant and incurious were they as to the names of men or ships,



<P>that it had made scarcely any impression at the time; and that Mrs Musgrove



<P>should have been suddenly struck, this very day, with a recollection



<P>of the name of Wentworth, as connected with her son, seemed one of those



<P>extraordinary bursts of mind which do sometimes occur.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She had gone to her letters, and found it all as she supposed;



<P>and the re-perusal of these letters, after so long an interval,



<P>her poor son gone for ever, and all the strength of his faults forgotten,



<P>had affected her spirits exceedingly, and thrown her into



<P>greater grief for him than she had know on first hearing of his death.



<P>Mr Musgrove was, in a lesser degree, affected likewise; and when



<P>they reached the cottage, they were evidently in want, first,



<P>of being listened to anew on this subject, and afterwards,



<P>of all the relief which cheerful companions could give them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To hear them talking so much of Captain Wentworth, repeating his name



<P>so often, puzzling over past years, and at last ascertaining that it

might,



<P>that it probably would, turn out to be the very same Captain Wentworth



<P>whom they recollected meeting, once or twice, after their coming back



<P>from Clifton--a very fine young man--but they could not say whether



<P>it was seven or eight years ago, was a new sort of trial to Anne's nerves.



<P>She found, however, that it was one to which she must inure herself.



<P>Since he actually was expected in the country, she must teach herself



<P>to be insensible on such points.&nbsp; And not only did it appear that



<P>he was expected, and speedily, but the Musgroves, in their warm gratitude



<P>for the kindness he had shewn poor Dick, and very high respect



<P>for his character, stamped as it was by poor Dick's having been



<P>six months under his care, and mentioning him in strong,



<P>though not perfectly well-spelt praise, as "a fine dashing felow,



<P>only two perticular about the schoolmaster," were bent on



<P>introducing themselves, and seeking his acquaintance, as soon as



<P>they could hear of his arrival.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The resolution of doing so helped to form the comfort of their evening.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 12</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 12</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Anne and Henrietta, finding themselves the earliest of the party



<P>the next morning, agreed to stroll down to the sea before breakfast.



<P>They went to the sands, to watch the flowing of the tide,



<P>which a fine south-easterly breeze was bringing in with all the grandeur



<P>which so flat a shore admitted.&nbsp; They praised the morning;



<P>gloried in the sea; sympathized in the delight of the fresh-feeling



<P>breeze--and were silent; till Henrietta suddenly began again with--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! yes,--I am quite convinced that, with very few exceptions,



<P>the sea-air always does good.&nbsp; There can be no doubt of its having

been



<P>of the greatest service to Dr Shirley, after his illness,



<P>last spring twelve-month.&nbsp; He declares himself, that coming to

Lyme



<P>for a month, did him more good than all the medicine he took;



<P>and, that being by the sea, always makes him feel young again.



<P>Now, I cannot help thinking it a pity that he does not live



<P>entirely by the sea.&nbsp; I do think he had better leave Uppercross

entirely,



<P>and fix at Lyme.&nbsp; Do not you, Anne?&nbsp; Do not you agree with

me,



<P>that it is the best thing he could do, both for himself and Mrs Shirley?



<P>She has cousins here, you know, and many acquaintance, which would



<P>make it cheerful for her, and I am sure she would be glad



<P>to get to a place where she could have medical attendance at hand,



<P>in case of his having another seizure.&nbsp; Indeed I think it quite

melancholy



<P>to have such excellent people as Dr and Mrs Shirley, who have been



<P>doing good all their lives, wearing out their last days in a place



<P>like Uppercross, where, excepting our family, they seem shut out



<P>from all the world.&nbsp; I wish his friends would propose it to him.



<P>I really think they ought.&nbsp; And, as to procuring a dispensation,



<P>there could be no difficulty at his time of life, and with his character.



<P>My only doubt is, whether anything could persuade him to leave his parish.



<P>He is so very strict and scrupulous in his notions; over-scrupulous



<P>I must say.&nbsp; Do not you think, Anne, it is being over-scrupulous?



<P>Do not you think it is quite a mistaken point of conscience,



<P>when a clergyman sacrifices his health for the sake of duties,



<P>which may be just as well performed by another person?&nbsp; And at

Lyme too,



<P>only seventeen miles off, he would be near enough to hear,



<P>if people thought there was anything to complain of."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne smiled more than once to herself during this speech,



<P>and entered into the subject, as ready to do good by entering into



<P>the feelings of a young lady as of a young man, though here it was good



<P>of a lower standard, for what could be offered but general acquiescence?



<P>She said all that was reasonable and proper on the business;



<P>felt the claims of Dr Shirley to repose as she ought; saw how very



<P>desirable it was that he should have some active, respectable young

man,



<P>as a resident curate, and was even courteous enough to hint at



<P>the advantage of such resident curate's being married.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I wish," said Henrietta, very well pleased with her companion,



<P>"I wish Lady Russell lived at Uppercross, and were intimate



<P>with Dr Shirley.&nbsp; I have always heard of Lady Russell as a woman

of



<P>the greatest influence with everybody!&nbsp; I always look upon her

as able



<P>to persuade a person to anything!&nbsp; I am afraid of her, as I have



<P>told you before, quite afraid of her, because she is so very clever;



<P>but I respect her amazingly, and wish we had such a neighbour



<P>at Uppercross."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne was amused by Henrietta's manner of being grateful,



<P>and amused also that the course of events and the new interests



<P>of Henrietta's views should have placed her friend at all in favour



<P>with any of the Musgrove family; she had only time, however,



<P>for a general answer, and a wish that such another woman



<P>were at Uppercross, before all subjects suddenly ceased,



<P>on seeing Louisa and Captain Wentworth coming towards them.



<P>They came also for a stroll till breakfast was likely to be ready;



<P>but Louisa recollecting, immediately afterwards that she had something



<P>to procure at a shop, invited them all to go back with her into the

town.



<P>They were all at her disposal.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When they came to the steps, leading upwards from the beach, a gentleman,



<P>at the same moment preparing to come down, politely drew back,



<P>and stopped to give them way.&nbsp; They ascended and passed him;



<P>and as they passed, Anne's face caught his eye, and he looked at her



<P>with a degree of earnest admiration, which she could not be insensible

of.



<P>She was looking remarkably well; her very regular, very pretty features,



<P>having the bloom and freshness of youth restored by the fine wind



<P>which had been blowing on her complexion, and by the animation of eye



<P>which it had also produced.&nbsp; It was evident that the gentleman,



<P>(completely a gentleman in manner) admired her exceedingly.



<P>Captain Wentworth looked round at her instantly in a way which



<P>shewed his noticing of it.&nbsp; He gave her a momentary glance,



<P>a glance of brightness, which seemed to say, "That man is struck with

you,



<P>and even I, at this moment, see something like Anne Elliot again."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After attending Louisa through her business, and loitering about



<P>a little longer, they returned to the inn; and Anne, in passing afterwards



<P>quickly from her own chamber to their dining-room, had nearly run against



<P>the very same gentleman, as he came out of an adjoining apartment.



<P>She had before conjectured him to be a stranger like themselves,



<P>and determined that a well-looking groom, who was strolling about



<P>near the two inns as they came back, should be his servant.



<P>Both master and man being in mourning assisted the idea.



<P>It was now proved that he belonged to the same inn as themselves;



<P>and this second meeting, short as it was, also proved again



<P>by the gentleman's looks, that he thought hers very lovely,



<P>and by the readiness and propriety of his apologies, that he was



<P>a man of exceedingly good manners.&nbsp; He seemed about thirty,



<P>and though not handsome, had an agreeable person.&nbsp; Anne felt that



<P>she should like to know who he was.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They had nearly done breakfast, when the sound of a carriage,



<P>(almost the first they had heard since entering Lyme) drew half the

party



<P>to the window.&nbsp; It was a gentleman's carriage, a curricle,



<P>but only coming round from the stable-yard to the front door;



<P>somebody must be going away.&nbsp; It was driven by a servant in mourning.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The word curricle made Charles Musgrove jump up that he might



<P>compare it with his own; the servant in mourning roused Anne's curiosity,



<P>and the whole six were collected to look, by the time the owner



<P>of the curricle was to be seen issuing from the door amidst the bows



<P>and civilities of the household, and taking his seat, to drive off.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ah!" cried Captain Wentworth, instantly, and with half a glance at

Anne,



<P>"it is the very man we passed."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The Miss Musgroves agreed to it; and having all kindly watched him



<P>as far up the hill as they could, they returned to the breakfast table.



<P>The waiter came into the room soon afterwards.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Pray," said Captain Wentworth, immediately, "can you tell us the name



<P>of the gentleman who is just gone away?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, Sir, a Mr Elliot, a gentleman of large fortune, came in last night



<P>from Sidmouth.&nbsp; Dare say you heard the carriage, sir, while you

were



<P>at dinner; and going on now for Crewkherne, in his way to Bath



<P>and London."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Elliot!"&nbsp; Many had looked on each other, and many had repeated

the name,



<P>before all this had been got through, even by the smart rapidity



<P>of a waiter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Bless me!" cried Mary; "it must be our cousin; it must be our Mr Elliot,



<P>it must, indeed!&nbsp; Charles, Anne, must not it?&nbsp; In mourning,

you see,



<P>just as our Mr Elliot must be.&nbsp; How very extraordinary!



<P>In the very same inn with us!&nbsp; Anne, must not it be our Mr Elliot?



<P>my father's next heir?&nbsp; Pray sir," turning to the waiter,



<P>"did not you hear, did not his servant say whether he belonged



<P>to the Kellynch family?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, ma'am, he did not mention no particular family; but he said



<P>his master was a very rich gentleman, and would be a baronight some

day."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"There! you see!" cried Mary in an ecstasy, "just as I said!



<P>Heir to Sir Walter Elliot!&nbsp; I was sure that would come out,



<P>if it was so.&nbsp; Depend upon it, that is a circumstance which his

servants



<P>take care to publish, wherever he goes.&nbsp; But, Anne, only conceive



<P>how extraordinary!&nbsp; I wish I had looked at him more.&nbsp; I wish

we had



<P>been aware in time, who it was, that he might have been introduced to

us.



<P>What a pity that we should not have been introduced to each other!



<P>Do you think he had the Elliot countenance?&nbsp; I hardly looked at

him,



<P>I was looking at the horses; but I think he had something



<P>of the Elliot countenance, I wonder the arms did not strike me!



<P>Oh! the great-coat was hanging over the panel, and hid the arms,



<P>so it did; otherwise, I am sure, I should have observed them,



<P>and the livery too; if the servant had not been in mourning,



<P>one should have known him by the livery."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Putting all these very extraordinary circumstances together,"



<P>said Captain Wentworth, "we must consider it to be the arrangement



<P>of Providence, that you should not be introduced to your cousin."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When she could command Mary's attention, Anne quietly tried



<P>to convince her that their father and Mr Elliot had not, for many years,



<P>been on such terms as to make the power of attempting an introduction



<P>at all desirable.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At the same time, however, it was a secret gratification to herself



<P>to have seen her cousin, and to know that the future owner of Kellynch



<P>was undoubtedly a gentleman, and had an air of good sense.



<P>She would not, upon any account, mention her having met with him



<P>the second time; luckily Mary did not much attend to their having



<P>passed close by him in their earlier walk, but she would have felt



<P>quite ill-used by Anne's having actually run against him in the passage,



<P>and received his very polite excuses, while she had never been



<P>near him at all; no, that cousinly little interview must remain



<P>a perfect secret.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Of course," said Mary, "you will mention our seeing Mr Elliot,



<P>the next time you write to Bath.&nbsp; I think my father certainly



<P>ought to hear of it; do mention all about him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne avoided a direct reply, but it was just the circumstance



<P>which she considered as not merely unnecessary to be communicated,



<P>but as what ought to be suppressed.&nbsp; The offence which had been

given



<P>her father, many years back, she knew; Elizabeth's particular share

in it



<P>she suspected; and that Mr Elliot's idea always produced irritation

in both



<P>was beyond a doubt.&nbsp; Mary never wrote to Bath herself; all the

toil



<P>of keeping up a slow and unsatisfactory correspondence with Elizabeth



<P>fell on Anne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Breakfast had not been long over, when they were joined by Captain



<P>and Mrs Harville and Captain Benwick; with whom they had appointed



<P>to take their last walk about Lyme.&nbsp; They ought to be setting off



<P>for Uppercross by one, and in the mean while were to be all together,



<P>and out of doors as long as they could.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne found Captain Benwick getting near her, as soon as they were all



<P>fairly in the street.&nbsp; Their conversation the preceding evening



<P>did not disincline him to seek her again; and they walked together



<P>some time, talking as before of Mr Scott and Lord Byron,



<P>and still as unable as before, and as unable as any other two readers,



<P>to think exactly alike of the merits of either, till something



<P>occasioned an almost general change amongst their party, and instead

of



<P>Captain Benwick, she had Captain Harville by her side.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Miss Elliot," said he, speaking rather low, "you have done a good deed



<P>in making that poor fellow talk so much.&nbsp; I wish he could have



<P>such company oftener.&nbsp; It is bad for him, I know, to be shut up

as he is;



<P>but what can we do?&nbsp; We cannot part."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No," said Anne, "that I can easily believe to be impossible;



<P>but in time, perhaps--we know what time does in every case of affliction,



<P>and you must remember, Captain Harville, that your friend



<P>may yet be called a young mourner--only last summer, I understand."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ay, true enough," (with a deep sigh) "only June."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And not known to him, perhaps, so soon."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Not till the first week of August, when he came home from the Cape,



<P>just made into the Grappler.&nbsp; I was at Plymouth dreading to hear

of him;



<P>he sent in letters, but the Grappler was under orders for Portsmouth.



<P>There the news must follow him, but who was to tell it? not I.



<P>I would as soon have been run up to the yard-arm.&nbsp; Nobody could

do it,



<P>but that good fellow" (pointing to Captain Wentworth.)&nbsp; "The Laconia



<P>had come into Plymouth the week before; no danger of her



<P>being sent to sea again.&nbsp; He stood his chance for the rest;



<P>wrote up for leave of absence, but without waiting the return,



<P>travelled night and day till he got to Portsmouth, rowed off



<P>to the Grappler that instant, and never left the poor fellow for a week.



<P>That's what he did, and nobody else could have saved poor James.



<P>You may think, Miss Elliot, whether he is dear to us!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne did think on the question with perfect decision, and said as much



<P>in reply as her own feeling could accomplish, or as his seemed



<P>able to bear, for he was too much affected to renew the subject,



<P>and when he spoke again, it was of something totally different.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Harville's giving it as her opinion that her husband would have



<P>quite walking enough by the time he reached home, determined the direction



<P>of all the party in what was to be their last walk; they would



<P>accompany them to their door, and then return and set off themselves.



<P>By all their calculations there was just time for this; but as they

drew



<P>near the Cobb, there was such a general wish to walk along it once more,



<P>all were so inclined, and Louisa soon grew so determined,



<P>that the difference of a quarter of an hour, it was found,



<P>would be no difference at all; so with all the kind leave-taking,



<P>and all the kind interchange of invitations and promises which



<P>may be imagined, they parted from Captain and Mrs Harville



<P>at their own door, and still accompanied by Captain Benwick,



<P>who seemed to cling to them to the last, proceeded to make



<P>the proper adieus to the Cobb.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne found Captain Benwick again drawing near her.&nbsp; Lord Byron's



<P>"dark blue seas" could not fail of being brought forward by



<P>their present view, and she gladly gave him all her attention as long

as



<P>attention was possible.&nbsp; It was soon drawn, perforce another way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was too much wind to make the high part of the new Cobb pleasant



<P>for the ladies, and they agreed to get down the steps to the lower,



<P>and all were contented to pass quietly and carefully down the steep

flight,



<P>excepting Louisa; she must be jumped down them by Captain Wentworth.



<P>In all their walks, he had had to jump her from the stiles;



<P>the sensation was delightful to her.&nbsp; The hardness of the pavement



<P>for her feet, made him less willing upon the present occasion;



<P>he did it, however.&nbsp; She was safely down, and instantly,



<P>to show her enjoyment, ran up the steps to be jumped down again.



<P>He advised her against it, thought the jar too great; but no,



<P>he reasoned and talked in vain, she smiled and said, "I am determined



<P>I will:" he put out his hands; she was too precipitate by half a second,



<P>she fell on the pavement on the Lower Cobb, and was taken up lifeless!



<P>There was no wound, no blood, no visible bruise; but her eyes were closed,



<P>she breathed not, her face was like death.&nbsp; The horror of the moment



<P>to all who stood around!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Wentworth, who had caught her up, knelt with her in his arms,



<P>looking on her with a face as pallid as her own, in an agony of silence.



<P>"She is dead! she is dead!" screamed Mary, catching hold of her



<P>husband, and contributing with his own horror to make him immoveable;



<P>and in another moment, Henrietta, sinking under the conviction, lost



<P>her senses too, and would have fallen on the steps, but for Captain



<P>Benwick and Anne, who caught and supported her between them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Is there no one to help me?" were the first words which



<P>burst from Captain Wentworth, in a tone of despair, and as if



<P>all his own strength were gone.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Go to him, go to him," cried Anne, "for heaven's sake go to him.



<P>I can support her myself.&nbsp; Leave me, and go to him.&nbsp; Rub her

hands,



<P>rub her temples; here are salts; take them, take them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Benwick obeyed, and Charles at the same moment,



<P>disengaging himself from his wife, they were both with him;



<P>and Louisa was raised up and supported more firmly between them,



<P>and everything was done that Anne had prompted, but in vain;



<P>while Captain Wentworth, staggering against the wall for his support,



<P>exclaimed in the bitterest agony--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh God! her father and mother!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"A surgeon!" said Anne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He caught the word; it seemed to rouse him at once, and saying only--



<P>"True, true, a surgeon this instant," was darting away,



<P>when Anne eagerly suggested--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Captain Benwick, would not it be better for Captain Benwick?



<P>He knows where a surgeon is to be found."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Every one capable of thinking felt the advantage of the idea,



<P>and in a moment (it was all done in rapid moments) Captain Benwick had



<P>resigned the poor corpse-like&nbsp; figure entirely to the brother's

care,



<P>and was off for the town with the utmost rapidity.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As to the wretched party left behind, it could scarcely be said



<P>which of the three, who were completely rational, was suffering most:



<P>Captain Wentworth, Anne, or Charles, who, really a very affectionate



<P>brother, hung over Louisa with sobs of grief, and could only turn his

eyes



<P>from one sister, to see the other in a state as insensible,



<P>or to witness the hysterical agitations of his wife, calling on him



<P>for help which he could not give.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne, attending with all the strength and zeal, and thought,



<P>which instinct supplied, to Henrietta, still tried, at intervals,



<P>to suggest comfort to the others, tried to quiet Mary, to animate Charles,



<P>to assuage the feelings of Captain Wentworth.&nbsp; Both seemed to look

to her



<P>for directions.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Anne, Anne," cried Charles, "What is to be done next?



<P>What, in heaven's name, is to be done next?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Wentworth's eyes were also turned towards her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Had not she better be carried to the inn?&nbsp; Yes, I am sure:



<P>carry her gently to the inn."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, yes, to the inn," repeated Captain Wentworth, comparatively



<P>collected, and eager to be doing something.&nbsp; "I will carry her

myself.



<P>Musgrove, take care of the others."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>By this time the report of the accident had spread among the workmen



<P>and boatmen about the Cobb, and many were collected near them,



<P>to be useful if wanted, at any rate, to enjoy the sight of



<P>a dead young lady, nay, two dead young ladies, for it proved twice as

fine



<P>as the first report.&nbsp; To some of the best-looking of these good

people



<P>Henrietta was consigned, for, though partially revived,



<P>she was quite helpless; and in this manner, Anne walking by her side,



<P>and Charles attending to his wife, they set forward, treading back



<P>with feelings unutterable, the ground, which so lately, so very lately,



<P>and so light of heart, they had passed along.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They were not off the Cobb, before the Harvilles met them.



<P>Captain Benwick had been seen flying by their house, with a countenance



<P>which showed something to be wrong; and they had set off immediately,



<P>informed and directed as they passed, towards the spot.



<P>Shocked as Captain Harville was, he brought senses and nerves



<P>that could be instantly useful; and a look between him and his wife



<P>decided what was to be done.&nbsp; She must be taken to their house;



<P>all must go to their house; and await the surgeon's arrival there.



<P>They would not listen to scruples:&nbsp; he was obeyed; they were all



<P>beneath his roof; and while Louisa, under Mrs Harville's direction,



<P>was conveyed up stairs, and given possession of her own bed,



<P>assistance, cordials, restoratives were supplied by her husband



<P>to all who needed them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Louisa had once opened her eyes, but soon closed them again,



<P>without apparent consciousness.&nbsp; This had been a proof of life,



<P>however, of service to her sister; and Henrietta, though perfectly



<P>incapable of being in the same room with Louisa, was kept,



<P>by the agitation of hope and fear, from a return of her own insensibility.



<P>Mary, too, was growing calmer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The surgeon was with them almost before it had seemed possible.



<P>They were sick with horror, while he examined; but he was not hopeless.



<P>The head had received a severe contusion, but he had seen greater injuries



<P>recovered from:&nbsp; he was by no means hopeless; he spoke cheerfully.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>That he did not regard it as a desperate case, that he did not say



<P>a few hours must end it, was at first felt, beyond the hope of most;



<P>and the ecstasy of such a reprieve, the rejoicing, deep and silent,



<P>after a few fervent ejaculations of gratitude to Heaven had been offered,



<P>may be conceived.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tone, the look, with which "Thank God!" was uttered



<P>by Captain Wentworth, Anne was sure could never be forgotten by her;



<P>nor the sight of him afterwards, as he sat near a table, leaning over

it



<P>with folded arms and face concealed, as if overpowered by



<P>the various feelings of his soul, and trying by prayer and reflection



<P>to calm them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Louisa's limbs had escaped.&nbsp; There was no injury but to the head.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It now became necessary for the party to consider what was best to be

done,



<P>as to their general situation.&nbsp; They were now able to speak to

each other



<P>and consult.&nbsp; That Louisa must remain where she was, however distressing



<P>to her friends to be involving the Harvilles in such trouble,



<P>did not admit a doubt.&nbsp; Her removal was impossible.&nbsp; The Harvilles



<P>silenced all scruples; and, as much as they could, all gratitude.



<P>They had looked forward and arranged everything before the others



<P>began to reflect.&nbsp; Captain Benwick must give up his room to them,



<P>and get another bed elsewhere; and the whole was settled.



<P>They were only concerned that the house could accommodate no more;



<P>and yet perhaps, by "putting the children away in the maid's room,



<P>or swinging a cot somewhere," they could hardly bear to think of not



<P>finding room for two or three besides, supposing they might wish to

stay;



<P>though, with regard to any attendance on Miss Musgrove, there need not

be



<P>the least uneasiness in leaving her to Mrs Harville's care entirely.



<P>Mrs Harville was a very experienced nurse, and her nursery-maid,



<P>who had lived with her long, and gone about with her everywhere,



<P>was just such another.&nbsp; Between these two, she could want



<P>no possible attendance by day or night.&nbsp; And all this was said



<P>with a truth and sincerity of feeling irresistible.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Charles, Henrietta, and Captain Wentworth were the three in consultation,



<P>and for a little while it was only an interchange of perplexity and

terror.



<P>"Uppercross, the necessity of some one's going to Uppercross;



<P>the news to be conveyed; how it could be broken to Mr and Mrs Musgrove;



<P>the lateness of the morning; an hour already gone since they



<P>ought to have been off; the impossibility of being in tolerable time."



<P>At first, they were capable of nothing more to the purpose



<P>than such exclamations; but, after a while, Captain Wentworth,



<P>exerting himself, said--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"We must be decided, and without the loss of another minute.



<P>Every minute is valuable.&nbsp; Some one must resolve on being off



<P>for Uppercross instantly.&nbsp; Musgrove, either you or I must go."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Charles agreed, but declared his resolution of not going away.



<P>He would be as little incumbrance as possible to Captain and Mrs Harville;



<P>but as to leaving his sister in such a state, he neither ought, nor

would.



<P>So far it was decided; and Henrietta at first declared the same.



<P>She, however, was soon persuaded to think differently.&nbsp; The usefulness



<P>of her staying!&nbsp; She who had not been able to remain in Louisa's

room,



<P>or to look at her, without sufferings which made her worse than helpless!



<P>She was forced to acknowledge that she could do no good,



<P>yet was still unwilling to be away, till, touched by the thought



<P>of her father and mother, she gave it up; she consented,



<P>she was anxious to be at home.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The plan had reached this point, when Anne, coming quietly



<P>down from Louisa's room, could not but hear what followed,



<P>for the parlour door was open.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Then it is settled, Musgrove," cried Captain Wentworth,



<P>"that you stay, and that I take care of your sister home.



<P>But as to the rest, as to the others, if one stays to assist Mrs Harville,



<P>I think it need be only one.&nbsp; Mrs Charles Musgrove will, of course,



<P>wish to get back to her children; but if Anne will stay, no one so proper,



<P>so capable as Anne."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She paused a moment to recover from the emotion of hearing herself



<P>so spoken of.&nbsp; The other two warmly agreed with what he said,



<P>and she then appeared.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You will stay, I am sure; you will stay and nurse her;" cried he,



<P>turning to her and speaking with a glow, and yet a gentleness,



<P>which seemed almost restoring the past.&nbsp; She coloured deeply,



<P>and he recollected himself and moved away.&nbsp; She expressed herself



<P>most willing, ready, happy to remain.&nbsp; "It was what she had been



<P>thinking of, and wishing to be allowed to do.&nbsp; A bed on the floor



<P>in Louisa's room would be sufficient for her, if Mrs Harville



<P>would but think so."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One thing more, and all seemed arranged.&nbsp; Though it was rather

desirable



<P>that Mr and Mrs Musgrove should be previously alarmed by some



<P>share of delay; yet the time required by the Uppercross horses



<P>to take them back, would be a dreadful extension of suspense;



<P>and Captain Wentworth proposed, and Charles Musgrove agreed,



<P>that it would be much better for him to take a chaise from the inn,



<P>and leave Mr Musgrove's carriage and horses to be sent home



<P>the next morning early, when there would be the farther advantage



<P>of sending an account of Louisa's night.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Wentworth now hurried off to get everything ready on his part,



<P>and to be soon followed by the two ladies.&nbsp; When the plan was



<P>made known to Mary, however, there was an end of all peace in it.



<P>She was so wretched and so vehement, complained so much of injustice



<P>in being expected to go away instead of Anne; Anne, who was



<P>nothing to Louisa, while she was her sister, and had the best right



<P>to stay in Henrietta's stead!&nbsp; Why was not she to be as useful

as Anne?



<P>And to go home without Charles, too, without her husband!



<P>No, it was too unkind.&nbsp; And in short, she said more than her husband



<P>could long withstand, and as none of the others could oppose



<P>when he gave way, there was no help for it; the change of Mary for Anne



<P>was inevitable.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne had never submitted more reluctantly to the jealous



<P>and ill-judging claims of Mary; but so it must be, and they set off



<P>for the town, Charles taking care of his sister, and Captain Benwick



<P>attending to her.&nbsp; She gave a moment's recollection, as they hurried

along,



<P>to the little circumstances which the same spots had witnessed



<P>earlier in the morning.&nbsp; There she had listened to Henrietta's

schemes



<P>for Dr Shirley's leaving Uppercross; farther on, she had



<P>first seen Mr Elliot; a moment seemed all that could now be given



<P>to any one but Louisa, or those who were wrapt up in her welfare.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Benwick was most considerately attentive to her; and,



<P>united as they all seemed by the distress of the day, she felt



<P>an increasing degree of good-will towards him, and a pleasure even



<P>in thinking that it might, perhaps, be the occasion of continuing



<P>their acquaintance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Wentworth was on the watch for them, and a chaise and four in

waiting,



<P>stationed for their convenience in the lowest part of the street;



<P>but his evident surprise and vexation at the substitution of one sister



<P>for the other, the change in his countenance, the astonishment,



<P>the expressions begun and suppressed, with which Charles was listened

to,



<P>made but a mortifying reception of Anne; or must at least convince her



<P>that she was valued only as she could be useful to Louisa.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She endeavoured to be composed, and to be just.&nbsp; Without emulating



<P>the feelings of an Emma towards her Henry, she would have



<P>attended on Louisa with a zeal above the common claims of regard,



<P>for his sake; and she hoped he would not long be so unjust



<P>as to suppose she would shrink unnecessarily from the office of a friend.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In the mean while she was in the carriage.&nbsp; He had handed them

both in,



<P>and placed himself between them; and in this manner, under these



<P>circumstances, full of astonishment and emotion to Anne, she quitted

Lyme.



<P>How the long stage would pass; how it was to affect their manners;



<P>what was to be their sort of intercourse, she could not foresee.



<P>It was all quite natural, however.&nbsp; He was devoted to Henrietta;



<P>always turning towards her; and when he spoke at all, always with the

view



<P>of supporting her hopes and raising her spirits.&nbsp; In general,



<P>his voice and manner were studiously calm.&nbsp; To spare Henrietta



<P>from agitation seemed the governing principle.&nbsp; Once only,



<P>when she had been grieving over the last ill-judged, ill-fated



<P>walk to the Cobb, bitterly lamenting that it ever had been thought of,



<P>he burst forth, as if wholly overcome--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Don't talk of it, don't talk of it," he cried.&nbsp; "Oh God! that

I had



<P>not given way to her at the fatal moment!&nbsp; Had I done as I ought!



<P>But so eager and so resolute! Dear, sweet Louisa!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne wondered whether it ever occurred to him now, to question the justness



<P>of his own previous opinion as to the universal felicity and advantage



<P>of firmness of character; and whether it might not strike him that,



<P>like all other qualities of the mind, it should have its proportions



<P>and limits.&nbsp; She thought it could scarcely escape him to feel



<P>that a persuadable temper might sometimes be as much in favour of happiness



<P>as a very resolute character.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They got on fast.&nbsp; Anne was astonished to recognise the same hills



<P>and the same objects so soon.&nbsp; Their actual speed, heightened by



<P>some dread of the conclusion, made the road appear but half as long



<P>as on the day before.&nbsp; It was growing quite dusk, however,



<P>before they were in the neighbourhood of Uppercross, and there had been



<P>total silence among them for some time, Henrietta leaning back



<P>in the corner, with a shawl over her face, giving the hope of her



<P>having cried herself to sleep; when, as they were going up their last

hill,



<P>Anne found herself all at once addressed by Captain Wentworth.



<P>In a low, cautious voice, he said: --

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I have been considering what we had best do.&nbsp; She must not



<P>appear at first.&nbsp; She could not stand it.&nbsp; I have been thinking

whether



<P>you had not better remain in the carriage with her, while I go in



<P>and break it to Mr and Mrs Musgrove.&nbsp; Do you think this is a good

plan?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She did:&nbsp; he was satisfied, and said no more.&nbsp; But the remembrance



<P>of the appeal remained a pleasure to her, as a proof of friendship,



<P>and of deference for her judgement, a great pleasure; and when it became



<P>a sort of parting proof, its value did not lessen.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When the distressing communication at Uppercross was over,



<P>and he had seen the father and mother quite as composed as could be

hoped,



<P>and the daughter all the better for being with them, he announced



<P>his intention of returning in the same carriage to Lyme;



<P>and when the horses were baited, he was off.

<BR>&nbsp;



<P><B>(End of volume one.)</B>



<P>
