<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 23</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 23</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>One day only had passed since Anne's conversation with Mrs Smith;



<P>but a keener interest had succeeded, and she was now so little touched



<P>by Mr Elliot's conduct, except by its effects in one quarter,



<P>that it became a matter of course the next morning, still to defer



<P>her explanatory visit in Rivers Street.&nbsp; She had promised to be



<P>with the Musgroves from breakfast to dinner.&nbsp; Her faith was plighted,



<P>and Mr Elliot's character, like the Sultaness Scheherazade's head,



<P>must live another day.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She could not keep her appointment punctually, however;



<P>the weather was unfavourable, and she had grieved over the rain



<P>on her friends' account, and felt it very much on her own,



<P>before she was able to attempt the walk.&nbsp; When she reached the

White Hart,



<P>and made her way to the proper apartment, she found herself



<P>neither arriving quite in time, nor the first to arrive.



<P>The party before her were, Mrs Musgrove, talking to Mrs Croft,



<P>and Captain Harville to Captain Wentworth; and she immediately heard



<P>that Mary and Henrietta, too impatient to wait, had gone out the moment



<P>it had cleared, but would be back again soon, and that the strictest



<P>injunctions had been left with Mrs Musgrove to keep her there



<P>till they returned.&nbsp; She had only to submit, sit down,



<P>be outwardly composed, and feel herself plunged at once



<P>in all the agitations which she had merely laid her account of



<P>tasting a little before the morning closed.&nbsp; There was no delay,



<P>no waste of time.&nbsp; She was deep in the happiness of such misery,



<P>or the misery of such happiness, instantly.&nbsp; Two minutes after



<P>her entering the room, Captain Wentworth said--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"We will write the letter we were talking of, Harville, now,



<P>if you will give me materials."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Materials were at hand, on a separate table; he went to it,



<P>and nearly turning his back to them all, was engrossed by writing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Musgrove was giving Mrs Croft the history of her eldest



<P>daughter's engagement, and just in that inconvenient tone of voice



<P>which was perfectly audible while it pretended to be a whisper.



<P>Anne felt that she did not belong to the conversation, and yet,



<P>as Captain Harville seemed thoughtful and not disposed to talk,



<P>she could not avoid hearing many undesirable particulars; such as,



<P>"how Mr Musgrove and my brother Hayter had met again and again



<P>to talk it over; what my brother Hayter had said one day,



<P>and what Mr Musgrove had proposed the next, and what had occurred



<P>to my sister Hayter, and what the young people had wished, and what



<P>I said at first I never could consent to, but was afterwards persuaded



<P>to think might do very well," and a great deal in the same style



<P>of open-hearted communication:&nbsp; minutiae which, even with every

advantage



<P>of taste and delicacy, which good Mrs Musgrove could not give,



<P>could be properly interesting only to the principals.&nbsp; Mrs Croft



<P>was attending with great good-humour, and whenever she spoke at all,



<P>it was very sensibly.&nbsp; Anne hoped the gentlemen might each be



<P>too much self-occupied to hear.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And so, ma'am, all these thing considered," said Mrs Musgrove,



<P>in her powerful whisper, "though we could have wished it different,



<P>yet, altogether, we did not think it fair to stand out any longer,



<P>for Charles Hayter was quite wild about it, and Henrietta was



<P>pretty near as bad; and so we thought they had better marry at once,



<P>and make the best of it, as many others have done before them.



<P>At any rate, said I, it will be better than a long engagement."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"That is precisely what I was going to observe," cried Mrs Croft.



<P>"I would rather have young people settle on a small income at once,



<P>and have to struggle with a few difficulties together, than be



<P>involved in a long engagement.&nbsp; I always think that no mutual--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! dear Mrs Croft," cried Mrs Musgrove, unable to let her



<P>finish her speech, "there is nothing I so abominate for young people



<P>as a long engagement.&nbsp; It is what I always protested against



<P>for my children.&nbsp; It is all very well, I used to say, for young

people



<P>to be engaged, if there is a certainty of their being able to marry



<P>in six months, or even in twelve; but a long engagement--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, dear ma'am," said Mrs Croft, "or an uncertain engagement,



<P>an engagement which may be long.&nbsp; To begin without knowing



<P>that at such a time there will be the means of marrying,



<P>I hold to be very unsafe and unwise, and what I think all parents



<P>should prevent as far as they can."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne found an unexpected interest here.&nbsp; She felt its application



<P>to herself, felt it in a nervous thrill all over her; and at the same



<P>moment that her eyes instinctively glanced towards the distant table,



<P>Captain Wentworth's pen ceased to move, his head was raised, pausing,



<P>listening, and he turned round the next instant to give a look,



<P>one quick, conscious look at her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The two ladies continued to talk, to re-urge the same admitted truths,



<P>and enforce them with such examples of the ill effect of



<P>a contrary practice as had fallen within their observation,



<P>but Anne heard nothing distinctly; it was only a buzz of words in her

ear,



<P>her mind was in confusion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Harville, who had in truth been hearing none of it,



<P>now left his seat, and moved to a window, and Anne seeming to watch

him,



<P>though it was from thorough absence of mind, became gradually sensible



<P>that he was inviting her to join him where he stood.&nbsp; He looked

at her



<P>with a smile, and a little motion of the head, which expressed,



<P>"Come to me, I have something to say;" and the unaffected,



<P>easy kindness of manner which denoted the feelings of an older acquaintance



<P>than he really was, strongly enforced the invitation.&nbsp; She roused

herself



<P>and went to him.&nbsp; The window at which he stood was at the other

end



<P>of the room from where the two ladies were sitting, and though nearer



<P>to Captain Wentworth's table, not very near.&nbsp; As she joined him,



<P>Captain Harville's countenance re-assumed the serious, thoughtful



<P>expression which seemed its natural character.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Look here," said he, unfolding a parcel in his hand, and displaying



<P>a small miniature painting, "do you know who that is?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Certainly:&nbsp; Captain Benwick."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, and you may guess who it is for.&nbsp; But," (in a deep tone,)



<P>"it was not done for her.&nbsp; Miss Elliot, do you remember our



<P>walking together at Lyme, and grieving for him?&nbsp; I little thought

then--



<P>but no matter.&nbsp; This was drawn at the Cape.&nbsp; He met with



<P>a clever young German artist at the Cape, and in compliance with a promise



<P>to my poor sister, sat to him, and was bringing it home for her;



<P>and I have now the charge of getting it properly set for another!



<P>It was a commission to me!&nbsp; But who else was there to employ?



<P>I hope I can allow for him.&nbsp; I am not sorry, indeed, to make it



<P>over to another.&nbsp; He undertakes it;" (looking towards Captain Wentworth,)



<P>"he is writing about it now."&nbsp; And with a quivering lip he wound

up



<P>the whole by adding, "Poor Fanny! she would not have forgotten him so

soon!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No," replied Anne, in a low, feeling voice. "That I can easily believe."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It was not in her nature.&nbsp; She doted on him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It would not be the nature of any woman who truly loved."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Harville smiled, as much as to say, "Do you claim that



<P>for your sex?" and she answered the question, smiling also,



<P>"Yes.&nbsp; We certainly do not forget you as soon as you forget us.



<P>It is, perhaps, our fate rather than our merit.&nbsp; We cannot help

ourselves.



<P>We live at home, quiet, confined, and our feelings prey upon us.



<P>You are forced on exertion.&nbsp; You have always a profession, pursuits,



<P>business of some sort or other, to take you back into the world immediately,



<P>and continual occupation and change soon weaken impressions."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Granting your assertion that the world does all this so soon for men



<P>(which, however, I do not think I shall grant), it does not apply



<P>to Benwick.&nbsp; He has not been forced upon any exertion.&nbsp; The

peace



<P>turned him on shore at the very moment, and he has been living with

us,



<P>in our little family circle, ever since."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"True," said Anne, "very true; I did not recollect; but what shall



<P>we say now, Captain Harville?&nbsp; If the change be not from



<P>outward circumstances, it must be from within; it must be nature,



<P>man's nature, which has done the business for Captain Benwick."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, no, it is not man's nature.&nbsp; I will not allow it to be more



<P>man's nature than woman's to be inconstant and forget those they do

love,



<P>or have loved.&nbsp; I believe the reverse.&nbsp; I believe in a true

analogy



<P>between our bodily frames and our mental; and that as our bodies are



<P>the strongest, so are our feelings; capable of bearing most rough usage,



<P>and riding out the heaviest weather."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Your feelings may be the strongest," replied Anne, "but the same spirit



<P>of analogy will authorise me to assert that ours are the most tender.



<P>Man is more robust than woman, but he is not longer lived;



<P>which exactly explains my view of the nature of their attachments.



<P>Nay, it would be too hard upon you, if it were otherwise.



<P>You have difficulties, and privations, and dangers enough to struggle

with.



<P>You are always labouring and toiling, exposed to every risk and hardship.



<P>Your home, country, friends, all quitted.&nbsp; Neither time, nor health,



<P>nor life, to be called your own.&nbsp; It would be hard, indeed"



<P>(with a faltering voice), "if woman's feelings were to be



<P>added to all this."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"We shall never agree upon this question," Captain Harville



<P>was beginning to say, when a slight noise called their attention



<P>to Captain Wentworth's hitherto perfectly quiet division of the room.



<P>It was nothing more than that his pen had fallen down; but Anne was



<P>startled at finding him nearer than she had supposed, and half inclined



<P>to suspect that the pen had only fallen because he had been



<P>occupied by them, striving to catch sounds, which yet she did not think



<P>he could have caught.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Have you finished your letter?" said Captain Harville.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Not quite, a few lines more.&nbsp; I shall have done in five minutes."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"There is no hurry on my side.&nbsp; I am only ready whenever you are.



<P>I am in very good anchorage here," (smiling at Anne,) "well supplied,



<P>and want for nothing.&nbsp; No hurry for a signal at all.&nbsp; Well,

Miss Elliot,"



<P>(lowering his voice,) "as I was saying we shall never agree,



<P>I suppose, upon this point.&nbsp; No man and woman, would, probably.



<P>But let me observe that all histories are against you--all stories,



<P>prose and verse.&nbsp; If I had such a memory as Benwick, I could bring

you



<P>fifty quotations in a moment on my side the argument, and I do not think



<P>I ever opened a book in my life which had not something to say



<P>upon woman's inconstancy.&nbsp; Songs and proverbs, all talk



<P>of woman's fickleness.&nbsp; But perhaps you will say, these were all



<P>written by men."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Perhaps I shall.&nbsp; Yes, yes, if you please, no reference to examples



<P>in books.&nbsp; Men have had every advantage of us in telling their

own story.



<P>Education has been theirs in so much higher a degree; the pen has



<P>been in their hands.&nbsp; I will not allow books to prove anything."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But how shall we prove anything?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"We never shall.&nbsp; We never can expect to prove any thing upon such

a point.



<P>It is a difference of opinion which does not admit of proof.



<P>We each begin, probably, with a little bias towards our own sex;



<P>and upon that bias build every circumstance in favour of it



<P>which has occurred within our own circle; many of which circumstances



<P>(perhaps those very cases which strike us the most) may be precisely

such



<P>as cannot be brought forward without betraying a confidence,



<P>or in some respect saying what should not be said."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ah!" cried Captain Harville, in a tone of strong feeling,



<P>"if I could but make you comprehend what a man suffers when he takes



<P>a last look at his wife and children, and watches the boat



<P>that he has sent them off in, as long as it is in sight,



<P>and then turns away and says, `God knows whether we ever meet again!'



<P>And then, if I could convey to you the glow of his soul when he does



<P>see them again; when, coming back after a twelvemonth's absence,



<P>perhaps, and obliged to put into another port, he calculates how soon



<P>it be possible to get them there, pretending to deceive himself,



<P>and saying, `They cannot be here till such a day,' but all the while



<P>hoping for them twelve hours sooner, and seeing them arrive at last,



<P>as if Heaven had given them wings, by many hours sooner still!



<P>If I could explain to you all this, and all that a man can bear and

do,



<P>and glories to do, for the sake of these treasures of his existence!



<P>I speak, you know, only of such men as have hearts!" pressing his own



<P>with emotion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh!" cried Anne eagerly, "I hope I do justice to all that is felt by

you,



<P>and by those who resemble you.&nbsp; God forbid that I should undervalue



<P>the warm and faithful feelings of any of my fellow-creatures!



<P>I should deserve utter contempt if I dared to suppose that true attachment



<P>and constancy were known only by woman.&nbsp; No, I believe you capable



<P>of everything great and good in your married lives.&nbsp; I believe

you equal



<P>to every important exertion, and to every domestic forbearance,



<P>so long as--if I may be allowed the expression--so long as you have



<P>an object.&nbsp; I mean while the woman you love lives, and lives for

you.



<P>All the privilege I claim for my own sex (it is not a very enviable

one;



<P>you need not covet it), is that of loving longest, when existence



<P>or when hope is gone."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She could not immediately have uttered another sentence; her heart



<P>was too full, her breath too much oppressed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You are a good soul," cried Captain Harville, putting his hand



<P>on her arm, quite affectionately.&nbsp; "There is no quarreling with

you.



<P>And when I think of Benwick, my tongue is tied."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Their attention was called towards the others.&nbsp; Mrs Croft was taking

leave.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Here, Frederick, you and I part company, I believe," said she.



<P>"I am going home, and you have an engagement with your friend.



<P>To-night we may have the pleasure of all meeting again at your party,"



<P>(turning to Anne.)&nbsp; "We had your sister's card yesterday,



<P>and I understood Frederick had a card too, though I did not see it;



<P>and you are disengaged, Frederick, are you not, as well as ourselves?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Wentworth was folding up a letter in great haste, and either



<P>could not or would not answer fully.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes," said he, "very true; here we separate, but Harville and I



<P>shall soon be after you; that is, Harville, if you are ready,



<P>I am in half a minute.&nbsp; I know you will not be sorry to be off.



<P>I shall be at your service in half a minute."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Croft left them, and Captain Wentworth, having sealed his letter



<P>with great rapidity, was indeed ready, and had even a hurried,



<P>agitated air, which shewed impatience to be gone.&nbsp; Anne know not

how



<P>to understand it.&nbsp; She had the kindest "Good morning, God bless

you!"



<P>from Captain Harville, but from him not a word, nor a look!



<P>He had passed out of the room without a look!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She had only time, however, to move closer to the table where



<P>he had been writing, when footsteps were heard returning;



<P>the door opened, it was himself.&nbsp; He begged their pardon,



<P>but he had forgotten his gloves, and instantly crossing the room



<P>to the writing table, he drew out a letter from under the scattered

paper,



<P>placed it before Anne with eyes of glowing entreaty fixed on her



<P>for a time, and hastily collecting his gloves, was again out of the

room,



<P>almost before Mrs Musgrove was aware of his being in it:



<P>the work of an instant!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The revolution which one instant had made in Anne, was almost



<P>beyond expression.&nbsp; The letter, with a direction hardly legible,



<P>to "Miss A. E.--," was evidently the one which he had been folding



<P>so hastily.&nbsp; While supposed to be writing only to Captain Benwick,



<P>he had been also addressing her!&nbsp; On the contents of that letter



<P>depended all which this world could do for her.&nbsp; Anything was possible,



<P>anything might be defied rather than suspense.&nbsp; Mrs Musgrove had



<P>little arrangements of her own at her own table; to their protection



<P>she must trust, and sinking into the chair which he had occupied,



<P>succeeding to the very spot where he had leaned and written,



<P>her eyes devoured the following words:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I can listen no longer in silence.&nbsp; I must speak to you by such

means



<P>as are within my reach.&nbsp; You pierce my soul.&nbsp; I am half agony,



<P>half hope.&nbsp; Tell me not that I am too late, that such precious

feelings



<P>are gone for ever.&nbsp; I offer myself to you again with a heart



<P>even more your own than when you almost broke it, eight years



<P>and a half ago.&nbsp; Dare not say that man forgets sooner than&nbsp;

woman,



<P>that his love has an earlier death.&nbsp; I have loved none but you.



<P>Unjust I may have been, weak and resentful I have been,



<P>but never inconstant.&nbsp; You alone have brought me to Bath.



<P>For you alone, I think and plan.&nbsp; Have you not seen this?



<P>Can you fail to have understood my wishes?&nbsp; I had not waited even



<P>these ten days, could I have read your feelings, as I think you must

have



<P>penetrated mine.&nbsp; I can hardly write.&nbsp; I am every instant

hearing



<P>something which overpowers me.&nbsp; You sink your voice, but I can



<P>distinguish the tones of that voice when they would be lost on others.



<P>Too good, too excellent creature!&nbsp; You do us justice, indeed.



<P>You do believe that there is true attachment and constancy among men.



<P>Believe it to be most fervent, most undeviating, in F. W.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I must go, uncertain of my fate; but I shall return hither,



<P>or follow your party, as soon as possible.&nbsp; A word, a look,



<P>will be enough to decide whether I enter your father's house



<P>this evening or never."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Such a letter was not to be soon recovered from.&nbsp; Half and hour's

solitude



<P>and reflection might have tranquillized her; but the ten minutes only



<P>which now passed before she was interrupted, with all the restraints



<P>of her situation, could do nothing towards tranquillity.&nbsp; Every

moment



<P>rather brought fresh agitation.&nbsp; It was overpowering happiness.



<P>And before she was beyond the first stage of full sensation,



<P>Charles, Mary, and Henrietta all came in.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The absolute necessity of seeming like herself produced then



<P>an immediate struggle; but after a while she could do no more.



<P>She began not to understand a word they said, and was obliged



<P>to plead indisposition and excuse herself.&nbsp; They could then see



<P>that she looked very ill, were shocked and concerned, and would not



<P>stir without her for the world.&nbsp; This was dreadful.&nbsp; Would

they only



<P>have gone away, and left her in the quiet possession of that room



<P>it would have been her cure; but to have them all standing or



<P>waiting around her was distracting, and in desperation,



<P>she said she would go home.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"By all means, my dear," cried Mrs Musgrove, "go home directly,



<P>and take care of yourself, that you may be fit for the evening.



<P>I wish Sarah was here to doctor you, but I am no doctor myself.



<P>Charles, ring and order a chair.&nbsp; She must not walk."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the chair would never do.&nbsp; Worse than all!&nbsp; To lose the

possibility



<P>of speaking two words to Captain Wentworth in the course of her quiet,



<P>solitary progress up the town (and she felt almost certain of meeting

him)



<P>could not be borne.&nbsp; The chair was earnestly protested against,



<P>and Mrs Musgrove, who thought only of one sort of illness,



<P>having assured herself with some anxiety, that there had been no fall



<P>in the case; that Anne had not at any time lately slipped down,



<P>and got a blow on her head; that she was perfectly convinced of having



<P>had no fall; could part with her cheerfully, and depend on



<P>finding her better at night.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anxious to omit no possible precaution, Anne struggled, and said--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I am afraid, ma'am, that it is not perfectly understood.



<P>Pray be so good as to mention to the other gentlemen that we hope



<P>to see your whole party this evening.&nbsp; I am afraid there had been



<P>some mistake; and I wish you particularly to assure Captain Harville



<P>and Captain Wentworth, that we hope to see them both."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! my dear, it is quite understood, I give you my word.



<P>Captain Harville has no thought but of going."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Do you think so?&nbsp; But I am afraid; and I should be so very sorry.



<P>Will you promise me to mention it, when you see them again?



<P>You will see them both this morning, I dare say.&nbsp; Do promise me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"To be sure I will, if you wish it.&nbsp; Charles, if you see Captain

Harville



<P>anywhere, remember to give Miss Anne's message.&nbsp; But indeed, my

dear,



<P>you need not be uneasy.&nbsp; Captain Harville holds himself quite engaged,



<P>I'll answer for it; and Captain Wentworth the same, I dare say."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne could do no more; but her heart prophesied some mischance



<P>to damp the perfection of her felicity.&nbsp; It could not be very lasting,



<P>however.&nbsp; Even if he did not come to Camden Place himself,



<P>it would be in her power to send an intelligible sentence



<P>by Captain Harville.&nbsp; Another momentary vexation occurred.



<P>Charles, in his real concern and good nature, would go home with her;



<P>there was no preventing him.&nbsp; This was almost cruel.&nbsp; But

she could not



<P>be long ungrateful; he was sacrificing an engagement at a gunsmith's,



<P>to be of use to her; and she set off with him, with no feeling



<P>but gratitude apparent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They were on Union Street, when a quicker step behind, a something



<P>of familiar sound, gave her two moments' preparation for the sight



<P>of Captain Wentworth.&nbsp; He joined them; but, as if irresolute



<P>whether to join or to pass on, said nothing, only looked.



<P>Anne could command herself enough to receive that look,



<P>and not repulsively.&nbsp; The cheeks which had been pale now glowed,



<P>and the movements which had hesitated were decided.&nbsp; He walked

by her side.



<P>Presently, struck by a sudden thought, Charles said--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Captain Wentworth, which way are you going?&nbsp; Only to Gay Street,



<P>or farther up the town?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I hardly know," replied Captain Wentworth, surprised.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Are you going as high as Belmont?&nbsp; Are you going near Camden Place?



<P>Because, if you are, I shall have no scruple in asking you



<P>to take my place, and give Anne your arm to her father's door.



<P>She is rather done for this morning, and must not go so far without

help,



<P>and I ought to be at that fellow's in the Market Place.



<P>He promised me the sight of a capital gun he is just going to send off;



<P>said he would keep it unpacked to the last possible moment,



<P>that I might see it; and if I do not turn back now, I have no chance.



<P>By his description, a good deal like the second size double-barrel of

mine,



<P>which you shot with one day round Winthrop."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There could not be an objection.&nbsp; There could be only the most



<P>proper alacrity, a most obliging compliance for public view;



<P>and smiles reined in and spirits dancing in private rapture.



<P>In half a minute Charles was at the bottom of Union Street again,



<P>and the other two proceeding together:&nbsp; and soon words enough had

passed



<P>between them to decide their direction towards the comparatively quiet



<P>and retired gravel walk, where the power of conversation would make



<P>the present hour a blessing indeed, and prepare it for all



<P>the immortality which the happiest recollections of their own future

lives



<P>could bestow.&nbsp; There they exchanged again those feelings



<P>and those promises which had once before seemed to secure everything,



<P>but which had been followed by so many, many years of division



<P>and estrangement.&nbsp; There they returned again into the past,



<P>more exquisitely happy, perhaps, in their re-union, than when



<P>it had been first projected; more tender, more tried, more fixed



<P>in a knowledge of each other's character, truth, and attachment;



<P>more equal to act, more justified in acting.&nbsp; And there, as they

slowly



<P>paced the gradual ascent, heedless of every group around them,



<P>seeing neither sauntering politicians, bustling housekeepers,



<P>flirting girls, nor nursery-maids and children, they could indulge in



<P>those retrospections and acknowledgements, and especially in



<P>those explanations of what had directly preceded the present moment,



<P>which were so poignant and so ceaseless in interest.&nbsp; All the little



<P>variations of the last week were gone through; and of yesterday



<P>and today there could scarcely be an end.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She had not mistaken him.&nbsp; Jealousy of Mr Elliot had been



<P>the retarding weight, the doubt, the torment.&nbsp; That had begun to

operate



<P>in the very hour of first meeting her in Bath; that had returned,



<P>after a short suspension, to ruin the concert; and that had influenced

him



<P>in everything he had said and done, or omitted to say and do,



<P>in the last four-and-twenty hours.&nbsp; It had been gradually yielding



<P>to the better hopes which her looks, or words, or actions



<P>occasionally encouraged; it had been vanquished at last by



<P>those sentiments and those tones which had reached him while she talked



<P>with Captain Harville; and under the irresistible governance of which



<P>he had seized a sheet of paper, and poured out his feelings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Of what he had then written, nothing was to be retracted or qualified.



<P>He persisted in having loved none but her.&nbsp; She had never been

supplanted.



<P>He never even believed himself to see her equal.&nbsp; Thus much indeed



<P>he was obliged to acknowledge:&nbsp; that he had been constant unconsciously,



<P>nay unintentionally; that he had meant to forget her, and believed it



<P>to be done.&nbsp; He had imagined himself indifferent, when he had only



<P>been angry; and he had been unjust to her merits, because he had been



<P>a sufferer from them.&nbsp; Her character was now fixed on his mind



<P>as perfection itself, maintaining the loveliest medium of fortitude



<P>and gentleness; but he was obliged to acknowledge that only at Uppercross



<P>had he learnt to do her justice, and only at Lyme had he begun



<P>to understand himself.&nbsp; At Lyme, he had received lessons



<P>of more than one sort.&nbsp; The passing admiration of Mr Elliot



<P>had at least roused him, and the scenes on the Cobb and at



<P>Captain Harville's had fixed her superiority.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In his preceding attempts to attach himself to Louisa Musgrove



<P>(the attempts of angry pride), he protested that he had for ever



<P>felt it to be impossible; that he had not cared, could not care,



<P>for Louisa; though till that day, till the leisure for reflection



<P>which followed it, he had not understood the perfect excellence



<P>of the mind with which Louisa's could so ill bear a comparison,



<P>or the perfect unrivalled hold it possessed over his own.



<P>There, he had learnt to distinguish between the steadiness of principle



<P>and the obstinacy of self-will, between the darings of heedlessness



<P>and the resolution of a collected mind.&nbsp; There he had seen everything



<P>to exalt in his estimation the woman he had lost; and there begun



<P>to deplore the pride, the folly, the madness of resentment,



<P>which had kept him from trying to regain her when thrown in his way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>From that period his penance had become severe.&nbsp; He had no sooner



<P>been free from the horror and remorse attending the first few days



<P>of Louisa's accident, no sooner begun to feel himself alive again,



<P>than he had begun to feel himself, though alive, not at liberty.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I found," said he, "that I was considered by Harville an engaged man!



<P>That neither Harville nor his wife entertained a doubt of our



<P>mutual attachment.&nbsp; I was startled and shocked.&nbsp; To a degree,



<P>I could contradict this instantly; but, when I began to reflect



<P>that others might have felt the same--her own family, nay,



<P>perhaps herself--I was no longer at my own disposal.&nbsp; I was hers

in honour



<P>if she wished it.&nbsp; I had been unguarded.&nbsp; I had not thought

seriously



<P>on this subject before.&nbsp; I had not considered that my excessive

intimacy



<P>must have its danger of ill consequence in many ways; and that I had



<P>no right to be trying whether I could attach myself to either of the

girls,



<P>at the risk of raising even an unpleasant report, were there no other



<P>ill effects.&nbsp; I had been grossly wrong, and must abide the consequences."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He found too late, in short, that he had entangled himself;



<P>and that precisely as he became fully satisfied of his not caring



<P>for Louisa at all, he must regard himself as bound to her,



<P>if her sentiments for him were what the Harvilles supposed.



<P>It determined him to leave Lyme, and await her complete recovery elsewhere.



<P>He would gladly weaken, by any fair means, whatever feelings or



<P>speculations concerning him might exist; and he went, therefore,



<P>to his brother's, meaning after a while to return to Kellynch,



<P>and act as circumstances might require.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I was six weeks with Edward," said he, "and saw him happy.



<P>I could have no other pleasure.&nbsp; I deserved none.&nbsp; He enquired

after you



<P>very particularly; asked even if you were personally altered,



<P>little suspecting that to my eye you could never alter."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne smiled, and let it pass.&nbsp; It was too pleasing a blunder



<P>for a reproach.&nbsp; It is something for a woman to be assured,



<P>in her eight-and-twentieth year, that she has not lost one charm



<P>of earlier youth; but the value of such homage was inexpressibly increased



<P>to Anne, by comparing it with former words, and feeling it to be



<P>the result, not the cause of a revival of his warm attachment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had remained in Shropshire, lamenting the blindness of his own pride,



<P>and the blunders of his own calculations, till at once released from

Louisa



<P>by the astonishing and felicitous intelligence of her engagement



<P>with Benwick.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Here," said he, "ended the worst of my state; for now I could at least



<P>put myself in the way of happiness; I could exert myself;



<P>I could do something.&nbsp; But to be waiting so long in inaction,



<P>and waiting only for evil, had been dreadful.&nbsp; Within the first



<P>five minutes I said, `I will be at Bath on Wednesday,' and I was.



<P>Was it unpardonable to think it worth my while to come? and to arrive



<P>with some degree of hope?&nbsp; You were single.&nbsp; It was possible

that



<P>you might retain the feelings of the past, as I did; and one encouragement



<P>happened to be mine.&nbsp; I could never doubt that you would be loved

and



<P>sought by others, but I knew to a certainty that you had refused one

man,



<P>at least, of better pretensions than myself; and I could not help



<P>often saying, `Was this for me?'"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Their first meeting in Milsom Street afforded much to be said,



<P>but the concert still more.&nbsp; That evening seemed to be made up



<P>of exquisite moments.&nbsp; The moment of her stepping forward



<P>in the Octagon Room to speak to him:&nbsp; the moment of Mr Elliot's

appearing



<P>and tearing her away, and one or two subsequent moments,



<P>marked by returning hope or increasing despondency, were dwelt on



<P>with energy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"To see you," cried he, "in the midst of those who could not be



<P>my well-wishers; to see your cousin close by you, conversing and smiling,



<P>and feel all the horrible eligibilities and proprieties of the match!



<P>To consider it as the certain wish of every being who could hope



<P>to influence you!&nbsp; Even if your own feelings were reluctant or

indifferent,



<P>to consider what powerful supports would be his!&nbsp; Was it not enough



<P>to make the fool of me which I appeared?&nbsp; How could I look on



<P>without agony?&nbsp; Was not the very sight of the friend who sat behind

you,



<P>was not the recollection of what had been, the knowledge of her influence,



<P>the indelible, immoveable impression of what persuasion had once done--



<P>was it not all against me?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You should have distinguished," replied Anne.&nbsp; "You should not

have



<P>suspected me now; the case is so different, and my age is so different.



<P>If I was wrong in yielding to persuasion once, remember that



<P>it was to persuasion exerted on the side of safety, not of risk.



<P>When I yielded, I thought it was to duty, but no duty could be called



<P>in aid here.&nbsp; In marrying a man indifferent to me, all risk



<P>would have been incurred, and all duty violated."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Perhaps I ought to have reasoned thus," he replied, "but I could not.



<P>I could not derive benefit from the late knowledge I had acquired



<P>of your character.&nbsp; I could not bring it into play; it was overwhelmed,



<P>buried, lost in those earlier feelings which I had been smarting under



<P>year after year.&nbsp; I could think of you only as one who had yielded,



<P>who had given me up, who had been influenced by any one rather than

by me.



<P>I saw you with the very person who had guided you in that year of misery.



<P>I had no reason to believe her of less authority now.&nbsp; The force

of habit



<P>was to be added."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I should have thought," said Anne, "that my manner to yourself



<P>might have spared you much or all of this."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, no! your manner might be only the ease which your engagement



<P>to another man would give.&nbsp; I left you in this belief; and yet,



<P>I was determined to see you again.&nbsp; My spirits rallied with the

morning,



<P>and I felt that I had still a motive for remaining here."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At last Anne was at home again, and happier than any one in that house



<P>could have conceived.&nbsp; All the surprise and suspense, and every

other



<P>painful part of the morning dissipated by this conversation,



<P>she re-entered the house so happy as to be obliged to find an alloy



<P>in some momentary apprehensions of its being impossible to last.



<P>An interval of meditation, serious and grateful, was the best corrective



<P>of everything dangerous in such high-wrought felicity; and she went



<P>to her room, and grew steadfast and fearless in the thankfulness



<P>of her enjoyment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The evening came, the drawing-rooms were lighted up, the company assembled.

<BR>It was but a card party, it was but a mixture of those who had



<P>never met before, and those who met too often; a commonplace business,



<P>too numerous for intimacy, too small for variety; but Anne had never

found



<P>an evening shorter.&nbsp; Glowing and lovely in sensibility and happiness,



<P>and more generally admired than she thought about or cared for,



<P>she had cheerful or forbearing feelings for every creature around her.

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Elliot was there; she avoided, but she could pity him.

<BR>The Wallises, she had amusement in understanding them.&nbsp; Lady Dalrymple



<P>and Miss Carteret--they would soon be innoxious cousins to her.

<BR>She cared not for Mrs Clay, and had nothing to blush for in



<P>the public manners of her father and sister.&nbsp; With the Musgroves,



<P>there was the happy chat of perfect ease; with Captain Harville,



<P>the kind-hearted intercourse of brother and sister; with Lady Russell,



<P>attempts at conversation, which a delicious consciousness cut short;



<P>with Admiral and Mrs Croft, everything of peculiar cordiality and



<P>fervent interest, which the same consciousness sought to conceal;



<P>and with Captain Wentworth, some moments of communications



<P>continually occurring, and always the hope of more, and always



<P>the knowledge of his being there.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was in one of these short meetings, each apparently occupied



<P>in admiring a fine display of greenhouse plants, that she said--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I have been thinking over the past, and trying impartially



<P>to judge of the right and wrong, I mean with regard to myself;



<P>and I must believe that I was right, much as I suffered from it,



<P>that I was perfectly right in being guided by the friend whom



<P>you will love better than you do now.&nbsp; To me, she was in the place



<P>of a parent.&nbsp; Do not mistake me, however.&nbsp; I am not saying



<P>that she did not err in her advice.&nbsp; It was, perhaps, one of those

cases



<P>in which advice is good or bad only as the event decides;



<P>and for myself, I certainly never should, in any circumstance



<P>of tolerable similarity, give such advice.&nbsp; But I mean, that I

was right



<P>in submitting to her, and that if I had done otherwise, I should have



<P>suffered more in continuing the engagement than I did even in giving

it up,



<P>because I should have suffered in my conscience.&nbsp; I have now,



<P>as far as such a sentiment is allowable in human nature, nothing



<P>to reproach myself with; and if I mistake not, a strong sense of duty



<P>is no bad part of a woman's portion."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He looked at her, looked at Lady Russell, and looking again at her,



<P>replied, as if in cool deliberation--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Not yet.&nbsp; But there are hopes of her being forgiven in time.

<BR>I trust to being in charity with her soon.&nbsp; But I too have been



<P>thinking over the past, and a&nbsp; question has suggested itself,



<P>whether there may not have been one person more my enemy



<P>even than that lady?&nbsp; My own self.&nbsp; Tell me if, when I returned



<P>to England in the year eight, with a few thousand pounds,



<P>and was posted into the Laconia, if I had then written to you,



<P>would you have answered my letter?&nbsp; Would you, in short,



<P>have renewed the engagement then?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Would I!" was all her answer; but the accent was decisive enough.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Good God!" he cried, "you would!&nbsp; It is not that I did not think

of it,



<P>or desire it, as what could alone crown all my other success;



<P>but I was proud, too proud to ask again.&nbsp; I did not understand

you.

<BR>I shut my eyes, and would not understand you, or do you justice.

<BR>This is a recollection which ought to make me forgive every one



<P>sooner than myself.&nbsp; Six years of separation and suffering



<P>might have been spared.&nbsp; It is a sort of pain, too, which is new

to me.

<BR>I have been used to the gratification of believing myself to earn



<P>every blessing that I enjoyed.&nbsp; I have valued myself on honourable

toils



<P>and just rewards.&nbsp; Like other great men under reverses," he added,



<P>with a smile. "I must endeavour to subdue my mind to my fortune.

<BR>I must learn to brook being happier than I deserve."



<P>

<HTML>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 22</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 22</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Anne went home to think over all that she had heard.&nbsp; In one point,



<P>her feelings were relieved by this knowledge of Mr Elliot.



<P>There was no longer anything of tenderness due to him.&nbsp; He stood

as



<P>opposed to Captain Wentworth, in all his own unwelcome obtrusiveness;



<P>and the evil of his attentions last night, the irremediable mischief



<P>he might have done, was considered with sensations unqualified, unperplexed.



<P>Pity for him was all over.&nbsp; But this was the only point of relief.



<P>In every other respect, in looking around her, or penetrating forward,



<P>she saw more to distrust and to apprehend.&nbsp; She was concerned



<P>for the disappointment and pain Lady Russell would be feeling;



<P>for the mortifications which must be hanging over her father and sister,



<P>and had all the distress of foreseeing many evils, without knowing



<P>how to avert any one of them.&nbsp; She was most thankful for her own



<P>knowledge of him.&nbsp; She had never considered herself as entitled

to reward



<P>for not slighting an old friend like Mrs Smith, but here was



<P>a reward indeed springing from it!&nbsp; Mrs Smith had been able to

tell her



<P>what no one else could have done.&nbsp; Could the knowledge have



<P>been extended through her family?&nbsp; But this was a vain idea.



<P>She must talk to Lady Russell, tell her, consult with her,



<P>and having done her best, wait the event with as much composure



<P>as possible; and after all, her greatest want of composure would be



<P>in that quarter of the mind which could not be opened to Lady Russell;



<P>in that flow of anxieties and fears which must be all to herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She found, on reaching home, that she had, as she intended,



<P>escaped seeing Mr Elliot; that he had called and paid them



<P>a long morning visit; but hardly had she congratulated herself,



<P>and felt safe, when she heard that he was coming again in the evening.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I had not the smallest intention of asking him," said Elizabeth,



<P>with affected carelessness, "but he gave so many hints;



<P>so Mrs Clay says, at least."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Indeed, I do say it.&nbsp; I never saw anybody in my life spell harder



<P>for an invitation.&nbsp; Poor man!&nbsp; I was really in pain for him;



<P>for your hard-hearted sister, Miss Anne, seems bent on cruelty."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh!" cried Elizabeth, "I have been rather too much used to the game



<P>to be soon overcome by a gentleman's hints.&nbsp; However, when I found



<P>how excessively he was regretting that he should miss my father



<P>this morning, I gave way immediately, for I would never really omit



<P>an opportunity of bring him and Sir Walter together.&nbsp; They appear

to



<P>so much advantage in company with each other.&nbsp; Each behaving so

pleasantly.



<P>Mr Elliot looking up with so much respect."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Quite delightful!" cried Mrs Clay, not daring, however,



<P>to turn her eyes towards Anne.&nbsp; "Exactly like father and son!



<P>Dear Miss Elliot, may I not say father and son?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! I lay no embargo on any body's words.&nbsp; If you will have such



<P>ideas!&nbsp; But, upon my word, I am scarcely sensible of his attentions



<P>being beyond those of other men."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"My dear Miss Elliot!" exclaimed Mrs Clay, lifting her hands and eyes,



<P>and sinking all the rest of her astonishment in a convenient silence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, my dear Penelope, you need not be so alarmed about him.



<P>I did invite him, you know.&nbsp; I sent him away with smiles.



<P>When I found he was really going to his friends at Thornberry Park



<P>for the whole day to-morrow, I had compassion on him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne admired the good acting of the friend, in being able to shew



<P>such pleasure as she did, in the expectation and in the actual arrival



<P>of the very person whose presence must really be interfering with



<P>her prime object.&nbsp; It was impossible but that Mrs Clay must hate



<P>the sight of Mr Elliot; and yet she could assume a most obliging,



<P>placid look, and appear quite satisfied with the curtailed license



<P>of devoting herself only half as much to Sir Walter as she would have



<P>done otherwise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To Anne herself it was most distressing to see Mr Elliot enter the room;



<P>and quite painful to have him approach and speak to her.



<P>She had been used before to feel that he could not be always quite sincere,



<P>but now she saw insincerity in everything.&nbsp; His attentive deference



<P>to her father, contrasted with his former language, was odious;



<P>and when she thought of his cruel conduct towards Mrs Smith,



<P>she could hardly bear the sight of his present smiles and mildness,



<P>or the sound of his artificial good sentiments.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She meant to avoid any such alteration of manners as might provoke



<P>a remonstrance on his side.&nbsp; It was a great object to her to escape



<P>all enquiry or eclat; but it was her intention to be as decidedly cool



<P>to him as might be compatible with their relationship; and to retrace,



<P>as quietly as she could, the few steps of unnecessary intimacy she had



<P>been gradually led along.&nbsp; She was accordingly more guarded,



<P>and more cool, than she had been the night before.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He wanted to animate her curiosity again as to how and where



<P>he could have heard her formerly praised; wanted very much



<P>to be gratified by more solicitation; but the charm was broken:



<P>he found that the heat and animation of a public room was necessary



<P>to kindle his modest cousin's vanity; he found, at least, that it was



<P>not to be done now, by any of those attempts which he could hazard



<P>among the too-commanding claims of the others.&nbsp; He little surmised



<P>that it was a subject acting now exactly against his interest,



<P>bringing immediately to her thoughts all those parts of his conduct



<P>which were least excusable.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She had some satisfaction in finding that he was really going out of

Bath



<P>the next morning, going early, and that he would be gone the greater

part



<P>of two days.&nbsp; He was invited again to Camden Place the very evening

of



<P>his return; but from Thursday to Saturday evening his absence was certain.



<P>It was bad enough that a Mrs Clay should be always before her;



<P>but that a deeper hypocrite should be added to their party,



<P>seemed the destruction of everything like peace and comfort.



<P>It was so humiliating to reflect on the constant deception practiced



<P>on her father and Elizabeth; to consider the various sources



<P>of mortification preparing for them!&nbsp; Mrs Clay's selfishness was



<P>not so complicate nor so revolting as his; and Anne would have compounded



<P>for the marriage at once, with all its evils, to be clear of Mr Elliot's



<P>subtleties in endeavouring to prevent it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>On Friday morning she meant to go very early to Lady Russell,



<P>and accomplish the necessary communication; and she would have gone



<P>directly after breakfast, but that Mrs Clay was also going out



<P>on some obliging purpose of saving her sister trouble, which



<P>determined her to wait till she might be safe from such a companion.



<P>She saw Mrs Clay fairly off, therefore, before she began to talk



<P>of spending the morning in Rivers Street.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Very well," said Elizabeth, "I have nothing to send but my love.



<P>Oh! you may as well take back that tiresome book she would lend me,



<P>and pretend I have read it through.&nbsp; I really cannot be plaguing

myself



<P>for ever with all the new poems and states of the nation that come out.



<P>Lady Russell quite bores one with her new publications.



<P>You need not tell her so, but I thought her dress hideous the other

night.



<P>I used to think she had some taste in dress, but I was ashamed of her



<P>at the concert.&nbsp; Something so formal and arrange in her air!



<P>and she sits so upright!&nbsp; My best love, of course."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And mine," added Sir Walter.&nbsp; "Kindest regards.&nbsp; And you

may say,



<P>that I mean to call upon her soon.&nbsp; Make a civil message;



<P>but I shall only leave my card.&nbsp; Morning visits are never fair



<P>by women at her time of life, who make themselves up so little.



<P>If she would only wear rouge she would not be afraid of being seen;



<P>but last time I called, I observed the blinds were let down immediately."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>While her father spoke, there was a knock at the door.&nbsp; Who could

it be?



<P>Anne, remembering the preconcerted visits, at all hours, of Mr Elliot,



<P>would have expected him, but for his known engagement seven miles off.



<P>After the usual period of suspense, the usual sounds of approach were

heard,



<P>and "Mr and Mrs Charles Musgrove" were ushered into the room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Surprise was the strongest emotion raised by their appearance;



<P>but Anne was really glad to see them; and the others were not so sorry



<P>but that they could put on a decent air of welcome; and as soon



<P>as it became clear that these, their nearest relations, were not arrived



<P>with an views of accommodation in that house, Sir Walter and Elizabeth



<P>were able to rise in cordiality, and do the honours of it very well.



<P>They were come to Bath for a few days with Mrs Musgrove, and were



<P>at the White Hart.&nbsp; So much was pretty soon understood;



<P>but till Sir Walter and Elizabeth were walking Mary into



<P>the other drawing-room, and regaling themselves with her admiration,



<P>Anne could not draw upon Charles's brain for a regular history



<P>of their coming, or an explanation of some smiling hints



<P>of particular business, which had been ostentatiously dropped by Mary,



<P>as well as of some apparent confusion as to whom their party consisted

of.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She then found that it consisted of Mrs Musgrove, Henrietta,



<P>and Captain Harville, beside their two selves.&nbsp; He gave her a very

plain,



<P>intelligible account of the whole; a narration in which she saw



<P>a great deal of most characteristic proceeding.&nbsp; The scheme



<P>had received its first impulse by Captain Harville's wanting to



<P>come to Bath on business.&nbsp; He had begun to talk of it a week ago;



<P>and by way of doing something, as shooting was over, Charles had proposed



<P>coming with him, and Mrs Harville had seemed to like the idea of it



<P>very much, as an advantage to her husband; but Mary could not bear



<P>to be left, and had made herself so unhappy about it, that for a day

or two



<P>everything seemed to be in suspense, or at an end.&nbsp; But then,



<P>it had been taken up by his father and mother.&nbsp; His mother had



<P>some old friends in Bath whom she wanted to see; it was thought



<P>a good opportunity for Henrietta to come and buy wedding-clothes



<P>for herself and her sister; and, in short, it ended in being



<P>his mother's party, that everything might be comfortable and easy



<P>to Captain Harville; and he and Mary were included in it



<P>by way of general convenience.&nbsp; They had arrived late the night

before.



<P>Mrs Harville, her children, and Captain Benwick, remained with



<P>Mr Musgrove and Louisa at Uppercross.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne's only surprise was, that affairs should be in forwardness enough



<P>for Henrietta's wedding-clothes to be talked of.&nbsp; She had imagined



<P>such difficulties of fortune to exist there as must prevent



<P>the marriage from being near at hand; but she learned from Charles that,



<P>very recently, (since Mary's last letter to herself), Charles Hayter



<P>had been applied to by a friend to hold a living for a youth



<P>who could not possibly claim it under many years; and that



<P>on the strength of his present income, with almost a certainty



<P>of something more permanent long before the term in question,



<P>the two families had consented to the young people's wishes,



<P>and that their marriage was likely to take place in a few months,



<P>quite as soon as Louisa's.&nbsp; "And a very good living it was,"



<P>Charles added:&nbsp; "only five-and-twenty miles from Uppercross,



<P>and in a very fine country:&nbsp;&nbsp; fine part of Dorsetshire.



<P>In the centre of some of the best preserves in the kingdom,



<P>surrounded by three great proprietors, each more careful and jealous



<P>than the other; and to two of the three at least, Charles Hayter might

get



<P>a special recommendation.&nbsp; Not that he will value it as he ought,"



<P>he observed, "Charles is too cool about sporting. That's the worst of

him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I am extremely glad, indeed," cried Anne, "particularly glad



<P>that this should happen; and that of two sisters, who both deserve



<P>equally well, and who have always been such good friends,



<P>the pleasant prospect of one should not be dimming those of the other--



<P>that they should be so equal in their prosperity and comfort.



<P>I hope your father and mother are quite happy with regard to both."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! yes.&nbsp; My father would be well pleased if the gentlemen were

richer,



<P>but he has no other fault to find.&nbsp; Money, you know, coming down

with



<P>money--two daughters at once--it cannot be a very agreeable operation,



<P>and it streightens him as to many things.&nbsp; However, I do not mean

to say



<P>they have not a right to it.&nbsp; It is very fit they should have



<P>daughters' shares; and I am sure he has always been a very kind,



<P>liberal father to me.&nbsp; Mary does not above half like Henrietta's

match.



<P>She never did, you know.&nbsp; But she does not do him justice,



<P>nor think enough about Winthrop.&nbsp; I cannot make her attend to



<P>the value of the property.&nbsp; It is a very fair match, as times go;



<P>and I have liked Charles Hayter all my life, and I shall not leave off

now."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Such excellent parents as Mr and Mrs Musgrove," exclaimed Anne,



<P>"should be happy in their children's marriages.&nbsp; They do everything



<P>to confer happiness, I am sure.&nbsp; What a blessing to young people



<P>to be in such hands!&nbsp; Your father and mother seem so totally free



<P>from all those ambitious feelings which have led to so much misconduct



<P>and misery, both in young and old.&nbsp; I hope you think Louisa



<P>perfectly recovered now?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He answered rather hesitatingly, "Yes, I believe I do; very much recovered;



<P>but she is altered; there is no running or jumping about, no laughing



<P>or dancing; it is quite different.&nbsp; If one happens only to shut

the door



<P>a little hard, she starts and wriggles like a young dab-chick in the

water;



<P>and Benwick sits at her elbow, reading verses, or whispering to her,



<P>all day long."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne could not help laughing.&nbsp; "That cannot be much to your taste,



<P>I know," said she; "but I do believe him to be an excellent young man."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"To be sure he is.&nbsp; Nobody doubts it; and I hope you do not think



<P>I am so illiberal as to want every man to have the same objects and



<P>pleasures as myself.&nbsp; I have a great value for Benwick; and when

one can



<P>but get him to talk, he has plenty to say.&nbsp; His reading has done

him



<P>no harm, for he has fought as well as read.&nbsp; He is a brave fellow.



<P>I got more acquainted with him last Monday than ever I did before.



<P>We had a famous set-to at rat-hunting all the morning in



<P>my father's great barns; and he played his part so well



<P>that I have liked him the better ever since."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Here they were interrupted by the absolute necessity of Charles's



<P>following the others to admire mirrors and china; but Anne had



<P>heard enough to understand the present state of Uppercross,



<P>and rejoice in its happiness; and though she sighed as she rejoiced,



<P>her sigh had none of the ill-will of envy in it.&nbsp; She would certainly



<P>have risen to their blessings if she could, but she did not want



<P>to lessen theirs.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The visit passed off altogether in high good humour.&nbsp; Mary was



<P>in excellent spirits, enjoying the gaiety and the change,



<P>and so well satisfied with the journey in her mother-in-law's carriage



<P>with four horses, and with her own complete independence of Camden Place,



<P>that she was exactly in a temper to admire everything as she ought,



<P>and enter most readily into all the superiorities of the house,



<P>as they were detailed to her.&nbsp; She had no demands on her father

or sister,



<P>and her consequence was just enough increased by their handsome



<P>drawing-rooms.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Elizabeth was, for a short time, suffering a good deal.



<P>She felt that Mrs Musgrove and all her party ought to be asked



<P>to dine with them; but she could not bear to have the difference of

style,



<P>the reduction of servants, which a dinner must betray, witnessed by

those



<P>who had been always so inferior to the Elliots of Kellynch.



<P>It was a struggle between propriety and vanity; but vanity got the better,



<P>and then Elizabeth was happy again.&nbsp; These were her internal persuasions:



<P>"Old fashioned notions; country hospitality; we do not profess



<P>to give dinners; few people in Bath do; Lady Alicia never does;



<P>did not even ask her own sister's family, though they were here a month:



<P>and I dare say it would be very inconvenient to Mrs Musgrove;



<P>put her quite out of her way.&nbsp; I am sure she would rather not come;



<P>she cannot feel easy with us.&nbsp; I will ask them all for an evening;



<P>that will be much better; that will be a novelty and a treat.



<P>They have not seen two such drawing rooms before.&nbsp; They will be

delighted



<P>to come to-morrow evening.&nbsp; It shall be a regular party, small,



<P>but most elegant."&nbsp; And this satisfied Elizabeth:&nbsp; and when

the invitation



<P>was given to the two present, and promised for the absent,



<P>Mary was as completely satisfied.&nbsp; She was particularly asked



<P>to meet Mr Elliot, and be introduced to Lady Dalrymple and Miss Carteret,



<P>who were fortunately already engaged to come; and she could not



<P>have received a more gratifying attention.&nbsp; Miss Elliot was to

have



<P>the honour of calling on Mrs Musgrove in the course of the morning;



<P>and Anne walked off with Charles and Mary, to go and see her



<P>and Henrietta directly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Her plan of sitting with Lady Russell must give way for the present.



<P>They all three called in Rivers Street for a couple of minutes;



<P>but Anne convinced herself that a day's delay of the intended communication



<P>could be of no consequence, and hastened forward to the White Hart,



<P>to see again the friends and companions of the last autumn,



<P>with an eagerness of good-will which many associations contributed to

form.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They found Mrs Musgrove and her daughter within, and by themselves,



<P>and Anne had the kindest welcome from each.&nbsp; Henrietta was exactly



<P>in that state of recently-improved views, of fresh-formed happiness,



<P>which made her full of regard and interest for everybody she had



<P>ever liked before at all; and Mrs Musgrove's real affection had been

won



<P>by her usefulness when they were in distress.&nbsp; It was a heartiness,



<P>and a warmth, and a sincerity which Anne delighted in the more,



<P>from the sad want of such blessings at home.&nbsp; She was entreated



<P>to give them as much of her time as possible, invited for every day



<P>and all day long, or rather claimed as part of the family; and, in return,



<P>she naturally fell into all her wonted ways of attention and assistance,



<P>and on Charles's leaving them together, was listening to Mrs Musgrove's



<P>history of Louisa, and to Henrietta's of herself, giving opinions



<P>on business, and recommendations to shops; with intervals of every help



<P>which Mary required, from altering her ribbon to settling her accounts;



<P>from finding her keys, and assorting her trinkets, to trying



<P>to convince her that she was not ill-used by anybody; which Mary,



<P>well amused as she generally was, in her station at a window



<P>overlooking the entrance to the Pump Room, could not but have



<P>her moments of imagining.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A morning of thorough confusion was to be expected.&nbsp; A large party



<P>in an hotel ensured a quick-changing, unsettled scene.&nbsp; One five

minutes



<P>brought a note, the next a parcel; and Anne had not been there



<P>half an hour, when their dining-room, spacious as it was,



<P>seemed more than half filled:&nbsp; a party of steady old friends



<P>were seated around Mrs Musgrove, and Charles came back with



<P>Captains Harville and Wentworth.&nbsp; The appearance of the latter



<P>could not be more than the surprise of the moment.&nbsp; It was impossible



<P>for her to have forgotten to feel that this arrival of their



<P>common friends must be soon bringing them together again.



<P>Their last meeting had been most important in opening his feelings;



<P>she had derived from it a delightful conviction; but she feared



<P>from his looks, that the same unfortunate persuasion, which had



<P>hastened him away from the Concert Room, still governed.



<P>He did not seem to want to be near enough for conversation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She tried to be calm, and leave things to take their course,



<P>and tried to dwell much on this argument of rational dependence:--



<P>"Surely, if there be constant attachment on each side, our hearts



<P>must understand each other ere long.&nbsp; We are not boy and girl,



<P>to be captiously irritable, misled by every moment's inadvertence,



<P>and wantonly playing with our own happiness."&nbsp; And yet,



<P>a few minutes afterwards, she felt as if their being in company



<P>with each other, under their present circumstances, could only be



<P>exposing them to inadvertencies and misconstructions of the most



<P>mischievous kind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Anne," cried Mary, still at her window, "there is Mrs Clay,



<P>I am sure, standing under the colonnade, and a gentleman with her.



<P>I saw them turn the corner from Bath Street just now.&nbsp; They seemed



<P>deep in talk.&nbsp; Who is it?&nbsp; Come, and tell me.&nbsp; Good heavens!

I recollect.



<P>It is Mr Elliot himself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No," cried Anne, quickly, "it cannot be Mr Elliot, I assure you.



<P>He was to leave Bath at nine this morning, and does not come back



<P>till to-morrow."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As she spoke, she felt that Captain Wentworth was looking at her,



<P>the consciousness of which vexed and embarrassed her, and made her regret



<P>that she had said so much, simple as it was.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mary, resenting that she should be supposed not to know her own cousin,



<P>began talking very warmly about the family features, and protesting



<P>still more positively that it was Mr Elliot, calling again upon Anne



<P>to come and look for herself, but Anne did not mean to stir,



<P>and tried to be cool and unconcerned.&nbsp; Her distress returned,



<P>however, on perceiving smiles and intelligent glances pass between



<P>two or three of the lady visitors, as if they believed themselves



<P>quite in the secret.&nbsp; It was evident that the report concerning

her



<P>had spread, and a short pause succeeded, which seemed to ensure



<P>that it would now spread farther.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Do come, Anne" cried Mary, "come and look yourself.&nbsp; You will

be too late



<P>if you do not make haste.&nbsp; They are parting; they are shaking hands.



<P>He is turning away.&nbsp; Not know Mr Elliot, indeed!&nbsp; You seem

to have



<P>forgot all about Lyme."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To pacify Mary, and perhaps screen her own embarrassment,



<P>Anne did move quietly to the window.&nbsp; She was just in time to ascertain



<P>that it really was Mr Elliot, which she had never believed,



<P>before he disappeared on one side, as Mrs Clay walked quickly off



<P>on the other; and checking the surprise which she could not but feel



<P>at such an appearance of friendly conference between two persons



<P>of totally opposite interest, she calmly said, "Yes, it is Mr Elliot,



<P>certainly.&nbsp; He has changed his hour of going, I suppose, that is

all,



<P>or I may be mistaken, I might not attend;" and walked back to her chair,



<P>recomposed, and with the comfortable hope of having acquitted herself

well.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The visitors took their leave; and Charles, having civilly seen them

off,



<P>and then made a face at them, and abused them for coming, began with--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, mother, I have done something for you that you will like.



<P>I have been to the theatre, and secured a box for to-morrow night.



<P>A'n't I a good boy?&nbsp; I know you love a play; and there is room

for us all.



<P>It holds nine.&nbsp; I have engaged Captain Wentworth.&nbsp; Anne will



<P>not be sorry to join us, I am sure.&nbsp; We all like a play.



<P>Have not I done well, mother?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Musgrove was good humouredly beginning to express her perfect readiness



<P>for the play, if Henrietta and all the others liked it, when Mary



<P>eagerly interrupted her by exclaiming--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Good heavens, Charles! how can you think of such a thing?



<P>Take a box for to-morrow night!&nbsp; Have you forgot that we are engaged



<P>to Camden Place to-morrow night? and that we were most particularly

asked



<P>to meet Lady Dalrymple and her daughter, and Mr Elliot, and all



<P>the principal family connexions, on purpose to be introduced to them?



<P>How can you be so forgetful?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Phoo! phoo!" replied Charles, "what's an evening party?



<P>Never worth remembering.&nbsp; Your father might have asked us to dinner,



<P>I think, if he had wanted to see us.&nbsp; You may do as you like,



<P>but I shall go to the play."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! Charles, I declare it will be too abominable if you do,



<P>when you promised to go."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, I did not promise.&nbsp; I only smirked and bowed, and said the

word



<P>`happy.'&nbsp; There was no promise."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But you must go, Charles.&nbsp; It would be unpardonable to fail.



<P>We were asked on purpose to be introduced.&nbsp; There was always



<P>such a great connexion between the Dalrymples and ourselves.



<P>Nothing ever happened on either side that was not announced immediately.



<P>We are quite near relations, you know; and Mr Elliot too,



<P>whom you ought so particularly to be acquainted with!&nbsp; Every attention



<P>is due to Mr Elliot.&nbsp; Consider, my father's heir:&nbsp; the future



<P>representative of the family."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Don't talk to me about heirs and representatives," cried Charles.



<P>"I am not one of those who neglect the reigning power to bow



<P>to the rising sun.&nbsp; If I would not go for the sake of your father,



<P>I should think it scandalous to go for the sake of his heir.



<P>What is Mr Elliot to me?"&nbsp; The careless expression was life to

Anne,



<P>who saw that Captain Wentworth was all attention, looking and



<P>listening with his whole soul; and that the last words brought



<P>his enquiring eyes from Charles to herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Charles and Mary still talked on in the same style; he, half serious



<P>and half jesting, maintaining the scheme for the play, and she,



<P>invariably serious, most warmly opposing it, and not omitting



<P>to make it known that, however determined to go to Camden Place herself,



<P>she should not think herself very well used, if they went to the play



<P>without her.&nbsp; Mrs Musgrove interposed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"We had better put it off.&nbsp; Charles, you had much better go back



<P>and change the box for Tuesday.&nbsp; It would be a pity to be divided,



<P>and we should be losing Miss Anne, too, if there is a party at her father's;



<P>and I am sure neither Henrietta nor I should care at all for the play,



<P>if Miss Anne could not be with us."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne felt truly obliged to her for such kindness; and quite as much



<P>so for the opportunity it gave her of decidedly saying--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"If it depended only on my inclination, ma'am, the party at home



<P>(excepting on Mary's account) would not be the smallest impediment.



<P>I have no pleasure in the sort of meeting, and should be too happy



<P>to change it for a play, and with you.&nbsp; But, it had better



<P>not be attempted, perhaps."&nbsp; She had spoken it; but she trembled



<P>when it was done, conscious that her words were listened to,



<P>and daring not even to try to observe their effect.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was soon generally agreed that Tuesday should be the day;



<P>Charles only reserving the advantage of still teasing his wife,



<P>by persisting that he would go to the play to-morrow if nobody else

would.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Wentworth left his seat, and walked to the fire-place;



<P>probably for the sake of walking away from it soon afterwards,



<P>and taking a station, with less bare-faced design, by Anne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You have not been long enough in Bath," said he, "to enjoy



<P>the evening parties of the place."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! no.&nbsp; The usual character of them has nothing for me.



<P>I am no card-player."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You were not formerly, I know.&nbsp; You did not use to like cards;



<P>but time makes many changes."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I am not yet so much changed," cried Anne, and stopped, fearing she



<P>hardly knew what misconstruction.&nbsp; After waiting a few moments



<P>he said, and as if it were the result of immediate feeling,



<P>"It is a period, indeed!&nbsp; Eight years and a half is a period."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Whether he would have proceeded farther was left to Anne's imagination



<P>to ponder over in a calmer hour; for while still hearing the sounds



<P>he had uttered, she was startled to other subjects by Henrietta,



<P>eager to make use of the present leisure for getting out,



<P>and calling on her companions to lose no time, lest somebody else



<P>should come in.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They were obliged to move.&nbsp; Anne talked of being perfectly ready,



<P>and tried to look it; but she felt that could Henrietta have known



<P>the regret and reluctance of her heart in quitting that chair,



<P>in preparing to quit the room, she would have found, in all her own



<P>sensations for her cousin, in the very security of his affection,



<P>wherewith to pity her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Their preparations, however, were stopped short.&nbsp; Alarming sounds



<P>were heard; other visitors approached, and the door was thrown open



<P>for Sir Walter and Miss Elliot, whose entrance seemed to give



<P>a general chill.&nbsp; Anne felt an instant oppression, and wherever

she looked



<P>saw symptoms of the same.&nbsp; The comfort, the freedom, the gaiety



<P>of the room was over, hushed into cold composure, determined silence,



<P>or insipid talk, to meet the heartless elegance of her father and sister.



<P>How mortifying to feel that it was so!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Her jealous eye was satisfied in one particular.&nbsp; Captain Wentworth



<P>was acknowledged again by each, by Elizabeth more graciously than before.



<P>She even addressed him once, and looked at him more than once.



<P>Elizabeth was, in fact, revolving a great measure.&nbsp; The sequel



<P>explained it.&nbsp; After the waste of a few minutes in saying



<P>the proper nothings, she began to give the invitation which



<P>was to comprise all the remaining dues of the Musgroves.



<P>"To-morrow evening, to meet a few friends:&nbsp; no formal party."



<P>It was all said very gracefully, and the cards with which she had



<P>provided herself, the "Miss Elliot at home," were laid on the table,



<P>with a courteous, comprehensive smile to all, and one smile and



<P>one card more decidedly for Captain Wentworth.&nbsp; The truth was,



<P>that Elizabeth had been long enough in Bath to understand



<P>the importance of a man of such an air and appearance as his.



<P>The past was nothing.&nbsp; The present was that Captain Wentworth



<P>would move about well in her drawing-room.&nbsp; The card was pointedly

given,



<P>and Sir Walter and Elizabeth arose and disappeared.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The interruption had been short, though severe, and ease and animation



<P>returned to most of those they left as the door shut them out,



<P>but not to Anne.&nbsp; She could think only of the invitation she had



<P>with such astonishment witnessed, and of the manner in which



<P>it had been received; a manner of doubtful meaning, of surprise rather



<P>than gratification, of polite acknowledgement rather than acceptance.



<P>She knew him; she saw disdain in his eye, and could not venture to believe



<P>that he had determined to accept such an offering, as an atonement



<P>for all the insolence of the past.&nbsp; Her spirits sank.&nbsp; He

held the card



<P>in his hand after they were gone, as if deeply considering it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Only think of Elizabeth's including everybody!" whispered Mary



<P>very audibly.&nbsp; "I do not wonder Captain Wentworth is delighted!



<P>You see he cannot put the card out of his hand."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne caught his eye, saw his cheeks glow, and his mouth form itself



<P>into a momentary expression of contempt, and turned away,



<P>that she might neither see nor hear more to vex her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The party separated.&nbsp; The gentlemen had their own pursuits,



<P>the ladies proceeded on their own business, and they met no more while



<P>Anne belonged to them.&nbsp; She was earnestly begged to return and

dine,



<P>and give them all the rest of the day, but her spirits had been



<P>so long exerted that at present she felt unequal to more,



<P>and fit only for home, where she might be sure of being as silent



<P>as she chose.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Promising to be with them the whole of the following morning, therefore,



<P>she closed the fatigues of the present by a toilsome walk to Camden

Place,



<P>there to spend the evening chiefly in listening to the busy arrangements



<P>of Elizabeth and Mrs Clay for the morrow's party, the frequent enumeration



<P>of the persons invited, and the continually improving detail of all



<P>the embellishments which were to make it the most completely elegant



<P>of its kind in Bath, while harassing herself with the never-ending



<P>question, of whether Captain Wentworth would come or not?&nbsp; They

were
