<P>reckoning him as certain, but with her it was a gnawing solicitude



<P>never appeased for five minutes together.&nbsp; She generally thought



<P>he would come, because she generally thought he ought; but it was a

case



<P>which she could not so shape into any positive act of duty or discretion,



<P>as inevitably to defy the suggestions of very opposite feelings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She only roused herself from the broodings of this restless agitation,



<P>to let Mrs Clay know that she had been seen with Mr Elliot



<P>three hours after his being supposed to be out of Bath,



<P>for having watched in vain for some intimation of the interview



<P>from the lady herself, she determined to mention it, and it seemed to

her



<P>there was guilt in Mrs Clay's face as she listened.&nbsp; It was transient:



<P>cleared away in an instant; but Anne could imagine she read there



<P>the consciousness of having, by some complication of mutual trick,



<P>or some overbearing authority of his, been obliged to attend



<P>(perhaps for half an hour) to his lectures and restrictions on her designs



<P>on Sir Walter.&nbsp; She exclaimed, however, with a very tolerable



<P>imitation of nature: --

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! dear! very true.&nbsp; Only think, Miss Elliot, to my great surprise



<P>I met with Mr Elliot in Bath Street.&nbsp; I was never more astonished.



<P>He turned back and walked with me to the Pump Yard.&nbsp; He had been

prevented



<P>setting off for Thornberry, but I really forget by what;



<P>for I was in a hurry, and could not much attend, and I can only answer



<P>for his being determined not to be delayed in his return.



<P>He wanted to know how early he might be admitted to-morrow.



<P>He was full of `to-morrow,' and it is very evident that I have been



<P>full of it too, ever since I entered the house, and learnt the extension



<P>of your plan and all that had happened, or my seeing him could never

have



<P>gone so entirely out of my head."



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 20</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 20</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Sir Walter, his two daughters, and Mrs Clay, were the earliest



<P>of all their party at the rooms in the evening; and as Lady Dalrymple



<P>must be waited for, they took their station by one of the fires



<P>in the Octagon Room.&nbsp; But hardly were they so settled, when the

door



<P>opened again, and Captain Wentworth walked in alone.&nbsp; Anne was



<P>the nearest to him, and making yet a little advance, she instantly spoke.



<P>He was preparing only to bow and pass on, but her gentle "How do you

do?"



<P>brought him out of the straight line to stand near her, and make enquiries



<P>in return, in spite of the formidable father and sister in the back

ground.



<P>Their being in the back ground was a support to Anne; she knew nothing



<P>of their looks, and felt equal to everything which she believed



<P>right to be done.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>While they were speaking, a whispering between her father and Elizabeth



<P>caught her ear.&nbsp; She could not distinguish, but she must guess

the subject;



<P>and on Captain Wentworth's making a distant bow, she comprehended



<P>that her father had judged so well as to give him that



<P>simple acknowledgement of acquaintance, and she was just in time



<P>by a side glance to see a slight curtsey from Elizabeth herself.



<P>This, though late, and reluctant, and ungracious, was yet



<P>better than nothing, and her spirits improved.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After talking, however, of the weather, and Bath, and the concert,



<P>their conversation began to flag, and so little was said at last,



<P>that she was expecting him to go every moment, but he did not;



<P>he seemed in no hurry to leave her; and presently with renewed spirit,



<P>with a little smile, a little glow, he said--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I have hardly seen you since our day at Lyme.&nbsp; I am afraid you

must have



<P>suffered from the shock, and the more from its not overpowering you



<P>at the time."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She assured him that she had not.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It was a frightful hour," said he, "a frightful day!" and he



<P>passed his hand across his eyes, as if the remembrance were still



<P>too painful, but in a moment, half smiling again, added,



<P>"The day has produced some effects however; has had some consequences



<P>which must be considered as the very reverse of frightful.



<P>When you had the presence of mind to suggest that Benwick would be



<P>the properest person to fetch a surgeon, you could have little idea



<P>of his being eventually one of those most concerned in her recovery."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Certainly I could have none.&nbsp; But it appears--I should hope it

would be



<P>a very happy match.&nbsp; There are on both sides good principles



<P>and good temper."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes," said he, looking not exactly forward; "but there, I think,



<P>ends the resemblance.&nbsp; With all my soul I wish them happy, and

rejoice



<P>over every circumstance in favour of it.&nbsp; They have no difficulties



<P>to contend with at home, no opposition, no caprice, no delays.



<P>The Musgroves are behaving like themselves, most honourably and kindly,



<P>only anxious with true parental hearts to promote their daughter's comfort.



<P>All this is much, very much in favour of their happiness;



<P>more than perhaps--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He stopped.&nbsp; A sudden recollection seemed to occur, and to give

him



<P>some taste of that emotion which was reddening Anne's cheeks



<P>and fixing her eyes on the ground.&nbsp; After clearing his throat,

however,



<P>he proceeded thus--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I confess that I do think there is a disparity, too great a disparity,



<P>and in a point no less essential than mind.&nbsp; I regard Louisa Musgrove



<P>as a very amiable, sweet-tempered girl, and not deficient in understanding,



<P>but Benwick is something more.&nbsp; He is a clever man, a reading man;



<P>and I confess, that I do consider his attaching himself to her



<P>with some surprise.&nbsp; Had it been the effect of gratitude,



<P>had he learnt to love her, because he believed her to be preferring

him,



<P>it would have been another thing.&nbsp; But I have no reason to suppose

it so.



<P>It seems, on the contrary, to have been a perfectly spontaneous,



<P>untaught feeling on his side, and this surprises me.&nbsp; A man like

him,



<P>in his situation! with a heart pierced, wounded, almost broken!



<P>Fanny Harville was a very superior creature, and his attachment to her



<P>was indeed attachment.&nbsp; A man does not recover from such



<P>a devotion of the heart to such a woman.&nbsp; He ought not; he does

not."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Either from the consciousness, however, that his friend had recovered,



<P>or from other consciousness, he went no farther; and Anne who,



<P>in spite of the agitated voice in which the latter part had been uttered,



<P>and in spite of all the various noises of the room, the almost ceaseless



<P>slam of the door, and ceaseless buzz of persons walking through,



<P>had distinguished every word, was struck, gratified, confused,



<P>and beginning to breathe very quick, and feel an hundred things



<P>in a moment.&nbsp; It was impossible for her to enter on such a subject;



<P>and yet, after a pause, feeling the necessity of speaking,



<P>and having not the smallest wish for a total change, she only deviated



<P>so far as to say--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You were a good while at Lyme, I think?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"About a fortnight.&nbsp; I could not leave it till Louisa's doing well



<P>was quite ascertained.&nbsp; I had been too deeply concerned in the

mischief



<P>to be soon at peace.&nbsp; It had been my doing, solely mine.



<P>She would not have been obstinate if I had not been weak.



<P>The country round Lyme is very fine.&nbsp; I walked and rode a great

deal;



<P>and the more I saw, the more I found to admire."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I should very much like to see Lyme again," said Anne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Indeed!&nbsp; I should not have supposed that you could have found



<P>anything in Lyme to inspire such a feeling.&nbsp; The horror and distress



<P>you were involved in, the stretch of mind, the wear of spirits!



<P>I should have thought your last impressions of Lyme must have been



<P>strong disgust."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"The last hours were certainly very painful," replied Anne;



<P>"but when pain is over, the remembrance of it often becomes a pleasure.



<P>One does not love a place the less for having suffered in it,



<P>unless it has been all suffering, nothing but suffering, which was



<P>by no means the case at Lyme.&nbsp; We were only in anxiety and distress



<P>during the last two hours, and previously there had been a great deal



<P>of enjoyment.&nbsp; So much novelty and beauty! I have travelled so

little,



<P>that every fresh place would be interesting to me; but there is real

beauty



<P>at Lyme; and in short" (with a faint blush at some recollections),



<P>"altogether my impressions of the place are very agreeable."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As she ceased, the entrance door opened again, and the very party appeared



<P>for whom they were waiting.&nbsp; "Lady Dalrymple, Lady Dalrymple,"



<P>was the rejoicing sound; and with all the eagerness compatible



<P>with anxious elegance, Sir Walter and his two ladies stepped forward



<P>to meet her.&nbsp; Lady Dalrymple and Miss Carteret, escorted by Mr

Elliot



<P>and Colonel Wallis, who had happened to arrive nearly at the same instant,



<P>advanced into the room.&nbsp; The others joined them, and it was



<P>a group in which Anne found herself also necessarily included.



<P>She was divided from Captain Wentworth.&nbsp; Their interesting,



<P>almost too interesting conversation must be broken up for a time,



<P>but slight was the penance compared with the happiness which brought

it on!



<P>She had learnt, in the last ten minutes, more of his feelings



<P>towards Louisa, more of all his feelings than she dared to think of;



<P>and she gave herself up to the demands of the party, to the needful



<P>civilities of the moment, with exquisite, though agitated sensations.



<P>She was in good humour with all.&nbsp; She had received ideas which



<P>disposed her to be courteous and kind to all, and to pity every one,



<P>as being less happy than herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The delightful emotions were a little subdued, when on stepping back



<P>from the group, to be joined again by Captain Wentworth, she saw



<P>that he was gone.&nbsp; She was just in time to see him turn into



<P>the Concert Room.&nbsp; He was gone; he had disappeared, she felt



<P>a moment's regret.&nbsp; But "they should meet again.&nbsp; He would

look for her,



<P>he would find her out before the evening were over, and at present,



<P>perhaps, it was as well to be asunder.&nbsp; She was in need of



<P>a little interval for recollection."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Upon Lady Russell's appearance soon afterwards, the whole party



<P>was collected, and all that remained was to marshal themselves,



<P>and proceed into the Concert Room; and be of all the consequence



<P>in their power, draw as many eyes, excite as many whispers,



<P>and disturb as many people as they could.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Very, very happy were both Elizabeth and Anne Elliot as they walked

in.



<P>Elizabeth arm in arm with Miss Carteret, and looking on the broad back



<P>of the dowager Viscountess Dalrymple before her, had nothing to wish

for



<P>which did not seem within her reach; and Anne--but it would be



<P>an insult to the nature of Anne's felicity, to draw any comparison



<P>between it and her sister's; the origin of one all selfish vanity,



<P>of the other all generous attachment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne saw nothing, thought nothing of the brilliancy of the room.



<P>Her happiness was from within.&nbsp; Her eyes were bright and her cheeks

glowed;



<P>but she knew nothing about it.&nbsp; She was thinking only of



<P>the last half hour, and as they passed to their seats, her mind took



<P>a hasty range over it.&nbsp; His choice of subjects, his expressions,



<P>and still more his manner and look, had been such as she could see



<P>in only one light.&nbsp; His opinion of Louisa Musgrove's inferiority,



<P>an opinion which he had seemed solicitous to give, his wonder



<P>at Captain Benwick, his feelings as to a first, strong attachment;



<P>sentences begun which he could not finish, his half averted eyes



<P>and more than half expressive glance, all, all declared that he had



<P>a heart returning to her at least; that anger, resentment, avoidance,



<P>were no more; and that they were succeeded, not merely by friendship



<P>and regard, but by the tenderness of the past.&nbsp; Yes, some share

of



<P>the tenderness of the past.&nbsp; She could not contemplate the change



<P>as implying less.&nbsp; He must love her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>These were thoughts, with their attendant visions, which occupied



<P>and flurried her too much to leave her any power of observation;



<P>and she passed along the room without having a glimpse of him,



<P>without even trying to discern him.&nbsp; When their places were determined

on,



<P>and they were all properly arranged, she looked round to see



<P>if he should happen to be in the same part of the room, but he was not;



<P>her eye could not reach him; and the concert being just opening,



<P>she must consent for a time to be happy in a humbler way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The party was divided and disposed of on two contiguous benches:



<P>Anne was among those on the foremost, and Mr Elliot had manoeuvred so

well,



<P>with the assistance of his friend Colonel Wallis, as to have a seat

by her.



<P>Miss Elliot, surrounded by her cousins, and the principal object



<P>of Colonel Wallis's gallantry, was quite contented.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne's mind was in a most favourable state for the entertainment



<P>of the evening; it was just occupation enough:&nbsp; she had feelings

for



<P>the tender, spirits for the gay, attention for the scientific,



<P>and patience for the wearisome; and had never liked a concert better,



<P>at least during the first act.&nbsp; Towards the close of it,



<P>in the interval succeeding an Italian song, she explained



<P>the words of the song to Mr Elliot.&nbsp; They had a concert bill between

them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"This," said she, "is nearly the sense, or rather the meaning of the

words,



<P>for certainly the sense of an Italian love-song must not be talked of,



<P>but it is as nearly the meaning as I can give; for I do not pretend



<P>to understand the language.&nbsp; I am a very poor Italian scholar."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, yes, I see you are.&nbsp; I see you know nothing of the matter.



<P>You have only knowledge enough of the language to translate at sight



<P>these inverted, transposed, curtailed Italian lines, into clear,



<P>comprehensible, elegant English.&nbsp; You need not say anything more



<P>of your ignorance.&nbsp; Here is complete proof."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I will not oppose such kind politeness; but I should be sorry to be



<P>examined by a real proficient."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I have not had the pleasure of visiting in Camden Place so long,"



<P>replied he, "without knowing something of Miss Anne Elliot;



<P>and I do regard her as one who is too modest for the world in general



<P>to be aware of half her accomplishments, and too highly accomplished



<P>for modesty to be natural in any other woman."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"For shame! for shame! this is too much flattery.&nbsp; I forget what

we are



<P>to have next," turning to the bill.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Perhaps," said Mr Elliot, speaking low, "I have had a longer acquaintance



<P>with your character than you are aware of."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Indeed!&nbsp; How so?&nbsp; You can have been acquainted with it only

since



<P>I came to Bath, excepting as you might hear me previously spoken of



<P>in my own family."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I knew you by report long before you came to Bath.&nbsp; I had heard

you



<P>described by those who knew you intimately.&nbsp; I have been acquainted



<P>with you by character many years.&nbsp; Your person, your disposition,



<P>accomplishments, manner; they were all present to me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Elliot was not disappointed in the interest he hoped to raise.



<P>No one can withstand the charm of such a mystery.&nbsp; To have been



<P>described long ago to a recent acquaintance, by nameless people,



<P>is irresistible; and Anne was all curiosity.&nbsp; She wondered,



<P>and questioned him eagerly; but in vain.&nbsp; He delighted in being

asked,



<P>but he would not tell.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, no, some time or other, perhaps, but not now.&nbsp; He would mention



<P>no names now; but such, he could assure her, had been the fact.



<P>He had many years ago received such a description of Miss Anne Elliot



<P>as had inspired him with the highest idea of her merit, and excited



<P>the warmest curiosity to know her."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne could think of no one so likely to have spoken with



<P>partiality of her many years ago as the Mr Wentworth of Monkford,



<P>Captain Wentworth's brother.&nbsp; He might have been in Mr Elliot's

company,



<P>but she had not courage to ask the question.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"The name of Anne Elliot," said he, "has long had an interesting sound

to me.



<P>Very long has it possessed a charm over my fancy; and, if I dared,



<P>I would breathe my wishes that the name might never change."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Such, she believed, were his words; but scarcely had she



<P>received their sound, than her attention was caught by other sounds



<P>immediately behind her, which rendered every thing else trivial.



<P>Her father and Lady Dalrymple were speaking.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"A well-looking man," said Sir Walter, "a very well-looking man."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"A very fine young man indeed!" said Lady Dalrymple.&nbsp; "More air



<P>than one often sees in Bath.&nbsp; Irish, I dare say."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, I just know his name.&nbsp; A bowing acquaintance.&nbsp; Wentworth;



<P>Captain Wentworth of the navy.&nbsp; His sister married my tenant



<P>in Somersetshire, the Croft, who rents Kellynch."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Before Sir Walter had reached this point, Anne's eyes had caught



<P>the right direction, and distinguished Captain Wentworth standing



<P>among a cluster of men at a little distance.&nbsp; As her eyes fell

on him,



<P>his seemed to be withdrawn from her.&nbsp; It had that appearance.



<P>It seemed as if she had been one moment too late; and as long as she



<P>dared observe, he did not look again:&nbsp; but the performance



<P>was recommencing, and she was forced to seem to restore her attention



<P>to the orchestra and look straight forward.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When she could give another glance, he had moved away.&nbsp; He could

not have



<P>come nearer to her if he would; she was so surrounded and shut in:



<P>but she would rather have caught his eye.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Elliot's speech, too, distressed her.&nbsp; She had no longer



<P>any inclination to talk to him.&nbsp; She wished him not so near her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The first act was over.&nbsp; Now she hoped for some beneficial change;



<P>and, after a period of nothing-saying amongst the party, some of them



<P>did decide on going in quest of tea.&nbsp; Anne was one of the few who



<P>did not choose to move.&nbsp; She remained in her seat, and so did Lady

Russell;



<P>but she had the pleasure of getting rid of Mr Elliot; and she did not

mean,



<P>whatever she might feel on Lady Russell's account, to shrink from



<P>conversation with Captain Wentworth, if he gave her the opportunity.



<P>She was persuaded by Lady Russell's countenance that she had seen him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He did not come however.&nbsp; Anne sometimes fancied she discerned

him



<P>at a distance, but he never came.&nbsp; The anxious interval



<P>wore away unproductively.&nbsp; The others returned, the room filled

again,



<P>benches were reclaimed and repossessed, and another hour of pleasure



<P>or of penance was to be sat out, another hour of music was to give



<P>delight or the gapes, as real or affected taste for it prevailed.



<P>To Anne, it chiefly wore the prospect of an hour of agitation.



<P>She could not quit that room in peace without seeing Captain Wentworth



<P>once more, without the interchange of one friendly look.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In re-settling themselves there were now many changes, the result of

which



<P>was favourable for her.&nbsp; Colonel Wallis declined sitting down again,



<P>and Mr Elliot was invited by Elizabeth and Miss Carteret, in a manner



<P>not to be refused, to sit between them; and by some other removals,



<P>and a little scheming of her own,&nbsp; Anne was enabled to place herself



<P>much nearer the end of the bench than she had been before,



<P>much more within reach of a passer-by.&nbsp; She could not do so,



<P>without comparing herself with Miss Larolles, the inimitable Miss Larolles;



<P>but still she did it, and not with much happier effect;



<P>though by what seemed prosperity in the shape of an early abdication



<P>in her next neighbours, she found herself at the very end of the bench



<P>before the concert closed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Such was her situation, with a vacant space at hand, when Captain Wentworth



<P>was again in sight.&nbsp; She saw him not far off.&nbsp; He saw her

too;



<P>yet he looked grave, and seemed irresolute, and only by very slow degrees



<P>came at last near enough to speak to her.&nbsp; She felt that something



<P>must be the matter.&nbsp; The change was indubitable.&nbsp; The difference



<P>between his present air and what it had been in the Octagon Room



<P>was strikingly great.&nbsp; Why was it?&nbsp; She thought of her father,



<P>of Lady Russell.&nbsp; Could there have been any unpleasant glances?



<P>He began by speaking of the concert gravely, more like the Captain



<P>Wentworth of Uppercross; owned himself disappointed, had expected singing;



<P>and in short, must confess that he should not be sorry when it was over.



<P>Anne replied, and spoke in defense of the performance so well,



<P>and yet in allowance for his feelings so pleasantly, that his countenance



<P>improved, and he replied again with almost a smile.&nbsp; They talked



<P>for a few minutes more; the improvement held; he even looked down



<P>towards the bench, as if he saw a place on it well worth occupying;



<P>when at that moment a touch on her shoulder obliged Anne to turn round.



<P>It came from Mr Elliot.&nbsp; He begged her pardon, but she must be

applied to,



<P>to explain Italian again.&nbsp; Miss Carteret was very anxious to have



<P>a general idea of what was next to be sung.&nbsp; Anne could not refuse;



<P>but never had she sacrificed to politeness with a more suffering spirit.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A few minutes, though as few as possible, were inevitably consumed;



<P>and when her own mistress again, when able to turn and look



<P>as she had done before, she found herself accosted by Captain Wentworth,



<P>in a reserved yet hurried sort of farewell.&nbsp; "He must wish her

good night;



<P>he was going; he should get home as fast as he could."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Is not this song worth staying for?" said Anne, suddenly struck



<P>by an idea which made her yet more anxious to be encouraging.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No!" he replied impressively, "there is nothing worth my staying for;"



<P>and he was gone directly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Jealousy of Mr Elliot!&nbsp; It was the only intelligible motive.



<P>Captain Wentworth jealous of her affection!&nbsp; Could she have believed

it



<P>a week ago; three hours ago!&nbsp; For a moment the gratification was

exquisite.



<P>But, alas! there were very different thoughts to succeed.



<P>How was such jealousy to be quieted?&nbsp; How was the truth to reach

him?



<P>How, in all the peculiar disadvantages of their respective situations,



<P>would he ever learn of her real sentiments?&nbsp; It was misery to think



<P>of Mr Elliot's attentions.&nbsp; Their evil was incalculable.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 08</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 8</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>From this time Captain Wentworth and Anne Elliot were repeatedly



<P>in the same circle.&nbsp; They were soon dining in company together



<P>at Mr Musgrove's, for the little boy's state could no longer



<P>supply his aunt with a pretence for absenting herself; and this was



<P>but the beginning of other dinings and other meetings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Whether former feelings were to be renewed must be brought to the proof;



<P>former times must undoubtedly be brought to the recollection of each;



<P>they could not but be reverted to; the year of their engagement



<P>could not but be named by him, in the little narratives or descriptions



<P>which conversation called forth.&nbsp; His profession qualified him,



<P>his disposition lead him, to talk; and "That was in the year six;"



<P>"That happened before I went to sea in the year six," occurred



<P>in the course of the first evening they spent together:



<P>and though his voice did not falter, and though she had no reason



<P>to suppose his eye wandering towards her while he spoke,



<P>Anne felt the utter impossibility, from her knowledge of his mind,



<P>that he could be unvisited by remembrance any more than herself.



<P>There must be the same immediate association of thought,



<P>though she was very far from conceiving it to be of equal pain.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They had no conversation together, no intercourse but what



<P>the commonest civility required.&nbsp; Once so much to each other!



<P>Now nothing!&nbsp; There had been a time, when of all the large party



<P>now filling the drawing-room at Uppercross, they would have found it



<P>most difficult to cease to speak to one another.&nbsp; With the exception,



<P>perhaps, of Admiral and Mrs Croft, who seemed particularly attached



<P>and happy, (Anne could allow no other exceptions even among



<P>the married couples), there could have been no two hearts so open,



<P>no tastes so similar, no feelings so in unison, no countenances so beloved.



<P>Now they were as strangers; nay, worse than strangers, for they could



<P>never become acquainted.&nbsp; It was a perpetual estrangement.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When he talked, she heard the same voice, and discerned the same mind.



<P>There was a very general ignorance of all naval matters throughout the

party;



<P>and he was very much questioned, and especially by the two Miss Musgroves,



<P>who seemed hardly to have any eyes but for him, as to the manner



<P>of living on board, daily regulations, food, hours, &amp;c., and their

surprise



<P>at his accounts, at learning the degree of accommodation and arrangement



<P>which was practicable, drew from him some pleasant ridicule,



<P>which reminded Anne of the early days when she too had been ignorant,



<P>and she too had been accused of supposing sailors to be living on board



<P>without anything to eat, or any cook to dress it if there were,



<P>or any servant to wait, or any knife and fork to use.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>From thus listening and thinking, she was roused by a whisper



<P>of Mrs Musgrove's who, overcome by fond regrets, could not help saying--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ah! Miss Anne, if it had pleased Heaven to spare my poor son,



<P>I dare say he would have been just such another by this time."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne suppressed a smile, and listened kindly, while Mrs Musgrove



<P>relieved her heart a little more; and for a few minutes, therefore,



<P>could not keep pace with the conversation of the others.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When she could let her attention take its natural course again,



<P>she found the Miss Musgroves just fetching the Navy List



<P>(their own navy list, the first that had ever been at Uppercross),



<P>and sitting down together to pore over it, with the professed view



<P>of finding out the ships that Captain Wentworth had commanded.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Your first was the Asp, I remember; we will look for the Asp."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You will not find her there.&nbsp; Quite worn out and broken up.



<P>I was the last man who commanded her.&nbsp; Hardly fit for service then.



<P>Reported fit for home service for a year or two, and so I was sent off



<P>to the West Indies."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The girls looked all amazement.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"The Admiralty," he continued, "entertain themselves now and then,



<P>with sending a few hundred men to sea, in a ship not fit to be employed.



<P>But they have a great many to provide for; and among the thousands



<P>that may just as well go to the bottom as not, it is impossible



<P>for them to distinguish the very set who may be least missed."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Phoo! phoo!" cried the Admiral, "what stuff these young fellows talk!



<P>Never was a better sloop than the Asp in her day.&nbsp; For an old built

sloop,



<P>you would not see her equal.&nbsp; Lucky fellow to get her!&nbsp; He

knows there



<P>must have been twenty better men than himself applying for her



<P>at the same time.&nbsp; Lucky fellow to get anything so soon,



<P>with no more interest than his."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I felt my luck, Admiral, I assure you;" replied Captain Wentworth,



<P>seriously.&nbsp; "I was as well satisfied with my appointment as you

can desire.



<P>It was a great object with me at that time to be at sea;



<P>a very great object, I wanted to be doing something."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"To be sure you did.&nbsp; What should a young fellow like you do ashore



<P>for half a year together?&nbsp; If a man had not a wife, he soon wants



<P>to be afloat again."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But, Captain Wentworth," cried Louisa, "how vexed you must have been



<P>when you came to the Asp, to see what an old thing they had given you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I knew pretty well what she was before that day;" said he, smiling.



<P>"I had no more discoveries to make than you would have as to



<P>the fashion and strength of any old pelisse, which you had seen



<P>lent about among half your acquaintance ever since you could remember,



<P>and which at last, on some very wet day, is lent to yourself.



<P>Ah! she was a dear old Asp to me.&nbsp; She did all that I wanted.



<P>I knew she would.&nbsp; I knew that we should either go to the bottom

together,



<P>or that she would be the making of me; and I never had two days



<P>of foul weather all the time I was at sea in her; and after



<P>taking privateers enough to be very entertaining, I had the good luck



<P>in my passage home the next autumn, to fall in with the very French

frigate



<P>I wanted.&nbsp; I brought her into Plymouth; and here another instance

of luck.



<P>We had not been six hours in the Sound, when a gale came on,



<P>which lasted four days and nights, and which would have done for



<P>poor old Asp in half the time; our touch with the Great Nation



<P>not having much improved our condition.&nbsp; Four-and-twenty hours

later,



<P>and I should only have been a gallant Captain Wentworth,



<P>in a small paragraph at one corner of the newspapers; and being lost



<P>in only a sloop, nobody would have thought about me." Anne's shudderings



<P>were to herself alone; but the Miss Musgroves could be as open



<P>as they were sincere, in their exclamations of pity and horror.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And so then, I suppose," said Mrs Musgrove, in a low voice,



<P>as if thinking aloud, "so then he went away to the Laconia, and there



<P>he met with our poor boy. Charles, my dear," (beckoning him to her),



<P>"do ask Captain Wentworth where it was he first met with your poor brother.



<P>I always forgot."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It was at Gibraltar, mother, I know.&nbsp; Dick had been left ill at

Gibraltar,



<P>with a recommendation from his former captain to Captain Wentworth."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! but, Charles, tell Captain Wentworth, he need not be afraid



<P>of mentioning poor Dick before me, for it would be rather a pleasure



<P>to hear him talked of by such a good friend."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Charles, being somewhat more mindful of the probabilities of the case,



<P>only nodded in reply, and walked away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The girls were now hunting for the Laconia; and Captain Wentworth



<P>could not deny himself the pleasure of taking the precious volume



<P>into his own hands to save them the trouble, and once more read aloud



<P>the little statement of her name and rate, and present



<P>non-commissioned class, observing over it that she too had been



<P>one of the best friends man ever had.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ah! those were pleasant days when I had the Laconia!&nbsp; How fast

I



<P>made money in her.&nbsp; A friend of mine and I had such a lovely cruise



<P>together off the Western Islands.&nbsp; Poor Harville, sister!



<P>You know how much he wanted money:&nbsp; worse than myself.&nbsp; He

had a wife.



<P>Excellent fellow.&nbsp; I shall never forget his happiness.&nbsp; He

felt it all,



<P>so much for her sake.&nbsp; I wished for him again the next summer,



<P>when I had still the same luck in the Mediterranean."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And I am sure, Sir." said Mrs Musgrove, "it was a lucky day for us,



<P>when you were put captain into that ship.&nbsp; We shall never forget



<P>what you did."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Her feelings made her speak low; and Captain Wentworth,



<P>hearing only in part, and probably not having Dick Musgrove at all



<P>near his thoughts, looked rather in suspense, and as if waiting for

more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"My brother," whispered one of the girls; "mamma is thinking



<P>of poor Richard."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Poor dear fellow!" continued Mrs Musgrove; "he was grown so steady,



<P>and such an excellent correspondent, while he was under your care!



<P>Ah! it would have been a happy thing, if he had never left you.



<P>I assure you, Captain Wentworth, we are very sorry he ever left you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was a momentary expression in Captain Wentworth's face at this

speech,



<P>a certain glance of his bright eye, and curl of his handsome mouth,



<P>which convinced Anne, that instead of sharing in Mrs Musgrove's kind

wishes,



<P>as to her son, he had probably been at some pains to get rid of him;



<P>but it was too transient an indulgence of self-amusement to be detected



<P>by any who understood him less than herself; in another moment



<P>he was perfectly collected and serious, and almost instantly afterwards



<P>coming up to the sofa, on which she and Mrs Musgrove were sitting,



<P>took a place by the latter, and entered into conversation with her,



<P>in a low voice, about her son, doing it with so much sympathy



<P>and natural grace, as shewed the kindest consideration for all



<P>that was real and unabsurd in the parent's feelings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They were actually on the same sofa, for Mrs Musgrove had



<P>most readily made room for him; they were divided only by Mrs Musgrove.



<P>It was no insignificant barrier, indeed.&nbsp; Mrs Musgrove was of



<P>a comfortable, substantial size, infinitely more fitted by nature



<P>to express good cheer and good humour, than tenderness and sentiment;



<P>and while the agitations of Anne's slender form, and pensive face,



<P>may be considered as very completely screened, Captain Wentworth



<P>should be allowed some credit for the self-command with which



<P>he attended to her large fat sighings over the destiny of a son,



<P>whom alive nobody had cared for.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Personal size and mental sorrow have certainly no necessary proportions.



<P>A large bulky figure has as good a right to be in deep affliction,



<P>as the most graceful set of limbs in the world.&nbsp; But, fair or not

fair,



<P>there are unbecoming conjunctions, which reason will patronize in vain--



<P>which taste cannot tolerate--which ridicule will seize.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The Admiral, after taking two or three refreshing turns about the room



<P>with his hands behind him, being called to order by his wife,



<P>now came up to Captain Wentworth, and without any observation



<P>of what he might be interrupting, thinking only of his own thoughts,



<P>began with--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"If you had been a week later at Lisbon, last spring, Frederick,



<P>you would have been asked to give a passage to Lady Mary Grierson



<P>and her daughters."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Should I?&nbsp; I am glad I was not a week later then."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The Admiral abused him for his want of gallantry.&nbsp; He defended

himself;



<P>though professing that he would never willingly admit any ladies



<P>on board a ship of his, excepting for a ball, or a visit,



<P>which a few hours might comprehend.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But, if I know myself," said he, "this is from no want of gallantry



<P>towards them.&nbsp; It is rather from feeling how impossible it is,



<P>with all one's efforts, and all one's sacrifices, to make



<P>the accommodations on board such as women ought to have.



<P>There can be no want of gallantry, Admiral, in rating the claims of

women



<P>to every personal comfort high, and this is what I do.&nbsp; I hate

to hear



<P>of women on board, or to see them on board; and no ship under my command



<P>shall ever convey a family of ladies anywhere, if I can help it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This brought his sister upon him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! Frederick!&nbsp; But I cannot believe it of you. --All idle refinement!



<P>--Women may be as comfortable on board, as in the best house in England.



<P>I believe I have lived as much on board as most women, and I know



<P>nothing superior to the accommodations of a man-of-war.&nbsp; I declare



<P>I have not a comfort or an indulgence about me, even at Kellynch Hall,"



<P>(with a kind bow to Anne), "beyond what I always had in most of



<P>the ships I have lived in; and they have been five altogether."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Nothing to the purpose," replied her brother.&nbsp; "You were living



<P>with your husband, and were the only woman on board."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But you, yourself, brought Mrs Harville, her sister, her cousin,



<P>and three children, round from Portsmouth to Plymouth.&nbsp; Where was

this



<P>superfine, extraordinary sort of gallantry of yours then?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"All merged in my friendship, Sophia.&nbsp; I would assist any



<P>brother officer's wife that I could, and I would bring anything



<P>of Harville's from the world's end, if he wanted it.&nbsp; But do not

imagine



<P>that I did not feel it an evil in itself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Depend upon it, they were all perfectly comfortable."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I might not like them the better for that perhaps.&nbsp; Such a number



<P>of women and children have no right to be comfortable on board."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"My dear Frederick, you are talking quite idly.&nbsp; Pray, what would



<P>become of us poor sailors' wives, who often want to be conveyed to



<P>one port or another, after our husbands, if everybody had your feelings?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"My feelings, you see, did not prevent my taking Mrs Harville



<P>and all her family to Plymouth."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But I hate to hear you talking so like a fine gentleman,



<P>and as if women were all fine ladies, instead of rational creatures.



<P>We none of us expect to be in smooth water all our days."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ah! my dear," said the Admiral, "when he had got a wife,



<P>he will sing a different tune.&nbsp; When he is married, if we have



<P>the good luck to live to another war, we shall see him do as you and

I,



<P>and a great many others, have done.&nbsp; We shall have him very thankful



<P>to anybody that will bring him his wife."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ay, that we shall."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Now I have done," cried Captain Wentworth.&nbsp; "When once married



<P>people begin to attack me with,--`Oh! you will think very differently,



<P>when you are married.'&nbsp; I can only say, `No, I shall not;' and

then



<P>they say again, `Yes, you will,' and there is an end of it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He got up and moved away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What a great traveller you must have been, ma'am!" said Mrs Musgrove



<P>to Mrs Croft.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Pretty well, ma'am in the fifteen years of my marriage;



<P>though many women have done more.&nbsp; I have crossed the Atlantic



<P>four times, and have been once to the East Indies, and back again,



<P>and only once; besides being in different places about home:



<P>Cork, and Lisbon, and Gibraltar.&nbsp; But I never went beyond the Streights,



<P>and never was in the West Indies.&nbsp; We do not call Bermuda or Bahama,



<P>you know, the West Indies."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Musgrove had not a word to say in dissent; she could not accuse

herself



<P>of having ever called them anything in the whole course of her life.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And I do assure you, ma'am," pursued Mrs Croft, "that nothing can exceed



<P>the accommodations of a man-of-war; I speak, you know, of the higher

rates.



<P>When you come to a frigate, of course, you are more confined;



<P>though any reasonable woman may be perfectly happy in one of them;



<P>and I can safely say, that the happiest part of my life has been spent



<P>on board a ship.&nbsp; While we were together, you know, there was nothing



<P>to be feared.&nbsp; Thank God!&nbsp; I have always been blessed with



<P>excellent health, and no climate disagrees with me.&nbsp; A little disordered



<P>always the first twenty-four hours of going to sea, but never knew



<P>what sickness was afterwards.&nbsp; The only time I ever really suffered



<P>in body or mind, the only time that I ever fancied myself unwell,



<P>or had any ideas of danger, was the winter that I passed by myself at

Deal,



<P>when the Admiral (Captain Croft then) was in the North Seas.



<P>I lived in perpetual fright at that time, and had all manner of



<P>imaginary complaints from not knowing what to do with myself,



<P>or when I should hear from him next; but as long as we could be together,



<P>nothing ever ailed me, and I never met with the smallest inconvenience."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Aye, to be sure.&nbsp; Yes, indeed, oh yes!&nbsp; I am quite of your

opinion,



<P>Mrs Croft," was Mrs Musgrove's hearty answer.&nbsp; "There is nothing

so bad



<P>as a separation.&nbsp; I am quite of your opinion.&nbsp; I know what

it is,



<P>for Mr Musgrove always attends the assizes, and I am so glad when



<P>they are over, and he is safe back again."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The evening ended with dancing.&nbsp; On its being proposed,



<P>Anne offered her services, as usual; and though her eyes would sometimes



<P>fill with tears as she sat at the instrument, she was extremely glad



<P>to be employed, and desired nothing in return but to be unobserved.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was a merry, joyous party, and no one seemed in higher spirits



<P>than Captain Wentworth.&nbsp; She felt that he had every thing to elevate



<P>him which general attention and deference, and especially the attention



<P>of all the young women, could do.&nbsp; The Miss Hayters, the females



<P>of the family of cousins already mentioned, were apparently admitted



<P>to the honour of being in love with him; and as for Henrietta and Louisa,



<P>they both seemed so entirely occupied by him, that nothing but



<P>the continued appearance of the most perfect good-will between themselves



<P>could have made it credible that they were not decided rivals.



<P>If he were a little spoilt by such universal, such eager admiration,



<P>who could wonder?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>These were some of the thoughts which occupied Anne, while her fingers



<P>were mechanically at work, proceeding for half an hour together,



<P>equally without error, and without consciousness.&nbsp; Once she felt



<P>that he was looking at herself,&nbsp; observing her altered features,



<P>perhaps, trying to trace in them the ruins of the face which had once



<P>charmed him; and once she knew that he must have spoken of her;



<P>she was hardly aware of it, till she heard the answer; but then she

was



<P>sure of his having asked his partner whether Miss Elliot never danced?



<P>The answer was, "Oh, no; never; she has quite given up dancing.



<P>She had rather play.&nbsp; She is never tired of playing."&nbsp; Once,

too,



<P>he spoke to her.&nbsp; She had left the instrument on the dancing being

over,



<P>and he had sat down to try to make out an air which he wished



<P>to give the Miss Musgroves an idea of.&nbsp; Unintentionally she returned



<P>to that part of the room; he saw her, and, instantly rising,



<P>said, with studied politeness--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I beg your pardon, madam, this is your seat;" and though she immediately



<P>drew back with a decided negative, he was not to be induced



<P>to sit down again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne did not wish for more of such looks and speeches.



<P>His cold politeness, his ceremonious grace, were worse than anything.



<P>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 09</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 9</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Captain Wentworth was come to Kellynch as to a home, to stay



<P>as long as he liked, being as thoroughly the object of



<P>the Admiral's fraternal kindness as of his wife's.&nbsp; He had intended,



<P>on first arriving, to proceed very soon into Shropshire,



<P>and visit the brother settled in that country, but the attractions



<P>of Uppercross induced him to put this off.&nbsp; There was so much



<P>of friendliness, and of flattery, and of everything most bewitching



<P>in his reception there; the old were so hospitable, the young so agreeable,



<P>that he could not but resolve to remain where he was, and take all



<P>the charms and perfections of Edward's wife upon credit a little longer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was soon Uppercross with him almost every day.&nbsp; The Musgroves



<P>could hardly be more ready to invite than he to come, particularly



<P>in the morning, when he had no companion at home, for the Admiral



<P>and Mrs Croft were generally out of doors together, interesting themselves



<P>in their new possessions, their grass, and their sheep, and dawdling

about



<P>in a way not endurable to a third person, or driving out in a gig,



<P>lately added to their establishment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Hitherto there had been but one opinion of Captain Wentworth



<P>among the Musgroves and their dependencies.&nbsp; It was unvarying,



<P>warm admiration everywhere; but this intimate footing was not more



<P>than established, when a certain Charles Hayter returned among them,



<P>to be a good deal disturbed by it, and to think Captain Wentworth



<P>very much in the way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Charles Hayter was the eldest of all the cousins, and a very amiable,



<P>pleasing young man, between whom and Henrietta there had been



<P>a considerable appearance of attachment previous to Captain Wentworth's



<P>introduction.&nbsp; He was in orders; and having a curacy in the neighbourhood,



<P>where residence was not required, lived at his father's house,



<P>only two miles from Uppercross.&nbsp; A short absence from home



<P>had left his fair one unguarded by his attentions at this critical period,



<P>and when he came back he had the pain of finding very altered manners,



<P>and of seeing Captain Wentworth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Musgrove and Mrs Hayter were sisters.&nbsp; They had each had money,



<P>but their marriages had made a material difference in



<P>their degree of consequence.&nbsp; Mr Hayter had some property of his

own,



<P>but it was insignificant compared with Mr Musgrove's; and while



<P>the Musgroves were in the first class of society in the country,



<P>the young Hayters would, from their parents' inferior, retired,



<P>and unpolished way of living, and their own defective education,



<P>have been hardly in any class at all, but for their connexion



<P>with Uppercross, this eldest son of course excepted, who had chosen



<P>to be a scholar and a gentleman, and who was very superior



<P>in cultivation and manners to all the rest.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The two families had always been on excellent terms, there being no

pride



<P>on one side, and no envy on the other, and only such a consciousness



<P>of superiority in the Miss Musgroves, as made them pleased



<P>to improve their cousins.&nbsp; Charles's attentions to Henrietta



<P>had been observed by her father and mother without any disapprobation.



<P>"It would not be a great match for her; but if Henrietta liked him,"--



<P>and Henrietta did seem to like him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Henrietta fully thought so herself, before Captain Wentworth came;



<P>but from that time Cousin Charles had been very much forgotten.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Which of the two sisters was preferred by Captain Wentworth was



<P>as yet quite doubtful, as far as Anne's observation reached.



<P>Henrietta was perhaps the prettiest, Louisa had the higher spirits;



<P>and she knew not now, whether the more gentle or the more lively character



<P>were most likely to attract him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr and Mrs Musgrove, either from seeing little, or from



<P>an entire confidence in the discretion of both their daughters,



<P>and of all the young men who came near them, seemed to leave everything



<P>to take its chance.&nbsp; There was not the smallest appearance of solicitude



<P>or remark about them in the Mansion-house; but it was different



<P>at the Cottage:&nbsp; the young couple there were more disposed



<P>to speculate and wonder; and Captain Wentworth had not been above



<P>four or five times in the Miss Musgroves' company, and Charles Hayter



<P>had but just reappeared, when Anne had to listen to the opinions



<P>of her brother and sister, as to which was the one liked best.



<P>Charles gave it for Louisa, Mary for Henrietta, but quite agreeing



<P>that to have him marry either could be extremely delightful.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Charles "had never seen a pleasanter man in his life; and from what



<P>he had once heard Captain Wentworth himself say, was very sure that



<P>he had not made less than twenty thousand pounds by the war.



<P>Here was a fortune at once; besides which, there would be the chance



<P>of what might be done in any future war; and he was sure Captain Wentworth



<P>was as likely a man to distinguish himself as any officer in the navy.



<P>Oh! it would be a capital match for either of his sisters."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Upon my word it would," replied Mary.&nbsp; "Dear me!&nbsp; If he should



<P>rise to any very great honours!&nbsp; If he should ever be made a baronet!



<P>`Lady Wentworth' sounds very well.&nbsp; That would be a noble thing,



<P>indeed, for Henrietta!&nbsp; She would take place of me then, and Henrietta



<P>would not dislike that.&nbsp; Sir Frederick and Lady Wentworth!



<P>It would be but a new creation, however, and I never think much



<P>of your new creations."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It suited Mary best to think Henrietta the one preferred



<P>on the very account of Charles Hayter, whose pretensions she wished



<P>to see put an end to.&nbsp; She looked down very decidedly upon the

Hayters,



<P>and thought it would be quite a misfortune to have the existing connection



<P>between the families renewed--very sad for herself and her children.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You know," said she, "I cannot think him at all a fit match for Henrietta;



<P>and considering the alliances which the Musgroves have made,



<P>she has no right to throw herself away.&nbsp; I do not think any young

woman



<P>has a right to make a choice that may be disagreeable and inconvenient



<P>to the principal part of her family, and be giving bad connections



<P>to those who have not been used to them.&nbsp; And, pray, who is Charles

Hayter?



<P>Nothing but a country curate.&nbsp; A most improper match for Miss Musgrove



<P>of Uppercross.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Her husband, however, would not agree with her here; for besides having



<P>a regard for his cousin, Charles Hayter was an eldest son,



<P>and he saw things as an eldest son himself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Now you are taking nonsense, Mary," was therefore his answer.



<P>"It would not be a great match for Henrietta, but Charles has



<P>a very fair chance, through the Spicers, of getting something from



<P>the Bishop in the course of a year or two; and you will please to remember,



<P>that he is the eldest son; whenever my uncle dies, he steps into very



<P>pretty property.&nbsp; The estate at Winthrop is not less than



<P>two hundred and fifty acres, besides the farm near Taunton,



<P>which is some of the best land in the country.&nbsp; I grant you,



<P>that any of them but Charles would be a very shocking match for Henrietta,



<P>and indeed it could not be; he is the only one that could be possible;



<P>but he is a very good-natured, good sort of a fellow; and whenever Winthrop



<P>comes into his hands, he will make a different sort of place of it,



<P>and live in a very different sort of way; and with that property,



<P>he will never be a contemptible man--good, freehold property.&nbsp;

No, no;



<P>Henrietta might do worse than marry Charles Hayter; and if she has him,



<P>and Louisa can get Captain Wentworth, I shall be very well satisfied."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Charles may say what he pleases," cried Mary to Anne, as soon as



<P>he was out of the room, "but it would be shocking to have Henrietta



<P>marry Charles Hayter; a very bad thing for her, and still worse



<P>for me; and therefore it is very much to be wished that Captain Wentworth



<P>may soon put him quite out of her head, and I have very little doubt



<P>that he has.&nbsp; She took hardly any notice of Charles Hayter yesterday.



<P>I wish you had been there to see her behaviour.&nbsp; And as to



<P>Captain Wentworth's liking Louisa as well as Henrietta, it is nonsense



<P>to say so; for he certainly does like Henrietta a great deal the best.



<P>But Charles is so positive!&nbsp; I wish you had been with us yesterday,



<P>for then you might have decided between us; and I am sure you



<P>would have thought as I did, unless you had been determined



<P>to give it against me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A dinner at Mr Musgrove's had been the occasion when all these things



<P>should have been seen by Anne; but she had staid at home,



<P>under the mixed plea of a headache of her own, and some return



<P>of indisposition in little Charles.&nbsp; She had thought only of avoiding



<P>Captain Wentworth; but an escape from being appealed to as umpire



<P>was now added to the advantages of a quiet evening.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As to Captain Wentworth's views, she deemed it of more consequence



<P>that he should know his own mind early enough not to be endangering



<P>the happiness of either sister, or impeaching his own honour,



<P>than that he should prefer Henrietta to Louisa, or Louisa to Henrietta.



<P>Either of them would, in all probability, make him an affectionate,



<P>good-humoured wife.&nbsp; With regard to Charles Hayter, she had delicacy



<P>which must be pained by any lightness of conduct in a well-meaning



<P>young woman, and a heart to sympathize in any of the sufferings



<P>it occasioned; but if Henrietta found herself mistaken in the nature



<P>of her feelings, the alternation could not be understood too soon.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Charles Hayter had met with much to disquiet and mortify him



<P>in his cousin's behaviour.&nbsp; She had too old a regard for him



<P>to be so wholly estranged as might in two meetings extinguish



<P>every past hope, and leave him nothing to do but to keep away



<P>from Uppercross:&nbsp; but there was such a change as became very alarming,



<P>when such a man as Captain Wentworth was to be regarded as



<P>the probable cause.&nbsp; He had been absent only two Sundays,



<P>and when they parted, had left her interested, even to the height



<P>of his wishes, in his prospect of soon quitting his present curacy,



<P>and obtaining that of Uppercross instead.&nbsp; It had then seemed the

object



<P>nearest her heart, that Dr Shirley, the rector, who for more than



<P>forty years had been zealously discharging all the duties of his office,



<P>but was now growing too infirm for many of them, should be quite fixed



<P>on engaging a curate; should make his curacy quite as good



<P>as he could afford, and should give Charles Hayter the promise of it.



<P>The advantage of his having to come only to Uppercross, instead of going



<P>six miles another way; of his having, in every respect, a better curacy;



<P>of his belonging to their dear Dr Shirley, and of dear, good Dr Shirley's



<P>being relieved from the duty which he could no longer get through



<P>without most injurious fatigue, had been a great deal, even to Louisa,



<P>but had been almost everything to Henrietta.&nbsp; When he came back,

alas!



<P>the zeal of the business was gone by.&nbsp; Louisa could not listen

at all



<P>to his account of a conversation which he had just held with Dr Shirley:



<P>she was at a window, looking out for Captain Wentworth; and even Henrietta



<P>had at best only a divided attention to give, and seemed to have forgotten



<P>all the former doubt and solicitude of the negotiation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, I am very glad indeed:&nbsp; but I always thought you would have

it;



<P>I always thought you sure.&nbsp; It did not appear to me that--in short,



<P>you know, Dr Shirley must have a curate, and you had secured his promise.



<P>Is he coming, Louisa?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One morning, very soon after the dinner at the Musgroves,



<P>at which Anne had not been present, Captain Wentworth walked into



<P>the drawing-room at the Cottage, where were only herself and the little



<P>invalid Charles, who was lying on the sofa.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The surprise of finding himself almost alone with Anne Elliot,



<P>deprived his manners of their usual composure:&nbsp; he started,



<P>and could only say, "I thought the Miss Musgroves had been here:



<P>Mrs Musgrove told me I should find them here," before he walked



<P>to the window to recollect himself, and feel how he ought to behave.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"They are up stairs with my sister:&nbsp; they will be down in a few

moments,



<P>I dare say," had been Anne's reply, in all the confusion that was natural;



<P>and if the child had not called her to come and do something for him,



<P>she would have been out of the room the next moment, and released



<P>Captain Wentworth as well as herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He continued at the window; and after calmly and politely saying,



<P>"I hope the little boy is better," was silent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She was obliged to kneel down by the sofa, and remain there



<P>to satisfy her patient; and thus they continued a few minutes,



<P>when, to her very great satisfaction, she heard some other person



<P>crossing the little vestibule.&nbsp; She hoped, on turning her head,



<P>to see the master of the house; but it proved to be one



<P>much less calculated for making matters easy--Charles Hayter,



<P>probably not at all better pleased by the sight of Captain Wentworth



<P>than Captain Wentworth had been by the sight of Anne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She only attempted to say, "How do you do?&nbsp; Will you not sit down?



<P>The others will be here presently."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Wentworth, however, came from his window, apparently



<P>not ill-disposed for conversation; but Charles Hayter soon put an end



<P>to his attempts by seating himself near the table, and taking up



<P>the newspaper; and Captain Wentworth returned to his window.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Another minute brought another addition.&nbsp; The younger boy,



<P>a remarkable stout, forward child, of two years old, having got the

door



<P>opened for him by some one without, made his determined appearance



<P>among them, and went straight to the sofa to see what was going on,



<P>and put in his claim to anything good that might be giving away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There being nothing to eat, he could only have some play;



<P>and as his aunt would not let him tease his sick brother,



<P>he began to fasten himself upon her, as she knelt, in such a way that,



<P>busy as she was about Charles, she could not shake him off.



<P>She spoke to him, ordered, entreated, and insisted in vain.



<P>Once she did contrive to push him away, but the boy had



<P>the greater pleasure in getting upon her back again directly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Walter," said she, "get down this moment.&nbsp; You are extremely troublesome.



<P>I am very angry with you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Walter," cried Charles Hayter, "why do you not do as you are bid?



<P>Do not you hear your aunt speak?&nbsp; Come to me, Walter, come to



<P>cousin Charles."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But not a bit did Walter stir.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In another moment, however, she found herself in the state of



<P>being released from him; some one was taking him from her,



<P>though he had bent down her head so much, that his little sturdy hands



<P>were unfastened from around her neck, and he was resolutely borne away,



<P>before she knew that Captain Wentworth had done it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Her sensations on the discovery made her perfectly speechless.



<P>She could not even thank him.&nbsp; She could only hang over little

Charles,



<P>with most disordered feelings.&nbsp; His kindness in stepping forward



<P>to her relief, the manner, the silence in which it had passed,



<P>the little particulars of the circumstance, with the conviction soon



<P>forced on her by the noise he was studiously making with the child,



<P>that he meant to avoid hearing her thanks, and rather sought



<P>to testify that her conversation was the last of his wants,



<P>produced such a confusion of varying, but very painful agitation,



<P>as she could not recover from, till enabled by the entrance of Mary



<P>and the Miss Musgroves to make over her little patient to their cares,



<P>and leave the room.&nbsp; She could not stay.&nbsp; It might have been



<P>an opportunity of watching the loves and jealousies of the four--



<P>they were now altogether; but she could stay for none of it.



<P>It was evident that Charles Hayter was not well inclined towards



<P>Captain Wentworth.&nbsp; She had a strong impression of his having said,



<P>in a vext tone of voice, after Captain Wentworth's interference,



<P>"You ought to have minded me, Walter; I told you not to teaze your aunt;"



<P>and could comprehend his regretting that Captain Wentworth should do



<P>what he ought to have done himself.&nbsp; But neither Charles Hayter's

feelings,



<P>nor anybody's feelings, could interest her, till she had a little better



<P>arranged her own.&nbsp; She was ashamed of herself, quite ashamed



<P>of being so nervous, so overcome by such a trifle; but so it was,



<P>and it required a long application of solitude and reflection



<P>to recover her.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 21</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 21</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Anne recollected with pleasure the next morning her promise



<P>of going to Mrs Smith, meaning that it should engage her from home



<P>at the time when Mr Elliot would be most likely to call; for to avoid



<P>Mr Elliot was almost a first object.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She felt a great deal of good-will towards him.&nbsp; In spite of



<P>the mischief of his attentions, she owed him gratitude and regard,



<P>perhaps compassion.&nbsp; She could not help thinking much of the extraordinary



<P>circumstances attending their acquaintance, of the right which



<P>he seemed to have to interest her, by everything in situation,



<P>by his own sentiments, by his early prepossession.&nbsp; It was altogether



<P>very extraordinary; flattering, but painful.&nbsp; There was much to

regret.



<P>How she might have felt had there been no Captain Wentworth in the case,



<P>was not worth enquiry; for there was a Captain Wentworth;



<P>and be the conclusion of the present suspense good or bad,



<P>her affection would be his for ever.&nbsp; Their union, she believed,



<P>could not divide her more from other men, than their final separation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Prettier musings of high-wrought love and eternal constancy,



<P>could never have passed along the streets of Bath, than Anne



<P>was sporting with from Camden Place to Westgate Buildings.



<P>It was almost enough to spread purification and perfume all the way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She was sure of a pleasant reception; and her friend seemed this morning



<P>particularly obliged to her for coming, seemed hardly to have expected

her,



<P>though it had been an appointment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>An account of the concert was immediately claimed; and Anne's recollections



<P>of the concert were quite happy enough to animate her features



<P>and make her rejoice to talk of it.&nbsp; All that she could tell



<P>she told most gladly, but the all was little for one who had been there,



<P>and unsatisfactory for such an enquirer as Mrs Smith, who had



<P>already heard, through the short cut of a laundress and a waiter,



<P>rather more of the general success and produce of the evening



<P>than Anne could relate, and who now asked in vain for several particulars



<P>of the company.&nbsp; Everybody of any consequence or notoriety in Bath



<P>was well know by name to Mrs Smith.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"The little Durands were there, I conclude," said she, "with their mouths



<P>open to catch the music, like unfledged sparrows ready to be fed.



<P>They never miss a concert."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes; I did not see them myself, but I heard Mr Elliot say they were



<P>in the room."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"The Ibbotsons, were they there? and the two new beauties,



<P>with the tall Irish officer, who is talked of for one of them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I do not know.&nbsp; I do not think they were."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Old Lady Mary Maclean?&nbsp; I need not ask after her.&nbsp; She never

misses,



<P>I know; and you must have seen her.&nbsp; She must have been in your

own circle;



<P>for as you went with Lady Dalrymple, you were in the seats of grandeur,



<P>round the orchestra, of course."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, that was what I dreaded.&nbsp; It would have been very unpleasant

to me



<P>in every respect.&nbsp; But happily Lady Dalrymple always chooses



<P>to be farther off; and we were exceedingly well placed, that is,



<P>for hearing; I must not say for seeing, because I appear to have seen



<P>very little."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! you saw enough for your own amusement.&nbsp; I can understand.



<P>There is a sort of domestic enjoyment to be known even in a crowd,



<P>and this you had.&nbsp; You were a large party in yourselves,



<P>and you wanted nothing beyond."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But I ought to have looked about me more," said Anne, conscious



<P>while she spoke that there had in fact been no want of looking about,



<P>that the object only had been deficient.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, no; you were better employed.&nbsp; You need not tell me that you



<P>had a pleasant evening.&nbsp; I see it in your eye.&nbsp; I perfectly

see



<P>how the hours passed:&nbsp; that you had always something agreeable



<P>to listen to.&nbsp; In the intervals of the concert it was conversation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne half smiled and said, "Do you see that in my eye?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, I do.&nbsp; Your countenance perfectly informs me that you were



<P>in company last night with the person whom you think the most agreeable



<P>in the world, the person who interests you at this present time



<P>more than all the rest of the world put together."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A blush overspread Anne's cheeks.&nbsp; She could say nothing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And such being the case," continued Mrs Smith, after a short pause,



<P>"I hope you believe that I do know how to value your kindness



<P>in coming to me this morning.&nbsp; It is really very good of you



<P>to come and sit with me, when you must have so many pleasanter demands



<P>upon your time."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne heard nothing of this.&nbsp; She was still in the astonishment

and



<P>confusion excited by her friend's penetration, unable to imagine



<P>how any report of Captain Wentworth could have reached her.



<P>After another short silence--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Pray," said Mrs Smith, "is Mr Elliot aware of your acquaintance with

me?



<P>Does he know that I am in Bath?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Mr Elliot!" repeated Anne, looking up surprised.&nbsp; A moment's reflection



<P>shewed her the mistake she had been under.&nbsp; She caught it instantaneously;



<P>and recovering her courage with the feeling of safety, soon added,



<P>more composedly, "Are you acquainted with Mr Elliot?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I have been a good deal acquainted with him," replied Mrs Smith, gravely,



<P>"but it seems worn out now.&nbsp; It is a great while since we met."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I was not at all aware of this.&nbsp; You never mentioned it before.



<P>Had I known it, I would have had the pleasure of talking to him about

you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"To confess the truth," said Mrs Smith, assuming her usual



<P>air of cheerfulness, "that is exactly the pleasure I want you to have.



<P>I want you to talk about me to Mr Elliot.&nbsp; I want your interest

with him.



<P>He can be of essential service to me; and if you would have the goodness,



<P>my dear Miss Elliot, to make it an object to yourself,



<P>of course it is done."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I should be extremely happy; I hope you cannot doubt my willingness



<P>to be of even the slightest use to you," replied Anne; "but I suspect



<P>that you are considering me as having a higher claim on Mr Elliot,



<P>a greater right to influence him, than is really the case.



<P>I am sure you have, somehow or other, imbibed such a notion.



<P>You must consider me only as Mr Elliot's relation.&nbsp; If in that

light



<P>there is anything which you suppose his cousin might fairly ask of him,



<P>I beg you would not hesitate to employ me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Smith gave her a penetrating glance, and then, smiling, said--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I have been a little premature, I perceive; I beg your pardon.



<P>I ought to have waited for official information,&nbsp; But now, my dear



<P>Miss Elliot, as an old friend, do give me a hint as to when I may speak.



<P>Next week?&nbsp; To be sure by next week I may be allowed to



<P>think it all settled, and build my own selfish schemes on



<P>Mr Elliot's good fortune."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No," replied Anne, "nor next week, nor next, nor next.



<P>I assure you that nothing of the sort you are thinking of



<P>will be settled any week.&nbsp; I am not going to marry Mr Elliot.



<P>I should like to know why you imagine I am?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Smith looked at her again, looked earnestly, smiled,



<P>shook her head, and exclaimed--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Now, how I do wish I understood you!&nbsp; How I do wish I knew



<P>what you were at!&nbsp; I have a great idea that you do not design to

be cruel,



<P>when the right moment occurs.&nbsp; Till it does come, you know,



<P>we women never mean to have anybody.&nbsp; It is a thing of course among

us,



<P>that every man is refused, till he offers.&nbsp; But why should you

be cruel?



<P>Let me plead for my--present friend I cannot call him, but for



<P>my former friend.&nbsp; Where can you look for a more suitable match?



<P>Where could you expect a more gentlemanlike, agreeable man?



<P>Let me recommend Mr Elliot.&nbsp; I am sure you hear nothing but good

of him



<P>from Colonel Wallis; and who can know him better than Colonel Wallis?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"My dear Mrs Smith, Mr Elliot's wife has not been dead much above



<P>half a year.&nbsp; He ought not to be supposed to be paying his addresses



<P>to any one."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! if these are your only objections," cried Mrs Smith, archly,



<P>"Mr Elliot is safe, and I shall give myself no more trouble about him.



<P>Do not forget me when you are married, that's all.&nbsp; Let him know

me to be



<P>a friend of yours, and then he will think little of the trouble required,



<P>which it is very natural for him now, with so many affairs and engagements



<P>of his own, to avoid and get rid of as he can; very natural, perhaps.



<P>Ninety-nine out of a hundred would do the same.&nbsp; Of course,



<P>he cannot be aware of the importance to me.&nbsp; Well, my dear Miss

Elliot,



<P>I hope and trust you will be very happy.&nbsp; Mr Elliot has sense



<P>to understand the value of such a woman.&nbsp; Your peace will not be



<P>shipwrecked as mine has been.&nbsp; You are safe in all worldly matters,



<P>and safe in his character.&nbsp; He will not be led astray; he will

not be



<P>misled by others to his ruin."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No," said Anne, "I can readily believe all that of my cousin.



<P>He seems to have a calm decided temper, not at all open



<P>to dangerous impressions.&nbsp; I consider him with great respect.



<P>I have no reason, from any thing that has fallen within my observation,



<P>to do otherwise.&nbsp; But I have not known him long; and he is not

a man,



<P>I think, to be known intimately soon.&nbsp; Will not this manner



<P>of speaking of him, Mrs Smith, convince you that he is nothing to me?



<P>Surely this must be calm enough.&nbsp; And, upon my word, he is nothing

to me.



<P>Should he ever propose to me (which I have very little reason to imagine



<P>he has any thought of doing), I shall not accept him.&nbsp; I assure

you



<P>I shall not.&nbsp; I assure you, Mr Elliot had not the share which



<P>you have been supposing, in whatever pleasure the concert



<P>of last night might afford:&nbsp; not Mr Elliot; it is not Mr Elliot

that--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She stopped, regretting with a deep blush that she had implied so much;



<P>but less would hardly have been sufficient.&nbsp; Mrs Smith would hardly



<P>have believed so soon in Mr Elliot's failure, but from the perception



<P>of there being a somebody else.&nbsp; As it was, she instantly submitted,



<P>and with all the semblance of seeing nothing beyond; and Anne,



<P>eager to escape farther notice, was impatient to know why Mrs Smith



<P>should have fancied she was to marry Mr Elliot; where she could have



<P>received the idea, or from whom she could have heard it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Do tell me how it first came into your head."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It first came into my head," replied Mrs Smith, "upon finding how much



<P>you were together, and feeling it to be the most probable thing



<P>in the world to be wished for by everybody belonging to either of you;



<P>and you may depend upon it that all your acquaintance have disposed

of you



<P>in the same way.&nbsp; But I never heard it spoken of till two days

ago."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And has it indeed been spoken of?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Did you observe the woman who opened the door to you when



<P>you called yesterday?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No.&nbsp; Was not it Mrs Speed, as usual, or the maid?&nbsp; I observed



<P>no one in particular."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It was my friend Mrs Rooke; Nurse Rooke; who, by-the-bye,
