<P>had a great curiosity to see you, and was delighted to be in the way



<P>to let you in.&nbsp; She came away from Marlborough Buildings only on

Sunday;



<P>and she it was who told me you were to marry Mr Elliot.



<P>She had had it from Mrs Wallis herself, which did not seem bad authority.



<P>She sat an hour with me on Monday evening, and gave me the whole history."



<P>"The whole history," repeated Anne, laughing.&nbsp; "She could not make



<P>a very long history, I think, of one such little article



<P>of unfounded news."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Smith said nothing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But," continued Anne, presently, "though there is no truth in my having



<P>this claim on Mr Elliot, I should be extremely happy to be of use to

you



<P>in any way that I could.&nbsp; Shall I mention to him your being in

Bath?



<P>Shall I take any message?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, I thank you:&nbsp; no, certainly not.&nbsp; In the warmth of the

moment,



<P>and under a mistaken impression, I might, perhaps, have endeavoured



<P>to interest you in some circumstances; but not now.&nbsp; No, I thank

you,



<P>I have nothing to trouble you with."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I think you spoke of having known Mr Elliot many years?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I did."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Not before he was married, I suppose?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes; he was not married when I knew him first."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And--were you much acquainted?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Intimately."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Indeed!&nbsp; Then do tell me what he was at that time of life.



<P>I have a great curiosity to know what Mr Elliot was as a very young

man.



<P>Was he at all such as he appears now?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I have not seen Mr Elliot these three years," was Mrs Smith's answer,



<P>given so gravely that it was impossible to pursue the subject farther;



<P>and Anne felt that she had gained nothing but an increase of curiosity.



<P>They were both silent:&nbsp; Mrs Smith very thoughtful.&nbsp; At last--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I beg your pardon, my dear Miss Elliot," she cried, in her



<P>natural tone of cordiality, "I beg your pardon for the short answers



<P>I have been giving you, but I have been uncertain what I ought to do.



<P>I have been doubting and considering as to what I ought to tell you.



<P>There were many things to be taken into the account.&nbsp; One hates



<P>to be officious, to be giving bad impressions, making mischief.



<P>Even the smooth surface of family-union seems worth preserving,



<P>though there may be nothing durable beneath.&nbsp; However, I have determined;



<P>I think I am right; I think you ought to be made acquainted



<P>with Mr Elliot's real character.&nbsp; Though I fully believe that,



<P>at present, you have not the smallest intention of accepting him,



<P>there is no saying what may happen.&nbsp; You might, some time or other,



<P>be differently affected towards him.&nbsp; Hear the truth, therefore,



<P>now, while you are unprejudiced.&nbsp; Mr Elliot is a man without heart



<P>or conscience; a designing, wary, cold-blooded being, who thinks



<P>only of himself; whom for his own interest or ease, would be guilty



<P>of any cruelty, or any treachery, that could be perpetrated without



<P>risk of his general character.&nbsp; He has no feeling for others.



<P>Those whom he has been the chief cause of leading into ruin,



<P>he can neglect and desert without the smallest compunction.



<P>He is totally beyond the reach of any sentiment of justice or compassion.



<P>Oh! he is black at heart, hollow and black!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne's astonished air, and exclamation of wonder, made her pause,



<P>and in a calmer manner, she added,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"My expressions startle you.&nbsp; You must allow for an injured, angry

woman.



<P>But I will try to command myself.&nbsp; I will not abuse him.



<P>I will only tell you what I have found him.&nbsp; Facts shall speak.



<P>He was the intimate friend of my dear husband, who trusted and loved

him,



<P>and thought him as good as himself.&nbsp; The intimacy had been formed



<P>before our marriage.&nbsp; I found them most intimate friends; and I,

too,



<P>became excessively pleased with Mr Elliot, and entertained



<P>the highest opinion of him.&nbsp; At nineteen, you know, one does not



<P>think very seriously; but Mr Elliot appeared to me quite as good as

others,



<P>and much more agreeable than most others, and we were almost



<P>always together.&nbsp; We were principally in town, living in very good

style.



<P>He was then the inferior in circumstances; he was then the poor one;



<P>he had chambers in the Temple, and it was as much as he could do



<P>to support the appearance of a gentleman.&nbsp; He had always a home



<P>with us whenever he chose it; he was always welcome; he was like a brother.



<P>My poor Charles, who had the finest, most generous spirit in the world,



<P>would have divided his last farthing with him; and I know that his purse



<P>was open to him; I know that he often assisted him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"This must have been about that very period of Mr Elliot's life,"



<P>said Anne, "which has always excited my particular curiosity.



<P>It must have been about the same time that he became known to



<P>my father and sister.&nbsp; I never knew him myself; I only heard of

him;



<P>but there was a something in his conduct then, with regard to



<P>my father and sister, and afterwards in the circumstances of his marriage,



<P>which I never could quite reconcile with present times.&nbsp; It seemed



<P>to announce a different sort of man."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I know it all, I know it all," cried Mrs Smith.&nbsp; "He had been



<P>introduced to Sir Walter and your sister before I was acquainted with

him,



<P>but I heard him speak of them for ever.&nbsp; I know he was invited



<P>and encouraged, and I know he did not choose to go.&nbsp; I can satisfy

you,



<P>perhaps, on points which you would little expect; and as to his marriage,



<P>I knew all about it at the time.&nbsp; I was privy to all the fors and

againsts;



<P>I was the friend to whom he confided his hopes and plans; and though



<P>I did not know his wife previously, her inferior situation in society,



<P>indeed, rendered that impossible, yet I knew her all her life afterwards,



<P>or at least till within the last two years of her life, and can answer



<P>any question you may wish to put."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Nay," said Anne, "I have no particular enquiry to make about her.



<P>I have always understood they were not a happy couple.&nbsp; But I should



<P>like to know why, at that time of his life, he should slight



<P>my father's acquaintance as he did.&nbsp; My father was certainly disposed



<P>to take very kind and proper notice of him.&nbsp; Why did Mr Elliot

draw back?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Mr Elliot," replied Mrs Smith, "at that period of his life,



<P>had one object in view:&nbsp; to make his fortune, and by a rather quicker



<P>process than the law.&nbsp; He was determined to make it by marriage.



<P>He was determined, at least, not to mar it by an imprudent marriage;



<P>and I know it was his belief (whether justly or not, of course



<P>I cannot decide), that your father and sister, in their civilities



<P>and invitations, were designing a match between the heir



<P>and the young lady, and it was impossible that such a match



<P>should have answered his ideas of wealth and independence.



<P>That was his motive for drawing back, I can assure you.



<P>He told me the whole story.&nbsp; He had no concealments with me.



<P>It was curious, that having just left you behind me in Bath,



<P>my first and principal acquaintance on marrying should be your cousin;



<P>and that, through him, I should be continually hearing of your father



<P>and sister.&nbsp; He described one Miss Elliot, and I thought



<P>very affectionately of the other."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Perhaps," cried Anne, struck by a sudden idea, "you sometimes



<P>spoke of me to Mr Elliot?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"To be sure I did; very often.&nbsp; I used to boast of my own Anne

Elliot,



<P>and vouch for your being a very different creature from--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She checked herself just in time.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"This accounts for something which Mr Elliot said last night,"



<P>cried Anne.&nbsp; "This explains it.&nbsp; I found he had been used

to hear of me.



<P>I could not comprehend how.&nbsp; What wild imaginations one forms where



<P>dear self is concerned!&nbsp; How sure to be mistaken!&nbsp; But I beg

your pardon;



<P>I have interrupted you.&nbsp; Mr Elliot married then completely for

money?



<P>The circumstances, probably, which first opened your eyes



<P>to his character."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Smith hesitated a little here.&nbsp; "Oh! those things are too common.



<P>When one lives in the world, a man or woman's marrying for money



<P>is too common to strike one as it ought.&nbsp; I was very young,



<P>and associated only with the young, and we were a thoughtless,



<P>gay set, without any strict rules of conduct.&nbsp; We lived for enjoyment.



<P>I think differently now; time and sickness and sorrow have given me



<P>other notions; but at that period I must own I saw nothing reprehensible



<P>in what Mr Elliot was doing.&nbsp; `To do the best for himself,'



<P>passed as a duty."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But was not she a very low woman?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes; which I objected to, but he would not regard.&nbsp; Money, money,



<P>was all that he wanted.&nbsp; Her father was a grazier, her grandfather



<P>had been a butcher, but that was all nothing.&nbsp; She was a fine woman,



<P>had had a decent education, was brought forward by some cousins,



<P>thrown by chance into Mr Elliot's company, and fell in love with him;



<P>and not a difficulty or a scruple was there on his side,



<P>with respect to her birth.&nbsp; All his caution was spent in being

secured



<P>of the real amount of her fortune, before he committed himself.



<P>Depend upon it, whatever esteem Mr Elliot may have for his own situation



<P>in life now, as a young man he had not the smallest value for it.



<P>His chance for the Kellynch estate was something, but all the honour



<P>of the family he held as cheap as dirt.&nbsp; I have often heard him

declare,



<P>that if baronetcies were saleable, anybody should have his



<P>for fifty pounds, arms and motto, name and livery included;



<P>but I will not pretend to repeat half that I used to hear him say



<P>on that subject.&nbsp; It would not be fair; and yet you ought to have

proof,



<P>for what is all this but assertion, and you shall have proof."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Indeed, my dear Mrs Smith, I want none," cried Anne.&nbsp; "You have

asserted



<P>nothing contradictory to what Mr Elliot appeared to be some years ago.



<P>This is all in confirmation, rather, of what we used to hear and believe.



<P>I am more curious to know why he should be so different now."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But for my satisfaction, if you will have the goodness to ring for

Mary;



<P>stay:&nbsp; I am sure you will have the still greater goodness of



<P>going yourself into my bedroom, and bringing me the small inlaid box



<P>which you will find on the upper shelf of the closet."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne, seeing her friend to be earnestly bent on it, did as she was desired.



<P>The box was brought and placed before her, and Mrs Smith, sighing over

it



<P>as she unlocked it, said--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"This is full of papers belonging to him, to my husband;



<P>a small portion only of what I had to look over when I lost him.



<P>The letter I am looking for was one written by Mr Elliot to him



<P>before our marriage, and happened to be saved; why, one can hardly imagine.



<P>But he was careless and immethodical, like other men, about those things;



<P>and when I came to examine his papers, I found it with others



<P>still more trivial, from different people scattered here and there,



<P>while many letters and memorandums of real importance had been destroyed.



<P>Here it is; I would not burn it, because being even then very little



<P>satisfied with Mr Elliot, I was determined to preserve every document



<P>of former intimacy.&nbsp; I have now another motive for being glad



<P>that I can produce it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This was the letter, directed to "Charles Smith, Esq. Tunbridge Wells,"



<P>and dated from London, as far back as July, 1803: --

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Dear Smith,--I have received yours.&nbsp; Your kindness almost overpowers

me.



<P>I wish nature had made such hearts as yours more common, but I have



<P>lived three-and-twenty years in the world, and have seen none like it.



<P>At present, believe me, I have no need of your services,



<P>being in cash again.&nbsp; Give me joy:&nbsp; I have got rid of Sir

Walter and Miss.



<P>They are gone back to Kellynch, and almost made me swear to visit them



<P>this summer; but my first visit to Kellynch will be with a surveyor,



<P>to tell me how to bring it with best advantage to the hammer.



<P>The baronet, nevertheless, is not unlikely to marry again;



<P>he is quite fool enough.&nbsp; If he does, however, they will leave

me in peace,



<P>which may be a decent equivalent&nbsp; for the reversion.&nbsp; He is

worse



<P>than last year.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I wish I had any name but Elliot.&nbsp; I am sick of it.&nbsp; The

name of Walter



<P>I can drop, thank God! and I desire you will never insult me



<P>with my second W. again, meaning, for the rest of my life,



<P>to be only yours truly,--Wm. Elliot."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Such a letter could not be read without putting Anne in a glow;



<P>and Mrs Smith, observing the high colour in her face, said--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"The language, I know, is highly disrespectful.&nbsp; Though I have

forgot



<P>the exact terms, I have a perfect impression of the general meaning.



<P>But it shows you the man.&nbsp; Mark his professions to my poor husband.



<P>Can any thing be stronger?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne could not immediately get over the shock and mortification



<P>of finding such words applied to her father.&nbsp; She was obliged to

recollect



<P>that her seeing the letter was a violation of the laws of honour,



<P>that no one ought to be judged or to be known by such testimonies,



<P>that no private correspondence could bear the eye of others,



<P>before she could recover calmness enough to return the letter



<P>which she had been meditating over, and say--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Thank you.&nbsp; This is full proof undoubtedly; proof of every thing



<P>you were saying.&nbsp; But why be acquainted with us now?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I can explain this too," cried Mrs Smith, smiling.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Can you really?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes.&nbsp; I have shewn you Mr Elliot as he was a dozen years ago,



<P>and I will shew him as he is now.&nbsp; I cannot produce written proof

again,



<P>but I can give as authentic oral testimony as you can desire, of what



<P>he is now wanting, and what he is now doing.&nbsp; He is no hypocrite

now.



<P>He truly wants to marry you.&nbsp; His present attentions to your family



<P>are very sincere:&nbsp; quite from the heart.&nbsp; I will give you

my authority:



<P>his friend Colonel Wallis."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Colonel Wallis! you are acquainted with him?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No.&nbsp; It does not come to me in quite so direct a line as that;



<P>it takes a bend or two, but nothing of consequence.&nbsp; The stream



<P>is as good as at first; the little rubbish it collects in the turnings



<P>is easily moved away.&nbsp; Mr Elliot talks unreservedly to Colonel

Wallis



<P>of his views on you, which said Colonel Wallis, I imagine to be,



<P>in himself, a sensible, careful, discerning sort of character;



<P>but Colonel Wallis has a very pretty silly wife, to whom



<P>he tells things which he had better not, and he repeats it all to her.



<P>She in the overflowing spirits of her recovery, repeats it all



<P>to her nurse; and the nurse&nbsp; knowing my acquaintance with you,



<P>very naturally brings it all to me.&nbsp; On Monday evening, my good

friend



<P>Mrs Rooke let me thus much into the secrets of Marlborough Buildings.



<P>When I talked of a whole history, therefore, you see I was



<P>not romancing so much as you supposed."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"My dear Mrs Smith, your authority is deficient.&nbsp; This will not

do.



<P>Mr Elliot's having any views on me will not in the least account



<P>for the efforts he made towards a reconciliation with my father.



<P>That was all prior to my coming to Bath.&nbsp; I found them on



<P>the most friendly terms when I arrived."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I know you did; I know it all perfectly, but--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Indeed, Mrs Smith, we must not expect to get real information



<P>in such a line.&nbsp; Facts or opinions which are to pass through the

hands



<P>of so many, to be misconceived by folly in one, and ignorance in another,



<P>can hardly have much truth left."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Only give me a hearing.&nbsp; You will soon be able to judge of



<P>the general credit due, by listening to some particulars



<P>which you can yourself immediately contradict or confirm.



<P>Nobody supposes that you were his first inducement.&nbsp; He had seen

you



<P>indeed, before he came to Bath, and admired you, but without



<P>knowing it to be you.&nbsp; So says my historian, at least.&nbsp; Is

this true?



<P>Did he see you last summer or autumn, `somewhere down in the west,'



<P>to use her own words, without knowing it to be you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"He certainly did.&nbsp; So far it is very true.&nbsp; At Lyme.



<P>I happened to be at Lyme."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well," continued Mrs Smith, triumphantly, "grant my friend the credit



<P>due to the establishment of the first point asserted.&nbsp; He saw you

then



<P>at Lyme, and liked you so well as to be exceedingly pleased



<P>to meet with you again in Camden Place, as Miss Anne Elliot,



<P>and from that moment, I have no doubt, had a double motive



<P>in his visits there.&nbsp; But there was another, and an earlier,



<P>which I will now explain.&nbsp; If there is anything in my story which

you know



<P>to be either false or improbable, stop me.&nbsp; My account states,



<P>that your sister's friend, the lady now staying with you,



<P>whom I have heard you mention, came to Bath with Miss Elliot and Sir

Walter



<P>as long ago as September (in short when they first came themselves),



<P>and has been staying there ever since; that she is a clever, insinuating,



<P>handsome woman, poor and plausible, and altogether such in situation



<P>and manner, as to give a general idea, among Sir Walter's acquaintance,



<P>of her meaning to be Lady Elliot, and as general a surprise



<P>that Miss Elliot should be apparently, blind to the danger."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Here Mrs Smith paused a moment; but Anne had not a word to say,



<P>and she continued--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"This was the light in which it appeared to those who knew the family,



<P>long before you returned to it; and Colonel Wallis had his eye



<P>upon your father enough to be sensible of it, though he did not then



<P>visit in Camden Place; but his regard for Mr Elliot gave him an interest



<P>in watching all that was going on there, and when Mr Elliot came to

Bath



<P>for a day or two, as he happened to do a little before Christmas,



<P>Colonel Wallis made him acquainted with the appearance of things,



<P>and the reports beginning to prevail.&nbsp; Now you are to understand,



<P>that time had worked a very material change in Mr Elliot's opinions



<P>as to the value of a baronetcy.&nbsp; Upon all points of blood and connexion



<P>he is a completely altered man.&nbsp; Having long had as much money



<P>as he could spend, nothing to wish for on the side of avarice



<P>or indulgence, he has been gradually learning to pin his happiness



<P>upon the consequence he is heir to.&nbsp; I thought it coming on



<P>before our acquaintance ceased, but it is now a confirmed feeling.



<P>He cannot bear the idea of not being Sir William.&nbsp; You may guess,



<P>therefore, that the news he heard from his friend could not be



<P>very agreeable, and you may guess what it produced; the resolution



<P>of coming back to Bath as soon as possible, and of fixing himself here



<P>for a time, with the view of renewing his former acquaintance,



<P>and recovering such a footing in the family as might give him the means



<P>of ascertaining the degree of his danger, and of circumventing the lady



<P>if he found it material.&nbsp; This was agreed upon between the two

friends



<P>as the only thing to be done; and Colonel Wallis was to assist



<P>in every way that he could.&nbsp; He was to be introduced, and Mrs Wallis



<P>was to be introduced, and everybody was to be introduced.



<P>Mr Elliot came back accordingly; and on application was forgiven,



<P>as you know, and re-admitted into the family; and there it was



<P>his constant object, and his only object (till your arrival



<P>added another motive), to watch Sir Walter and Mrs Clay.



<P>He omitted no opportunity of being with them, threw himself in their

way,



<P>called at all hours; but I need not be particular on this subject.



<P>You can imagine what an artful man would do; and with this guide,



<P>perhaps, may recollect what you have seen him do."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes," said Anne, "you tell me nothing which does not accord with



<P>what I have known, or could imagine.&nbsp; There is always something

offensive



<P>in the details of cunning.&nbsp; The manoeuvres of selfishness and duplicity



<P>must ever be revolting, but I have heard nothing which really surprises

me.



<P>I know those who would be shocked by such a representation of Mr Elliot,



<P>who would have difficulty in believing it; but I have never been satisfied.



<P>I have always wanted some other motive for his conduct than appeared.



<P>I should like to know his present opinion, as to the probability



<P>of the event he has been in dread of; whether he considers the danger



<P>to be lessening or not."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Lessening, I understand," replied Mrs Smith.&nbsp; "He thinks Mrs Clay



<P>afraid of him, aware that he sees through her, and not daring to proceed



<P>as she might do in his absence.&nbsp; But since he must be absent



<P>some time or other, I do not perceive how he can ever be secure



<P>while she holds her present influence.&nbsp; Mrs Wallis has an amusing

idea,



<P>as nurse tells me, that it is to be put into the marriage articles



<P>when you and Mr Elliot marry, that your father is not to marry Mrs Clay.



<P>A scheme, worthy of Mrs Wallis's understanding, by all accounts;



<P>but my sensible nurse Rooke sees the absurdity of it.&nbsp; `Why, to

be sure,



<P>ma'am,' said she, `it would not prevent his marrying anybody else.'



<P>And, indeed, to own the truth, I do not think nurse, in her heart,



<P>is a very strenuous opposer of Sir Walter's making a second match.



<P>She must be allowed to be a favourer of matrimony, you know;



<P>and (since self will intrude) who can say that she may not have



<P>some flying visions of attending the next Lady Elliot, through



<P>Mrs Wallis's recommendation?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I am very glad to know all this," said Anne, after a little



<P>thoughtfulness.&nbsp; "It will be more painful to me in some respects



<P>to be in company with him, but I shall know better what to do.



<P>My line of conduct will be more direct.&nbsp; Mr Elliot is evidently



<P>a disingenuous, artificial, worldly man, who has never had



<P>any better principle to guide him than selfishness."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But Mr Elliot was not done with.&nbsp; Mrs Smith had been carried away



<P>from her first direction, and Anne had forgotten, in the interest



<P>of her own family concerns, how much had been originally implied



<P>against him; but her attention was now called to the explanation



<P>of those first hints, and she listened to a recital which,



<P>if it did not perfectly justify the unqualified bitterness of Mrs Smith,



<P>proved him to have been very unfeeling in his conduct towards her;



<P>very deficient both in justice and compassion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She learned that (the intimacy between them continuing unimpaired



<P>by Mr Elliot's marriage) they had been as before always together,



<P>and Mr Elliot had led his friend into expenses much beyond his fortune.



<P>Mrs Smith did not want to take blame to herself, and was most tender



<P>of throwing any on her husband; but Anne could collect that their income



<P>had never been equal to their style of living, and that from the first



<P>there had been a great deal of general and joint extravagance.



<P>From his wife's account of him she could discern Mr Smith to have been



<P>a man of warm feelings, easy temper, careless habits, and not strong



<P>understanding, much more amiable than his friend, and very unlike him,



<P>led by him, and probably despised by him.&nbsp; Mr Elliot, raised by



<P>his marriage to great affluence, and disposed to every gratification



<P>of pleasure and vanity which could be commanded without involving himself,



<P>(for with all his self-indulgence he had become a prudent man),



<P>and beginning to be rich, just as his friend ought to have found himself



<P>to be poor, seemed to have had no concern at all for that friend's



<P>probable finances, but, on the contrary, had been prompting and



<P>encouraging expenses which could end only in ruin; and the Smiths



<P>accordingly had been ruined.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The husband had died just in time to be spared the full knowledge of

it.



<P>They had previously known embarrassments enough to try the friendship



<P>of their friends, and to prove that Mr Elliot's had better not be tried;



<P>but it was not till his death that the wretched state of his affairs



<P>was fully known.&nbsp; With a confidence in Mr Elliot's regard,



<P>more creditable to his feelings than his judgement, Mr Smith had



<P>appointed him the executor of his will; but Mr Elliot would not act,



<P>and the difficulties and distress which this refusal had heaped on her,



<P>in addition to the inevitable sufferings of her situation, had been

such



<P>as could not be related without anguish of spirit, or listened to



<P>without corresponding indignation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne was shewn some letters of his on the occasion, answers to



<P>urgent applications from Mrs Smith, which all breathed the same



<P>stern resolution of not engaging in a fruitless trouble, and,



<P>under a cold civility, the same hard-hearted indifference



<P>to any of the evils it might bring on her.&nbsp; It was a dreadful picture



<P>of ingratitude and inhumanity; and Anne felt, at some moments,



<P>that no flagrant open crime could have been worse.&nbsp; She had a great

deal



<P>to listen to; all the particulars of past sad scenes, all the minutiae



<P>of distress upon distress, which in former conversations had been



<P>merely hinted at, were dwelt on now with a natural indulgence.



<P>Anne could perfectly comprehend the exquisite relief, and was only



<P>the more inclined to wonder at the composure of her friend's



<P>usual state of mind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was one circumstance in the history of her grievances



<P>of particular irritation.&nbsp; She had good reason to believe



<P>that some property of her husband in the West Indies, which had been



<P>for many years under a sort of sequestration for the payment of



<P>its own incumbrances, might be recoverable by proper measures;



<P>and this property, though not large, would be enough to make her



<P>comparatively rich.&nbsp; But there was nobody to stir in it.



<P>Mr Elliot would do nothing, and she could do nothing herself,



<P>equally disabled from personal exertion by her state of bodily weakness,



<P>and from employing others by her want of money.&nbsp; She had no



<P>natural connexions to assist her even with their counsel,



<P>and she could not afford to purchase the assistance of the law.



<P>This was a cruel aggravation of actually streightened means.



<P>To feel that she ought to be in better circumstances, that a little

trouble



<P>in the right place might do it, and to fear that delay might be



<P>even weakening her claims, was hard to bear.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was on this point that she had hoped to engage Anne's good offices



<P>with Mr Elliot.&nbsp; She had previously, in the anticipation



<P>of their marriage, been very apprehensive of losing her friend by it;



<P>but on being assured that he could have made no attempt of that nature,



<P>since he did not even know her to be in Bath, it immediately occurred,



<P>that something might be done in her favour by the influence of the woman



<P>he loved, and she had been hastily preparing to interest Anne's feelings,



<P>as far as the observances due to Mr Elliot's character would allow,



<P>when Anne's refutation of the supposed engagement changed



<P>the face of everything; and while it took from her the new-formed hope



<P>of succeeding in the object of her first anxiety, left her at least



<P>the comfort of telling the whole story her own way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After listening to this full description of Mr Elliot, Anne could not

but



<P>express some surprise at Mrs Smith's having spoken of him so favourably



<P>in the beginning of their conversation.&nbsp; "She had seemed to recommend



<P>and praise him!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"My dear," was Mrs Smith's reply, "there was nothing else to be done.



<P>I considered your marrying him as certain, though he might not yet



<P>have made the offer, and I could no more speak the truth of him,



<P>than if he had been your husband.&nbsp; My heart bled for you,



<P>as I talked of happiness; and yet he is sensible, he is agreeable,



<P>and with such a woman as you, it was not absolutely hopeless.



<P>He was very unkind to his first wife.&nbsp; They were wretched together.



<P>But she was too ignorant and giddy for respect, and he had never loved

her.



<P>I was willing to hope that you must fare better."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne could just acknowledge within herself such a possibility



<P>of having been induced to marry him, as made her shudder at the idea



<P>of the misery which must have followed.&nbsp; It was just possible that



<P>she might have been persuaded by Lady Russell!&nbsp; And under such



<P>a supposition, which would have been most miserable, when time had



<P>disclosed all, too late?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was very desirable that Lady Russell should be no longer deceived;



<P>and one of the concluding arrangements of this important conference,



<P>which carried them through the greater part of the morning,



<P>was, that Anne had full liberty to communicate to her friend



<P>everything relative to Mrs Smith, in which his conduct was involved.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 19</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 19</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>While Admiral Croft was taking this walk with Anne, and expressing



<P>his wish of getting Captain Wentworth to Bath, Captain Wentworth



<P>was already on his way thither.&nbsp; Before Mrs Croft had written,



<P>he was arrived, and the very next time Anne walked out, she saw him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr Elliot was attending his two cousins and Mrs Clay.&nbsp; They were



<P>in Milsom Street.&nbsp; It began to rain, not much, but enough to



<P>make shelter desirable for women, and quite enough to make it



<P>very desirable for Miss Elliot to have the advantage of being



<P>conveyed home in Lady Dalrymple's carriage, which was seen waiting



<P>at a little distance; she, Anne, and Mrs Clay, therefore,



<P>turned into Molland's, while Mr Elliot stepped to Lady Dalrymple,



<P>to request her assistance.&nbsp; He soon joined them again, successful,



<P>of course; Lady Dalrymple would be most happy to take them home,



<P>and would call for them in a few minutes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Her ladyship's carriage was a barouche, and did not hold



<P>more than four with any comfort.&nbsp; Miss Carteret was with her mother;



<P>consequently it was not reasonable to expect accommodation



<P>for all the three Camden Place ladies.&nbsp; There could be no doubt



<P>as to Miss Elliot.&nbsp; Whoever suffered inconvenience, she must suffer

none,



<P>but it occupied a little time to settle the point of civility



<P>between the other two.&nbsp; The rain was a mere trifle, and Anne was



<P>most sincere in preferring a walk with Mr Elliot.&nbsp; But the rain

was also



<P>a mere trifle to Mrs Clay; she would hardly allow it even to drop at

all,



<P>and her boots were so thick! much thicker than Miss Anne's;



<P>and, in short, her civility rendered her quite as anxious to be left



<P>to walk with Mr Elliot as Anne could be, and it was discussed between

them



<P>with a generosity so polite and so determined, that the others were



<P>obliged to settle it for them; Miss Elliot maintaining that Mrs Clay



<P>had a little cold already, and Mr Elliot deciding on appeal,



<P>that his cousin Anne's boots were rather the thickest.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was fixed accordingly, that Mrs Clay should be of the party



<P>in the carriage; and they had just reached this point, when Anne,



<P>as she sat near the window, descried, most decidedly and distinctly,



<P>Captain Wentworth walking down the street.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Her start was perceptible only to herself; but she instantly felt that



<P>she was the greatest simpleton in the world, the most unaccountable



<P>and absurd!&nbsp; For a few minutes she saw nothing before her;



<P>it was all confusion.&nbsp; She was lost, and when she had scolded



<P>back her senses, she found the others still waiting for the carriage,



<P>and Mr Elliot (always obliging) just setting off for Union Street



<P>on a commission of Mrs Clay's.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She now felt a great inclination to go to the outer door;



<P>she wanted to see if it rained.&nbsp; Why was she to suspect herself



<P>of another motive?&nbsp; Captain Wentworth must be out of sight.



<P>She left her seat, she would go; one half of her should not be always



<P>so much wiser than the other half, or always suspecting the other



<P>of being worse than it was.&nbsp; She would see if it rained.



<P>She was sent back, however, in a moment by the entrance of



<P>Captain Wentworth himself, among a party of gentlemen and ladies,



<P>evidently his acquaintance, and whom he must have joined



<P>a little below Milsom Street.&nbsp; He was more obviously struck



<P>and confused by the sight of her than she had ever observed before;



<P>he looked quite red.&nbsp; For the first time, since their renewed acquaintance,



<P>she felt that she was betraying the least sensibility of the two.



<P>She had the advantage of him in the preparation of the last few moments.



<P>All the overpowering, blinding, bewildering, first effects



<P>of strong surprise were over with her.&nbsp; Still, however,



<P>she had enough to feel!&nbsp; It was agitation, pain, pleasure,



<P>a something between delight and misery.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He spoke to her, and then turned away.&nbsp; The character of his manner



<P>was embarrassment.&nbsp; She could not have called it either cold or

friendly,



<P>or anything so certainly as embarrassed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a short interval, however, he came towards her, and spoke again.



<P>Mutual enquiries on common subjects passed:&nbsp; neither of them, probably,



<P>much the wiser for what they heard, and Anne continuing fully sensible



<P>of his being less at ease than formerly.&nbsp; They had by dint of being



<P>so very much together, got to speak to each other with a considerable



<P>portion of apparent indifference and calmness; but he could not do it

now.



<P>Time had changed him, or Louisa had changed him.&nbsp; There was consciousness



<P>of some sort or other.&nbsp; He looked very well, not as if he had been



<P>suffering in health or spirits, and he talked of Uppercross,



<P>of the Musgroves, nay, even of Louisa, and had even a momentary look



<P>of his own arch significance as he named her; but yet it was



<P>Captain Wentworth not comfortable, not easy, not able to feign that

he was.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It did not surprise, but it grieved Anne to observe that Elizabeth



<P>would not know him.&nbsp; She saw that he saw Elizabeth, that Elizabeth

saw him,



<P>that there was complete internal recognition on each side;



<P>she was convinced that he was ready to be acknowledged as an acquaintance,



<P>expecting it, and she had the pain of seeing her sister turn away



<P>with unalterable coldness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lady Dalrymple's carriage, for which Miss Elliot was growing



<P>very impatient, now drew up; the servant came in to announce it.



<P>It was beginning to rain again, and altogether there was a delay,



<P>and a bustle, and a talking, which must make all the little crowd



<P>in the shop understand that Lady Dalrymple was calling to convey



<P>Miss Elliot.&nbsp; At last Miss Elliot and her friend, unattended but



<P>by the servant, (for there was no cousin returned), were walking off;



<P>and Captain Wentworth, watching them, turned again to Anne,



<P>and by manner, rather than words, was offering his services to her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I am much obliged to you," was her answer, "but I am not going with

them.



<P>The carriage would not accommodate so many.&nbsp; I walk:&nbsp; I prefer

walking."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"But it rains."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! very little,&nbsp; Nothing that I regard."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a moment's pause he said:&nbsp; "Though I came only yesterday,



<P>I have equipped myself properly for Bath already, you see,"



<P>(pointing to a new umbrella); "I wish you would make use of it,



<P>if you are determined to walk; though I think it would be more prudent



<P>to let me get you a chair."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She was very much obliged to him, but declined it all, repeating



<P>her conviction, that the rain would come to nothing at present,



<P>and adding, "I am only waiting for Mr Elliot.&nbsp; He will be here

in a moment,



<P>I am sure."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She had hardly spoken the words when Mr Elliot walked in.



<P>Captain Wentworth recollected him perfectly.&nbsp; There was no difference



<P>between him and the man who had stood on the steps at Lyme,



<P>admiring Anne as she passed, except in the air and look and manner



<P>of the privileged relation and friend.&nbsp; He came in with eagerness,



<P>appeared to see and think only of her, apologised for his stay,



<P>was grieved to have kept her waiting, and anxious to get her away



<P>without further loss of time and before the rain increased;



<P>and in another moment they walked off together, her arm under his,



<P>a gentle and embarrassed glance, and a "Good morning to you!"



<P>being all that she had time for, as she passed away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As soon as they were out of sight, the ladies of Captain Wentworth's

party



<P>began talking of them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Mr Elliot does not dislike his cousin, I fancy?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! no, that is clear enough.&nbsp; One can guess what will happen

there.



<P>He is always with them; half lives in the family, I believe.



<P>What a very good-looking man!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, and Miss Atkinson, who dined with him once at the Wallises,



<P>says he is the most agreeable man she ever was in company with."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"She is pretty, I think; Anne Elliot; very pretty, when one comes



<P>to look at her.&nbsp; It is not the fashion to say so, but I confess



<P>I admire her more than her sister."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! so do I."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"And so do I.&nbsp; No comparison.&nbsp; But the men are all wild after

Miss Elliot.



<P>Anne is too delicate for them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne would have been particularly obliged to her cousin, if he would

have



<P>walked by her side all the way to Camden Place, without saying a word.



<P>She had never found it so difficult to listen to him, though nothing



<P>could exceed his solicitude and care, and though his subjects



<P>were principally such as were wont to be always interesting:



<P>praise, warm, just, and discriminating, of Lady Russell,



<P>and insinuations highly rational against Mrs Clay.&nbsp; But just now



<P>she could think only of Captain Wentworth.&nbsp; She could not understand



<P>his present feelings, whether he were really suffering much



<P>from disappointment or not; and till that point were settled,



<P>she could not be quite herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She hoped to be wise and reasonable in time; but alas! alas!



<P>she must confess to herself that she was not wise yet.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Another circumstance very essential for her to know, was how long



<P>he meant to be in Bath; he had not mentioned it, or she could not



<P>recollect it.&nbsp; He might be only passing through.&nbsp; But it was

more probable



<P>that he should be come to stay.&nbsp; In that case, so liable as every

body was



<P>to meet every body in Bath, Lady Russell would in all likelihood



<P>see him somewhere.&nbsp; Would she recollect him?&nbsp; How would it

all be?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She had already been obliged to tell Lady Russell that Louisa Musgrove



<P>was to marry Captain Benwick.&nbsp; It had cost her something to encounter



<P>Lady Russell's surprise; and now, if she were by any chance



<P>to be thrown into company with Captain Wentworth, her imperfect knowledge



<P>of the matter might add another shade of prejudice against him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The following morning Anne was out with her friend, and for the first

hour,



<P>in an incessant and fearful sort of watch for him in vain; but at last,



<P>in returning down Pulteney Street, she distinguished him



<P>on the right hand pavement at such a distance as to have him in view



<P>the greater part of the street.&nbsp; There were many other men about

him,



<P>many groups walking the same way, but there was no mistaking him.



<P>She looked instinctively at Lady Russell; but not from any mad idea



<P>of her recognising him so soon as she did herself.&nbsp; No, it was



<P>not to be supposed that Lady Russell would perceive him till they



<P>were nearly opposite.&nbsp; She looked at her however, from time to

time,



<P>anxiously; and when the moment approached which must point him out,



<P>though not daring to look again (for her own countenance she knew



<P>was unfit to be seen), she was yet perfectly conscious of



<P>Lady Russell's eyes being turned exactly in the direction for him--



<P>of her being, in short, intently observing him.&nbsp; She could thoroughly



<P>comprehend the sort of fascination he must possess over Lady Russell's

mind,



<P>the difficulty it must be for her to withdraw her eyes, the astonishment



<P>she must be feeling that eight or nine years should have passed over

him,



<P>and in foreign climes and in active service too, without robbing him



<P>of one personal grace!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At last, Lady Russell drew back her head.&nbsp; "Now, how would she



<P>speak of him?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You will wonder," said she, "what has been fixing my eye so long;



<P>but I was looking after some window-curtains, which Lady Alicia and



<P>Mrs Frankland were telling me of last night.&nbsp; They described



<P>the drawing-room window-curtains of one of the houses on this



<P>side of the way, and this part of the street, as being the handsomest



<P>and best hung of any in Bath, but could not recollect the exact number,



<P>and I have been trying to find out which it could be; but I confess



<P>I can see no curtains hereabouts that answer their description."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne sighed and blushed and smiled, in pity and disdain,



<P>either at her friend or herself.&nbsp; The part which provoked her most,



<P>was that in all this waste of foresight and caution, she should have



<P>lost the right moment for seeing whether he saw them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A day or two passed without producing anything.&nbsp; The theatre or

the rooms,



<P>where he was most likely to be, were not fashionable enough



<P>for the Elliots, whose evening amusements were solely in the



<P>elegant stupidity of private parties, in which they were getting



<P>more and more engaged; and Anne, wearied of such a state of stagnation,



<P>sick of knowing nothing, and fancying herself stronger because



<P>her strength was not tried, was quite impatient for the concert evening.



<P>It was a concert for the benefit of a person patronised by Lady Dalrymple.



<P>Of course they must attend.&nbsp; It was really expected to be a good

one,



<P>and Captain Wentworth was very fond of music.&nbsp; If she could only

have



<P>a few minutes conversation with him again, she fancied she should



<P>be satisfied; and as to the power of addressing him, she felt all over



<P>courage if the opportunity occurred.&nbsp; Elizabeth had turned from

him,



<P>Lady Russell overlooked him; her nerves were strengthened



<P>by these circumstances; she felt that she owed him attention.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She had once partly promised Mrs Smith to spend the evening with her;



<P>but in a short hurried call she excused herself and put it off,



<P>with the more decided promise of a longer visit on the morrow.



<P>Mrs Smith gave a most good-humoured acquiescence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"By all means," said she; "only tell me all about it, when you do come.



<P>Who is your party?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne named them all.&nbsp; Mrs Smith made no reply; but when she was



<P>leaving her said, and with an expression half serious, half arch,



<P>"Well, I heartily wish your concert may answer; and do not fail me



<P>to-morrow if you can come; for I begin to have a foreboding



<P>that I may not have many more visits from you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne was startled and confused; but after standing in a moment's suspense,



<P>was obliged, and not sorry to be obliged, to hurry away.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 24</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 24</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Who can be in doubt of what followed?&nbsp; When any two young people



<P>take it into their heads to marry, they are pretty sure by perseverance



<P>to carry their point, be they ever so poor, or ever so imprudent,



<P>or ever so little likely to be necessary to each other's ultimate comfort.

<BR>This may be bad morality to conclude with, but I believe it to be truth;



<P>and if such parties succeed, how should a Captain Wentworth and



<P>an Anne Elliot, with the advantage of maturity of mind,



<P>consciousness of right, and one independent fortune between them,



<P>fail of bearing down every opposition?&nbsp; They might in fact,



<P>have borne down a great deal more than they met with, for there was



<P>little to distress them beyond the want of graciousness and warmth.

<BR>Sir Walter made no objection, and Elizabeth did nothing worse



<P>than look cold and unconcerned.&nbsp; Captain Wentworth, with five-and-twenty



<P>thousand pounds, and as high in his profession as merit and activity



<P>could place him, was no longer nobody.&nbsp; He was now esteemed quite

worthy



<P>to address the daughter of a foolish, spendthrift baronet,



<P>who had not had principle or sense enough to maintain himself



<P>in the situation in which Providence had placed him, and who could



<P>give his daughter at present but a small part of the share



<P>of ten thousand pounds which must be hers hereafter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sir Walter, indeed, though he had no affection for Anne,



<P>and no vanity flattered, to make him really happy on the occasion,



<P>was very far from thinking it a bad match for her.&nbsp; On the contrary,



<P>when he saw more of Captain Wentworth, saw him repeatedly by daylight,



<P>and eyed him well, he was very much struck by his personal claims,



<P>and felt that his superiority of appearance might be not unfairly balanced



<P>against her superiority of rank; and all this, assisted by



<P>his well-sounding name, enabled Sir Walter at last to prepare his pen,



<P>with a very good grace, for the insertion of the marriage



<P>in the volume of honour.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The only one among them, whose opposition of feeling could excite



<P>any serious anxiety was Lady Russell.&nbsp; Anne knew that Lady Russell



<P>must be suffering some pain in understanding and relinquishing Mr Elliot,



<P>and be making some struggles to become truly acquainted with,



<P>and do justice to Captain Wentworth.&nbsp; This however was what



<P>Lady Russell had now to do.&nbsp; She must learn to feel that she had



<P>been mistaken with regard to both; that she had been unfairly influenced



<P>by appearances in each; that because Captain Wentworth's manners



<P>had not suited her own ideas, she had been too quick in suspecting them



<P>to indicate a character of dangerous impetuosity; and that because



<P>Mr Elliot's manners had precisely pleased her in their propriety



<P>and correctness, their general politeness and suavity, she had been



<P>too quick in receiving them as the certain result of the most correct



<P>opinions and well-regulated mind.&nbsp; There was nothing less



<P>for Lady Russell to do, than to admit that she had been



<P>pretty completely wrong, and to take up a new set of opinions



<P>and of hopes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There is a quickness of perception in some, a nicety in the discernment



<P>of character, a natural penetration, in short, which no experience



<P>in others can equal, and Lady Russell had been less gifted



<P>in this part of understanding than her young friend.&nbsp; But she was



<P>a very good woman, and if her second object was to be sensible



<P>and well-judging, her first was to see Anne happy.&nbsp; She loved Anne



<P>better than she loved her own abilities; and when the awkwardness



<P>of the beginning was over, found little hardship in attaching herself



<P>as a mother to the man who was securing the happiness of her other child.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Of all the family, Mary was probably the one most immediately gratified



<P>by the circumstance.&nbsp; It was creditable to have a sister married,



<P>and she might flatter herself with having been greatly instrumental



<P>to the connexion, by keeping Anne with her in the autumn;



<P>and as her own sister must be better than her husband's sisters,



<P>it was very agreeable that Captain Wentworth should be a richer man

than



<P>either Captain Benwick or Charles Hayter.&nbsp; She had something to

suffer,



<P>perhaps, when they came into contact again, in seeing Anne restored



<P>to the rights of seniority, and the mistress of a very pretty landaulette;



<P>but she had a future to look forward to, of powerful consolation.

<BR>Anne had no Uppercross Hall before her, no landed estate,



<P>no headship of a family; and if they could but keep Captain Wentworth



<P>from being made a baronet, she would not change situations with Anne.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It would be well for the eldest sister if she were equally satisfied



<P>with her situation, for a change is not very probable there.

<BR>She had soon the mortification of seeing Mr Elliot withdraw,



<P>and no one of proper condition has since presented himself to raise



<P>even the unfounded hopes which sunk with him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The news of his cousins Anne's engagement burst on Mr Elliot



<P>most unexpectedly.&nbsp; It deranged his best plan of domestic happiness,



<P>his best hope of keeping Sir Walter single by the watchfulness



<P>which a son-in-law's rights would have given.&nbsp; But, though discomfited



<P>and disappointed, he could still do something for his own interest



<P>and his own enjoyment.&nbsp; He soon quitted Bath; and on Mrs Clay's



<P>quitting it soon afterwards, and being next heard of as established



<P>under his protection in London, it was evident how double a game



<P>he had been playing, and how determined he was to save himself



<P>from being cut out by one artful woman, at least.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Clay's affections had overpowered her interest, and she had sacrificed,



<P>for the young man's sake, the possibility of scheming longer



<P>for Sir Walter.&nbsp; She has abilities, however, as well as affections;



<P>and it is now a doubtful point whether his cunning, or hers,



<P>may finally carry the day; whether, after preventing her from being



<P>the wife of Sir Walter, he may not be wheedled and caressed at last



<P>into making her the wife of Sir William.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It cannot be doubted that Sir Walter and Elizabeth were shocked



<P>and mortified by the loss of their companion, and the discovery of



<P>their deception in her.&nbsp; They had their great cousins, to be sure,



<P>to resort to for comfort; but they must long feel that to flatter



<P>and follow others, without being flattered and followed in turn,



<P>is but a state of half enjoyment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne, satisfied at a very early period of Lady Russell's meaning



<P>to love Captain Wentworth as she ought, had no other alloy



<P>to the happiness of her prospects than what arose from the consciousness



<P>of having no relations to bestow on him which a man of sense could value.

<BR>There she felt her own inferiority very keenly.&nbsp; The disproportion



<P>in their fortune was nothing; it did not give her a moment's regret;



<P>but to have no family to receive and estimate him properly,



<P>nothing of respectability, of harmony, of good will to offer



<P>in return for all the worth and all the prompt welcome which met her



<P>in his brothers and sisters, was a source of as lively pain



<P>as her mind could well be sensible of under circumstances of otherwise



<P>strong felicity.&nbsp; She had but two friends in the world to add to

his list,



<P>Lady Russell and Mrs Smith.&nbsp; To those, however, he was very well

disposed



<P>to attach himself.&nbsp; Lady Russell, in spite of all her former transgressions,



<P>he could now value from his heart.&nbsp; While he was not obliged to

say



<P>that he believed her to have been right in originally dividing them,



<P>he was ready to say almost everything else in her favour,



<P>and as for Mrs Smith, she had claims of various kinds to recommend her



<P>quickly and permanently.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Her recent good offices by Anne had been enough in themselves,



<P>and their marriage, instead of depriving her of one friend,



<P>secured her two.&nbsp; She was their earliest visitor in their settled

life;



<P>and Captain Wentworth, by putting her in the way of recovering



<P>her husband's property in the West Indies, by writing for her,



<P>acting for her, and seeing her through all the petty difficulties



<P>of the case with the activity and exertion of a fearless man



<P>and a determined friend, fully requited the services which



<P>she had rendered, or ever meant to render, to his wife.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs Smith's enjoyments were not spoiled by this improvement of income,



<P>with some improvement of health, and the acquisition of such friends



<P>to be often with, for her cheerfulness and mental alacrity did not



<P>fail her; and while these prime supplies of good remained, she might

have



<P>bid defiance even to greater accessions of worldly prosperity.

<BR>She might have been absolutely rich and perfectly healthy,



<P>and yet be happy.&nbsp; Her spring of felicity was in the glow



<P>of her spirits, as her friend Anne's was in the warmth of her heart.

<BR>Anne was tenderness itself, and she had the full worth of it



<P>in Captain Wentworth's affection.&nbsp; His profession was all that

could ever



<P>make her friends wish that tenderness less, the dread of a future war



<P>all that could dim her sunshine.&nbsp; She gloried in being a sailor's

wife,



<P>but she must pay the tax of quick alarm for belonging to that profession



<P>which is, if possible, more distinguished in its domestic virtues



<P>than in its national importance.

<BR>&nbsp;



<P><B>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Finis</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Chapter 18</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

<B>Chapter 18</B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>It was the beginning of February; and Anne, having been a month in Bath,



<P>was growing very eager for news from Uppercross and Lyme.



<P>She wanted to hear much more than Mary had communicated.



<P>It was three weeks since she had heard at all.&nbsp; She only knew



<P>that Henrietta was at home again; and that Louisa, though considered

to be



<P>recovering fast, was still in Lyme; and she was thinking of them all



<P>very intently one evening, when a thicker letter than usual from Mary



<P>was delivered to her; and, to quicken the pleasure and surprise,



<P>with Admiral and Mrs Croft's compliments.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The Crofts must be in Bath!&nbsp; A circumstance to interest her.



<P>They were people whom her heart turned to very naturally.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What is this?" cried Sir Walter.&nbsp; "The Crofts have arrived in

Bath?



<P>The Crofts who rent Kellynch?&nbsp; What have they brought you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"A letter from Uppercross Cottage, Sir."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh! those letters are convenient passports.&nbsp; They secure an introduction.



<P>I should have visited Admiral Croft, however, at any rate.



<P>I know what is due to my tenant."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne could listen no longer; she could not even have told how



<P>the poor Admiral's complexion escaped; her letter engrossed her.



<P>It had been begun several days back.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; "February 1st.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"My dear Anne,--I make no apology for my silence, because I know



<P>how little people think of letters in such a place as Bath.



<P>You must be a great deal too happy to care for Uppercross, which,



<P>as you well know, affords little to write about.&nbsp; We have had



<P>a very dull Christmas; Mr and Mrs Musgrove have not had one dinner party



<P>all the holidays.&nbsp; I do not reckon the Hayters as anybody.



<P>The holidays, however, are over at last:&nbsp; I believe no children

ever had



<P>such long ones.&nbsp; I am sure I had not.&nbsp; The house was cleared

yesterday,



<P>except of the little Harvilles; but you will be surprised to hear



<P>they have never gone home.&nbsp; Mrs Harville must be an odd mother



<P>to part with them so long.&nbsp; I do not understand it.&nbsp; They

are



<P>not at all nice children, in my opinion; but Mrs Musgrove seems to



<P>like them quite as well, if not better, than her grandchildren.



<P>What dreadful weather we have had!&nbsp; It may not be felt in Bath,



<P>with your nice pavements; but in the country it is of some consequence.



<P>I have not had a creature call on me since the second week in January,



<P>except Charles Hayter, who had been calling much oftener than was welcome.



<P>Between ourselves, I think it a great pity Henrietta did not remain

at Lyme



<P>as long as Louisa; it would have kept her a little out of his way.



<P>The carriage is gone to-day, to bring Louisa and the Harvilles to-morrow.



<P>We are not asked to dine with them, however, till the day after,



<P>Mrs Musgrove is so afraid of her being fatigued by the journey,



<P>which is not very likely, considering the care that will be taken of

her;



<P>and it would be much more convenient to me to dine there to-morrow.



<P>I am glad you find Mr Elliot so agreeable, and wish I could be acquainted



<P>with him too; but I have my usual luck:&nbsp; I am always out of the

way



<P>when any thing desirable is going on; always the last of my family



<P>to be noticed.&nbsp; What an immense time Mrs Clay has been staying



<P>with Elizabeth!&nbsp; Does she never mean to go away?&nbsp; But perhaps



<P>if she were to leave the room vacant, we might not be invited.



<P>Let me know what you think of this.&nbsp; I do not expect my children



<P>to be asked, you know.&nbsp; I can leave them at the Great House very

well,



<P>for a month or six weeks.&nbsp; I have this moment heard that the Crofts



<P>are going to Bath almost immediately; they think the Admiral gouty.



<P>Charles heard it quite by chance; they have not had the civility



<P>to give me any notice, or of offering to take anything.



<P>I do not think they improve at all as neighbours.&nbsp; We see nothing

of them,



<P>and this is really an instance of gross inattention.&nbsp; Charles joins

me



<P>in love, and everything proper.&nbsp; Yours affectionately,



<P>&nbsp;"Mary M---.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I am sorry to say that I am very far from well; and Jemima has



<P>just told me that the butcher says there is a bad sore-throat



<P>very much about.&nbsp; I dare say I shall catch it; and my sore-throats,



<P>you know, are always worse than anybody's."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So ended the first part, which had been afterwards put into an envelope,



<P>containing nearly as much more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I kept my letter open, that I might send you word how Louisa



<P>bore her journey, and now I am extremely glad I did, having a great

deal



<P>to add.&nbsp; In the first place, I had a note from Mrs Croft yesterday,



<P>offering to convey anything to you; a very kind, friendly note indeed,



<P>addressed to me, just as it ought; I shall therefore be able to



<P>make my letter as long as I like.&nbsp; The Admiral does not seem very

ill,



<P>and I sincerely hope Bath will do him all the good he wants.



<P>I shall be truly glad to have them back again.&nbsp; Our neighbourhood



<P>cannot spare such a pleasant family.&nbsp; But now for Louisa.



<P>I have something to communicate that will astonish you not a little.



<P>She and the Harvilles came on Tuesday very safely, and in the evening



<P>we went to ask her how she did, when we were rather surprised



<P>not to find Captain Benwick of the party, for he had been invited



<P>as well as the Harvilles; and what do you think was the reason?



<P>Neither more nor less than his being in love with Louisa,



<P>and not choosing to venture to Uppercross till he had had an answer



<P>from Mr Musgrove; for it was all settled between him and her



<P>before she came away, and he had written to her father by Captain Harville.



<P>True, upon my honour!&nbsp; Are not you astonished?&nbsp; I shall be

surprised



<P>at least if you ever received a hint of it, for I never did.



<P>Mrs Musgrove protests solemnly that she knew nothing of the matter.



<P>We are all very well pleased, however, for though it is not equal to

her



<P>marrying Captain Wentworth, it is infinitely better than Charles Hayter;



<P>and Mr Musgrove has written his consent, and Captain Benwick



<P>is expected to-day.&nbsp; Mrs Harville says her husband feels a good

deal



<P>on his poor sister's account; but, however, Louisa is a great favourite



<P>with both.&nbsp; Indeed, Mrs Harville and I quite agree that we love

her



<P>the better for having nursed her.&nbsp; Charles wonders what Captain

Wentworth



<P>will say; but if you remember, I never thought him attached to Louisa;



<P>I never could see anything of it.&nbsp; And this is the end, you see,



<P>of Captain Benwick's being supposed to be an admirer of yours.



<P>How Charles could take such a thing into his head was always



<P>incomprehensible to me.&nbsp; I hope he will be more agreeable now.



<P>Certainly not a great match for Louisa Musgrove, but a million times

better



<P>than marrying among the Hayters."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mary need not have feared her sister's being in any degree prepared



<P>for the news.&nbsp; She had never in her life been more astonished.



<P>Captain Benwick and Louisa Musgrove!&nbsp; It was almost too wonderful



<P>for belief, and it was with the greatest effort that she could remain



<P>in the room, preserve an air of calmness, and answer the common questions



<P>of the moment.&nbsp; Happily for her, they were not many.&nbsp; Sir

Walter



<P>wanted to know whether the Crofts travelled with four horses,



<P>and whether they were likely to be situated in such a part of Bath



<P>as it might suit Miss Elliot and himself to visit in; but had



<P>little curiosity beyond.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"How is Mary?" said Elizabeth; and without waiting for an answer,



<P>"And pray what brings the Crofts to Bath?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"They come on the Admiral's account.&nbsp; He is thought to be gouty."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Gout and decrepitude!" said Sir Walter.&nbsp; "Poor old gentleman."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Have they any acquaintance here?" asked Elizabeth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I do not know; but I can hardly suppose that, at Admiral Croft's



<P>time of life, and in his profession, he should not have many acquaintance



<P>in such a place as this."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I suspect," said Sir Walter coolly, "that Admiral Croft



<P>will be best known in Bath as the renter of Kellynch Hall.



<P>Elizabeth, may we venture to present him and his wife in Laura Place?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, no! I think not.&nbsp; Situated as we are with Lady Dalrymple,

cousins,



<P>we ought to be very careful not to embarrass her with acquaintance



<P>she might not approve.&nbsp; If we were not related, it would not signify;



<P>but as cousins, she would feel scrupulous as to any proposal of ours.



<P>We had better leave the Crofts to find their own level.



<P>There are several odd-looking men walking about here, who,



<P>I am told, are sailors.&nbsp; The Crofts will associate with them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This was Sir Walter and Elizabeth's share of interest in the letter;



<P>when Mrs Clay had paid her tribute of more decent attention,



<P>in an enquiry after Mrs Charles Musgrove, and her fine little boys,



<P>Anne was at liberty.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In her own room, she tried to comprehend it.&nbsp; Well might Charles

wonder



<P>how Captain Wentworth would feel!&nbsp; Perhaps he had quitted the field,



<P>had given Louisa up, had ceased to love, had found he did not love her.



<P>She could not endure the idea of treachery or levity, or anything



<P>akin to ill usage between him and his friend.&nbsp; She could not endure



<P>that such a friendship as theirs should be severed unfairly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Captain Benwick and Louisa Musgrove!&nbsp; The high-spirited,



<P>joyous-talking Louisa Musgrove, and the dejected, thinking,



<P>feeling, reading, Captain Benwick, seemed each of them everything



<P>that would not suit the other.&nbsp; Their minds most dissimilar!



<P>Where could have been the attraction?&nbsp; The answer soon presented

itself.



<P>It had been in situation.&nbsp; They had been thrown together several

weeks;



<P>they had been living in the same small family party:&nbsp; since Henrietta's



<P>coming away, they must have been depending almost entirely on each other,



<P>and Louisa, just recovering from illness, had been in an interesting

state,



<P>and Captain Benwick was not inconsolable.&nbsp; That was a point which

Anne



<P>had not been able to avoid suspecting before; and instead of drawing



<P>the same conclusion as Mary, from the present course of events,



<P>they served only to confirm the idea of his having felt some



<P>dawning of tenderness toward herself.&nbsp; She did not mean, however,



<P>to derive much more from it to gratify her vanity, than Mary



<P>might have allowed.&nbsp; She was persuaded that any tolerably pleasing



<P>young woman who had listened and seemed to feel for him would have



<P>received the same compliment.&nbsp; He had an affectionate heart.



<P>He must love somebody.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She saw no reason against their being happy.&nbsp; Louisa had fine



<P>naval fervour to begin with, and they would soon grow more alike.



<P>He would gain cheerfulness, and she would learn to be an enthusiast



<P>for Scott and Lord Byron; nay, that was probably learnt already;



<P>of course they had fallen in love over poetry.&nbsp; The idea of



<P>Louisa Musgrove turned into a person of literary taste,



<P>and sentimental reflection was amusing, but she had no doubt



<P>of its being so.&nbsp; The day at Lyme, the fall from the Cobb,



<P>might influence her health, her nerves, her courage, her character to



<P>the end of her life, as thoroughly as it appeared to have



<P>influenced her fate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The conclusion of the whole was, that if the woman who had been sensible



<P>of Captain Wentworth's merits could be allowed to prefer another man,



<P>there was nothing in the engagement to excite lasting wonder;



<P>and if Captain Wentworth lost no friend by it, certainly nothing



<P>to be regretted.&nbsp; No, it was not regret which made Anne's heart



<P>beat in spite of herself, and brought the colour into her cheeks



<P>when she thought of Captain Wentworth unshackled and free.



<P>She had some feelings which she was ashamed to investigate.



<P>They were too much like joy, senseless joy!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She longed to see the Crofts; but when the meeting took place,



<P>it was evident that no rumour of the news had yet reached them.



<P>The visit of ceremony was paid and returned; and Louisa Musgrove



<P>was mentioned, and Captain Benwick, too, without even half a smile.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The Crofts had placed themselves in lodgings in Gay Street,



<P>perfectly to Sir Walter's satisfaction.&nbsp; He was not at all ashamed



<P>of the acquaintance, and did, in fact, think and talk a great deal more



<P>about the Admiral, than the Admiral ever thought or talked about him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The Crofts knew quite as many people in Bath as they wished for,



<P>and considered their intercourse with the Elliots as a mere matter of

form,



<P>and not in the least likely to afford them any pleasure.



<P>They brought with them their country habit of being almost always together.



<P>He was ordered to walk to keep off the gout, and Mrs Croft



<P>seemed to go shares with him in everything, and to walk



<P>for her life to do him good.&nbsp; Anne saw them wherever she went.



<P>Lady Russell took her out in her carriage almost every morning,



<P>and she never failed to think of them, and never failed to see them.



<P>Knowing their feelings as she did, it was a most attractive picture



<P>of happiness to her.&nbsp; She always watched them as long as she could,



<P>delighted to fancy she understood what they might be talking of,



<P>as they walked along in happy independence, or equally delighted



<P>to see the Admiral's hearty shake of the hand when he encountered



<P>an old friend, and observe their eagerness of conversation



<P>when occasionally forming into a little knot of the navy, Mrs Croft



<P>looking as intelligent and keen as any of the officers around her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne was too much engaged with Lady Russell to be often walking herself;



<P>but it so happened that one morning, about a week or ten days



<P>after the Croft's arrival, it suited her best to leave her friend,



<P>or her friend's carriage, in the lower part of the town,



<P>and return alone to Camden Place, and in walking up Milsom Street



<P>she had the good fortune to meet with the Admiral.&nbsp; He was standing



<P>by himself at a printshop window, with his hands behind him,



<P>in earnest contemplation of some print, and she not only might have



<P>passed him unseen, but was obliged to touch as well as address him



<P>before she could catch his notice.&nbsp; When he did perceive and



<P>acknowledge her, however, it was done with all his usual frankness



<P>and good humour.&nbsp; "Ha! is it you?&nbsp; Thank you, thank you.



<P>This is treating me like a friend.&nbsp; Here I am, you see,



<P>staring at a picture.&nbsp; I can never get by this shop without stopping.



<P>But what a thing here is, by way of a boat!&nbsp; Do look at it.



<P>Did you ever see the like?&nbsp; What queer fellows your fine painters

must be,



<P>to think that anybody would venture their lives in such a shapeless



<P>old cockleshell as that?&nbsp; And yet here are two gentlemen



<P>stuck up in it mightily at their ease, and looking about them at the

rocks



<P>and mountains, as if they were not to be upset the next moment,



<P>which they certainly must be.&nbsp; I wonder where that boat was built!"



<P>(laughing heartily); "I would not venture over a horsepond in it.



<P>Well," (turning away), "now, where are you bound?&nbsp; Can I go anywhere



<P>for you, or with you?&nbsp; Can I be of any use?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"None, I thank you, unless you will give me the pleasure of your company



<P>the little way our road lies together.&nbsp; I am going home."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"That I will, with all my heart, and farther, too.&nbsp; Yes, yes



<P>we will have a snug walk together, and I have something to tell you



<P>as we go along.&nbsp; There, take my arm; that's right; I do not



<P>feel comfortable if I have not a woman there.&nbsp; Lord! what a boat

it is!"



<P>taking a last look at the picture, as they began to be in motion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Did you say that you had something to tell me, sir?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, I have, presently.&nbsp; But here comes a friend, Captain Brigden;



<P>I shall only say, `How d'ye do?' as we pass, however.&nbsp; I shall

not stop.



<P>`How d'ye do?'&nbsp; Brigden stares to see anybody with me but my wife.



<P>She, poor soul, is tied by the leg.&nbsp; She has a blister on one of

her heels,



<P>as large as a three-shilling piece.&nbsp; If you look across the street,



<P>you will see Admiral Brand coming down and his brother.&nbsp; Shabby

fellows,



<P>both of them!&nbsp; I am glad they are not on this side of the way.



<P>Sophy cannot bear them.&nbsp; They played me a pitiful trick once:



<P>got away with some of my best men.&nbsp; I will tell you the whole story



<P>another time.&nbsp; There comes old Sir Archibald Drew and his grandson.



<P>Look, he sees us; he kisses his hand to you; he takes you for my wife.



<P>Ah! the peace has come too soon for that younker.&nbsp; Poor old Sir

Archibald!



<P>How do you like Bath, Miss Elliot?&nbsp; It suits us very well.



<P>We are always meeting with some old friend or other; the streets



<P>full of them every morning; sure to have plenty of chat;



<P>and then we get away from them all, and shut ourselves in our lodgings,



<P>and draw in our chairs, and are snug as if we were at Kellynch,



<P>ay, or as we used to be even at North Yarmouth and Deal.



<P>We do not like our lodgings here the worse, I can tell you,



<P>for putting us in mind of those we first had at North Yarmouth.



<P>The wind blows through one of the cupboards just in the same way."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When they were got a little farther, Anne ventured to press again



<P>for what he had to communicate.&nbsp; She hoped when clear of Milsom

Street



<P>to have her curiosity gratified; but she was still obliged to wait,



<P>for the Admiral had made up his mind not to begin till they had



<P>gained the greater space and quiet of Belmont; and as she was



<P>not really Mrs Croft, she must let him have his own way.



<P>As soon as they were fairly ascending Belmont, he began--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, now you shall hear something that will surprise you.



<P>But first of all, you must tell me the name of the young lady



<P>I am going to talk about.&nbsp; That young lady, you know, that we have



<P>all been so concerned for.&nbsp; The Miss Musgrove, that all this has

been



<P>happening to.&nbsp; Her Christian name:&nbsp; I always forget her Christian

name."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anne had been ashamed to appear to comprehend so soon as she really



<P>did; but now she could safely suggest the name of "Louisa."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;
